    68	
    69	
    70	ADVENTURE I. A SCANDAL IN BOHEMIA
    71	
    72	I.
    73	
    74	To Sherlock Holmes she is always THE woman. I have seldom heard
    75	him mention her under any other name. In his eyes she eclipses
    76	and predominates the whole of her sex. It was not that he felt
    77	any emotion akin to love for Irene Adler. All emotions, and that
    78	one particularly, were abhorrent to his cold, precise but
    79	admirably balanced mind. He was, I take it, the most perfect
    80	reasoning and observing machine that the world has seen, but as a
    81	lover he would have placed himself in a false position. He never
    82	spoke of the softer passions, save with a gibe and a sneer. They
    83	were admirable things for the observer--excellent for drawing the
    84	veil from men's motives and actions. But for the trained reasoner
    85	to admit such intrusions into his own delicate and finely
    86	adjusted temperament was to introduce a distracting factor which
    87	might throw a doubt upon all his mental results. Grit in a
    88	sensitive instrument, or a crack in one of his own high-power
    89	lenses, would not be more disturbing than a strong emotion in a
    90	nature such as his. And yet there was but one woman to him, and
    91	that woman was the late Irene Adler, of dubious and questionable
    92	memory.
    93	
    94	I had seen little of Holmes lately. My marriage had drifted us
    95	away from each other. My own complete happiness, and the
    96	home-centred interests which rise up around the man who first
    97	finds himself master of his own establishment, were sufficient to
    98	absorb all my attention, while Holmes, who loathed every form of
    99	society with his whole Bohemian soul, remained in our lodgings in
   100	Baker Street, buried among his old books, and alternating from
   101	week to week between cocaine and ambition, the drowsiness of the
   102	drug, and the fierce energy of his own keen nature. He was still,
   103	as ever, deeply attracted by the study of crime, and occupied his
   104	immense faculties and extraordinary powers of observation in
   105	following out those clues, and clearing up those mysteries which
   106	had been abandoned as hopeless by the official police. From time
   107	to time I heard some vague account of his doings: of his summons
   108	to Odessa in the case of the Trepoff murder, of his clearing up
   109	of the singular tragedy of the Atkinson brothers at Trincomalee,
   110	and finally of the mission which he had accomplished so
   111	delicately and successfully for the reigning family of Holland.
   112	Beyond these signs of his activity, however, which I merely
   113	shared with all the readers of the daily press, I knew little of
   114	my former friend and companion.
   115	
   116	One night--it was on the twentieth of March, 1888--I was
   117	returning from a journey to a patient (for I had now returned to
   118	civil practice), when my way led me through Baker Street. As I
   119	passed the well-remembered door, which must always be associated
   120	in my mind with my wooing, and with the dark incidents of the
   121	Study in Scarlet, I was seized with a keen desire to see Holmes
   122	again, and to know how he was employing his extraordinary powers.
   123	His rooms were brilliantly lit, and, even as I looked up, I saw
   124	his tall, spare figure pass twice in a dark silhouette against
   125	the blind. He was pacing the room swiftly, eagerly, with his head
   126	sunk upon his chest and his hands clasped behind him. To me, who
   127	knew his every mood and habit, his attitude and manner told their
   128	own story. He was at work again. He had risen out of his
   129	drug-created dreams and was hot upon the scent of some new
   130	problem. I rang the bell and was shown up to the chamber which
   131	had formerly been in part my own.
   132	
   133	His manner was not effusive. It seldom was; but he was glad, I
   134	think, to see me. With hardly a word spoken, but with a kindly
   135	eye, he waved me to an armchair, threw across his case of cigars,
   136	and indicated a spirit case and a gasogene in the corner. Then he
   137	stood before the fire and looked me over in his singular
   138	introspective fashion.
   139	
   140	"Wedlock suits you," he remarked. "I think, Watson, that you have
   141	put on seven and a half pounds since I saw you."
   142	
   143	"Seven!" I answered.
   144	
   145	"Indeed, I should have thought a little more. Just a trifle more,
   146	I fancy, Watson. And in practice again, I observe. You did not
   147	tell me that you intended to go into harness."
   148	
   149	"Then, how do you know?"
   150	
   151	"I see it, I deduce it. How do I know that you have been getting
   152	yourself very wet lately, and that you have a most clumsy and
   153	careless servant girl?"
   154	
   155	"My dear Holmes," said I, "this is too much. You would certainly
   156	have been burned, had you lived a few centuries ago. It is true
   157	that I had a country walk on Thursday and came home in a dreadful
   158	mess, but as I have changed my clothes I can't imagine how you
   159	deduce it. As to Mary Jane, she is incorrigible, and my wife has
   160	given her notice, but there, again, I fail to see how you work it
   161	out."
   162	
   163	He chuckled to himself and rubbed his long, nervous hands
   164	together.
   165	
   166	"It is simplicity itself," said he; "my eyes tell me that on the
   167	inside of your left shoe, just where the firelight strikes it,
   168	the leather is scored by six almost parallel cuts. Obviously they
   169	have been caused by someone who has very carelessly scraped round
   170	the edges of the sole in order to remove crusted mud from it.
   171	Hence, you see, my double deduction that you had been out in vile
   172	weather, and that you had a particularly malignant boot-slitting
   173	specimen of the London slavey. As to your practice, if a
   174	gentleman walks into my rooms smelling of iodoform, with a black
   175	mark of nitrate of silver upon his right forefinger, and a bulge
   176	on the right side of his top-hat to show where he has secreted
   177	his stethoscope, I must be dull, indeed, if I do not pronounce
   178	him to be an active member of the medical profession."
   179	
   180	I could not help laughing at the ease with which he explained his
   181	process of deduction. "When I hear you give your reasons," I
   182	remarked, "the thing always appears to me to be so ridiculously
   183	simple that I could easily do it myself, though at each
   184	successive instance of your reasoning I am baffled until you
   185	explain your process. And yet I believe that my eyes are as good
   186	as yours."
   187	
   188	"Quite so," he answered, lighting a cigarette, and throwing
   189	himself down into an armchair. "You see, but you do not observe.
   190	The distinction is clear. For example, you have frequently seen
   191	the steps which lead up from the hall to this room."
   192	
   193	"Frequently."
   194	
   195	"How often?"
   196	
   197	"Well, some hundreds of times."
   198	
   199	"Then how many are there?"
   200	
   201	"How many? I don't know."
   202	
   203	"Quite so! You have not observed. And yet you have seen. That is
   204	just my point. Now, I know that there are seventeen steps,
   205	because I have both seen and observed. By-the-way, since you are
   206	interested in these little problems, and since you are good
   207	enough to chronicle one or two of my trifling experiences, you
   208	may be interested in this." He threw over a sheet of thick,
   209	pink-tinted note-paper which had been lying open upon the table.
   210	"It came by the last post," said he. "Read it aloud."
   211	
   212	The note was undated, and without either signature or address.
   213	
   214	"There will call upon you to-night, at a quarter to eight
   215	o'clock," it said, "a gentleman who desires to consult you upon a
   216	matter of the very deepest moment. Your recent services to one of
   217	the royal houses of Europe have shown that you are one who may
   218	safely be trusted with matters which are of an importance which
   219	can hardly be exaggerated. This account of you we have from all
   220	quarters received. Be in your chamber then at that hour, and do
   221	not take it amiss if your visitor wear a mask."
   222	
   223	"This is indeed a mystery," I remarked. "What do you imagine that
   224	it means?"
   225	
   226	"I have no data yet. It is a capital mistake to theorize before
   227	one has data. Insensibly one begins to twist facts to suit
   228	theories, instead of theories to suit facts. But the note itself.
   229	What do you deduce from it?"
   230	
   231	I carefully examined the writing, and the paper upon which it was
   232	written.
   233	
   234	"The man who wrote it was presumably well to do," I remarked,
   235	endeavouring to imitate my companion's processes. "Such paper
   236	could not be bought under half a crown a packet. It is peculiarly
   237	strong and stiff."
   238	
   239	"Peculiar--that is the very word," said Holmes. "It is not an
   240	English paper at all. Hold it up to the light."
   241	
   242	I did so, and saw a large "E" with a small "g," a "P," and a
   243	large "G" with a small "t" woven into the texture of the paper.
   244	
   245	"What do you make of that?" asked Holmes.
   246	
   247	"The name of the maker, no doubt; or his monogram, rather."
   248	
   249	"Not at all. The 'G' with the small 't' stands for
   250	'Gesellschaft,' which is the German for 'Company.' It is a
   251	customary contraction like our 'Co.' 'P,' of course, stands for
   252	'Papier.' Now for the 'Eg.' Let us glance at our Continental
   253	Gazetteer." He took down a heavy brown volume from his shelves.
   254	"Eglow, Eglonitz--here we are, Egria. It is in a German-speaking
   255	country--in Bohemia, not far from Carlsbad. 'Remarkable as being
   256	the scene of the death of Wallenstein, and for its numerous
   257	glass-factories and paper-mills.' Ha, ha, my boy, what do you
   258	make of that?" His eyes sparkled, and he sent up a great blue
   259	triumphant cloud from his cigarette.
   260	
   261	"The paper was made in Bohemia," I said.
   262	
   263	"Precisely. And the man who wrote the note is a German. Do you
   264	note the peculiar construction of the sentence--'This account of
   265	you we have from all quarters received.' A Frenchman or Russian
   266	could not have written that. It is the German who is so
   267	uncourteous to his verbs. It only remains, therefore, to discover
   268	what is wanted by this German who writes upon Bohemian paper and
   269	prefers wearing a mask to showing his face. And here he comes, if
   270	I am not mistaken, to resolve all our doubts."
   271	
   272	As he spoke there was the sharp sound of horses' hoofs and
   273	grating wheels against the curb, followed by a sharp pull at the
   274	bell. Holmes whistled.
   275	
   276	"A pair, by the sound," said he. "Yes," he continued, glancing
   277	out of the window. "A nice little brougham and a pair of
   278	beauties. A hundred and fifty guineas apiece. There's money in
   279	this case, Watson, if there is nothing else."
   280	
   281	"I think that I had better go, Holmes."
   282	
   283	"Not a bit, Doctor. Stay where you are. I am lost without my
   284	Boswell. And this promises to be interesting. It would be a pity
   285	to miss it."
   286	
   287	"But your client--"
   288	
   289	"Never mind him. I may want your help, and so may he. Here he
   290	comes. Sit down in that armchair, Doctor, and give us your best
   291	attention."
   292	
   293	A slow and heavy step, which had been heard upon the stairs and
   294	in the passage, paused immediately outside the door. Then there
   295	was a loud and authoritative tap.
   296	
   297	"Come in!" said Holmes.
   298	
   299	A man entered who could hardly have been less than six feet six
   300	inches in height, with the chest and limbs of a Hercules. His
   301	dress was rich with a richness which would, in England, be looked
   302	upon as akin to bad taste. Heavy bands of astrakhan were slashed
   303	across the sleeves and fronts of his double-breasted coat, while
   304	the deep blue cloak which was thrown over his shoulders was lined
   305	with flame-coloured silk and secured at the neck with a brooch
   306	which consisted of a single flaming beryl. Boots which extended
   307	halfway up his calves, and which were trimmed at the tops with
   308	rich brown fur, completed the impression of barbaric opulence
   309	which was suggested by his whole appearance. He carried a
   310	broad-brimmed hat in his hand, while he wore across the upper
   311	part of his face, extending down past the cheekbones, a black
   312	vizard mask, which he had apparently adjusted that very moment,
   313	for his hand was still raised to it as he entered. From the lower
   314	part of the face he appeared to be a man of strong character,
   315	with a thick, hanging lip, and a long, straight chin suggestive
   316	of resolution pushed to the length of obstinacy.
   317	
   318	"You had my note?" he asked with a deep harsh voice and a
   319	strongly marked German accent. "I told you that I would call." He
   320	looked from one to the other of us, as if uncertain which to
   321	address.
   322	
   323	"Pray take a seat," said Holmes. "This is my friend and
   324	colleague, Dr. Watson, who is occasionally good enough to help me
   325	in my cases. Whom have I the honour to address?"
   326	
   327	"You may address me as the Count Von Kramm, a Bohemian nobleman.
   328	I understand that this gentleman, your friend, is a man of honour
   329	and discretion, whom I may trust with a matter of the most
   330	extreme importance. If not, I should much prefer to communicate
   331	with you alone."
   332	
   333	I rose to go, but Holmes caught me by the wrist and pushed me
   334	back into my chair. "It is both, or none," said he. "You may say
   335	before this gentleman anything which you may say to me."
   336	
   337	The Count shrugged his broad shoulders. "Then I must begin," said
   338	he, "by binding you both to absolute secrecy for two years; at
   339	the end of that time the matter will be of no importance. At
   340	present it is not too much to say that it is of such weight it
   341	may have an influence upon European history."
   342	
   343	"I promise," said Holmes.
   344	
   345	"And I."
   346	
   347	"You will excuse this mask," continued our strange visitor. "The
   348	august person who employs me wishes his agent to be unknown to
   349	you, and I may confess at once that the title by which I have
   350	just called myself is not exactly my own."
   351	
   352	"I was aware of it," said Holmes dryly.
   353	
   354	"The circumstances are of great delicacy, and every precaution
   355	has to be taken to quench what might grow to be an immense
   356	scandal and seriously compromise one of the reigning families of
   357	Europe. To speak plainly, the matter implicates the great House
   358	of Ormstein, hereditary kings of Bohemia."
   359	
   360	"I was also aware of that," murmured Holmes, settling himself
   361	down in his armchair and closing his eyes.
   362	
   363	Our visitor glanced with some apparent surprise at the languid,
   364	lounging figure of the man who had been no doubt depicted to him
   365	as the most incisive reasoner and most energetic agent in Europe.
   366	Holmes slowly reopened his eyes and looked impatiently at his
   367	gigantic client.
   368	
   369	"If your Majesty would condescend to state your case," he
   370	remarked, "I should be better able to advise you."
   371	
   372	The man sprang from his chair and paced up and down the room in
   373	uncontrollable agitation. Then, with a gesture of desperation, he
   374	tore the mask from his face and hurled it upon the ground. "You
   375	are right," he cried; "I am the King. Why should I attempt to
   376	conceal it?"
   377	
   378	"Why, indeed?" murmured Holmes. "Your Majesty had not spoken
   379	before I was aware that I was addressing Wilhelm Gottsreich
   380	Sigismond von Ormstein, Grand Duke of Cassel-Felstein, and
   381	hereditary King of Bohemia."
   382	
   383	"But you can understand," said our strange visitor, sitting down
   384	once more and passing his hand over his high white forehead, "you
   385	can understand that I am not accustomed to doing such business in
   386	my own person. Yet the matter was so delicate that I could not
   387	confide it to an agent without putting myself in his power. I
   388	have come incognito from Prague for the purpose of consulting
   389	you."
   390	
   391	"Then, pray consult," said Holmes, shutting his eyes once more.
   392	
   393	"The facts are briefly these: Some five years ago, during a
   394	lengthy visit to Warsaw, I made the acquaintance of the well-known
   395	adventuress, Irene Adler. The name is no doubt familiar to you."
   396	
   397	"Kindly look her up in my index, Doctor," murmured Holmes without
   398	opening his eyes. For many years he had adopted a system of
   399	docketing all paragraphs concerning men and things, so that it
   400	was difficult to name a subject or a person on which he could not
   401	at once furnish information. In this case I found her biography
   402	sandwiched in between that of a Hebrew rabbi and that of a
   403	staff-commander who had written a monograph upon the deep-sea
   404	fishes.
   405	
   406	"Let me see!" said Holmes. "Hum! Born in New Jersey in the year
   407	1858. Contralto--hum! La Scala, hum! Prima donna Imperial Opera
   408	of Warsaw--yes! Retired from operatic stage--ha! Living in
   409	London--quite so! Your Majesty, as I understand, became entangled
   410	with this young person, wrote her some compromising letters, and
   411	is now desirous of getting those letters back."
   412	
   413	"Precisely so. But how--"
   414	
   415	"Was there a secret marriage?"
   416	
   417	"None."
   418	
   419	"No legal papers or certificates?"
   420	
   421	"None."
   422	
   423	"Then I fail to follow your Majesty. If this young person should
   424	produce her letters for blackmailing or other purposes, how is
   425	she to prove their authenticity?"
   426	
   427	"There is the writing."
   428	
   429	"Pooh, pooh! Forgery."
   430	
   431	"My private note-paper."
   432	
   433	"Stolen."
   434	
   435	"My own seal."
   436	
   437	"Imitated."
   438	
   439	"My photograph."
   440	
   441	"Bought."
   442	
   443	"We were both in the photograph."
   444	
   445	"Oh, dear! That is very bad! Your Majesty has indeed committed an
   446	indiscretion."
   447	
   448	"I was mad--insane."
   449	
   450	"You have compromised yourself seriously."
   451	
   452	"I was only Crown Prince then. I was young. I am but thirty now."
   453	
   454	"It must be recovered."
   455	
   456	"We have tried and failed."
   457	
   458	"Your Majesty must pay. It must be bought."
   459	
   460	"She will not sell."
   461	
   462	"Stolen, then."
   463	
   464	"Five attempts have been made. Twice burglars in my pay ransacked
   465	her house. Once we diverted her luggage when she travelled. Twice
   466	she has been waylaid. There has been no result."
   467	
   468	"No sign of it?"
   469	
   470	"Absolutely none."
   471	
   472	Holmes laughed. "It is quite a pretty little problem," said he.
   473	
   474	"But a very serious one to me," returned the King reproachfully.
   475	
   476	"Very, indeed. And what does she propose to do with the
   477	photograph?"
   478	
   479	"To ruin me."
   480	
   481	"But how?"
   482	
   483	"I am about to be married."
   484	
   485	"So I have heard."
   486	
   487	"To Clotilde Lothman von Saxe-Meningen, second daughter of the
   488	King of Scandinavia. You may know the strict principles of her
   489	family. She is herself the very soul of delicacy. A shadow of a
   490	doubt as to my conduct would bring the matter to an end."
   491	
   492	"And Irene Adler?"
   493	
   494	"Threatens to send them the photograph. And she will do it. I
   495	know that she will do it. You do not know her, but she has a soul
   496	of steel. She has the face of the most beautiful of women, and
   497	the mind of the most resolute of men. Rather than I should marry
   498	another woman, there are no lengths to which she would not
   499	go--none."
   500	
   501	"You are sure that she has not sent it yet?"
   502	
   503	"I am sure."
   504	
   505	"And why?"
   506	
   507	"Because she has said that she would send it on the day when the
   508	betrothal was publicly proclaimed. That will be next Monday."
   509	
   510	"Oh, then we have three days yet," said Holmes with a yawn. "That
   511	is very fortunate, as I have one or two matters of importance to
   512	look into just at present. Your Majesty will, of course, stay in
   513	London for the present?"
   514	
   515	"Certainly. You will find me at the Langham under the name of the
   516	Count Von Kramm."
   517	
   518	"Then I shall drop you a line to let you know how we progress."
   519	
   520	"Pray do so. I shall be all anxiety."
   521	
   522	"Then, as to money?"
   523	
   524	"You have carte blanche."
   525	
   526	"Absolutely?"
   527	
   528	"I tell you that I would give one of the provinces of my kingdom
   529	to have that photograph."
   530	
   531	"And for present expenses?"
   532	
   533	The King took a heavy chamois leather bag from under his cloak
   534	and laid it on the table.
   535	
   536	"There are three hundred pounds in gold and seven hundred in
   537	notes," he said.
   538	
   539	Holmes scribbled a receipt upon a sheet of his note-book and
   540	handed it to him.
   541	
   542	"And Mademoiselle's address?" he asked.
   543	
   544	"Is Briony Lodge, Serpentine Avenue, St. John's Wood."
   545	
   546	Holmes took a note of it. "One other question," said he. "Was the
   547	photograph a cabinet?"
   548	
   549	"It was."
   550	
   551	"Then, good-night, your Majesty, and I trust that we shall soon
   552	have some good news for you. And good-night, Watson," he added,
   553	as the wheels of the royal brougham rolled down the street. "If
   554	you will be good enough to call to-morrow afternoon at three
   555	o'clock I should like to chat this little matter over with you."
   556	
   557	
   558	II.
   559	
   560	At three o'clock precisely I was at Baker Street, but Holmes had
   561	not yet returned. The landlady informed me that he had left the
   562	house shortly after eight o'clock in the morning. I sat down
   563	beside the fire, however, with the intention of awaiting him,
   564	however long he might be. I was already deeply interested in his
   565	inquiry, for, though it was surrounded by none of the grim and
   566	strange features which were associated with the two crimes which
   567	I have already recorded, still, the nature of the case and the
   568	exalted station of his client gave it a character of its own.
   569	Indeed, apart from the nature of the investigation which my
   570	friend had on hand, there was something in his masterly grasp of
   571	a situation, and his keen, incisive reasoning, which made it a
   572	pleasure to me to study his system of work, and to follow the
   573	quick, subtle methods by which he disentangled the most
   574	inextricable mysteries. So accustomed was I to his invariable
   575	success that the very possibility of his failing had ceased to
   576	enter into my head.
   577	
   578	It was close upon four before the door opened, and a
   579	drunken-looking groom, ill-kempt and side-whiskered, with an
   580	inflamed face and disreputable clothes, walked into the room.
   581	Accustomed as I was to my friend's amazing powers in the use of
   582	disguises, I had to look three times before I was certain that it
   583	was indeed he. With a nod he vanished into the bedroom, whence he
   584	emerged in five minutes tweed-suited and respectable, as of old.
   585	Putting his hands into his pockets, he stretched out his legs in
   586	front of the fire and laughed heartily for some minutes.
   587	
   588	"Well, really!" he cried, and then he choked and laughed again
   589	until he was obliged to lie back, limp and helpless, in the
   590	chair.
   591	
   592	"What is it?"
   593	
   594	"It's quite too funny. I am sure you could never guess how I
   595	employed my morning, or what I ended by doing."
   596	
   597	"I can't imagine. I suppose that you have been watching the
   598	habits, and perhaps the house, of Miss Irene Adler."
   599	
   600	"Quite so; but the sequel was rather unusual. I will tell you,
   601	however. I left the house a little after eight o'clock this
   602	morning in the character of a groom out of work. There is a
   603	wonderful sympathy and freemasonry among horsey men. Be one of
   604	them, and you will know all that there is to know. I soon found
   605	Briony Lodge. It is a bijou villa, with a garden at the back, but
   606	built out in front right up to the road, two stories. Chubb lock
   607	to the door. Large sitting-room on the right side, well
   608	furnished, with long windows almost to the floor, and those
   609	preposterous English window fasteners which a child could open.
   610	Behind there was nothing remarkable, save that the passage window
   611	could be reached from the top of the coach-house. I walked round
   612	it and examined it closely from every point of view, but without
   613	noting anything else of interest.
   614	
   615	"I then lounged down the street and found, as I expected, that
   616	there was a mews in a lane which runs down by one wall of the
   617	garden. I lent the ostlers a hand in rubbing down their horses,
   618	and received in exchange twopence, a glass of half and half, two
   619	fills of shag tobacco, and as much information as I could desire
   620	about Miss Adler, to say nothing of half a dozen other people in
   621	the neighbourhood in whom I was not in the least interested, but
   622	whose biographies I was compelled to listen to."
   623	
   624	"And what of Irene Adler?" I asked.
   625	
   626	"Oh, she has turned all the men's heads down in that part. She is
   627	the daintiest thing under a bonnet on this planet. So say the
   628	Serpentine-mews, to a man. She lives quietly, sings at concerts,
   629	drives out at five every day, and returns at seven sharp for
   630	dinner. Seldom goes out at other times, except when she sings.
   631	Has only one male visitor, but a good deal of him. He is dark,
   632	handsome, and dashing, never calls less than once a day, and
   633	often twice. He is a Mr. Godfrey Norton, of the Inner Temple. See
   634	the advantages of a cabman as a confidant. They had driven him
   635	home a dozen times from Serpentine-mews, and knew all about him.
   636	When I had listened to all they had to tell, I began to walk up
   637	and down near Briony Lodge once more, and to think over my plan
   638	of campaign.
   639	
   640	"This Godfrey Norton was evidently an important factor in the
   641	matter. He was a lawyer. That sounded ominous. What was the
   642	relation between them, and what the object of his repeated
   643	visits? Was she his client, his friend, or his mistress? If the
   644	former, she had probably transferred the photograph to his
   645	keeping. If the latter, it was less likely. On the issue of this
   646	question depended whether I should continue my work at Briony
   647	Lodge, or turn my attention to the gentleman's chambers in the
   648	Temple. It was a delicate point, and it widened the field of my
   649	inquiry. I fear that I bore you with these details, but I have to
   650	let you see my little difficulties, if you are to understand the
   651	situation."
   652	
   653	"I am following you closely," I answered.
   654	
   655	"I was still balancing the matter in my mind when a hansom cab
   656	drove up to Briony Lodge, and a gentleman sprang out. He was a
   657	remarkably handsome man, dark, aquiline, and moustached--
   658	evidently the man of whom I had heard. He appeared to be in a
   659	great hurry, shouted to the cabman to wait, and brushed past the
   660	maid who opened the door with the air of a man who was thoroughly
   661	at home.
   662	
   663	"He was in the house about half an hour, and I could catch
   664	glimpses of him in the windows of the sitting-room, pacing up and
   665	down, talking excitedly, and waving his arms. Of her I could see
   666	nothing. Presently he emerged, looking even more flurried than
   667	before. As he stepped up to the cab, he pulled a gold watch from
   668	his pocket and looked at it earnestly, 'Drive like the devil,' he
   669	shouted, 'first to Gross & Hankey's in Regent Street, and then to
   670	the Church of St. Monica in the Edgeware Road. Half a guinea if
   671	you do it in twenty minutes!'
   672	
   673	"Away they went, and I was just wondering whether I should not do
   674	well to follow them when up the lane came a neat little landau,
   675	the coachman with his coat only half-buttoned, and his tie under
   676	his ear, while all the tags of his harness were sticking out of
   677	the buckles. It hadn't pulled up before she shot out of the hall
   678	door and into it. I only caught a glimpse of her at the moment,
   679	but she was a lovely woman, with a face that a man might die for.
   680	
   681	"'The Church of St. Monica, John,' she cried, 'and half a
   682	sovereign if you reach it in twenty minutes.'
   683	
   684	"This was quite too good to lose, Watson. I was just balancing
   685	whether I should run for it, or whether I should perch behind her
   686	landau when a cab came through the street. The driver looked
   687	twice at such a shabby fare, but I jumped in before he could
   688	object. 'The Church of St. Monica,' said I, 'and half a sovereign
   689	if you reach it in twenty minutes.' It was twenty-five minutes to
   690	twelve, and of course it was clear enough what was in the wind.
   691	
   692	"My cabby drove fast. I don't think I ever drove faster, but the
   693	others were there before us. The cab and the landau with their
   694	steaming horses were in front of the door when I arrived. I paid
   695	the man and hurried into the church. There was not a soul there
   696	save the two whom I had followed and a surpliced clergyman, who
   697	seemed to be expostulating with them. They were all three
   698	standing in a knot in front of the altar. I lounged up the side
   699	aisle like any other idler who has dropped into a church.
   700	Suddenly, to my surprise, the three at the altar faced round to
   701	me, and Godfrey Norton came running as hard as he could towards
   702	me.
   703	
   704	"'Thank God,' he cried. 'You'll do. Come! Come!'
   705	
   706	"'What then?' I asked.
   707	
   708	"'Come, man, come, only three minutes, or it won't be legal.'
   709	
   710	"I was half-dragged up to the altar, and before I knew where I was
   711	I found myself mumbling responses which were whispered in my ear,
   712	and vouching for things of which I knew nothing, and generally
   713	assisting in the secure tying up of Irene Adler, spinster, to
   714	Godfrey Norton, bachelor. It was all done in an instant, and
   715	there was the gentleman thanking me on the one side and the lady
   716	on the other, while the clergyman beamed on me in front. It was
   717	the most preposterous position in which I ever found myself in my
   718	life, and it was the thought of it that started me laughing just
   719	now. It seems that there had been some informality about their
   720	license, that the clergyman absolutely refused to marry them
   721	without a witness of some sort, and that my lucky appearance
   722	saved the bridegroom from having to sally out into the streets in
   723	search of a best man. The bride gave me a sovereign, and I mean
   724	to wear it on my watch-chain in memory of the occasion."
   725	
   726	"This is a very unexpected turn of affairs," said I; "and what
   727	then?"
   728	
   729	"Well, I found my plans very seriously menaced. It looked as if
   730	the pair might take an immediate departure, and so necessitate
   731	very prompt and energetic measures on my part. At the church
   732	door, however, they separated, he driving back to the Temple, and
   733	she to her own house. 'I shall drive out in the park at five as
   734	usual,' she said as she left him. I heard no more. They drove
   735	away in different directions, and I went off to make my own
   736	arrangements."
   737	
   738	"Which are?"
   739	
   740	"Some cold beef and a glass of beer," he answered, ringing the
   741	bell. "I have been too busy to think of food, and I am likely to
   742	be busier still this evening. By the way, Doctor, I shall want
   743	your co-operation."
   744	
   745	"I shall be delighted."
   746	
   747	"You don't mind breaking the law?"
   748	
   749	"Not in the least."
   750	
   751	"Nor running a chance of arrest?"
   752	
   753	"Not in a good cause."
   754	
   755	"Oh, the cause is excellent!"
   756	
   757	"Then I am your man."
   758	
   759	"I was sure that I might rely on you."
   760	
   761	"But what is it you wish?"
   762	
   763	"When Mrs. Turner has brought in the tray I will make it clear to
   764	you. Now," he said as he turned hungrily on the simple fare that
   765	our landlady had provided, "I must discuss it while I eat, for I
   766	have not much time. It is nearly five now. In two hours we must
   767	be on the scene of action. Miss Irene, or Madame, rather, returns
   768	from her drive at seven. We must be at Briony Lodge to meet her."
   769	
   770	"And what then?"
   771	
   772	"You must leave that to me. I have already arranged what is to
   773	occur. There is only one point on which I must insist. You must
   774	not interfere, come what may. You understand?"
   775	
   776	"I am to be neutral?"
   777	
   778	"To do nothing whatever. There will probably be some small
   779	unpleasantness. Do not join in it. It will end in my being
   780	conveyed into the house. Four or five minutes afterwards the
   781	sitting-room window will open. You are to station yourself close
   782	to that open window."
   783	
   784	"Yes."
   785	
   786	"You are to watch me, for I will be visible to you."
   787	
   788	"Yes."
   789	
   790	"And when I raise my hand--so--you will throw into the room what
   791	I give you to throw, and will, at the same time, raise the cry of
   792	fire. You quite follow me?"
   793	
   794	"Entirely."
   795	
   796	"It is nothing very formidable," he said, taking a long cigar-
   797	shaped roll from his pocket. "It is an ordinary plumber's smoke-
   798	rocket, fitted with a cap at either end to make it self-lighting.
   799	Your task is confined to that. When you raise your cry of fire,
   800	it will be taken up by quite a number of people. You may then
   801	walk to the end of the street, and I will rejoin you in ten
   802	minutes. I hope that I have made myself clear?"
   803	
   804	"I am to remain neutral, to get near the window, to watch you,
   805	and at the signal to throw in this object, then to raise the cry
   806	of fire, and to wait you at the corner of the street."
   807	
   808	"Precisely."
   809	
   810	"Then you may entirely rely on me."
   811	
   812	"That is excellent. I think, perhaps, it is almost time that I
   813	prepare for the new role I have to play."
   814	
   815	He disappeared into his bedroom and returned in a few minutes in
   816	the character of an amiable and simple-minded Nonconformist
   817	clergyman. His broad black hat, his baggy trousers, his white
   818	tie, his sympathetic smile, and general look of peering and
   819	benevolent curiosity were such as Mr. John Hare alone could have
   820	equalled. It was not merely that Holmes changed his costume. His
   821	expression, his manner, his very soul seemed to vary with every
   822	fresh part that he assumed. The stage lost a fine actor, even as
   823	science lost an acute reasoner, when he became a specialist in
   824	crime.
   825	
   826	It was a quarter past six when we left Baker Street, and it still
   827	wanted ten minutes to the hour when we found ourselves in
   828	Serpentine Avenue. It was already dusk, and the lamps were just
   829	being lighted as we paced up and down in front of Briony Lodge,
   830	waiting for the coming of its occupant. The house was just such
   831	as I had pictured it from Sherlock Holmes' succinct description,
   832	but the locality appeared to be less private than I expected. On
   833	the contrary, for a small street in a quiet neighbourhood, it was
   834	remarkably animated. There was a group of shabbily dressed men
   835	smoking and laughing in a corner, a scissors-grinder with his
   836	wheel, two guardsmen who were flirting with a nurse-girl, and
   837	several well-dressed young men who were lounging up and down with
   838	cigars in their mouths.
   839	
   840	"You see," remarked Holmes, as we paced to and fro in front of
   841	the house, "this marriage rather simplifies matters. The
   842	photograph becomes a double-edged weapon now. The chances are
   843	that she would be as averse to its being seen by Mr. Godfrey
   844	Norton, as our client is to its coming to the eyes of his
   845	princess. Now the question is, Where are we to find the
   846	photograph?"
   847	
   848	"Where, indeed?"
   849	
   850	"It is most unlikely that she carries it about with her. It is
   851	cabinet size. Too large for easy concealment about a woman's
   852	dress. She knows that the King is capable of having her waylaid
   853	and searched. Two attempts of the sort have already been made. We
   854	may take it, then, that she does not carry it about with her."
   855	
   856	"Where, then?"
   857	
   858	"Her banker or her lawyer. There is that double possibility. But
   859	I am inclined to think neither. Women are naturally secretive,
   860	and they like to do their own secreting. Why should she hand it
   861	over to anyone else? She could trust her own guardianship, but
   862	she could not tell what indirect or political influence might be
   863	brought to bear upon a business man. Besides, remember that she
   864	had resolved to use it within a few days. It must be where she
   865	can lay her hands upon it. It must be in her own house."
   866	
   867	"But it has twice been burgled."
   868	
   869	"Pshaw! They did not know how to look."
   870	
   871	"But how will you look?"
   872	
   873	"I will not look."
   874	
   875	"What then?"
   876	
   877	"I will get her to show me."
   878	
   879	"But she will refuse."
   880	
   881	"She will not be able to. But I hear the rumble of wheels. It is
   882	her carriage. Now carry out my orders to the letter."
   883	
   884	As he spoke the gleam of the side-lights of a carriage came round
   885	the curve of the avenue. It was a smart little landau which
   886	rattled up to the door of Briony Lodge. As it pulled up, one of
   887	the loafing men at the corner dashed forward to open the door in
   888	the hope of earning a copper, but was elbowed away by another
   889	loafer, who had rushed up with the same intention. A fierce
   890	quarrel broke out, which was increased by the two guardsmen, who
   891	took sides with one of the loungers, and by the scissors-grinder,
   892	who was equally hot upon the other side. A blow was struck, and
   893	in an instant the lady, who had stepped from her carriage, was
   894	the centre of a little knot of flushed and struggling men, who
   895	struck savagely at each other with their fists and sticks. Holmes
   896	dashed into the crowd to protect the lady; but just as he reached
   897	her he gave a cry and dropped to the ground, with the blood
   898	running freely down his face. At his fall the guardsmen took to
   899	their heels in one direction and the loungers in the other, while
   900	a number of better-dressed people, who had watched the scuffle
   901	without taking part in it, crowded in to help the lady and to
   902	attend to the injured man. Irene Adler, as I will still call her,
   903	had hurried up the steps; but she stood at the top with her
   904	superb figure outlined against the lights of the hall, looking
   905	back into the street.
   906	
   907	"Is the poor gentleman much hurt?" she asked.
   908	
   909	"He is dead," cried several voices.
   910	
   911	"No, no, there's life in him!" shouted another. "But he'll be
   912	gone before you can get him to hospital."
   913	
   914	"He's a brave fellow," said a woman. "They would have had the
   915	lady's purse and watch if it hadn't been for him. They were a
   916	gang, and a rough one, too. Ah, he's breathing now."
   917	
   918	"He can't lie in the street. May we bring him in, marm?"
   919	
   920	"Surely. Bring him into the sitting-room. There is a comfortable
   921	sofa. This way, please!"
   922	
   923	Slowly and solemnly he was borne into Briony Lodge and laid out
   924	in the principal room, while I still observed the proceedings
   925	from my post by the window. The lamps had been lit, but the
   926	blinds had not been drawn, so that I could see Holmes as he lay
   927	upon the couch. I do not know whether he was seized with
   928	compunction at that moment for the part he was playing, but I
   929	know that I never felt more heartily ashamed of myself in my life
   930	than when I saw the beautiful creature against whom I was
   931	conspiring, or the grace and kindliness with which she waited
   932	upon the injured man. And yet it would be the blackest treachery
   933	to Holmes to draw back now from the part which he had intrusted
   934	to me. I hardened my heart, and took the smoke-rocket from under
   935	my ulster. After all, I thought, we are not injuring her. We are
   936	but preventing her from injuring another.
   937	
   938	Holmes had sat up upon the couch, and I saw him motion like a man
   939	who is in need of air. A maid rushed across and threw open the
   940	window. At the same instant I saw him raise his hand and at the
   941	signal I tossed my rocket into the room with a cry of "Fire!" The
   942	word was no sooner out of my mouth than the whole crowd of
   943	spectators, well dressed and ill--gentlemen, ostlers, and
   944	servant-maids--joined in a general shriek of "Fire!" Thick clouds
   945	of smoke curled through the room and out at the open window. I
   946	caught a glimpse of rushing figures, and a moment later the voice
   947	of Holmes from within assuring them that it was a false alarm.
   948	Slipping through the shouting crowd I made my way to the corner
   949	of the street, and in ten minutes was rejoiced to find my
   950	friend's arm in mine, and to get away from the scene of uproar.
   951	He walked swiftly and in silence for some few minutes until we
   952	had turned down one of the quiet streets which lead towards the
   953	Edgeware Road.
   954	
   955	"You did it very nicely, Doctor," he remarked. "Nothing could
   956	have been better. It is all right."
   957	
   958	"You have the photograph?"
   959	
   960	"I know where it is."
   961	
   962	"And how did you find out?"
   963	
   964	"She showed me, as I told you she would."
   965	
   966	"I am still in the dark."
   967	
   968	"I do not wish to make a mystery," said he, laughing. "The matter
   969	was perfectly simple. You, of course, saw that everyone in the
   970	street was an accomplice. They were all engaged for the evening."
   971	
   972	"I guessed as much."
   973	
   974	"Then, when the row broke out, I had a little moist red paint in
   975	the palm of my hand. I rushed forward, fell down, clapped my hand
   976	to my face, and became a piteous spectacle. It is an old trick."
   977	
   978	"That also I could fathom."
   979	
   980	"Then they carried me in. She was bound to have me in. What else
   981	could she do? And into her sitting-room, which was the very room
   982	which I suspected. It lay between that and her bedroom, and I was
   983	determined to see which. They laid me on a couch, I motioned for
   984	air, they were compelled to open the window, and you had your
   985	chance."
   986	
   987	"How did that help you?"
   988	
   989	"It was all-important. When a woman thinks that her house is on
   990	fire, her instinct is at once to rush to the thing which she
   991	values most. It is a perfectly overpowering impulse, and I have
   992	more than once taken advantage of it. In the case of the
   993	Darlington substitution scandal it was of use to me, and also in
   994	the Arnsworth Castle business. A married woman grabs at her baby;
   995	an unmarried one reaches for her jewel-box. Now it was clear to
   996	me that our lady of to-day had nothing in the house more precious
   997	to her than what we are in quest of. She would rush to secure it.
   998	The alarm of fire was admirably done. The smoke and shouting were
   999	enough to shake nerves of steel. She responded beautifully. The
  1000	photograph is in a recess behind a sliding panel just above the
  1001	right bell-pull. She was there in an instant, and I caught a
  1002	glimpse of it as she half-drew it out. When I cried out that it
  1003	was a false alarm, she replaced it, glanced at the rocket, rushed
  1004	from the room, and I have not seen her since. I rose, and, making
  1005	my excuses, escaped from the house. I hesitated whether to
  1006	attempt to secure the photograph at once; but the coachman had
  1007	come in, and as he was watching me narrowly it seemed safer to
  1008	wait. A little over-precipitance may ruin all."
  1009	
  1010	"And now?" I asked.
  1011	
  1012	"Our quest is practically finished. I shall call with the King
  1013	to-morrow, and with you, if you care to come with us. We will be
  1014	shown into the sitting-room to wait for the lady, but it is
  1015	probable that when she comes she may find neither us nor the
  1016	photograph. It might be a satisfaction to his Majesty to regain
  1017	it with his own hands."
  1018	
  1019	"And when will you call?"
  1020	
  1021	"At eight in the morning. She will not be up, so that we shall
  1022	have a clear field. Besides, we must be prompt, for this marriage
  1023	may mean a complete change in her life and habits. I must wire to
  1024	the King without delay."
  1025	
  1026	We had reached Baker Street and had stopped at the door. He was
  1027	searching his pockets for the key when someone passing said:
  1028	
  1029	"Good-night, Mister Sherlock Holmes."
  1030	
  1031	There were several people on the pavement at the time, but the
  1032	greeting appeared to come from a slim youth in an ulster who had
  1033	hurried by.
  1034	
  1035	"I've heard that voice before," said Holmes, staring down the
  1036	dimly lit street. "Now, I wonder who the deuce that could have
  1037	been."
  1038	
  1039	
  1040	III.
  1041	
  1042	I slept at Baker Street that night, and we were engaged upon our
  1043	toast and coffee in the morning when the King of Bohemia rushed
  1044	into the room.
  1045	
  1046	"You have really got it!" he cried, grasping Sherlock Holmes by
  1047	either shoulder and looking eagerly into his face.
  1048	
  1049	"Not yet."
  1050	
  1051	"But you have hopes?"
  1052	
  1053	"I have hopes."
  1054	
  1055	"Then, come. I am all impatience to be gone."
  1056	
  1057	"We must have a cab."
  1058	
  1059	"No, my brougham is waiting."
  1060	
  1061	"Then that will simplify matters." We descended and started off
  1062	once more for Briony Lodge.
  1063	
  1064	"Irene Adler is married," remarked Holmes.
  1065	
  1066	"Married! When?"
  1067	
  1068	"Yesterday."
  1069	
  1070	"But to whom?"
  1071	
  1072	"To an English lawyer named Norton."
  1073	
  1074	"But she could not love him."
  1075	
  1076	"I am in hopes that she does."
  1077	
  1078	"And why in hopes?"
  1079	
  1080	"Because it would spare your Majesty all fear of future
  1081	annoyance. If the lady loves her husband, she does not love your
  1082	Majesty. If she does not love your Majesty, there is no reason
  1083	why she should interfere with your Majesty's plan."
  1084	
  1085	"It is true. And yet--Well! I wish she had been of my own
  1086	station! What a queen she would have made!" He relapsed into a
  1087	moody silence, which was not broken until we drew up in
  1088	Serpentine Avenue.
  1089	
  1090	The door of Briony Lodge was open, and an elderly woman stood
  1091	upon the steps. She watched us with a sardonic eye as we stepped
  1092	from the brougham.
  1093	
  1094	"Mr. Sherlock Holmes, I believe?" said she.
  1095	
  1096	"I am Mr. Holmes," answered my companion, looking at her with a
  1097	questioning and rather startled gaze.
  1098	
  1099	"Indeed! My mistress told me that you were likely to call. She
  1100	left this morning with her husband by the 5:15 train from Charing
  1101	Cross for the Continent."
  1102	
  1103	"What!" Sherlock Holmes staggered back, white with chagrin and
  1104	surprise. "Do you mean that she has left England?"
  1105	
  1106	"Never to return."
  1107	
  1108	"And the papers?" asked the King hoarsely. "All is lost."
  1109	
  1110	"We shall see." He pushed past the servant and rushed into the
  1111	drawing-room, followed by the King and myself. The furniture was
  1112	scattered about in every direction, with dismantled shelves and
  1113	open drawers, as if the lady had hurriedly ransacked them before
  1114	her flight. Holmes rushed at the bell-pull, tore back a small
  1115	sliding shutter, and, plunging in his hand, pulled out a
  1116	photograph and a letter. The photograph was of Irene Adler
  1117	herself in evening dress, the letter was superscribed to
  1118	"Sherlock Holmes, Esq. To be left till called for." My friend
  1119	tore it open and we all three read it together. It was dated at
  1120	midnight of the preceding night and ran in this way:
  1121	
  1122	"MY DEAR MR. SHERLOCK HOLMES,--You really did it very well. You
  1123	took me in completely. Until after the alarm of fire, I had not a
  1124	suspicion. But then, when I found how I had betrayed myself, I
  1125	began to think. I had been warned against you months ago. I had
  1126	been told that if the King employed an agent it would certainly
  1127	be you. And your address had been given me. Yet, with all this,
  1128	you made me reveal what you wanted to know. Even after I became
  1129	suspicious, I found it hard to think evil of such a dear, kind
  1130	old clergyman. But, you know, I have been trained as an actress
  1131	myself. Male costume is nothing new to me. I often take advantage
  1132	of the freedom which it gives. I sent John, the coachman, to
  1133	watch you, ran up stairs, got into my walking-clothes, as I call
  1134	them, and came down just as you departed.
  1135	
  1136	"Well, I followed you to your door, and so made sure that I was
  1137	really an object of interest to the celebrated Mr. Sherlock
  1138	Holmes. Then I, rather imprudently, wished you good-night, and
  1139	started for the Temple to see my husband.
  1140	
  1141	"We both thought the best resource was flight, when pursued by
  1142	so formidable an antagonist; so you will find the nest empty when
  1143	you call to-morrow. As to the photograph, your client may rest in
  1144	peace. I love and am loved by a better man than he. The King may
  1145	do what he will without hindrance from one whom he has cruelly
  1146	wronged. I keep it only to safeguard myself, and to preserve a
  1147	weapon which will always secure me from any steps which he might
  1148	take in the future. I leave a photograph which he might care to
  1149	possess; and I remain, dear Mr. Sherlock Holmes,
  1150	
  1151	                                      "Very truly yours,
  1152	                                   "IRENE NORTON, n?e ADLER."
  1153	
  1154	"What a woman--oh, what a woman!" cried the King of Bohemia, when
  1155	we had all three read this epistle. "Did I not tell you how quick
  1156	and resolute she was? Would she not have made an admirable queen?
  1157	Is it not a pity that she was not on my level?"
  1158	
  1159	"From what I have seen of the lady she seems indeed to be on a
  1160	very different level to your Majesty," said Holmes coldly. "I am
  1161	sorry that I have not been able to bring your Majesty's business
  1162	to a more successful conclusion."
  1163	
  1164	"On the contrary, my dear sir," cried the King; "nothing could be
  1165	more successful. I know that her word is inviolate. The
  1166	photograph is now as safe as if it were in the fire."
  1167	
  1168	"I am glad to hear your Majesty say so."
  1169	
  1170	"I am immensely indebted to you. Pray tell me in what way I can
  1171	reward you. This ring--" He slipped an emerald snake ring from
  1172	his finger and held it out upon the palm of his hand.
  1173	
  1174	"Your Majesty has something which I should value even more
  1175	highly," said Holmes.
  1176	
  1177	"You have but to name it."
  1178	
  1179	"This photograph!"
  1180	
  1181	The King stared at him in amazement.
  1182	
  1183	"Irene's photograph!" he cried. "Certainly, if you wish it."
  1184	
  1185	"I thank your Majesty. Then there is no more to be done in the
  1186	matter. I have the honour to wish you a very good-morning." He
  1187	bowed, and, turning away without observing the hand which the
  1188	King had stretched out to him, he set off in my company for his
  1189	chambers.
  1190	
  1191	And that was how a great scandal threatened to affect the kingdom
  1192	of Bohemia, and how the best plans of Mr. Sherlock Holmes were
  1193	beaten by a woman's wit. He used to make merry over the
  1194	cleverness of women, but I have not heard him do it of late. And
  1195	when he speaks of Irene Adler, or when he refers to her
  1196	photograph, it is always under the honourable title of the woman.
  1197	
  1198	
  1199	
  1200	ADVENTURE II. THE RED-HEADED LEAGUE
  1201	
  1202	I had called upon my friend, Mr. Sherlock Holmes, one day in the
  1203	autumn of last year and found him in deep conversation with a
  1204	very stout, florid-faced, elderly gentleman with fiery red hair.
  1205	With an apology for my intrusion, I was about to withdraw when
  1206	Holmes pulled me abruptly into the room and closed the door
  1207	behind me.
  1208	
  1209	"You could not possibly have come at a better time, my dear
  1210	Watson," he said cordially.
  1211	
  1212	"I was afraid that you were engaged."
  1213	
  1214	"So I am. Very much so."
  1215	
  1216	"Then I can wait in the next room."
  1217	
  1218	"Not at all. This gentleman, Mr. Wilson, has been my partner and
  1219	helper in many of my most successful cases, and I have no
  1220	doubt that he will be of the utmost use to me in yours also."
  1221	
  1222	The stout gentleman half rose from his chair and gave a bob of
  1223	greeting, with a quick little questioning glance from his small
  1224	fat-encircled eyes.
  1225	
  1226	"Try the settee," said Holmes, relapsing into his armchair and
  1227	putting his fingertips together, as was his custom when in
  1228	judicial moods. "I know, my dear Watson, that you share my love
  1229	of all that is bizarre and outside the conventions and humdrum
  1230	routine of everyday life. You have shown your relish for it by
  1231	the enthusiasm which has prompted you to chronicle, and, if you
  1232	will excuse my saying so, somewhat to embellish so many of my own
  1233	little adventures."
  1234	
  1235	"Your cases have indeed been of the greatest interest to me," I
  1236	observed.
  1237	
  1238	"You will remember that I remarked the other day, just before we
  1239	went into the very simple problem presented by Miss Mary
  1240	Sutherland, that for strange effects and extraordinary
  1241	combinations we must go to life itself, which is always far more
  1242	daring than any effort of the imagination."
  1243	
  1244	"A proposition which I took the liberty of doubting."
  1245	
  1246	"You did, Doctor, but none the less you must come round to my
  1247	view, for otherwise I shall keep on piling fact upon fact on you
  1248	until your reason breaks down under them and acknowledges me to
  1249	be right. Now, Mr. Jabez Wilson here has been good enough to call
  1250	upon me this morning, and to begin a narrative which promises to
  1251	be one of the most singular which I have listened to for some
  1252	time. You have heard me remark that the strangest and most unique
  1253	things are very often connected not with the larger but with the
  1254	smaller crimes, and occasionally, indeed, where there is room for
  1255	doubt whether any positive crime has been committed. As far as I
  1256	have heard it is impossible for me to say whether the present
  1257	case is an instance of crime or not, but the course of events is
  1258	certainly among the most singular that I have ever listened to.
  1259	Perhaps, Mr. Wilson, you would have the great kindness to
  1260	recommence your narrative. I ask you not merely because my friend
  1261	Dr. Watson has not heard the opening part but also because the
  1262	peculiar nature of the story makes me anxious to have every
  1263	possible detail from your lips. As a rule, when I have heard some
  1264	slight indication of the course of events, I am able to guide
  1265	myself by the thousands of other similar cases which occur to my
  1266	memory. In the present instance I am forced to admit that the
  1267	facts are, to the best of my belief, unique."
  1268	
  1269	The portly client puffed out his chest with an appearance of some
  1270	little pride and pulled a dirty and wrinkled newspaper from the
  1271	inside pocket of his greatcoat. As he glanced down the
  1272	advertisement column, with his head thrust forward and the paper
  1273	flattened out upon his knee, I took a good look at the man and
  1274	endeavoured, after the fashion of my companion, to read the
  1275	indications which might be presented by his dress or appearance.
  1276	
  1277	I did not gain very much, however, by my inspection. Our visitor
  1278	bore every mark of being an average commonplace British
  1279	tradesman, obese, pompous, and slow. He wore rather baggy grey
  1280	shepherd's check trousers, a not over-clean black frock-coat,
  1281	unbuttoned in the front, and a drab waistcoat with a heavy brassy
  1282	Albert chain, and a square pierced bit of metal dangling down as
  1283	an ornament. A frayed top-hat and a faded brown overcoat with a
  1284	wrinkled velvet collar lay upon a chair beside him. Altogether,
  1285	look as I would, there was nothing remarkable about the man save
  1286	his blazing red head, and the expression of extreme chagrin and
  1287	discontent upon his features.
  1288	
  1289	Sherlock Holmes' quick eye took in my occupation, and he shook
  1290	his head with a smile as he noticed my questioning glances.
  1291	"Beyond the obvious facts that he has at some time done manual
  1292	labour, that he takes snuff, that he is a Freemason, that he has
  1293	been in China, and that he has done a considerable amount of
  1294	writing lately, I can deduce nothing else."
  1295	
  1296	Mr. Jabez Wilson started up in his chair, with his forefinger
  1297	upon the paper, but his eyes upon my companion.
  1298	
  1299	"How, in the name of good-fortune, did you know all that, Mr.
  1300	Holmes?" he asked. "How did you know, for example, that I did
  1301	manual labour. It's as true as gospel, for I began as a ship's
  1302	carpenter."
  1303	
  1304	"Your hands, my dear sir. Your right hand is quite a size larger
  1305	than your left. You have worked with it, and the muscles are more
  1306	developed."
  1307	
  1308	"Well, the snuff, then, and the Freemasonry?"
  1309	
  1310	"I won't insult your intelligence by telling you how I read that,
  1311	especially as, rather against the strict rules of your order, you
  1312	use an arc-and-compass breastpin."
  1313	
  1314	"Ah, of course, I forgot that. But the writing?"
  1315	
  1316	"What else can be indicated by that right cuff so very shiny for
  1317	five inches, and the left one with the smooth patch near the
  1318	elbow where you rest it upon the desk?"
  1319	
  1320	"Well, but China?"
  1321	
  1322	"The fish that you have tattooed immediately above your right
  1323	wrist could only have been done in China. I have made a small
  1324	study of tattoo marks and have even contributed to the literature
  1325	of the subject. That trick of staining the fishes' scales of a
  1326	delicate pink is quite peculiar to China. When, in addition, I
  1327	see a Chinese coin hanging from your watch-chain, the matter
  1328	becomes even more simple."
  1329	
  1330	Mr. Jabez Wilson laughed heavily. "Well, I never!" said he. "I
  1331	thought at first that you had done something clever, but I see
  1332	that there was nothing in it, after all."
  1333	
  1334	"I begin to think, Watson," said Holmes, "that I make a mistake
  1335	in explaining. 'Omne ignotum pro magnifico,' you know, and my
  1336	poor little reputation, such as it is, will suffer shipwreck if I
  1337	am so candid. Can you not find the advertisement, Mr. Wilson?"
  1338	
  1339	"Yes, I have got it now," he answered with his thick red finger
  1340	planted halfway down the column. "Here it is. This is what began
  1341	it all. You just read it for yourself, sir."
  1342	
  1343	I took the paper from him and read as follows:
  1344	
  1345	"TO THE RED-HEADED LEAGUE: On account of the bequest of the late
  1346	Ezekiah Hopkins, of Lebanon, Pennsylvania, U. S. A., there is now
  1347	another vacancy open which entitles a member of the League to a
  1348	salary of 4 pounds a week for purely nominal services. All
  1349	red-headed men who are sound in body and mind and above the age
  1350	of twenty-one years, are eligible. Apply in person on Monday, at
  1351	eleven o'clock, to Duncan Ross, at the offices of the League, 7
  1352	Pope's Court, Fleet Street."
  1353	
  1354	"What on earth does this mean?" I ejaculated after I had twice
  1355	read over the extraordinary announcement.
  1356	
  1357	Holmes chuckled and wriggled in his chair, as was his habit when
  1358	in high spirits. "It is a little off the beaten track, isn't it?"
  1359	said he. "And now, Mr. Wilson, off you go at scratch and tell us
  1360	all about yourself, your household, and the effect which this
  1361	advertisement had upon your fortunes. You will first make a note,
  1362	Doctor, of the paper and the date."
  1363	
  1364	"It is The Morning Chronicle of April 27, 1890. Just two months
  1365	ago."
  1366	
  1367	"Very good. Now, Mr. Wilson?"
  1368	
  1369	"Well, it is just as I have been telling you, Mr. Sherlock
  1370	Holmes," said Jabez Wilson, mopping his forehead; "I have a small
  1371	pawnbroker's business at Coburg Square, near the City. It's not a
  1372	very large affair, and of late years it has not done more than
  1373	just give me a living. I used to be able to keep two assistants,
  1374	but now I only keep one; and I would have a job to pay him but
  1375	that he is willing to come for half wages so as to learn the
  1376	business."
  1377	
  1378	"What is the name of this obliging youth?" asked Sherlock Holmes.
  1379	
  1380	"His name is Vincent Spaulding, and he's not such a youth,
  1381	either. It's hard to say his age. I should not wish a smarter
  1382	assistant, Mr. Holmes; and I know very well that he could better
  1383	himself and earn twice what I am able to give him. But, after
  1384	all, if he is satisfied, why should I put ideas in his head?"
  1385	
  1386	"Why, indeed? You seem most fortunate in having an employ? who
  1387	comes under the full market price. It is not a common experience
  1388	among employers in this age. I don't know that your assistant is
  1389	not as remarkable as your advertisement."
  1390	
  1391	"Oh, he has his faults, too," said Mr. Wilson. "Never was such a
  1392	fellow for photography. Snapping away with a camera when he ought
  1393	to be improving his mind, and then diving down into the cellar
  1394	like a rabbit into its hole to develop his pictures. That is his
  1395	main fault, but on the whole he's a good worker. There's no vice
  1396	in him."
  1397	
  1398	"He is still with you, I presume?"
  1399	
  1400	"Yes, sir. He and a girl of fourteen, who does a bit of simple
  1401	cooking and keeps the place clean--that's all I have in the
  1402	house, for I am a widower and never had any family. We live very
  1403	quietly, sir, the three of us; and we keep a roof over our heads
  1404	and pay our debts, if we do nothing more.
  1405	
  1406	"The first thing that put us out was that advertisement.
  1407	Spaulding, he came down into the office just this day eight
  1408	weeks, with this very paper in his hand, and he says:
  1409	
  1410	"'I wish to the Lord, Mr. Wilson, that I was a red-headed man.'
  1411	
  1412	"'Why that?' I asks.
  1413	
  1414	"'Why,' says he, 'here's another vacancy on the League of the
  1415	Red-headed Men. It's worth quite a little fortune to any man who
  1416	gets it, and I understand that there are more vacancies than
  1417	there are men, so that the trustees are at their wits' end what
  1418	to do with the money. If my hair would only change colour, here's
  1419	a nice little crib all ready for me to step into.'
  1420	
  1421	"'Why, what is it, then?' I asked. You see, Mr. Holmes, I am a
  1422	very stay-at-home man, and as my business came to me instead of
  1423	my having to go to it, I was often weeks on end without putting
  1424	my foot over the door-mat. In that way I didn't know much of what
  1425	was going on outside, and I was always glad of a bit of news.
  1426	
  1427	"'Have you never heard of the League of the Red-headed Men?' he
  1428	asked with his eyes open.
  1429	
  1430	"'Never.'
  1431	
  1432	"'Why, I wonder at that, for you are eligible yourself for one
  1433	of the vacancies.'
  1434	
  1435	"'And what are they worth?' I asked.
  1436	
  1437	"'Oh, merely a couple of hundred a year, but the work is slight,
  1438	and it need not interfere very much with one's other
  1439	occupations.'
  1440	
  1441	"Well, you can easily think that that made me prick up my ears,
  1442	for the business has not been over-good for some years, and an
  1443	extra couple of hundred would have been very handy.
  1444	
  1445	"'Tell me all about it,' said I.
  1446	
  1447	"'Well,' said he, showing me the advertisement, 'you can see for
  1448	yourself that the League has a vacancy, and there is the address
  1449	where you should apply for particulars. As far as I can make out,
  1450	the League was founded by an American millionaire, Ezekiah
  1451	Hopkins, who was very peculiar in his ways. He was himself
  1452	red-headed, and he had a great sympathy for all red-headed men;
  1453	so when he died it was found that he had left his enormous
  1454	fortune in the hands of trustees, with instructions to apply the
  1455	interest to the providing of easy berths to men whose hair is of
  1456	that colour. From all I hear it is splendid pay and very little to
  1457	do.'
  1458	
  1459	"'But,' said I, 'there would be millions of red-headed men who
  1460	would apply.'
  1461	
  1462	"'Not so many as you might think,' he answered. 'You see it is
  1463	really confined to Londoners, and to grown men. This American had
  1464	started from London when he was young, and he wanted to do the
  1465	old town a good turn. Then, again, I have heard it is no use your
  1466	applying if your hair is light red, or dark red, or anything but
  1467	real bright, blazing, fiery red. Now, if you cared to apply, Mr.
  1468	Wilson, you would just walk in; but perhaps it would hardly be
  1469	worth your while to put yourself out of the way for the sake of a
  1470	few hundred pounds.'
  1471	
  1472	"Now, it is a fact, gentlemen, as you may see for yourselves,
  1473	that my hair is of a very full and rich tint, so that it seemed
  1474	to me that if there was to be any competition in the matter I
  1475	stood as good a chance as any man that I had ever met. Vincent
  1476	Spaulding seemed to know so much about it that I thought he might
  1477	prove useful, so I just ordered him to put up the shutters for
  1478	the day and to come right away with me. He was very willing to
  1479	have a holiday, so we shut the business up and started off for
  1480	the address that was given us in the advertisement.
  1481	
  1482	"I never hope to see such a sight as that again, Mr. Holmes. From
  1483	north, south, east, and west every man who had a shade of red in
  1484	his hair had tramped into the city to answer the advertisement.
  1485	Fleet Street was choked with red-headed folk, and Pope's Court
  1486	looked like a coster's orange barrow. I should not have thought
  1487	there were so many in the whole country as were brought together
  1488	by that single advertisement. Every shade of colour they
  1489	were--straw, lemon, orange, brick, Irish-setter, liver, clay;
  1490	but, as Spaulding said, there were not many who had the real
  1491	vivid flame-coloured tint. When I saw how many were waiting, I
  1492	would have given it up in despair; but Spaulding would not hear
  1493	of it. How he did it I could not imagine, but he pushed and
  1494	pulled and butted until he got me through the crowd, and right up
  1495	to the steps which led to the office. There was a double stream
  1496	upon the stair, some going up in hope, and some coming back
  1497	dejected; but we wedged in as well as we could and soon found
  1498	ourselves in the office."
  1499	
  1500	"Your experience has been a most entertaining one," remarked
  1501	Holmes as his client paused and refreshed his memory with a huge
  1502	pinch of snuff. "Pray continue your very interesting statement."
  1503	
  1504	"There was nothing in the office but a couple of wooden chairs
  1505	and a deal table, behind which sat a small man with a head that
  1506	was even redder than mine. He said a few words to each candidate
  1507	as he came up, and then he always managed to find some fault in
  1508	them which would disqualify them. Getting a vacancy did not seem
  1509	to be such a very easy matter, after all. However, when our turn
  1510	came the little man was much more favourable to me than to any of
  1511	the others, and he closed the door as we entered, so that he
  1512	might have a private word with us.
  1513	
  1514	"'This is Mr. Jabez Wilson,' said my assistant, 'and he is
  1515	willing to fill a vacancy in the League.'
  1516	
  1517	"'And he is admirably suited for it,' the other answered. 'He has
  1518	every requirement. I cannot recall when I have seen anything so
  1519	fine.' He took a step backward, cocked his head on one side, and
  1520	gazed at my hair until I felt quite bashful. Then suddenly he
  1521	plunged forward, wrung my hand, and congratulated me warmly on my
  1522	success.
  1523	
  1524	"'It would be injustice to hesitate,' said he. 'You will,
  1525	however, I am sure, excuse me for taking an obvious precaution.'
  1526	With that he seized my hair in both his hands, and tugged until I
  1527	yelled with the pain. 'There is water in your eyes,' said he as
  1528	he released me. 'I perceive that all is as it should be. But we
  1529	have to be careful, for we have twice been deceived by wigs and
  1530	once by paint. I could tell you tales of cobbler's wax which
  1531	would disgust you with human nature.' He stepped over to the
  1532	window and shouted through it at the top of his voice that the
  1533	vacancy was filled. A groan of disappointment came up from below,
  1534	and the folk all trooped away in different directions until there
  1535	was not a red-head to be seen except my own and that of the
  1536	manager.
  1537	
  1538	"'My name,' said he, 'is Mr. Duncan Ross, and I am myself one of
  1539	the pensioners upon the fund left by our noble benefactor. Are
  1540	you a married man, Mr. Wilson? Have you a family?'
  1541	
  1542	"I answered that I had not.
  1543	
  1544	"His face fell immediately.
  1545	
  1546	"'Dear me!' he said gravely, 'that is very serious indeed! I am
  1547	sorry to hear you say that. The fund was, of course, for the
  1548	propagation and spread of the red-heads as well as for their
  1549	maintenance. It is exceedingly unfortunate that you should be a
  1550	bachelor.'
  1551	
  1552	"My face lengthened at this, Mr. Holmes, for I thought that I was
  1553	not to have the vacancy after all; but after thinking it over for
  1554	a few minutes he said that it would be all right.
  1555	
  1556	"'In the case of another,' said he, 'the objection might be
  1557	fatal, but we must stretch a point in favour of a man with such a
  1558	head of hair as yours. When shall you be able to enter upon your
  1559	new duties?'
  1560	
  1561	"'Well, it is a little awkward, for I have a business already,'
  1562	said I.
  1563	
  1564	"'Oh, never mind about that, Mr. Wilson!' said Vincent Spaulding.
  1565	'I should be able to look after that for you.'
  1566	
  1567	"'What would be the hours?' I asked.
  1568	
  1569	"'Ten to two.'
  1570	
  1571	"Now a pawnbroker's business is mostly done of an evening, Mr.
  1572	Holmes, especially Thursday and Friday evening, which is just
  1573	before pay-day; so it would suit me very well to earn a little in
  1574	the mornings. Besides, I knew that my assistant was a good man,
  1575	and that he would see to anything that turned up.
  1576	
  1577	"'That would suit me very well,' said I. 'And the pay?'
  1578	
  1579	"'Is 4 pounds a week.'
  1580	
  1581	"'And the work?'
  1582	
  1583	"'Is purely nominal.'
  1584	
  1585	"'What do you call purely nominal?'
  1586	
  1587	"'Well, you have to be in the office, or at least in the
  1588	building, the whole time. If you leave, you forfeit your whole
  1589	position forever. The will is very clear upon that point. You
  1590	don't comply with the conditions if you budge from the office
  1591	during that time.'
  1592	
  1593	"'It's only four hours a day, and I should not think of leaving,'
  1594	said I.
  1595	
  1596	"'No excuse will avail,' said Mr. Duncan Ross; 'neither sickness
  1597	nor business nor anything else. There you must stay, or you lose
  1598	your billet.'
  1599	
  1600	"'And the work?'
  1601	
  1602	"'Is to copy out the "Encyclopaedia Britannica." There is the first
  1603	volume of it in that press. You must find your own ink, pens, and
  1604	blotting-paper, but we provide this table and chair. Will you be
  1605	ready to-morrow?'
  1606	
  1607	"'Certainly,' I answered.
  1608	
  1609	"'Then, good-bye, Mr. Jabez Wilson, and let me congratulate you
  1610	once more on the important position which you have been fortunate
  1611	enough to gain.' He bowed me out of the room and I went home with
  1612	my assistant, hardly knowing what to say or do, I was so pleased
  1613	at my own good fortune.
  1614	
  1615	"Well, I thought over the matter all day, and by evening I was in
  1616	low spirits again; for I had quite persuaded myself that the
  1617	whole affair must be some great hoax or fraud, though what its
  1618	object might be I could not imagine. It seemed altogether past
  1619	belief that anyone could make such a will, or that they would pay
  1620	such a sum for doing anything so simple as copying out the
  1621	'Encyclopaedia Britannica.' Vincent Spaulding did what he could to
  1622	cheer me up, but by bedtime I had reasoned myself out of the
  1623	whole thing. However, in the morning I determined to have a look
  1624	at it anyhow, so I bought a penny bottle of ink, and with a
  1625	quill-pen, and seven sheets of foolscap paper, I started off for
  1626	Pope's Court.
  1627	
  1628	"Well, to my surprise and delight, everything was as right as
  1629	possible. The table was set out ready for me, and Mr. Duncan Ross
  1630	was there to see that I got fairly to work. He started me off
  1631	upon the letter A, and then he left me; but he would drop in from
  1632	time to time to see that all was right with me. At two o'clock he
  1633	bade me good-day, complimented me upon the amount that I had
  1634	written, and locked the door of the office after me.
  1635	
  1636	"This went on day after day, Mr. Holmes, and on Saturday the
  1637	manager came in and planked down four golden sovereigns for my
  1638	week's work. It was the same next week, and the same the week
  1639	after. Every morning I was there at ten, and every afternoon I
  1640	left at two. By degrees Mr. Duncan Ross took to coming in only
  1641	once of a morning, and then, after a time, he did not come in at
  1642	all. Still, of course, I never dared to leave the room for an
  1643	instant, for I was not sure when he might come, and the billet
  1644	was such a good one, and suited me so well, that I would not risk
  1645	the loss of it.
  1646	
  1647	"Eight weeks passed away like this, and I had written about
  1648	Abbots and Archery and Armour and Architecture and Attica, and
  1649	hoped with diligence that I might get on to the B's before very
  1650	long. It cost me something in foolscap, and I had pretty nearly
  1651	filled a shelf with my writings. And then suddenly the whole
  1652	business came to an end."
  1653	
  1654	"To an end?"
  1655	
  1656	"Yes, sir. And no later than this morning. I went to my work as
  1657	usual at ten o'clock, but the door was shut and locked, with a
  1658	little square of cardboard hammered on to the middle of the
  1659	panel with a tack. Here it is, and you can read for yourself."
  1660	
  1661	He held up a piece of white cardboard about the size of a sheet
  1662	of note-paper. It read in this fashion:
