  1663	
  1664	                  THE RED-HEADED LEAGUE
  1665	
  1666	                           IS
  1667	
  1668	                        DISSOLVED.
  1669	
  1670	                     October 9, 1890.
  1671	
  1672	Sherlock Holmes and I surveyed this curt announcement and the
  1673	rueful face behind it, until the comical side of the affair so
  1674	completely overtopped every other consideration that we both
  1675	burst out into a roar of laughter.
  1676	
  1677	"I cannot see that there is anything very funny," cried our
  1678	client, flushing up to the roots of his flaming head. "If you can
  1679	do nothing better than laugh at me, I can go elsewhere."
  1680	
  1681	"No, no," cried Holmes, shoving him back into the chair from
  1682	which he had half risen. "I really wouldn't miss your case for
  1683	the world. It is most refreshingly unusual. But there is, if you
  1684	will excuse my saying so, something just a little funny about it.
  1685	Pray what steps did you take when you found the card upon the
  1686	door?"
  1687	
  1688	"I was staggered, sir. I did not know what to do. Then I called
  1689	at the offices round, but none of them seemed to know anything
  1690	about it. Finally, I went to the landlord, who is an accountant
  1691	living on the ground-floor, and I asked him if he could tell me
  1692	what had become of the Red-headed League. He said that he had
  1693	never heard of any such body. Then I asked him who Mr. Duncan
  1694	Ross was. He answered that the name was new to him.
  1695	
  1696	"'Well,' said I, 'the gentleman at No. 4.'
  1697	
  1698	"'What, the red-headed man?'
  1699	
  1700	"'Yes.'
  1701	
  1702	"'Oh,' said he, 'his name was William Morris. He was a solicitor
  1703	and was using my room as a temporary convenience until his new
  1704	premises were ready. He moved out yesterday.'
  1705	
  1706	"'Where could I find him?'
  1707	
  1708	"'Oh, at his new offices. He did tell me the address. Yes, 17
  1709	King Edward Street, near St. Paul's.'
  1710	
  1711	"I started off, Mr. Holmes, but when I got to that address it was
  1712	a manufactory of artificial knee-caps, and no one in it had ever
  1713	heard of either Mr. William Morris or Mr. Duncan Ross."
  1714	
  1715	"And what did you do then?" asked Holmes.
  1716	
  1717	"I went home to Saxe-Coburg Square, and I took the advice of my
  1718	assistant. But he could not help me in any way. He could only say
  1719	that if I waited I should hear by post. But that was not quite
  1720	good enough, Mr. Holmes. I did not wish to lose such a place
  1721	without a struggle, so, as I had heard that you were good enough
  1722	to give advice to poor folk who were in need of it, I came right
  1723	away to you."
  1724	
  1725	"And you did very wisely," said Holmes. "Your case is an
  1726	exceedingly remarkable one, and I shall be happy to look into it.
  1727	From what you have told me I think that it is possible that
  1728	graver issues hang from it than might at first sight appear."
  1729	
  1730	"Grave enough!" said Mr. Jabez Wilson. "Why, I have lost four
  1731	pound a week."
  1732	
  1733	"As far as you are personally concerned," remarked Holmes, "I do
  1734	not see that you have any grievance against this extraordinary
  1735	league. On the contrary, you are, as I understand, richer by some
  1736	30 pounds, to say nothing of the minute knowledge which you have
  1737	gained on every subject which comes under the letter A. You have
  1738	lost nothing by them."
  1739	
  1740	"No, sir. But I want to find out about them, and who they are,
  1741	and what their object was in playing this prank--if it was a
  1742	prank--upon me. It was a pretty expensive joke for them, for it
  1743	cost them two and thirty pounds."
  1744	
  1745	"We shall endeavour to clear up these points for you. And, first,
  1746	one or two questions, Mr. Wilson. This assistant of yours who
  1747	first called your attention to the advertisement--how long had he
  1748	been with you?"
  1749	
  1750	"About a month then."
  1751	
  1752	"How did he come?"
  1753	
  1754	"In answer to an advertisement."
  1755	
  1756	"Was he the only applicant?"
  1757	
  1758	"No, I had a dozen."
  1759	
  1760	"Why did you pick him?"
  1761	
  1762	"Because he was handy and would come cheap."
  1763	
  1764	"At half-wages, in fact."
  1765	
  1766	"Yes."
  1767	
  1768	"What is he like, this Vincent Spaulding?"
  1769	
  1770	"Small, stout-built, very quick in his ways, no hair on his face,
  1771	though he's not short of thirty. Has a white splash of acid upon
  1772	his forehead."
  1773	
  1774	Holmes sat up in his chair in considerable excitement. "I thought
  1775	as much," said he. "Have you ever observed that his ears are
  1776	pierced for earrings?"
  1777	
  1778	"Yes, sir. He told me that a gipsy had done it for him when he
  1779	was a lad."
  1780	
  1781	"Hum!" said Holmes, sinking back in deep thought. "He is still
  1782	with you?"
  1783	
  1784	"Oh, yes, sir; I have only just left him."
  1785	
  1786	"And has your business been attended to in your absence?"
  1787	
  1788	"Nothing to complain of, sir. There's never very much to do of a
  1789	morning."
  1790	
  1791	"That will do, Mr. Wilson. I shall be happy to give you an
  1792	opinion upon the subject in the course of a day or two. To-day is
  1793	Saturday, and I hope that by Monday we may come to a conclusion."
  1794	
  1795	"Well, Watson," said Holmes when our visitor had left us, "what
  1796	do you make of it all?"
  1797	
  1798	"I make nothing of it," I answered frankly. "It is a most
  1799	mysterious business."
  1800	
  1801	"As a rule," said Holmes, "the more bizarre a thing is the less
  1802	mysterious it proves to be. It is your commonplace, featureless
  1803	crimes which are really puzzling, just as a commonplace face is
  1804	the most difficult to identify. But I must be prompt over this
  1805	matter."
  1806	
  1807	"What are you going to do, then?" I asked.
  1808	
  1809	"To smoke," he answered. "It is quite a three pipe problem, and I
  1810	beg that you won't speak to me for fifty minutes." He curled
  1811	himself up in his chair, with his thin knees drawn up to his
  1812	hawk-like nose, and there he sat with his eyes closed and his
  1813	black clay pipe thrusting out like the bill of some strange bird.
  1814	I had come to the conclusion that he had dropped asleep, and
  1815	indeed was nodding myself, when he suddenly sprang out of his
  1816	chair with the gesture of a man who has made up his mind and put
  1817	his pipe down upon the mantelpiece.
  1818	
  1819	"Sarasate plays at the St. James's Hall this afternoon," he
  1820	remarked. "What do you think, Watson? Could your patients spare
  1821	you for a few hours?"
  1822	
  1823	"I have nothing to do to-day. My practice is never very
  1824	absorbing."
  1825	
  1826	"Then put on your hat and come. I am going through the City
  1827	first, and we can have some lunch on the way. I observe that
  1828	there is a good deal of German music on the programme, which is
  1829	rather more to my taste than Italian or French. It is
  1830	introspective, and I want to introspect. Come along!"
  1831	
  1832	We travelled by the Underground as far as Aldersgate; and a short
  1833	walk took us to Saxe-Coburg Square, the scene of the singular
  1834	story which we had listened to in the morning. It was a poky,
  1835	little, shabby-genteel place, where four lines of dingy
  1836	two-storied brick houses looked out into a small railed-in
  1837	enclosure, where a lawn of weedy grass and a few clumps of faded
  1838	laurel-bushes made a hard fight against a smoke-laden and
  1839	uncongenial atmosphere. Three gilt balls and a brown board with
  1840	"JABEZ WILSON" in white letters, upon a corner house, announced
  1841	the place where our red-headed client carried on his business.
  1842	Sherlock Holmes stopped in front of it with his head on one side
  1843	and looked it all over, with his eyes shining brightly between
  1844	puckered lids. Then he walked slowly up the street, and then down
  1845	again to the corner, still looking keenly at the houses. Finally
  1846	he returned to the pawnbroker's, and, having thumped vigorously
  1847	upon the pavement with his stick two or three times, he went up
  1848	to the door and knocked. It was instantly opened by a
  1849	bright-looking, clean-shaven young fellow, who asked him to step
  1850	in.
  1851	
  1852	"Thank you," said Holmes, "I only wished to ask you how you would
  1853	go from here to the Strand."
  1854	
  1855	"Third right, fourth left," answered the assistant promptly,
  1856	closing the door.
  1857	
  1858	"Smart fellow, that," observed Holmes as we walked away. "He is,
  1859	in my judgment, the fourth smartest man in London, and for daring
  1860	I am not sure that he has not a claim to be third. I have known
  1861	something of him before."
  1862	
  1863	"Evidently," said I, "Mr. Wilson's assistant counts for a good
  1864	deal in this mystery of the Red-headed League. I am sure that you
  1865	inquired your way merely in order that you might see him."
  1866	
  1867	"Not him."
  1868	
  1869	"What then?"
  1870	
  1871	"The knees of his trousers."
  1872	
  1873	"And what did you see?"
  1874	
  1875	"What I expected to see."
  1876	
  1877	"Why did you beat the pavement?"
  1878	
  1879	"My dear doctor, this is a time for observation, not for talk. We
  1880	are spies in an enemy's country. We know something of Saxe-Coburg
  1881	Square. Let us now explore the parts which lie behind it."
  1882	
  1883	The road in which we found ourselves as we turned round the
  1884	corner from the retired Saxe-Coburg Square presented as great a
  1885	contrast to it as the front of a picture does to the back. It was
  1886	one of the main arteries which conveyed the traffic of the City
  1887	to the north and west. The roadway was blocked with the immense
  1888	stream of commerce flowing in a double tide inward and outward,
  1889	while the footpaths were black with the hurrying swarm of
  1890	pedestrians. It was difficult to realise as we looked at the line
  1891	of fine shops and stately business premises that they really
  1892	abutted on the other side upon the faded and stagnant square
  1893	which we had just quitted.
  1894	
  1895	"Let me see," said Holmes, standing at the corner and glancing
  1896	along the line, "I should like just to remember the order of the
  1897	houses here. It is a hobby of mine to have an exact knowledge of
  1898	London. There is Mortimer's, the tobacconist, the little
  1899	newspaper shop, the Coburg branch of the City and Suburban Bank,
  1900	the Vegetarian Restaurant, and McFarlane's carriage-building
  1901	depot. That carries us right on to the other block. And now,
  1902	Doctor, we've done our work, so it's time we had some play. A
  1903	sandwich and a cup of coffee, and then off to violin-land, where
  1904	all is sweetness and delicacy and harmony, and there are no
  1905	red-headed clients to vex us with their conundrums."
  1906	
  1907	My friend was an enthusiastic musician, being himself not only a
  1908	very capable performer but a composer of no ordinary merit. All
  1909	the afternoon he sat in the stalls wrapped in the most perfect
  1910	happiness, gently waving his long, thin fingers in time to the
  1911	music, while his gently smiling face and his languid, dreamy eyes
  1912	were as unlike those of Holmes the sleuth-hound, Holmes the
  1913	relentless, keen-witted, ready-handed criminal agent, as it was
  1914	possible to conceive. In his singular character the dual nature
  1915	alternately asserted itself, and his extreme exactness and
  1916	astuteness represented, as I have often thought, the reaction
  1917	against the poetic and contemplative mood which occasionally
  1918	predominated in him. The swing of his nature took him from
  1919	extreme languor to devouring energy; and, as I knew well, he was
  1920	never so truly formidable as when, for days on end, he had been
  1921	lounging in his armchair amid his improvisations and his
  1922	black-letter editions. Then it was that the lust of the chase
  1923	would suddenly come upon him, and that his brilliant reasoning
  1924	power would rise to the level of intuition, until those who were
  1925	unacquainted with his methods would look askance at him as on a
  1926	man whose knowledge was not that of other mortals. When I saw him
  1927	that afternoon so enwrapped in the music at St. James's Hall I
  1928	felt that an evil time might be coming upon those whom he had set
  1929	himself to hunt down.
  1930	
  1931	"You want to go home, no doubt, Doctor," he remarked as we
  1932	emerged.
  1933	
  1934	"Yes, it would be as well."
  1935	
  1936	"And I have some business to do which will take some hours. This
  1937	business at Coburg Square is serious."
  1938	
  1939	"Why serious?"
  1940	
  1941	"A considerable crime is in contemplation. I have every reason to
  1942	believe that we shall be in time to stop it. But to-day being
  1943	Saturday rather complicates matters. I shall want your help
  1944	to-night."
  1945	
  1946	"At what time?"
  1947	
  1948	"Ten will be early enough."
  1949	
  1950	"I shall be at Baker Street at ten."
  1951	
  1952	"Very well. And, I say, Doctor, there may be some little danger,
  1953	so kindly put your army revolver in your pocket." He waved his
  1954	hand, turned on his heel, and disappeared in an instant among the
  1955	crowd.
  1956	
  1957	I trust that I am not more dense than my neighbours, but I was
  1958	always oppressed with a sense of my own stupidity in my dealings
  1959	with Sherlock Holmes. Here I had heard what he had heard, I had
  1960	seen what he had seen, and yet from his words it was evident that
  1961	he saw clearly not only what had happened but what was about to
  1962	happen, while to me the whole business was still confused and
  1963	grotesque. As I drove home to my house in Kensington I thought
  1964	over it all, from the extraordinary story of the red-headed
  1965	copier of the "Encyclopaedia" down to the visit to Saxe-Coburg
  1966	Square, and the ominous words with which he had parted from me.
  1967	What was this nocturnal expedition, and why should I go armed?
  1968	Where were we going, and what were we to do? I had the hint from
  1969	Holmes that this smooth-faced pawnbroker's assistant was a
  1970	formidable man--a man who might play a deep game. I tried to
  1971	puzzle it out, but gave it up in despair and set the matter aside
  1972	until night should bring an explanation.
  1973	
  1974	It was a quarter-past nine when I started from home and made my
  1975	way across the Park, and so through Oxford Street to Baker
  1976	Street. Two hansoms were standing at the door, and as I entered
  1977	the passage I heard the sound of voices from above. On entering
  1978	his room I found Holmes in animated conversation with two men,
  1979	one of whom I recognised as Peter Jones, the official police
  1980	agent, while the other was a long, thin, sad-faced man, with a
  1981	very shiny hat and oppressively respectable frock-coat.
  1982	
  1983	"Ha! Our party is complete," said Holmes, buttoning up his
  1984	pea-jacket and taking his heavy hunting crop from the rack.
  1985	"Watson, I think you know Mr. Jones, of Scotland Yard? Let me
  1986	introduce you to Mr. Merryweather, who is to be our companion in
  1987	to-night's adventure."
  1988	
  1989	"We're hunting in couples again, Doctor, you see," said Jones in
  1990	his consequential way. "Our friend here is a wonderful man for
  1991	starting a chase. All he wants is an old dog to help him to do
  1992	the running down."
  1993	
  1994	"I hope a wild goose may not prove to be the end of our chase,"
  1995	observed Mr. Merryweather gloomily.
  1996	
  1997	"You may place considerable confidence in Mr. Holmes, sir," said
  1998	the police agent loftily. "He has his own little methods, which
  1999	are, if he won't mind my saying so, just a little too theoretical
  2000	and fantastic, but he has the makings of a detective in him. It
  2001	is not too much to say that once or twice, as in that business of
  2002	the Sholto murder and the Agra treasure, he has been more nearly
  2003	correct than the official force."
  2004	
  2005	"Oh, if you say so, Mr. Jones, it is all right," said the
  2006	stranger with deference. "Still, I confess that I miss my rubber.
  2007	It is the first Saturday night for seven-and-twenty years that I
  2008	have not had my rubber."
  2009	
  2010	"I think you will find," said Sherlock Holmes, "that you will
  2011	play for a higher stake to-night than you have ever done yet, and
  2012	that the play will be more exciting. For you, Mr. Merryweather,
  2013	the stake will be some 30,000 pounds; and for you, Jones, it will
  2014	be the man upon whom you wish to lay your hands."
  2015	
  2016	"John Clay, the murderer, thief, smasher, and forger. He's a
  2017	young man, Mr. Merryweather, but he is at the head of his
  2018	profession, and I would rather have my bracelets on him than on
  2019	any criminal in London. He's a remarkable man, is young John
  2020	Clay. His grandfather was a royal duke, and he himself has been
  2021	to Eton and Oxford. His brain is as cunning as his fingers, and
  2022	though we meet signs of him at every turn, we never know where to
  2023	find the man himself. He'll crack a crib in Scotland one week,
  2024	and be raising money to build an orphanage in Cornwall the next.
  2025	I've been on his track for years and have never set eyes on him
  2026	yet."
  2027	
  2028	"I hope that I may have the pleasure of introducing you to-night.
  2029	I've had one or two little turns also with Mr. John Clay, and I
  2030	agree with you that he is at the head of his profession. It is
  2031	past ten, however, and quite time that we started. If you two
  2032	will take the first hansom, Watson and I will follow in the
  2033	second."
  2034	
  2035	Sherlock Holmes was not very communicative during the long drive
  2036	and lay back in the cab humming the tunes which he had heard in
  2037	the afternoon. We rattled through an endless labyrinth of gas-lit
  2038	streets until we emerged into Farrington Street.
  2039	
  2040	"We are close there now," my friend remarked. "This fellow
  2041	Merryweather is a bank director, and personally interested in the
  2042	matter. I thought it as well to have Jones with us also. He is
  2043	not a bad fellow, though an absolute imbecile in his profession.
  2044	He has one positive virtue. He is as brave as a bulldog and as
  2045	tenacious as a lobster if he gets his claws upon anyone. Here we
  2046	are, and they are waiting for us."
  2047	
  2048	We had reached the same crowded thoroughfare in which we had
  2049	found ourselves in the morning. Our cabs were dismissed, and,
  2050	following the guidance of Mr. Merryweather, we passed down a
  2051	narrow passage and through a side door, which he opened for us.
  2052	Within there was a small corridor, which ended in a very massive
  2053	iron gate. This also was opened, and led down a flight of winding
  2054	stone steps, which terminated at another formidable gate. Mr.
  2055	Merryweather stopped to light a lantern, and then conducted us
  2056	down a dark, earth-smelling passage, and so, after opening a
  2057	third door, into a huge vault or cellar, which was piled all
  2058	round with crates and massive boxes.
  2059	
  2060	"You are not very vulnerable from above," Holmes remarked as he
  2061	held up the lantern and gazed about him.
  2062	
  2063	"Nor from below," said Mr. Merryweather, striking his stick upon
  2064	the flags which lined the floor. "Why, dear me, it sounds quite
  2065	hollow!" he remarked, looking up in surprise.
  2066	
  2067	"I must really ask you to be a little more quiet!" said Holmes
  2068	severely. "You have already imperilled the whole success of our
  2069	expedition. Might I beg that you would have the goodness to sit
  2070	down upon one of those boxes, and not to interfere?"
  2071	
  2072	The solemn Mr. Merryweather perched himself upon a crate, with a
  2073	very injured expression upon his face, while Holmes fell upon his
  2074	knees upon the floor and, with the lantern and a magnifying lens,
  2075	began to examine minutely the cracks between the stones. A few
  2076	seconds sufficed to satisfy him, for he sprang to his feet again
  2077	and put his glass in his pocket.
  2078	
  2079	"We have at least an hour before us," he remarked, "for they can
  2080	hardly take any steps until the good pawnbroker is safely in bed.
  2081	Then they will not lose a minute, for the sooner they do their
  2082	work the longer time they will have for their escape. We are at
  2083	present, Doctor--as no doubt you have divined--in the cellar of
  2084	the City branch of one of the principal London banks. Mr.
  2085	Merryweather is the chairman of directors, and he will explain to
  2086	you that there are reasons why the more daring criminals of
  2087	London should take a considerable interest in this cellar at
  2088	present."
  2089	
  2090	"It is our French gold," whispered the director. "We have had
  2091	several warnings that an attempt might be made upon it."
  2092	
  2093	"Your French gold?"
  2094	
  2095	"Yes. We had occasion some months ago to strengthen our resources
  2096	and borrowed for that purpose 30,000 napoleons from the Bank of
  2097	France. It has become known that we have never had occasion to
  2098	unpack the money, and that it is still lying in our cellar. The
  2099	crate upon which I sit contains 2,000 napoleons packed between
  2100	layers of lead foil. Our reserve of bullion is much larger at
  2101	present than is usually kept in a single branch office, and the
  2102	directors have had misgivings upon the subject."
  2103	
  2104	"Which were very well justified," observed Holmes. "And now it is
  2105	time that we arranged our little plans. I expect that within an
  2106	hour matters will come to a head. In the meantime Mr.
  2107	Merryweather, we must put the screen over that dark lantern."
  2108	
  2109	"And sit in the dark?"
  2110	
  2111	"I am afraid so. I had brought a pack of cards in my pocket, and
  2112	I thought that, as we were a partie carr?e, you might have your
  2113	rubber after all. But I see that the enemy's preparations have
  2114	gone so far that we cannot risk the presence of a light. And,
  2115	first of all, we must choose our positions. These are daring men,
  2116	and though we shall take them at a disadvantage, they may do us
  2117	some harm unless we are careful. I shall stand behind this crate,
  2118	and do you conceal yourselves behind those. Then, when I flash a
  2119	light upon them, close in swiftly. If they fire, Watson, have no
  2120	compunction about shooting them down."
  2121	
  2122	I placed my revolver, cocked, upon the top of the wooden case
  2123	behind which I crouched. Holmes shot the slide across the front
  2124	of his lantern and left us in pitch darkness--such an absolute
  2125	darkness as I have never before experienced. The smell of hot
  2126	metal remained to assure us that the light was still there, ready
  2127	to flash out at a moment's notice. To me, with my nerves worked
  2128	up to a pitch of expectancy, there was something depressing and
  2129	subduing in the sudden gloom, and in the cold dank air of the
  2130	vault.
  2131	
  2132	"They have but one retreat," whispered Holmes. "That is back
  2133	through the house into Saxe-Coburg Square. I hope that you have
  2134	done what I asked you, Jones?"
  2135	
  2136	"I have an inspector and two officers waiting at the front door."
  2137	
  2138	"Then we have stopped all the holes. And now we must be silent
  2139	and wait."
  2140	
  2141	What a time it seemed! From comparing notes afterwards it was but
  2142	an hour and a quarter, yet it appeared to me that the night must
  2143	have almost gone and the dawn be breaking above us. My limbs
  2144	were weary and stiff, for I feared to change my position; yet my
  2145	nerves were worked up to the highest pitch of tension, and my
  2146	hearing was so acute that I could not only hear the gentle
  2147	breathing of my companions, but I could distinguish the deeper,
  2148	heavier in-breath of the bulky Jones from the thin, sighing note
  2149	of the bank director. From my position I could look over the case
  2150	in the direction of the floor. Suddenly my eyes caught the glint
  2151	of a light.
  2152	
  2153	At first it was but a lurid spark upon the stone pavement. Then
  2154	it lengthened out until it became a yellow line, and then,
  2155	without any warning or sound, a gash seemed to open and a hand
  2156	appeared, a white, almost womanly hand, which felt about in the
  2157	centre of the little area of light. For a minute or more the
  2158	hand, with its writhing fingers, protruded out of the floor. Then
  2159	it was withdrawn as suddenly as it appeared, and all was dark
  2160	again save the single lurid spark which marked a chink between
  2161	the stones.
  2162	
  2163	Its disappearance, however, was but momentary. With a rending,
  2164	tearing sound, one of the broad, white stones turned over upon
  2165	its side and left a square, gaping hole, through which streamed
  2166	the light of a lantern. Over the edge there peeped a clean-cut,
  2167	boyish face, which looked keenly about it, and then, with a hand
  2168	on either side of the aperture, drew itself shoulder-high and
  2169	waist-high, until one knee rested upon the edge. In another
  2170	instant he stood at the side of the hole and was hauling after
  2171	him a companion, lithe and small like himself, with a pale face
  2172	and a shock of very red hair.
  2173	
  2174	"It's all clear," he whispered. "Have you the chisel and the
  2175	bags? Great Scott! Jump, Archie, jump, and I'll swing for it!"
  2176	
  2177	Sherlock Holmes had sprung out and seized the intruder by the
  2178	collar. The other dived down the hole, and I heard the sound of
  2179	rending cloth as Jones clutched at his skirts. The light flashed
  2180	upon the barrel of a revolver, but Holmes' hunting crop came
  2181	down on the man's wrist, and the pistol clinked upon the stone
  2182	floor.
  2183	
  2184	"It's no use, John Clay," said Holmes blandly. "You have no
  2185	chance at all."
  2186	
  2187	"So I see," the other answered with the utmost coolness. "I fancy
  2188	that my pal is all right, though I see you have got his
  2189	coat-tails."
  2190	
  2191	"There are three men waiting for him at the door," said Holmes.
  2192	
  2193	"Oh, indeed! You seem to have done the thing very completely. I
  2194	must compliment you."
  2195	
  2196	"And I you," Holmes answered. "Your red-headed idea was very new
  2197	and effective."
  2198	
  2199	"You'll see your pal again presently," said Jones. "He's quicker
  2200	at climbing down holes than I am. Just hold out while I fix the
  2201	derbies."
  2202	
  2203	"I beg that you will not touch me with your filthy hands,"
  2204	remarked our prisoner as the handcuffs clattered upon his wrists.
  2205	"You may not be aware that I have royal blood in my veins. Have
  2206	the goodness, also, when you address me always to say 'sir' and
  2207	'please.'"
  2208	
  2209	"All right," said Jones with a stare and a snigger. "Well, would
  2210	you please, sir, march upstairs, where we can get a cab to carry
  2211	your Highness to the police-station?"
  2212	
  2213	"That is better," said John Clay serenely. He made a sweeping bow
  2214	to the three of us and walked quietly off in the custody of the
  2215	detective.
  2216	
  2217	"Really, Mr. Holmes," said Mr. Merryweather as we followed them
  2218	from the cellar, "I do not know how the bank can thank you or
  2219	repay you. There is no doubt that you have detected and defeated
  2220	in the most complete manner one of the most determined attempts
  2221	at bank robbery that have ever come within my experience."
  2222	
  2223	"I have had one or two little scores of my own to settle with Mr.
  2224	John Clay," said Holmes. "I have been at some small expense over
  2225	this matter, which I shall expect the bank to refund, but beyond
  2226	that I am amply repaid by having had an experience which is in
  2227	many ways unique, and by hearing the very remarkable narrative of
  2228	the Red-headed League."
  2229	
  2230	
  2231	"You see, Watson," he explained in the early hours of the morning
  2232	as we sat over a glass of whisky and soda in Baker Street, "it
  2233	was perfectly obvious from the first that the only possible
  2234	object of this rather fantastic business of the advertisement of
  2235	the League, and the copying of the 'Encyclopaedia,' must be to get
  2236	this not over-bright pawnbroker out of the way for a number of
  2237	hours every day. It was a curious way of managing it, but,
  2238	really, it would be difficult to suggest a better. The method was
  2239	no doubt suggested to Clay's ingenious mind by the colour of his
  2240	accomplice's hair. The 4 pounds a week was a lure which must draw
  2241	him, and what was it to them, who were playing for thousands?
  2242	They put in the advertisement, one rogue has the temporary
  2243	office, the other rogue incites the man to apply for it, and
  2244	together they manage to secure his absence every morning in the
  2245	week. From the time that I heard of the assistant having come for
  2246	half wages, it was obvious to me that he had some strong motive
  2247	for securing the situation."
  2248	
  2249	"But how could you guess what the motive was?"
  2250	
  2251	"Had there been women in the house, I should have suspected a
  2252	mere vulgar intrigue. That, however, was out of the question. The
  2253	man's business was a small one, and there was nothing in his
  2254	house which could account for such elaborate preparations, and
  2255	such an expenditure as they were at. It must, then, be something
  2256	out of the house. What could it be? I thought of the assistant's
  2257	fondness for photography, and his trick of vanishing into the
  2258	cellar. The cellar! There was the end of this tangled clue. Then
  2259	I made inquiries as to this mysterious assistant and found that I
  2260	had to deal with one of the coolest and most daring criminals in
  2261	London. He was doing something in the cellar--something which
  2262	took many hours a day for months on end. What could it be, once
  2263	more? I could think of nothing save that he was running a tunnel
  2264	to some other building.
  2265	
  2266	"So far I had got when we went to visit the scene of action. I
  2267	surprised you by beating upon the pavement with my stick. I was
  2268	ascertaining whether the cellar stretched out in front or behind.
  2269	It was not in front. Then I rang the bell, and, as I hoped, the
  2270	assistant answered it. We have had some skirmishes, but we had
  2271	never set eyes upon each other before. I hardly looked at his
  2272	face. His knees were what I wished to see. You must yourself have
  2273	remarked how worn, wrinkled, and stained they were. They spoke of
  2274	those hours of burrowing. The only remaining point was what they
  2275	were burrowing for. I walked round the corner, saw the City and
  2276	Suburban Bank abutted on our friend's premises, and felt that I
  2277	had solved my problem. When you drove home after the concert I
  2278	called upon Scotland Yard and upon the chairman of the bank
  2279	directors, with the result that you have seen."
  2280	
  2281	"And how could you tell that they would make their attempt
  2282	to-night?" I asked.
  2283	
  2284	"Well, when they closed their League offices that was a sign that
  2285	they cared no longer about Mr. Jabez Wilson's presence--in other
  2286	words, that they had completed their tunnel. But it was essential
  2287	that they should use it soon, as it might be discovered, or the
  2288	bullion might be removed. Saturday would suit them better than
  2289	any other day, as it would give them two days for their escape.
  2290	For all these reasons I expected them to come to-night."
  2291	
  2292	"You reasoned it out beautifully," I exclaimed in unfeigned
  2293	admiration. "It is so long a chain, and yet every link rings
  2294	true."
  2295	
  2296	"It saved me from ennui," he answered, yawning. "Alas! I already
  2297	feel it closing in upon me. My life is spent in one long effort
  2298	to escape from the commonplaces of existence. These little
  2299	problems help me to do so."
  2300	
  2301	"And you are a benefactor of the race," said I.
  2302	
  2303	He shrugged his shoulders. "Well, perhaps, after all, it is of
  2304	some little use," he remarked. "'L'homme c'est rien--l'oeuvre
  2305	c'est tout,' as Gustave Flaubert wrote to George Sand."
  2306	
  2307	
  2308	
  2309	ADVENTURE III. A CASE OF IDENTITY
  2310	
  2311	"My dear fellow," said Sherlock Holmes as we sat on either side
  2312	of the fire in his lodgings at Baker Street, "life is infinitely
  2313	stranger than anything which the mind of man could invent. We
  2314	would not dare to conceive the things which are really mere
  2315	commonplaces of existence. If we could fly out of that window
  2316	hand in hand, hover over this great city, gently remove the
  2317	roofs, and peep in at the queer things which are going on, the
  2318	strange coincidences, the plannings, the cross-purposes, the
  2319	wonderful chains of events, working through generations, and
  2320	leading to the most outr? results, it would make all fiction with
  2321	its conventionalities and foreseen conclusions most stale and
  2322	unprofitable."
  2323	
  2324	"And yet I am not convinced of it," I answered. "The cases which
  2325	come to light in the papers are, as a rule, bald enough, and
  2326	vulgar enough. We have in our police reports realism pushed to
  2327	its extreme limits, and yet the result is, it must be confessed,
  2328	neither fascinating nor artistic."
  2329	
  2330	"A certain selection and discretion must be used in producing a
  2331	realistic effect," remarked Holmes. "This is wanting in the
  2332	police report, where more stress is laid, perhaps, upon the
  2333	platitudes of the magistrate than upon the details, which to an
  2334	observer contain the vital essence of the whole matter. Depend
  2335	upon it, there is nothing so unnatural as the commonplace."
  2336	
  2337	I smiled and shook my head. "I can quite understand your thinking
  2338	so." I said. "Of course, in your position of unofficial adviser
  2339	and helper to everybody who is absolutely puzzled, throughout
  2340	three continents, you are brought in contact with all that is
  2341	strange and bizarre. But here"--I picked up the morning paper
  2342	from the ground--"let us put it to a practical test. Here is the
  2343	first heading upon which I come. 'A husband's cruelty to his
  2344	wife.' There is half a column of print, but I know without
  2345	reading it that it is all perfectly familiar to me. There is, of
  2346	course, the other woman, the drink, the push, the blow, the
  2347	bruise, the sympathetic sister or landlady. The crudest of
  2348	writers could invent nothing more crude."
  2349	
  2350	"Indeed, your example is an unfortunate one for your argument,"
  2351	said Holmes, taking the paper and glancing his eye down it. "This
  2352	is the Dundas separation case, and, as it happens, I was engaged
  2353	in clearing up some small points in connection with it. The
  2354	husband was a teetotaler, there was no other woman, and the
  2355	conduct complained of was that he had drifted into the habit of
  2356	winding up every meal by taking out his false teeth and hurling
  2357	them at his wife, which, you will allow, is not an action likely
  2358	to occur to the imagination of the average story-teller. Take a
  2359	pinch of snuff, Doctor, and acknowledge that I have scored over
  2360	you in your example."
  2361	
  2362	He held out his snuffbox of old gold, with a great amethyst in
  2363	the centre of the lid. Its splendour was in such contrast to his
  2364	homely ways and simple life that I could not help commenting upon
  2365	it.
  2366	
  2367	"Ah," said he, "I forgot that I had not seen you for some weeks.
  2368	It is a little souvenir from the King of Bohemia in return for my
  2369	assistance in the case of the Irene Adler papers."
  2370	
  2371	"And the ring?" I asked, glancing at a remarkable brilliant which
  2372	sparkled upon his finger.
  2373	
  2374	"It was from the reigning family of Holland, though the matter in
  2375	which I served them was of such delicacy that I cannot confide it
  2376	even to you, who have been good enough to chronicle one or two of
  2377	my little problems."
  2378	
  2379	"And have you any on hand just now?" I asked with interest.
  2380	
  2381	"Some ten or twelve, but none which present any feature of
  2382	interest. They are important, you understand, without being
  2383	interesting. Indeed, I have found that it is usually in
  2384	unimportant matters that there is a field for the observation,
  2385	and for the quick analysis of cause and effect which gives the
  2386	charm to an investigation. The larger crimes are apt to be the
  2387	simpler, for the bigger the crime the more obvious, as a rule, is
  2388	the motive. In these cases, save for one rather intricate matter
  2389	which has been referred to me from Marseilles, there is nothing
  2390	which presents any features of interest. It is possible, however,
  2391	that I may have something better before very many minutes are
  2392	over, for this is one of my clients, or I am much mistaken."
  2393	
  2394	He had risen from his chair and was standing between the parted
  2395	blinds gazing down into the dull neutral-tinted London street.
  2396	Looking over his shoulder, I saw that on the pavement opposite
  2397	there stood a large woman with a heavy fur boa round her neck,
  2398	and a large curling red feather in a broad-brimmed hat which was
  2399	tilted in a coquettish Duchess of Devonshire fashion over her
  2400	ear. From under this great panoply she peeped up in a nervous,
  2401	hesitating fashion at our windows, while her body oscillated
  2402	backward and forward, and her fingers fidgeted with her glove
  2403	buttons. Suddenly, with a plunge, as of the swimmer who leaves
  2404	the bank, she hurried across the road, and we heard the sharp
  2405	clang of the bell.
  2406	
  2407	"I have seen those symptoms before," said Holmes, throwing his
  2408	cigarette into the fire. "Oscillation upon the pavement always
  2409	means an affaire de coeur. She would like advice, but is not sure
  2410	that the matter is not too delicate for communication. And yet
  2411	even here we may discriminate. When a woman has been seriously
  2412	wronged by a man she no longer oscillates, and the usual symptom
  2413	is a broken bell wire. Here we may take it that there is a love
  2414	matter, but that the maiden is not so much angry as perplexed, or
  2415	grieved. But here she comes in person to resolve our doubts."
  2416	
  2417	As he spoke there was a tap at the door, and the boy in buttons
  2418	entered to announce Miss Mary Sutherland, while the lady herself
  2419	loomed behind his small black figure like a full-sailed
  2420	merchant-man behind a tiny pilot boat. Sherlock Holmes welcomed
  2421	her with the easy courtesy for which he was remarkable, and,
  2422	having closed the door and bowed her into an armchair, he looked
  2423	her over in the minute and yet abstracted fashion which was
  2424	peculiar to him.
  2425	
  2426	"Do you not find," he said, "that with your short sight it is a
  2427	little trying to do so much typewriting?"
  2428	
  2429	"I did at first," she answered, "but now I know where the letters
  2430	are without looking." Then, suddenly realising the full purport
  2431	of his words, she gave a violent start and looked up, with fear
  2432	and astonishment upon her broad, good-humoured face. "You've
  2433	heard about me, Mr. Holmes," she cried, "else how could you know
  2434	all that?"
  2435	
  2436	"Never mind," said Holmes, laughing; "it is my business to know
  2437	things. Perhaps I have trained myself to see what others
  2438	overlook. If not, why should you come to consult me?"
  2439	
  2440	"I came to you, sir, because I heard of you from Mrs. Etherege,
  2441	whose husband you found so easy when the police and everyone had
  2442	given him up for dead. Oh, Mr. Holmes, I wish you would do as
  2443	much for me. I'm not rich, but still I have a hundred a year in
  2444	my own right, besides the little that I make by the machine, and
  2445	I would give it all to know what has become of Mr. Hosmer Angel."
  2446	
  2447	"Why did you come away to consult me in such a hurry?" asked
  2448	Sherlock Holmes, with his finger-tips together and his eyes to
  2449	the ceiling.
  2450	
  2451	Again a startled look came over the somewhat vacuous face of Miss
  2452	Mary Sutherland. "Yes, I did bang out of the house," she said,
  2453	"for it made me angry to see the easy way in which Mr.
  2454	Windibank--that is, my father--took it all. He would not go to
  2455	the police, and he would not go to you, and so at last, as he
  2456	would do nothing and kept on saying that there was no harm done,
  2457	it made me mad, and I just on with my things and came right away
  2458	to you."
  2459	
  2460	"Your father," said Holmes, "your stepfather, surely, since the
  2461	name is different."
  2462	
  2463	"Yes, my stepfather. I call him father, though it sounds funny,
  2464	too, for he is only five years and two months older than myself."
  2465	
  2466	"And your mother is alive?"
  2467	
  2468	"Oh, yes, mother is alive and well. I wasn't best pleased, Mr.
  2469	Holmes, when she married again so soon after father's death, and
  2470	a man who was nearly fifteen years younger than herself. Father
  2471	was a plumber in the Tottenham Court Road, and he left a tidy
  2472	business behind him, which mother carried on with Mr. Hardy, the
  2473	foreman; but when Mr. Windibank came he made her sell the
  2474	business, for he was very superior, being a traveller in wines.
  2475	They got 4700 pounds for the goodwill and interest, which wasn't
  2476	near as much as father could have got if he had been alive."
  2477	
  2478	I had expected to see Sherlock Holmes impatient under this
  2479	rambling and inconsequential narrative, but, on the contrary, he
  2480	had listened with the greatest concentration of attention.
  2481	
  2482	"Your own little income," he asked, "does it come out of the
  2483	business?"
  2484	
  2485	"Oh, no, sir. It is quite separate and was left me by my uncle
  2486	Ned in Auckland. It is in New Zealand stock, paying 4 1/2 per
  2487	cent. Two thousand five hundred pounds was the amount, but I can
  2488	only touch the interest."
  2489	
  2490	"You interest me extremely," said Holmes. "And since you draw so
  2491	large a sum as a hundred a year, with what you earn into the
  2492	bargain, you no doubt travel a little and indulge yourself in
  2493	every way. I believe that a single lady can get on very nicely
  2494	upon an income of about 60 pounds."
  2495	
  2496	"I could do with much less than that, Mr. Holmes, but you
  2497	understand that as long as I live at home I don't wish to be a
  2498	burden to them, and so they have the use of the money just while
  2499	I am staying with them. Of course, that is only just for the
  2500	time. Mr. Windibank draws my interest every quarter and pays it
  2501	over to mother, and I find that I can do pretty well with what I
  2502	earn at typewriting. It brings me twopence a sheet, and I can
  2503	often do from fifteen to twenty sheets in a day."
  2504	
  2505	"You have made your position very clear to me," said Holmes.
  2506	"This is my friend, Dr. Watson, before whom you can speak as
  2507	freely as before myself. Kindly tell us now all about your
  2508	connection with Mr. Hosmer Angel."
  2509	
  2510	A flush stole over Miss Sutherland's face, and she picked
  2511	nervously at the fringe of her jacket. "I met him first at the
  2512	gasfitters' ball," she said. "They used to send father tickets
  2513	when he was alive, and then afterwards they remembered us, and
  2514	sent them to mother. Mr. Windibank did not wish us to go. He
  2515	never did wish us to go anywhere. He would get quite mad if I
  2516	wanted so much as to join a Sunday-school treat. But this time I
  2517	was set on going, and I would go; for what right had he to
  2518	prevent? He said the folk were not fit for us to know, when all
  2519	father's friends were to be there. And he said that I had nothing
  2520	fit to wear, when I had my purple plush that I had never so much
  2521	as taken out of the drawer. At last, when nothing else would do,
  2522	he went off to France upon the business of the firm, but we went,
  2523	mother and I, with Mr. Hardy, who used to be our foreman, and it
  2524	was there I met Mr. Hosmer Angel."
  2525	
  2526	"I suppose," said Holmes, "that when Mr. Windibank came back from
  2527	France he was very annoyed at your having gone to the ball."
  2528	
  2529	"Oh, well, he was very good about it. He laughed, I remember, and
  2530	shrugged his shoulders, and said there was no use denying
  2531	anything to a woman, for she would have her way."
  2532	
  2533	"I see. Then at the gasfitters' ball you met, as I understand, a
  2534	gentleman called Mr. Hosmer Angel."
  2535	
  2536	"Yes, sir. I met him that night, and he called next day to ask if
  2537	we had got home all safe, and after that we met him--that is to
  2538	say, Mr. Holmes, I met him twice for walks, but after that father
  2539	came back again, and Mr. Hosmer Angel could not come to the house
  2540	any more."
  2541	
  2542	"No?"
  2543	
  2544	"Well, you know father didn't like anything of the sort. He
  2545	wouldn't have any visitors if he could help it, and he used to
  2546	say that a woman should be happy in her own family circle. But
  2547	then, as I used to say to mother, a woman wants her own circle to
  2548	begin with, and I had not got mine yet."
  2549	
  2550	"But how about Mr. Hosmer Angel? Did he make no attempt to see
  2551	you?"
  2552	
  2553	"Well, father was going off to France again in a week, and Hosmer
  2554	wrote and said that it would be safer and better not to see each
  2555	other until he had gone. We could write in the meantime, and he
  2556	used to write every day. I took the letters in in the morning, so
  2557	there was no need for father to know."
  2558	
  2559	"Were you engaged to the gentleman at this time?"
  2560	
  2561	"Oh, yes, Mr. Holmes. We were engaged after the first walk that
  2562	we took. Hosmer--Mr. Angel--was a cashier in an office in
  2563	Leadenhall Street--and--"
  2564	
  2565	"What office?"
  2566	
  2567	"That's the worst of it, Mr. Holmes, I don't know."
  2568	
  2569	"Where did he live, then?"
  2570	
  2571	"He slept on the premises."
  2572	
  2573	"And you don't know his address?"
  2574	
  2575	"No--except that it was Leadenhall Street."
  2576	
  2577	"Where did you address your letters, then?"
  2578	
  2579	"To the Leadenhall Street Post Office, to be left till called
  2580	for. He said that if they were sent to the office he would be
  2581	chaffed by all the other clerks about having letters from a lady,
  2582	so I offered to typewrite them, like he did his, but he wouldn't
  2583	have that, for he said that when I wrote them they seemed to come
  2584	from me, but when they were typewritten he always felt that the
  2585	machine had come between us. That will just show you how fond he
  2586	was of me, Mr. Holmes, and the little things that he would think
  2587	of."
  2588	
  2589	"It was most suggestive," said Holmes. "It has long been an axiom
  2590	of mine that the little things are infinitely the most important.
  2591	Can you remember any other little things about Mr. Hosmer Angel?"
  2592	
  2593	"He was a very shy man, Mr. Holmes. He would rather walk with me
  2594	in the evening than in the daylight, for he said that he hated to
  2595	be conspicuous. Very retiring and gentlemanly he was. Even his
  2596	voice was gentle. He'd had the quinsy and swollen glands when he
  2597	was young, he told me, and it had left him with a weak throat,
  2598	and a hesitating, whispering fashion of speech. He was always
  2599	well dressed, very neat and plain, but his eyes were weak, just
  2600	as mine are, and he wore tinted glasses against the glare."
  2601	
  2602	"Well, and what happened when Mr. Windibank, your stepfather,
  2603	returned to France?"
  2604	
  2605	"Mr. Hosmer Angel came to the house again and proposed that we
  2606	should marry before father came back. He was in dreadful earnest
  2607	and made me swear, with my hands on the Testament, that whatever
  2608	happened I would always be true to him. Mother said he was quite
  2609	right to make me swear, and that it was a sign of his passion.
  2610	Mother was all in his favour from the first and was even fonder
  2611	of him than I was. Then, when they talked of marrying within the
  2612	week, I began to ask about father; but they both said never to
  2613	mind about father, but just to tell him afterwards, and mother
  2614	said she would make it all right with him. I didn't quite like
  2615	that, Mr. Holmes. It seemed funny that I should ask his leave, as
  2616	he was only a few years older than me; but I didn't want to do
  2617	anything on the sly, so I wrote to father at Bordeaux, where the
  2618	company has its French offices, but the letter came back to me on
  2619	the very morning of the wedding."
  2620	
  2621	"It missed him, then?"
  2622	
  2623	"Yes, sir; for he had started to England just before it arrived."
  2624	
  2625	"Ha! that was unfortunate. Your wedding was arranged, then, for
  2626	the Friday. Was it to be in church?"
  2627	
  2628	"Yes, sir, but very quietly. It was to be at St. Saviour's, near
  2629	King's Cross, and we were to have breakfast afterwards at the St.
  2630	Pancras Hotel. Hosmer came for us in a hansom, but as there were
  2631	two of us he put us both into it and stepped himself into a
  2632	four-wheeler, which happened to be the only other cab in the
  2633	street. We got to the church first, and when the four-wheeler
  2634	drove up we waited for him to step out, but he never did, and
  2635	when the cabman got down from the box and looked there was no one
  2636	there! The cabman said that he could not imagine what had become
  2637	of him, for he had seen him get in with his own eyes. That was
  2638	last Friday, Mr. Holmes, and I have never seen or heard anything
  2639	since then to throw any light upon what became of him."
  2640	
  2641	"It seems to me that you have been very shamefully treated," said
  2642	Holmes.
  2643	
  2644	"Oh, no, sir! He was too good and kind to leave me so. Why, all
  2645	the morning he was saying to me that, whatever happened, I was to
  2646	be true; and that even if something quite unforeseen occurred to
  2647	separate us, I was always to remember that I was pledged to him,
  2648	and that he would claim his pledge sooner or later. It seemed
  2649	strange talk for a wedding-morning, but what has happened since
  2650	gives a meaning to it."
  2651	
  2652	"Most certainly it does. Your own opinion is, then, that some
  2653	unforeseen catastrophe has occurred to him?"
  2654	
  2655	"Yes, sir. I believe that he foresaw some danger, or else he
  2656	would not have talked so. And then I think that what he foresaw
  2657	happened."
  2658	
  2659	"But you have no notion as to what it could have been?"
  2660	
  2661	"None."
  2662	
  2663	"One more question. How did your mother take the matter?"
  2664	
  2665	"She was angry, and said that I was never to speak of the matter
  2666	again."
  2667	
  2668	"And your father? Did you tell him?"
  2669	
  2670	"Yes; and he seemed to think, with me, that something had
  2671	happened, and that I should hear of Hosmer again. As he said,
  2672	what interest could anyone have in bringing me to the doors of
  2673	the church, and then leaving me? Now, if he had borrowed my
  2674	money, or if he had married me and got my money settled on him,
  2675	there might be some reason, but Hosmer was very independent about
  2676	money and never would look at a shilling of mine. And yet, what
  2677	could have happened? And why could he not write? Oh, it drives me
  2678	half-mad to think of it, and I can't sleep a wink at night." She
  2679	pulled a little handkerchief out of her muff and began to sob
  2680	heavily into it.
  2681	
  2682	"I shall glance into the case for you," said Holmes, rising, "and
  2683	I have no doubt that we shall reach some definite result. Let the
  2684	weight of the matter rest upon me now, and do not let your mind
  2685	dwell upon it further. Above all, try to let Mr. Hosmer Angel
  2686	vanish from your memory, as he has done from your life."
  2687	
  2688	"Then you don't think I'll see him again?"
  2689	
  2690	"I fear not."
  2691	
  2692	"Then what has happened to him?"
  2693	
  2694	"You will leave that question in my hands. I should like an
  2695	accurate description of him and any letters of his which you can
  2696	spare."
  2697	
  2698	"I advertised for him in last Saturday's Chronicle," said she.
  2699	"Here is the slip and here are four letters from him."
  2700	
  2701	"Thank you. And your address?"
  2702	
  2703	"No. 31 Lyon Place, Camberwell."
  2704	
  2705	"Mr. Angel's address you never had, I understand. Where is your
  2706	father's place of business?"
  2707	
  2708	"He travels for Westhouse & Marbank, the great claret importers
  2709	of Fenchurch Street."
  2710	
  2711	"Thank you. You have made your statement very clearly. You will
  2712	leave the papers here, and remember the advice which I have given
  2713	you. Let the whole incident be a sealed book, and do not allow it
  2714	to affect your life."
  2715	
  2716	"You are very kind, Mr. Holmes, but I cannot do that. I shall be
  2717	true to Hosmer. He shall find me ready when he comes back."
  2718	
  2719	For all the preposterous hat and the vacuous face, there was
  2720	something noble in the simple faith of our visitor which
  2721	compelled our respect. She laid her little bundle of papers upon
  2722	the table and went her way, with a promise to come again whenever
  2723	she might be summoned.
  2724	
  2725	Sherlock Holmes sat silent for a few minutes with his fingertips
  2726	still pressed together, his legs stretched out in front of him,
  2727	and his gaze directed upward to the ceiling. Then he took down
  2728	from the rack the old and oily clay pipe, which was to him as a
  2729	counsellor, and, having lit it, he leaned back in his chair, with
  2730	the thick blue cloud-wreaths spinning up from him, and a look of
  2731	infinite languor in his face.
  2732	
  2733	"Quite an interesting study, that maiden," he observed. "I found
  2734	her more interesting than her little problem, which, by the way,
  2735	is rather a trite one. You will find parallel cases, if you
  2736	consult my index, in Andover in '77, and there was something of
  2737	the sort at The Hague last year. Old as is the idea, however,
  2738	there were one or two details which were new to me. But the
  2739	maiden herself was most instructive."
  2740	
  2741	"You appeared to read a good deal upon her which was quite
  2742	invisible to me," I remarked.
  2743	
  2744	"Not invisible but unnoticed, Watson. You did not know where to
  2745	look, and so you missed all that was important. I can never bring
  2746	you to realise the importance of sleeves, the suggestiveness of
  2747	thumb-nails, or the great issues that may hang from a boot-lace.
  2748	Now, what did you gather from that woman's appearance? Describe
  2749	it."
  2750	
  2751	"Well, she had a slate-coloured, broad-brimmed straw hat, with a
  2752	feather of a brickish red. Her jacket was black, with black beads
  2753	sewn upon it, and a fringe of little black jet ornaments. Her
  2754	dress was brown, rather darker than coffee colour, with a little
  2755	purple plush at the neck and sleeves. Her gloves were greyish and
  2756	were worn through at the right forefinger. Her boots I didn't
  2757	observe. She had small round, hanging gold earrings, and a
  2758	general air of being fairly well-to-do in a vulgar, comfortable,
  2759	easy-going way."
  2760	
  2761	Sherlock Holmes clapped his hands softly together and chuckled.
  2762	
  2763	"'Pon my word, Watson, you are coming along wonderfully. You have
  2764	really done very well indeed. It is true that you have missed
  2765	everything of importance, but you have hit upon the method, and
  2766	you have a quick eye for colour. Never trust to general
  2767	impressions, my boy, but concentrate yourself upon details. My
  2768	first glance is always at a woman's sleeve. In a man it is
  2769	perhaps better first to take the knee of the trouser. As you
  2770	observe, this woman had plush upon her sleeves, which is a most
  2771	useful material for showing traces. The double line a little
  2772	above the wrist, where the typewritist presses against the table,
  2773	was beautifully defined. The sewing-machine, of the hand type,
  2774	leaves a similar mark, but only on the left arm, and on the side
  2775	of it farthest from the thumb, instead of being right across the
  2776	broadest part, as this was. I then glanced at her face, and,
  2777	observing the dint of a pince-nez at either side of her nose, I
  2778	ventured a remark upon short sight and typewriting, which seemed
  2779	to surprise her."
  2780	
  2781	"It surprised me."
  2782	
  2783	"But, surely, it was obvious. I was then much surprised and
  2784	interested on glancing down to observe that, though the boots
  2785	which she was wearing were not unlike each other, they were
  2786	really odd ones; the one having a slightly decorated toe-cap, and
  2787	the other a plain one. One was buttoned only in the two lower
  2788	buttons out of five, and the other at the first, third, and
  2789	fifth. Now, when you see that a young lady, otherwise neatly
  2790	dressed, has come away from home with odd boots, half-buttoned,
  2791	it is no great deduction to say that she came away in a hurry."
  2792	
  2793	"And what else?" I asked, keenly interested, as I always was, by
  2794	my friend's incisive reasoning.
  2795	
  2796	"I noted, in passing, that she had written a note before leaving
  2797	home but after being fully dressed. You observed that her right
  2798	glove was torn at the forefinger, but you did not apparently see
  2799	that both glove and finger were stained with violet ink. She had
  2800	written in a hurry and dipped her pen too deep. It must have been
  2801	this morning, or the mark would not remain clear upon the finger.
  2802	All this is amusing, though rather elementary, but I must go back
  2803	to business, Watson. Would you mind reading me the advertised
  2804	description of Mr. Hosmer Angel?"
  2805	
  2806	I held the little printed slip to the light.
  2807	
  2808	"Missing," it said, "on the morning of the fourteenth, a gentleman
  2809	named Hosmer Angel. About five ft. seven in. in height;
  2810	strongly built, sallow complexion, black hair, a little bald in
  2811	the centre, bushy, black side-whiskers and moustache; tinted
  2812	glasses, slight infirmity of speech. Was dressed, when last seen,
  2813	in black frock-coat faced with silk, black waistcoat, gold Albert
  2814	chain, and grey Harris tweed trousers, with brown gaiters over
  2815	elastic-sided boots. Known to have been employed in an office in
  2816	Leadenhall Street. Anybody bringing--"
  2817	
  2818	"That will do," said Holmes. "As to the letters," he continued,
  2819	glancing over them, "they are very commonplace. Absolutely no
  2820	clue in them to Mr. Angel, save that he quotes Balzac once. There
  2821	is one remarkable point, however, which will no doubt strike
  2822	you."
  2823	
  2824	"They are typewritten," I remarked.
  2825	
  2826	"Not only that, but the signature is typewritten. Look at the
  2827	neat little 'Hosmer Angel' at the bottom. There is a date, you
  2828	see, but no superscription except Leadenhall Street, which is
  2829	rather vague. The point about the signature is very suggestive
  2830	--in fact, we may call it conclusive."
  2831	
  2832	"Of what?"
  2833	
  2834	"My dear fellow, is it possible you do not see how strongly it
  2835	bears upon the case?"
  2836	
  2837	"I cannot say that I do unless it were that he wished to be able
  2838	to deny his signature if an action for breach of promise were
  2839	instituted."
  2840	
  2841	"No, that was not the point. However, I shall write two letters,
  2842	which should settle the matter. One is to a firm in the City, the
  2843	other is to the young lady's stepfather, Mr. Windibank, asking
  2844	him whether he could meet us here at six o'clock tomorrow
  2845	evening. It is just as well that we should do business with the
  2846	male relatives. And now, Doctor, we can do nothing until the
  2847	answers to those letters come, so we may put our little problem
  2848	upon the shelf for the interim."
  2849	
  2850	I had had so many reasons to believe in my friend's subtle powers
  2851	of reasoning and extraordinary energy in action that I felt that
  2852	he must have some solid grounds for the assured and easy
  2853	demeanour with which he treated the singular mystery which he had
  2854	been called upon to fathom. Once only had I known him to fail, in
  2855	the case of the King of Bohemia and of the Irene Adler
  2856	photograph; but when I looked back to the weird business of the
  2857	Sign of Four, and the extraordinary circumstances connected with
  2858	the Study in Scarlet, I felt that it would be a strange tangle
  2859	indeed which he could not unravel.
  2860	
  2861	I left him then, still puffing at his black clay pipe, with the
  2862	conviction that when I came again on the next evening I would
  2863	find that he held in his hands all the clues which would lead up
  2864	to the identity of the disappearing bridegroom of Miss Mary
  2865	Sutherland.
  2866	
  2867	A professional case of great gravity was engaging my own
  2868	attention at the time, and the whole of next day I was busy at
  2869	the bedside of the sufferer. It was not until close upon six
  2870	o'clock that I found myself free and was able to spring into a
  2871	hansom and drive to Baker Street, half afraid that I might be too
  2872	late to assist at the d?nouement of the little mystery. I found
  2873	Sherlock Holmes alone, however, half asleep, with his long, thin
  2874	form curled up in the recesses of his armchair. A formidable
  2875	array of bottles and test-tubes, with the pungent cleanly smell
  2876	of hydrochloric acid, told me that he had spent his day in the
  2877	chemical work which was so dear to him.
  2878	
  2879	"Well, have you solved it?" I asked as I entered.
  2880	
  2881	"Yes. It was the bisulphate of baryta."
  2882	
  2883	"No, no, the mystery!" I cried.
  2884	
  2885	"Oh, that! I thought of the salt that I have been working upon.
  2886	There was never any mystery in the matter, though, as I said
  2887	yesterday, some of the details are of interest. The only drawback
  2888	is that there is no law, I fear, that can touch the scoundrel."
  2889	
  2890	"Who was he, then, and what was his object in deserting Miss
  2891	Sutherland?"
  2892	
  2893	The question was hardly out of my mouth, and Holmes had not yet
  2894	opened his lips to reply, when we heard a heavy footfall in the
  2895	passage and a tap at the door.
  2896	
  2897	"This is the girl's stepfather, Mr. James Windibank," said
  2898	Holmes. "He has written to me to say that he would be here at
  2899	six. Come in!"
  2900	
  2901	The man who entered was a sturdy, middle-sized fellow, some
  2902	thirty years of age, clean-shaven, and sallow-skinned, with a
  2903	bland, insinuating manner, and a pair of wonderfully sharp and
  2904	penetrating grey eyes. He shot a questioning glance at each of
  2905	us, placed his shiny top-hat upon the sideboard, and with a
  2906	slight bow sidled down into the nearest chair.
  2907	
  2908	"Good-evening, Mr. James Windibank," said Holmes. "I think that
  2909	this typewritten letter is from you, in which you made an
  2910	appointment with me for six o'clock?"
  2911	
  2912	"Yes, sir. I am afraid that I am a little late, but I am not
  2913	quite my own master, you know. I am sorry that Miss Sutherland
  2914	has troubled you about this little matter, for I think it is far
  2915	better not to wash linen of the sort in public. It was quite
  2916	against my wishes that she came, but she is a very excitable,
  2917	impulsive girl, as you may have noticed, and she is not easily
  2918	controlled when she has made up her mind on a point. Of course, I
  2919	did not mind you so much, as you are not connected with the
  2920	official police, but it is not pleasant to have a family
  2921	misfortune like this noised abroad. Besides, it is a useless
  2922	expense, for how could you possibly find this Hosmer Angel?"
  2923	
  2924	"On the contrary," said Holmes quietly; "I have every reason to
  2925	believe that I will succeed in discovering Mr. Hosmer Angel."
  2926	
  2927	Mr. Windibank gave a violent start and dropped his gloves. "I am
  2928	delighted to hear it," he said.
  2929	
  2930	"It is a curious thing," remarked Holmes, "that a typewriter has
  2931	really quite as much individuality as a man's handwriting. Unless
  2932	they are quite new, no two of them write exactly alike. Some
  2933	letters get more worn than others, and some wear only on one
  2934	side. Now, you remark in this note of yours, Mr. Windibank, that
  2935	in every case there is some little slurring over of the 'e,' and
  2936	a slight defect in the tail of the 'r.' There are fourteen other
  2937	characteristics, but those are the more obvious."
  2938	
  2939	"We do all our correspondence with this machine at the office,
  2940	and no doubt it is a little worn," our visitor answered, glancing
  2941	keenly at Holmes with his bright little eyes.
  2942	
  2943	"And now I will show you what is really a very interesting study,
  2944	Mr. Windibank," Holmes continued. "I think of writing another
  2945	little monograph some of these days on the typewriter and its
  2946	relation to crime. It is a subject to which I have devoted some
  2947	little attention. I have here four letters which purport to come
  2948	from the missing man. They are all typewritten. In each case, not
  2949	only are the 'e's' slurred and the 'r's' tailless, but you will
  2950	observe, if you care to use my magnifying lens, that the fourteen
  2951	other characteristics to which I have alluded are there as well."
  2952	
  2953	Mr. Windibank sprang out of his chair and picked up his hat. "I
  2954	cannot waste time over this sort of fantastic talk, Mr. Holmes,"
  2955	he said. "If you can catch the man, catch him, and let me know
  2956	when you have done it."
  2957	
  2958	"Certainly," said Holmes, stepping over and turning the key in
  2959	the door. "I let you know, then, that I have caught him!"
  2960	
  2961	"What! where?" shouted Mr. Windibank, turning white to his lips
  2962	and glancing about him like a rat in a trap.
  2963	
  2964	"Oh, it won't do--really it won't," said Holmes suavely. "There
  2965	is no possible getting out of it, Mr. Windibank. It is quite too
  2966	transparent, and it was a very bad compliment when you said that
  2967	it was impossible for me to solve so simple a question. That's
  2968	right! Sit down and let us talk it over."
  2969	
  2970	Our visitor collapsed into a chair, with a ghastly face and a
  2971	glitter of moisture on his brow. "It--it's not actionable," he
  2972	stammered.
  2973	
  2974	"I am very much afraid that it is not. But between ourselves,
  2975	Windibank, it was as cruel and selfish and heartless a trick in a
  2976	petty way as ever came before me. Now, let me just run over the
  2977	course of events, and you will contradict me if I go wrong."
  2978	
  2979	The man sat huddled up in his chair, with his head sunk upon his
  2980	breast, like one who is utterly crushed. Holmes stuck his feet up
  2981	on the corner of the mantelpiece and, leaning back with his hands
  2982	in his pockets, began talking, rather to himself, as it seemed,
  2983	than to us.
  2984	
  2985	"The man married a woman very much older than himself for her
  2986	money," said he, "and he enjoyed the use of the money of the
  2987	daughter as long as she lived with them. It was a considerable
  2988	sum, for people in their position, and the loss of it would have
  2989	made a serious difference. It was worth an effort to preserve it.
  2990	The daughter was of a good, amiable disposition, but affectionate
  2991	and warm-hearted in her ways, so that it was evident that with
  2992	her fair personal advantages, and her little income, she would
  2993	not be allowed to remain single long. Now her marriage would
  2994	mean, of course, the loss of a hundred a year, so what does her
  2995	stepfather do to prevent it? He takes the obvious course of
  2996	keeping her at home and forbidding her to seek the company of
  2997	people of her own age. But soon he found that that would not
  2998	answer forever. She became restive, insisted upon her rights, and
  2999	finally announced her positive intention of going to a certain
  3000	ball. What does her clever stepfather do then? He conceives an
  3001	idea more creditable to his head than to his heart. With the
  3002	connivance and assistance of his wife he disguised himself,
  3003	covered those keen eyes with tinted glasses, masked the face with
  3004	a moustache and a pair of bushy whiskers, sunk that clear voice
  3005	into an insinuating whisper, and doubly secure on account of the
  3006	girl's short sight, he appears as Mr. Hosmer Angel, and keeps off
  3007	other lovers by making love himself."
  3008	
  3009	"It was only a joke at first," groaned our visitor. "We never
  3010	thought that she would have been so carried away."
  3011	
  3012	"Very likely not. However that may be, the young lady was very
  3013	decidedly carried away, and, having quite made up her mind that
  3014	her stepfather was in France, the suspicion of treachery never
  3015	for an instant entered her mind. She was flattered by the
  3016	gentleman's attentions, and the effect was increased by the
  3017	loudly expressed admiration of her mother. Then Mr. Angel began
  3018	to call, for it was obvious that the matter should be pushed as
  3019	far as it would go if a real effect were to be produced. There
  3020	were meetings, and an engagement, which would finally secure the
  3021	girl's affections from turning towards anyone else. But the
  3022	deception could not be kept up forever. These pretended journeys
  3023	to France were rather cumbrous. The thing to do was clearly to
  3024	bring the business to an end in such a dramatic manner that it
  3025	would leave a permanent impression upon the young lady's mind and
  3026	prevent her from looking upon any other suitor for some time to
  3027	come. Hence those vows of fidelity exacted upon a Testament, and
  3028	hence also the allusions to a possibility of something happening
  3029	on the very morning of the wedding. James Windibank wished Miss
  3030	Sutherland to be so bound to Hosmer Angel, and so uncertain as to
  3031	his fate, that for ten years to come, at any rate, she would not
  3032	listen to another man. As far as the church door he brought her,
  3033	and then, as he could go no farther, he conveniently vanished
  3034	away by the old trick of stepping in at one door of a
  3035	four-wheeler and out at the other. I think that was the chain of
  3036	events, Mr. Windibank!"
  3037	
  3038	Our visitor had recovered something of his assurance while Holmes
  3039	had been talking, and he rose from his chair now with a cold
  3040	sneer upon his pale face.
  3041	
  3042	"It may be so, or it may not, Mr. Holmes," said he, "but if you
  3043	are so very sharp you ought to be sharp enough to know that it is
  3044	you who are breaking the law now, and not me. I have done nothing
  3045	actionable from the first, but as long as you keep that door
  3046	locked you lay yourself open to an action for assault and illegal
  3047	constraint."
  3048	
  3049	"The law cannot, as you say, touch you," said Holmes, unlocking
  3050	and throwing open the door, "yet there never was a man who
  3051	deserved punishment more. If the young lady has a brother or a
  3052	friend, he ought to lay a whip across your shoulders. By Jove!"
  3053	he continued, flushing up at the sight of the bitter sneer upon
  3054	the man's face, "it is not part of my duties to my client, but
  3055	here's a hunting crop handy, and I think I shall just treat
  3056	myself to--" He took two swift steps to the whip, but before he
  3057	could grasp it there was a wild clatter of steps upon the stairs,
  3058	the heavy hall door banged, and from the window we could see Mr.
  3059	James Windibank running at the top of his speed down the road.
  3060	
  3061	"There's a cold-blooded scoundrel!" said Holmes, laughing, as he
  3062	threw himself down into his chair once more. "That fellow will
  3063	rise from crime to crime until he does something very bad, and
  3064	ends on a gallows. The case has, in some respects, been not
  3065	entirely devoid of interest."
  3066	
  3067	"I cannot now entirely see all the steps of your reasoning," I
  3068	remarked.
  3069	
  3070	"Well, of course it was obvious from the first that this Mr.
  3071	Hosmer Angel must have some strong object for his curious
  3072	conduct, and it was equally clear that the only man who really
  3073	profited by the incident, as far as we could see, was the
  3074	stepfather. Then the fact that the two men were never together,
  3075	but that the one always appeared when the other was away, was
  3076	suggestive. So were the tinted spectacles and the curious voice,
  3077	which both hinted at a disguise, as did the bushy whiskers. My
  3078	suspicions were all confirmed by his peculiar action in
  3079	typewriting his signature, which, of course, inferred that his
  3080	handwriting was so familiar to her that she would recognise even
  3081	the smallest sample of it. You see all these isolated facts,
  3082	together with many minor ones, all pointed in the same
  3083	direction."
  3084	
  3085	"And how did you verify them?"
  3086	
  3087	"Having once spotted my man, it was easy to get corroboration. I
  3088	knew the firm for which this man worked. Having taken the printed
  3089	description. I eliminated everything from it which could be the
  3090	result of a disguise--the whiskers, the glasses, the voice, and I
  3091	sent it to the firm, with a request that they would inform me
  3092	whether it answered to the description of any of their
  3093	travellers. I had already noticed the peculiarities of the
  3094	typewriter, and I wrote to the man himself at his business
  3095	address asking him if he would come here. As I expected, his
  3096	reply was typewritten and revealed the same trivial but
  3097	characteristic defects. The same post brought me a letter from
  3098	Westhouse & Marbank, of Fenchurch Street, to say that the
  3099	description tallied in every respect with that of their employ?,
  3100	James Windibank. Voil? tout!"
  3101	
  3102	"And Miss Sutherland?"
  3103	
  3104	"If I tell her she will not believe me. You may remember the old
  3105	Persian saying, 'There is danger for him who taketh the tiger
  3106	cub, and danger also for whoso snatches a delusion from a woman.'
  3107	There is as much sense in Hafiz as in Horace, and as much
  3108	knowledge of the world."
  3109	
  3110	
  3111	
  3112	ADVENTURE IV. THE BOSCOMBE VALLEY MYSTERY
  3113	
  3114	We were seated at breakfast one morning, my wife and I, when the
  3115	maid brought in a telegram. It was from Sherlock Holmes and ran
  3116	in this way:
  3117	
  3118	"Have you a couple of days to spare? Have just been wired for from
  3119	the west of England in connection with Boscombe Valley tragedy.
  3120	Shall be glad if you will come with me. Air and scenery perfect.
  3121	Leave Paddington by the 11:15."
  3122	
  3123	"What do you say, dear?" said my wife, looking across at me.
  3124	"Will you go?"
  3125	
  3126	"I really don't know what to say. I have a fairly long list at
  3127	present."
  3128	
  3129	"Oh, Anstruther would do your work for you. You have been looking
  3130	a little pale lately. I think that the change would do you good,
  3131	and you are always so interested in Mr. Sherlock Holmes' cases."
  3132	
  3133	"I should be ungrateful if I were not, seeing what I gained
  3134	through one of them," I answered. "But if I am to go, I must pack
  3135	at once, for I have only half an hour."
  3136	
  3137	My experience of camp life in Afghanistan had at least had the
  3138	effect of making me a prompt and ready traveller. My wants were
  3139	few and simple, so that in less than the time stated I was in a
  3140	cab with my valise, rattling away to Paddington Station. Sherlock
  3141	Holmes was pacing up and down the platform, his tall, gaunt
  3142	figure made even gaunter and taller by his long grey
  3143	travelling-cloak and close-fitting cloth cap.
  3144	
  3145	"It is really very good of you to come, Watson," said he. "It
  3146	makes a considerable difference to me, having someone with me on
