  3147	whom I can thoroughly rely. Local aid is always either worthless
  3148	or else biassed. If you will keep the two corner seats I shall
  3149	get the tickets."
  3150	
  3151	We had the carriage to ourselves save for an immense litter of
  3152	papers which Holmes had brought with him. Among these he rummaged
  3153	and read, with intervals of note-taking and of meditation, until
  3154	we were past Reading. Then he suddenly rolled them all into a
  3155	gigantic ball and tossed them up onto the rack.
  3156	
  3157	"Have you heard anything of the case?" he asked.
  3158	
  3159	"Not a word. I have not seen a paper for some days."
  3160	
  3161	"The London press has not had very full accounts. I have just
  3162	been looking through all the recent papers in order to master the
  3163	particulars. It seems, from what I gather, to be one of those
  3164	simple cases which are so extremely difficult."
  3165	
  3166	"That sounds a little paradoxical."
  3167	
  3168	"But it is profoundly true. Singularity is almost invariably a
  3169	clue. The more featureless and commonplace a crime is, the more
  3170	difficult it is to bring it home. In this case, however, they
  3171	have established a very serious case against the son of the
  3172	murdered man."
  3173	
  3174	"It is a murder, then?"
  3175	
  3176	"Well, it is conjectured to be so. I shall take nothing for
  3177	granted until I have the opportunity of looking personally into
  3178	it. I will explain the state of things to you, as far as I have
  3179	been able to understand it, in a very few words.
  3180	
  3181	"Boscombe Valley is a country district not very far from Ross, in
  3182	Herefordshire. The largest landed proprietor in that part is a
  3183	Mr. John Turner, who made his money in Australia and returned
  3184	some years ago to the old country. One of the farms which he
  3185	held, that of Hatherley, was let to Mr. Charles McCarthy, who was
  3186	also an ex-Australian. The men had known each other in the
  3187	colonies, so that it was not unnatural that when they came to
  3188	settle down they should do so as near each other as possible.
  3189	Turner was apparently the richer man, so McCarthy became his
  3190	tenant but still remained, it seems, upon terms of perfect
  3191	equality, as they were frequently together. McCarthy had one son,
  3192	a lad of eighteen, and Turner had an only daughter of the same
  3193	age, but neither of them had wives living. They appear to have
  3194	avoided the society of the neighbouring English families and to
  3195	have led retired lives, though both the McCarthys were fond of
  3196	sport and were frequently seen at the race-meetings of the
  3197	neighbourhood. McCarthy kept two servants--a man and a girl.
  3198	Turner had a considerable household, some half-dozen at the
  3199	least. That is as much as I have been able to gather about the
  3200	families. Now for the facts.
  3201	
  3202	"On June 3rd, that is, on Monday last, McCarthy left his house at
  3203	Hatherley about three in the afternoon and walked down to the
  3204	Boscombe Pool, which is a small lake formed by the spreading out
  3205	of the stream which runs down the Boscombe Valley. He had been
  3206	out with his serving-man in the morning at Ross, and he had told
  3207	the man that he must hurry, as he had an appointment of
  3208	importance to keep at three. From that appointment he never came
  3209	back alive.
  3210	
  3211	"From Hatherley Farm-house to the Boscombe Pool is a quarter of a
  3212	mile, and two people saw him as he passed over this ground. One
  3213	was an old woman, whose name is not mentioned, and the other was
  3214	William Crowder, a game-keeper in the employ of Mr. Turner. Both
  3215	these witnesses depose that Mr. McCarthy was walking alone. The
  3216	game-keeper adds that within a few minutes of his seeing Mr.
  3217	McCarthy pass he had seen his son, Mr. James McCarthy, going the
  3218	same way with a gun under his arm. To the best of his belief, the
  3219	father was actually in sight at the time, and the son was
  3220	following him. He thought no more of the matter until he heard in
  3221	the evening of the tragedy that had occurred.
  3222	
  3223	"The two McCarthys were seen after the time when William Crowder,
  3224	the game-keeper, lost sight of them. The Boscombe Pool is thickly
  3225	wooded round, with just a fringe of grass and of reeds round the
  3226	edge. A girl of fourteen, Patience Moran, who is the daughter of
  3227	the lodge-keeper of the Boscombe Valley estate, was in one of the
  3228	woods picking flowers. She states that while she was there she
  3229	saw, at the border of the wood and close by the lake, Mr.
  3230	McCarthy and his son, and that they appeared to be having a
  3231	violent quarrel. She heard Mr. McCarthy the elder using very
  3232	strong language to his son, and she saw the latter raise up his
  3233	hand as if to strike his father. She was so frightened by their
  3234	violence that she ran away and told her mother when she reached
  3235	home that she had left the two McCarthys quarrelling near
  3236	Boscombe Pool, and that she was afraid that they were going to
  3237	fight. She had hardly said the words when young Mr. McCarthy came
  3238	running up to the lodge to say that he had found his father dead
  3239	in the wood, and to ask for the help of the lodge-keeper. He was
  3240	much excited, without either his gun or his hat, and his right
  3241	hand and sleeve were observed to be stained with fresh blood. On
  3242	following him they found the dead body stretched out upon the
  3243	grass beside the pool. The head had been beaten in by repeated
  3244	blows of some heavy and blunt weapon. The injuries were such as
  3245	might very well have been inflicted by the butt-end of his son's
  3246	gun, which was found lying on the grass within a few paces of the
  3247	body. Under these circumstances the young man was instantly
  3248	arrested, and a verdict of 'wilful murder' having been returned
  3249	at the inquest on Tuesday, he was on Wednesday brought before the
  3250	magistrates at Ross, who have referred the case to the next
  3251	Assizes. Those are the main facts of the case as they came out
  3252	before the coroner and the police-court."
  3253	
  3254	"I could hardly imagine a more damning case," I remarked. "If
  3255	ever circumstantial evidence pointed to a criminal it does so
  3256	here."
  3257	
  3258	"Circumstantial evidence is a very tricky thing," answered Holmes
  3259	thoughtfully. "It may seem to point very straight to one thing,
  3260	but if you shift your own point of view a little, you may find it
  3261	pointing in an equally uncompromising manner to something
  3262	entirely different. It must be confessed, however, that the case
  3263	looks exceedingly grave against the young man, and it is very
  3264	possible that he is indeed the culprit. There are several people
  3265	in the neighbourhood, however, and among them Miss Turner, the
  3266	daughter of the neighbouring landowner, who believe in his
  3267	innocence, and who have retained Lestrade, whom you may recollect
  3268	in connection with the Study in Scarlet, to work out the case in
  3269	his interest. Lestrade, being rather puzzled, has referred the
  3270	case to me, and hence it is that two middle-aged gentlemen are
  3271	flying westward at fifty miles an hour instead of quietly
  3272	digesting their breakfasts at home."
  3273	
  3274	"I am afraid," said I, "that the facts are so obvious that you
  3275	will find little credit to be gained out of this case."
  3276	
  3277	"There is nothing more deceptive than an obvious fact," he
  3278	answered, laughing. "Besides, we may chance to hit upon some
  3279	other obvious facts which may have been by no means obvious to
  3280	Mr. Lestrade. You know me too well to think that I am boasting
  3281	when I say that I shall either confirm or destroy his theory by
  3282	means which he is quite incapable of employing, or even of
  3283	understanding. To take the first example to hand, I very clearly
  3284	perceive that in your bedroom the window is upon the right-hand
  3285	side, and yet I question whether Mr. Lestrade would have noted
  3286	even so self-evident a thing as that."
  3287	
  3288	"How on earth--"
  3289	
  3290	"My dear fellow, I know you well. I know the military neatness
  3291	which characterises you. You shave every morning, and in this
  3292	season you shave by the sunlight; but since your shaving is less
  3293	and less complete as we get farther back on the left side, until
  3294	it becomes positively slovenly as we get round the angle of the
  3295	jaw, it is surely very clear that that side is less illuminated
  3296	than the other. I could not imagine a man of your habits looking
  3297	at himself in an equal light and being satisfied with such a
  3298	result. I only quote this as a trivial example of observation and
  3299	inference. Therein lies my m?tier, and it is just possible that
  3300	it may be of some service in the investigation which lies before
  3301	us. There are one or two minor points which were brought out in
  3302	the inquest, and which are worth considering."
  3303	
  3304	"What are they?"
  3305	
  3306	"It appears that his arrest did not take place at once, but after
  3307	the return to Hatherley Farm. On the inspector of constabulary
  3308	informing him that he was a prisoner, he remarked that he was not
  3309	surprised to hear it, and that it was no more than his deserts.
  3310	This observation of his had the natural effect of removing any
  3311	traces of doubt which might have remained in the minds of the
  3312	coroner's jury."
  3313	
  3314	"It was a confession," I ejaculated.
  3315	
  3316	"No, for it was followed by a protestation of innocence."
  3317	
  3318	"Coming on the top of such a damning series of events, it was at
  3319	least a most suspicious remark."
  3320	
  3321	"On the contrary," said Holmes, "it is the brightest rift which I
  3322	can at present see in the clouds. However innocent he might be,
  3323	he could not be such an absolute imbecile as not to see that the
  3324	circumstances were very black against him. Had he appeared
  3325	surprised at his own arrest, or feigned indignation at it, I
  3326	should have looked upon it as highly suspicious, because such
  3327	surprise or anger would not be natural under the circumstances,
  3328	and yet might appear to be the best policy to a scheming man. His
  3329	frank acceptance of the situation marks him as either an innocent
  3330	man, or else as a man of considerable self-restraint and
  3331	firmness. As to his remark about his deserts, it was also not
  3332	unnatural if you consider that he stood beside the dead body of
  3333	his father, and that there is no doubt that he had that very day
  3334	so far forgotten his filial duty as to bandy words with him, and
  3335	even, according to the little girl whose evidence is so
  3336	important, to raise his hand as if to strike him. The
  3337	self-reproach and contrition which are displayed in his remark
  3338	appear to me to be the signs of a healthy mind rather than of a
  3339	guilty one."
  3340	
  3341	I shook my head. "Many men have been hanged on far slighter
  3342	evidence," I remarked.
  3343	
  3344	"So they have. And many men have been wrongfully hanged."
  3345	
  3346	"What is the young man's own account of the matter?"
  3347	
  3348	"It is, I am afraid, not very encouraging to his supporters,
  3349	though there are one or two points in it which are suggestive.
  3350	You will find it here, and may read it for yourself."
  3351	
  3352	He picked out from his bundle a copy of the local Herefordshire
  3353	paper, and having turned down the sheet he pointed out the
  3354	paragraph in which the unfortunate young man had given his own
  3355	statement of what had occurred. I settled myself down in the
  3356	corner of the carriage and read it very carefully. It ran in this
  3357	way:
  3358	
  3359	"Mr. James McCarthy, the only son of the deceased, was then called
  3360	and gave evidence as follows: 'I had been away from home for
  3361	three days at Bristol, and had only just returned upon the
  3362	morning of last Monday, the 3rd. My father was absent from home at
  3363	the time of my arrival, and I was informed by the maid that he
  3364	had driven over to Ross with John Cobb, the groom. Shortly after
  3365	my return I heard the wheels of his trap in the yard, and,
  3366	looking out of my window, I saw him get out and walk rapidly out
  3367	of the yard, though I was not aware in which direction he was
  3368	going. I then took my gun and strolled out in the direction of
  3369	the Boscombe Pool, with the intention of visiting the rabbit
  3370	warren which is upon the other side. On my way I saw William
  3371	Crowder, the game-keeper, as he had stated in his evidence; but
  3372	he is mistaken in thinking that I was following my father. I had
  3373	no idea that he was in front of me. When about a hundred yards
  3374	from the pool I heard a cry of "Cooee!" which was a usual signal
  3375	between my father and myself. I then hurried forward, and found
  3376	him standing by the pool. He appeared to be much surprised at
  3377	seeing me and asked me rather roughly what I was doing there. A
  3378	conversation ensued which led to high words and almost to blows,
  3379	for my father was a man of a very violent temper. Seeing that his
  3380	passion was becoming ungovernable, I left him and returned
  3381	towards Hatherley Farm. I had not gone more than 150 yards,
  3382	however, when I heard a hideous outcry behind me, which caused me
  3383	to run back again. I found my father expiring upon the ground,
  3384	with his head terribly injured. I dropped my gun and held him in
  3385	my arms, but he almost instantly expired. I knelt beside him for
  3386	some minutes, and then made my way to Mr. Turner's lodge-keeper,
  3387	his house being the nearest, to ask for assistance. I saw no one
  3388	near my father when I returned, and I have no idea how he came by
  3389	his injuries. He was not a popular man, being somewhat cold and
  3390	forbidding in his manners, but he had, as far as I know, no
  3391	active enemies. I know nothing further of the matter.'
  3392	
  3393	"The Coroner: Did your father make any statement to you before
  3394	he died?
  3395	
  3396	"Witness: He mumbled a few words, but I could only catch some
  3397	allusion to a rat.
  3398	
  3399	"The Coroner: What did you understand by that?
  3400	
  3401	"Witness: It conveyed no meaning to me. I thought that he was
  3402	delirious.
  3403	
  3404	"The Coroner: What was the point upon which you and your father
  3405	had this final quarrel?
  3406	
  3407	"Witness: I should prefer not to answer.
  3408	
  3409	"The Coroner: I am afraid that I must press it.
  3410	
  3411	"Witness: It is really impossible for me to tell you. I can
  3412	assure you that it has nothing to do with the sad tragedy which
  3413	followed.
  3414	
  3415	"The Coroner: That is for the court to decide. I need not point
  3416	out to you that your refusal to answer will prejudice your case
  3417	considerably in any future proceedings which may arise.
  3418	
  3419	"Witness: I must still refuse.
  3420	
  3421	"The Coroner: I understand that the cry of 'Cooee' was a common
  3422	signal between you and your father?
  3423	
  3424	"Witness: It was.
  3425	
  3426	"The Coroner: How was it, then, that he uttered it before he saw
  3427	you, and before he even knew that you had returned from Bristol?
  3428	
  3429	"Witness (with considerable confusion): I do not know.
  3430	
  3431	"A Juryman: Did you see nothing which aroused your suspicions
  3432	when you returned on hearing the cry and found your father
  3433	fatally injured?
  3434	
  3435	"Witness: Nothing definite.
  3436	
  3437	"The Coroner: What do you mean?
  3438	
  3439	"Witness: I was so disturbed and excited as I rushed out into
  3440	the open, that I could think of nothing except of my father. Yet
  3441	I have a vague impression that as I ran forward something lay
  3442	upon the ground to the left of me. It seemed to me to be
  3443	something grey in colour, a coat of some sort, or a plaid perhaps.
  3444	When I rose from my father I looked round for it, but it was
  3445	gone.
  3446	
  3447	"'Do you mean that it disappeared before you went for help?'
  3448	
  3449	"'Yes, it was gone.'
  3450	
  3451	"'You cannot say what it was?'
  3452	
  3453	"'No, I had a feeling something was there.'
  3454	
  3455	"'How far from the body?'
  3456	
  3457	"'A dozen yards or so.'
  3458	
  3459	"'And how far from the edge of the wood?'
  3460	
  3461	"'About the same.'
  3462	
  3463	"'Then if it was removed it was while you were within a dozen
  3464	yards of it?'
  3465	
  3466	"'Yes, but with my back towards it.'
  3467	
  3468	"This concluded the examination of the witness."
  3469	
  3470	"I see," said I as I glanced down the column, "that the coroner
  3471	in his concluding remarks was rather severe upon young McCarthy.
  3472	He calls attention, and with reason, to the discrepancy about his
  3473	father having signalled to him before seeing him, also to his
  3474	refusal to give details of his conversation with his father, and
  3475	his singular account of his father's dying words. They are all,
  3476	as he remarks, very much against the son."
  3477	
  3478	Holmes laughed softly to himself and stretched himself out upon
  3479	the cushioned seat. "Both you and the coroner have been at some
  3480	pains," said he, "to single out the very strongest points in the
  3481	young man's favour. Don't you see that you alternately give him
  3482	credit for having too much imagination and too little? Too
  3483	little, if he could not invent a cause of quarrel which would
  3484	give him the sympathy of the jury; too much, if he evolved from
  3485	his own inner consciousness anything so outr? as a dying
  3486	reference to a rat, and the incident of the vanishing cloth. No,
  3487	sir, I shall approach this case from the point of view that what
  3488	this young man says is true, and we shall see whither that
  3489	hypothesis will lead us. And now here is my pocket Petrarch, and
  3490	not another word shall I say of this case until we are on the
  3491	scene of action. We lunch at Swindon, and I see that we shall be
  3492	there in twenty minutes."
  3493	
  3494	It was nearly four o'clock when we at last, after passing through
  3495	the beautiful Stroud Valley, and over the broad gleaming Severn,
  3496	found ourselves at the pretty little country-town of Ross. A
  3497	lean, ferret-like man, furtive and sly-looking, was waiting for
  3498	us upon the platform. In spite of the light brown dustcoat and
  3499	leather-leggings which he wore in deference to his rustic
  3500	surroundings, I had no difficulty in recognising Lestrade, of
  3501	Scotland Yard. With him we drove to the Hereford Arms where a
  3502	room had already been engaged for us.
  3503	
  3504	"I have ordered a carriage," said Lestrade as we sat over a cup
  3505	of tea. "I knew your energetic nature, and that you would not be
  3506	happy until you had been on the scene of the crime."
  3507	
  3508	"It was very nice and complimentary of you," Holmes answered. "It
  3509	is entirely a question of barometric pressure."
  3510	
  3511	Lestrade looked startled. "I do not quite follow," he said.
  3512	
  3513	"How is the glass? Twenty-nine, I see. No wind, and not a cloud
  3514	in the sky. I have a caseful of cigarettes here which need
  3515	smoking, and the sofa is very much superior to the usual country
  3516	hotel abomination. I do not think that it is probable that I
  3517	shall use the carriage to-night."
  3518	
  3519	Lestrade laughed indulgently. "You have, no doubt, already formed
  3520	your conclusions from the newspapers," he said. "The case is as
  3521	plain as a pikestaff, and the more one goes into it the plainer
  3522	it becomes. Still, of course, one can't refuse a lady, and such a
  3523	very positive one, too. She has heard of you, and would have your
  3524	opinion, though I repeatedly told her that there was nothing
  3525	which you could do which I had not already done. Why, bless my
  3526	soul! here is her carriage at the door."
  3527	
  3528	He had hardly spoken before there rushed into the room one of the
  3529	most lovely young women that I have ever seen in my life. Her
  3530	violet eyes shining, her lips parted, a pink flush upon her
  3531	cheeks, all thought of her natural reserve lost in her
  3532	overpowering excitement and concern.
  3533	
  3534	"Oh, Mr. Sherlock Holmes!" she cried, glancing from one to the
  3535	other of us, and finally, with a woman's quick intuition,
  3536	fastening upon my companion, "I am so glad that you have come. I
  3537	have driven down to tell you so. I know that James didn't do it.
  3538	I know it, and I want you to start upon your work knowing it,
  3539	too. Never let yourself doubt upon that point. We have known each
  3540	other since we were little children, and I know his faults as no
  3541	one else does; but he is too tender-hearted to hurt a fly. Such a
  3542	charge is absurd to anyone who really knows him."
  3543	
  3544	"I hope we may clear him, Miss Turner," said Sherlock Holmes.
  3545	"You may rely upon my doing all that I can."
  3546	
  3547	"But you have read the evidence. You have formed some conclusion?
  3548	Do you not see some loophole, some flaw? Do you not yourself
  3549	think that he is innocent?"
  3550	
  3551	"I think that it is very probable."
  3552	
  3553	"There, now!" she cried, throwing back her head and looking
  3554	defiantly at Lestrade. "You hear! He gives me hopes."
  3555	
  3556	Lestrade shrugged his shoulders. "I am afraid that my colleague
  3557	has been a little quick in forming his conclusions," he said.
  3558	
  3559	"But he is right. Oh! I know that he is right. James never did
  3560	it. And about his quarrel with his father, I am sure that the
  3561	reason why he would not speak about it to the coroner was because
  3562	I was concerned in it."
  3563	
  3564	"In what way?" asked Holmes.
  3565	
  3566	"It is no time for me to hide anything. James and his father had
  3567	many disagreements about me. Mr. McCarthy was very anxious that
  3568	there should be a marriage between us. James and I have always
  3569	loved each other as brother and sister; but of course he is young
  3570	and has seen very little of life yet, and--and--well, he
  3571	naturally did not wish to do anything like that yet. So there
  3572	were quarrels, and this, I am sure, was one of them."
  3573	
  3574	"And your father?" asked Holmes. "Was he in favour of such a
  3575	union?"
  3576	
  3577	"No, he was averse to it also. No one but Mr. McCarthy was in
  3578	favour of it." A quick blush passed over her fresh young face as
  3579	Holmes shot one of his keen, questioning glances at her.
  3580	
  3581	"Thank you for this information," said he. "May I see your father
  3582	if I call to-morrow?"
  3583	
  3584	"I am afraid the doctor won't allow it."
  3585	
  3586	"The doctor?"
  3587	
  3588	"Yes, have you not heard? Poor father has never been strong for
  3589	years back, but this has broken him down completely. He has taken
  3590	to his bed, and Dr. Willows says that he is a wreck and that his
  3591	nervous system is shattered. Mr. McCarthy was the only man alive
  3592	who had known dad in the old days in Victoria."
  3593	
  3594	"Ha! In Victoria! That is important."
  3595	
  3596	"Yes, at the mines."
  3597	
  3598	"Quite so; at the gold-mines, where, as I understand, Mr. Turner
  3599	made his money."
  3600	
  3601	"Yes, certainly."
  3602	
  3603	"Thank you, Miss Turner. You have been of material assistance to
  3604	me."
  3605	
  3606	"You will tell me if you have any news to-morrow. No doubt you
  3607	will go to the prison to see James. Oh, if you do, Mr. Holmes, do
  3608	tell him that I know him to be innocent."
  3609	
  3610	"I will, Miss Turner."
  3611	
  3612	"I must go home now, for dad is very ill, and he misses me so if
  3613	I leave him. Good-bye, and God help you in your undertaking." She
  3614	hurried from the room as impulsively as she had entered, and we
  3615	heard the wheels of her carriage rattle off down the street.
  3616	
  3617	"I am ashamed of you, Holmes," said Lestrade with dignity after a
  3618	few minutes' silence. "Why should you raise up hopes which you
  3619	are bound to disappoint? I am not over-tender of heart, but I
  3620	call it cruel."
  3621	
  3622	"I think that I see my way to clearing James McCarthy," said
  3623	Holmes. "Have you an order to see him in prison?"
  3624	
  3625	"Yes, but only for you and me."
  3626	
  3627	"Then I shall reconsider my resolution about going out. We have
  3628	still time to take a train to Hereford and see him to-night?"
  3629	
  3630	"Ample."
  3631	
  3632	"Then let us do so. Watson, I fear that you will find it very
  3633	slow, but I shall only be away a couple of hours."
  3634	
  3635	I walked down to the station with them, and then wandered through
  3636	the streets of the little town, finally returning to the hotel,
  3637	where I lay upon the sofa and tried to interest myself in a
  3638	yellow-backed novel. The puny plot of the story was so thin,
  3639	however, when compared to the deep mystery through which we were
  3640	groping, and I found my attention wander so continually from the
  3641	action to the fact, that I at last flung it across the room and
  3642	gave myself up entirely to a consideration of the events of the
  3643	day. Supposing that this unhappy young man's story were
  3644	absolutely true, then what hellish thing, what absolutely
  3645	unforeseen and extraordinary calamity could have occurred between
  3646	the time when he parted from his father, and the moment when,
  3647	drawn back by his screams, he rushed into the glade? It was
  3648	something terrible and deadly. What could it be? Might not the
  3649	nature of the injuries reveal something to my medical instincts?
  3650	I rang the bell and called for the weekly county paper, which
  3651	contained a verbatim account of the inquest. In the surgeon's
  3652	deposition it was stated that the posterior third of the left
  3653	parietal bone and the left half of the occipital bone had been
  3654	shattered by a heavy blow from a blunt weapon. I marked the spot
  3655	upon my own head. Clearly such a blow must have been struck from
  3656	behind. That was to some extent in favour of the accused, as when
  3657	seen quarrelling he was face to face with his father. Still, it
  3658	did not go for very much, for the older man might have turned his
  3659	back before the blow fell. Still, it might be worth while to call
  3660	Holmes' attention to it. Then there was the peculiar dying
  3661	reference to a rat. What could that mean? It could not be
  3662	delirium. A man dying from a sudden blow does not commonly become
  3663	delirious. No, it was more likely to be an attempt to explain how
  3664	he met his fate. But what could it indicate? I cudgelled my
  3665	brains to find some possible explanation. And then the incident
  3666	of the grey cloth seen by young McCarthy. If that were true the
  3667	murderer must have dropped some part of his dress, presumably his
  3668	overcoat, in his flight, and must have had the hardihood to
  3669	return and to carry it away at the instant when the son was
  3670	kneeling with his back turned not a dozen paces off. What a
  3671	tissue of mysteries and improbabilities the whole thing was! I
  3672	did not wonder at Lestrade's opinion, and yet I had so much faith
  3673	in Sherlock Holmes' insight that I could not lose hope as long
  3674	as every fresh fact seemed to strengthen his conviction of young
  3675	McCarthy's innocence.
  3676	
  3677	It was late before Sherlock Holmes returned. He came back alone,
  3678	for Lestrade was staying in lodgings in the town.
  3679	
  3680	"The glass still keeps very high," he remarked as he sat down.
  3681	"It is of importance that it should not rain before we are able
  3682	to go over the ground. On the other hand, a man should be at his
  3683	very best and keenest for such nice work as that, and I did not
  3684	wish to do it when fagged by a long journey. I have seen young
  3685	McCarthy."
  3686	
  3687	"And what did you learn from him?"
  3688	
  3689	"Nothing."
  3690	
  3691	"Could he throw no light?"
  3692	
  3693	"None at all. I was inclined to think at one time that he knew
  3694	who had done it and was screening him or her, but I am convinced
  3695	now that he is as puzzled as everyone else. He is not a very
  3696	quick-witted youth, though comely to look at and, I should think,
  3697	sound at heart."
  3698	
  3699	"I cannot admire his taste," I remarked, "if it is indeed a fact
  3700	that he was averse to a marriage with so charming a young lady as
  3701	this Miss Turner."
  3702	
  3703	"Ah, thereby hangs a rather painful tale. This fellow is madly,
  3704	insanely, in love with her, but some two years ago, when he was
  3705	only a lad, and before he really knew her, for she had been away
  3706	five years at a boarding-school, what does the idiot do but get
  3707	into the clutches of a barmaid in Bristol and marry her at a
  3708	registry office? No one knows a word of the matter, but you can
  3709	imagine how maddening it must be to him to be upbraided for not
  3710	doing what he would give his very eyes to do, but what he knows
  3711	to be absolutely impossible. It was sheer frenzy of this sort
  3712	which made him throw his hands up into the air when his father,
  3713	at their last interview, was goading him on to propose to Miss
  3714	Turner. On the other hand, he had no means of supporting himself,
  3715	and his father, who was by all accounts a very hard man, would
  3716	have thrown him over utterly had he known the truth. It was with
  3717	his barmaid wife that he had spent the last three days in
  3718	Bristol, and his father did not know where he was. Mark that
  3719	point. It is of importance. Good has come out of evil, however,
  3720	for the barmaid, finding from the papers that he is in serious
  3721	trouble and likely to be hanged, has thrown him over utterly and
  3722	has written to him to say that she has a husband already in the
  3723	Bermuda Dockyard, so that there is really no tie between them. I
  3724	think that that bit of news has consoled young McCarthy for all
  3725	that he has suffered."
  3726	
  3727	"But if he is innocent, who has done it?"
  3728	
  3729	"Ah! who? I would call your attention very particularly to two
  3730	points. One is that the murdered man had an appointment with
  3731	someone at the pool, and that the someone could not have been his
  3732	son, for his son was away, and he did not know when he would
  3733	return. The second is that the murdered man was heard to cry
  3734	'Cooee!' before he knew that his son had returned. Those are the
  3735	crucial points upon which the case depends. And now let us talk
  3736	about George Meredith, if you please, and we shall leave all
  3737	minor matters until to-morrow."
  3738	
  3739	There was no rain, as Holmes had foretold, and the morning broke
  3740	bright and cloudless. At nine o'clock Lestrade called for us with
  3741	the carriage, and we set off for Hatherley Farm and the Boscombe
  3742	Pool.
  3743	
  3744	"There is serious news this morning," Lestrade observed. "It is
  3745	said that Mr. Turner, of the Hall, is so ill that his life is
  3746	despaired of."
  3747	
  3748	"An elderly man, I presume?" said Holmes.
  3749	
  3750	"About sixty; but his constitution has been shattered by his life
  3751	abroad, and he has been in failing health for some time. This
  3752	business has had a very bad effect upon him. He was an old friend
  3753	of McCarthy's, and, I may add, a great benefactor to him, for I
  3754	have learned that he gave him Hatherley Farm rent free."
  3755	
  3756	"Indeed! That is interesting," said Holmes.
  3757	
  3758	"Oh, yes! In a hundred other ways he has helped him. Everybody
  3759	about here speaks of his kindness to him."
  3760	
  3761	"Really! Does it not strike you as a little singular that this
  3762	McCarthy, who appears to have had little of his own, and to have
  3763	been under such obligations to Turner, should still talk of
  3764	marrying his son to Turner's daughter, who is, presumably,
  3765	heiress to the estate, and that in such a very cocksure manner,
  3766	as if it were merely a case of a proposal and all else would
  3767	follow? It is the more strange, since we know that Turner himself
  3768	was averse to the idea. The daughter told us as much. Do you not
  3769	deduce something from that?"
  3770	
  3771	"We have got to the deductions and the inferences," said
  3772	Lestrade, winking at me. "I find it hard enough to tackle facts,
  3773	Holmes, without flying away after theories and fancies."
  3774	
  3775	"You are right," said Holmes demurely; "you do find it very hard
  3776	to tackle the facts."
  3777	
  3778	"Anyhow, I have grasped one fact which you seem to find it
  3779	difficult to get hold of," replied Lestrade with some warmth.
  3780	
  3781	"And that is--"
  3782	
  3783	"That McCarthy senior met his death from McCarthy junior and that
  3784	all theories to the contrary are the merest moonshine."
  3785	
  3786	"Well, moonshine is a brighter thing than fog," said Holmes,
  3787	laughing. "But I am very much mistaken if this is not Hatherley
  3788	Farm upon the left."
  3789	
  3790	"Yes, that is it." It was a widespread, comfortable-looking
  3791	building, two-storied, slate-roofed, with great yellow blotches
  3792	of lichen upon the grey walls. The drawn blinds and the smokeless
  3793	chimneys, however, gave it a stricken look, as though the weight
  3794	of this horror still lay heavy upon it. We called at the door,
  3795	when the maid, at Holmes' request, showed us the boots which her
  3796	master wore at the time of his death, and also a pair of the
  3797	son's, though not the pair which he had then had. Having measured
  3798	these very carefully from seven or eight different points, Holmes
  3799	desired to be led to the court-yard, from which we all followed
  3800	the winding track which led to Boscombe Pool.
  3801	
  3802	Sherlock Holmes was transformed when he was hot upon such a scent
  3803	as this. Men who had only known the quiet thinker and logician of
  3804	Baker Street would have failed to recognise him. His face flushed
  3805	and darkened. His brows were drawn into two hard black lines,
  3806	while his eyes shone out from beneath them with a steely glitter.
  3807	His face was bent downward, his shoulders bowed, his lips
  3808	compressed, and the veins stood out like whipcord in his long,
  3809	sinewy neck. His nostrils seemed to dilate with a purely animal
  3810	lust for the chase, and his mind was so absolutely concentrated
  3811	upon the matter before him that a question or remark fell
  3812	unheeded upon his ears, or, at the most, only provoked a quick,
  3813	impatient snarl in reply. Swiftly and silently he made his way
  3814	along the track which ran through the meadows, and so by way of
  3815	the woods to the Boscombe Pool. It was damp, marshy ground, as is
  3816	all that district, and there were marks of many feet, both upon
  3817	the path and amid the short grass which bounded it on either
  3818	side. Sometimes Holmes would hurry on, sometimes stop dead, and
  3819	once he made quite a little detour into the meadow. Lestrade and
  3820	I walked behind him, the detective indifferent and contemptuous,
  3821	while I watched my friend with the interest which sprang from the
  3822	conviction that every one of his actions was directed towards a
  3823	definite end.
  3824	
  3825	The Boscombe Pool, which is a little reed-girt sheet of water
  3826	some fifty yards across, is situated at the boundary between the
  3827	Hatherley Farm and the private park of the wealthy Mr. Turner.
  3828	Above the woods which lined it upon the farther side we could see
  3829	the red, jutting pinnacles which marked the site of the rich
  3830	landowner's dwelling. On the Hatherley side of the pool the woods
  3831	grew very thick, and there was a narrow belt of sodden grass
  3832	twenty paces across between the edge of the trees and the reeds
  3833	which lined the lake. Lestrade showed us the exact spot at which
  3834	the body had been found, and, indeed, so moist was the ground,
  3835	that I could plainly see the traces which had been left by the
  3836	fall of the stricken man. To Holmes, as I could see by his eager
  3837	face and peering eyes, very many other things were to be read
  3838	upon the trampled grass. He ran round, like a dog who is picking
  3839	up a scent, and then turned upon my companion.
  3840	
  3841	"What did you go into the pool for?" he asked.
  3842	
  3843	"I fished about with a rake. I thought there might be some weapon
  3844	or other trace. But how on earth--"
  3845	
  3846	"Oh, tut, tut! I have no time! That left foot of yours with its
  3847	inward twist is all over the place. A mole could trace it, and
  3848	there it vanishes among the reeds. Oh, how simple it would all
  3849	have been had I been here before they came like a herd of buffalo
  3850	and wallowed all over it. Here is where the party with the
  3851	lodge-keeper came, and they have covered all tracks for six or
  3852	eight feet round the body. But here are three separate tracks of
  3853	the same feet." He drew out a lens and lay down upon his
  3854	waterproof to have a better view, talking all the time rather to
  3855	himself than to us. "These are young McCarthy's feet. Twice he
  3856	was walking, and once he ran swiftly, so that the soles are
  3857	deeply marked and the heels hardly visible. That bears out his
  3858	story. He ran when he saw his father on the ground. Then here are
  3859	the father's feet as he paced up and down. What is this, then? It
  3860	is the butt-end of the gun as the son stood listening. And this?
  3861	Ha, ha! What have we here? Tiptoes! tiptoes! Square, too, quite
  3862	unusual boots! They come, they go, they come again--of course
  3863	that was for the cloak. Now where did they come from?" He ran up
  3864	and down, sometimes losing, sometimes finding the track until we
  3865	were well within the edge of the wood and under the shadow of a
  3866	great beech, the largest tree in the neighbourhood. Holmes traced
  3867	his way to the farther side of this and lay down once more upon
  3868	his face with a little cry of satisfaction. For a long time he
  3869	remained there, turning over the leaves and dried sticks,
  3870	gathering up what seemed to me to be dust into an envelope and
  3871	examining with his lens not only the ground but even the bark of
  3872	the tree as far as he could reach. A jagged stone was lying among
  3873	the moss, and this also he carefully examined and retained. Then
  3874	he followed a pathway through the wood until he came to the
  3875	highroad, where all traces were lost.
  3876	
  3877	"It has been a case of considerable interest," he remarked,
  3878	returning to his natural manner. "I fancy that this grey house on
  3879	the right must be the lodge. I think that I will go in and have a
  3880	word with Moran, and perhaps write a little note. Having done
  3881	that, we may drive back to our luncheon. You may walk to the cab,
  3882	and I shall be with you presently."
  3883	
  3884	It was about ten minutes before we regained our cab and drove
  3885	back into Ross, Holmes still carrying with him the stone which he
  3886	had picked up in the wood.
  3887	
  3888	"This may interest you, Lestrade," he remarked, holding it out.
  3889	"The murder was done with it."
  3890	
  3891	"I see no marks."
  3892	
  3893	"There are none."
  3894	
  3895	"How do you know, then?"
  3896	
  3897	"The grass was growing under it. It had only lain there a few
  3898	days. There was no sign of a place whence it had been taken. It
  3899	corresponds with the injuries. There is no sign of any other
  3900	weapon."
  3901	
  3902	"And the murderer?"
  3903	
  3904	"Is a tall man, left-handed, limps with the right leg, wears
  3905	thick-soled shooting-boots and a grey cloak, smokes Indian
  3906	cigars, uses a cigar-holder, and carries a blunt pen-knife in his
  3907	pocket. There are several other indications, but these may be
  3908	enough to aid us in our search."
  3909	
  3910	Lestrade laughed. "I am afraid that I am still a sceptic," he
  3911	said. "Theories are all very well, but we have to deal with a
  3912	hard-headed British jury."
  3913	
  3914	"Nous verrons," answered Holmes calmly. "You work your own
  3915	method, and I shall work mine. I shall be busy this afternoon,
  3916	and shall probably return to London by the evening train."
  3917	
  3918	"And leave your case unfinished?"
  3919	
  3920	"No, finished."
  3921	
  3922	"But the mystery?"
  3923	
  3924	"It is solved."
  3925	
  3926	"Who was the criminal, then?"
  3927	
  3928	"The gentleman I describe."
  3929	
  3930	"But who is he?"
  3931	
  3932	"Surely it would not be difficult to find out. This is not such a
  3933	populous neighbourhood."
  3934	
  3935	Lestrade shrugged his shoulders. "I am a practical man," he said,
  3936	"and I really cannot undertake to go about the country looking
  3937	for a left-handed gentleman with a game leg. I should become the
  3938	laughing-stock of Scotland Yard."
  3939	
  3940	"All right," said Holmes quietly. "I have given you the chance.
  3941	Here are your lodgings. Good-bye. I shall drop you a line before
  3942	I leave."
  3943	
  3944	Having left Lestrade at his rooms, we drove to our hotel, where
  3945	we found lunch upon the table. Holmes was silent and buried in
  3946	thought with a pained expression upon his face, as one who finds
  3947	himself in a perplexing position.
  3948	
  3949	"Look here, Watson," he said when the cloth was cleared "just sit
  3950	down in this chair and let me preach to you for a little. I don't
  3951	know quite what to do, and I should value your advice. Light a
  3952	cigar and let me expound."
  3953	
  3954	 "Pray do so."
  3955	
  3956	"Well, now, in considering this case there are two points about
  3957	young McCarthy's narrative which struck us both instantly,
  3958	although they impressed me in his favour and you against him. One
  3959	was the fact that his father should, according to his account,
  3960	cry 'Cooee!' before seeing him. The other was his singular dying
  3961	reference to a rat. He mumbled several words, you understand, but
  3962	that was all that caught the son's ear. Now from this double
  3963	point our research must commence, and we will begin it by
  3964	presuming that what the lad says is absolutely true."
  3965	
  3966	"What of this 'Cooee!' then?"
  3967	
  3968	"Well, obviously it could not have been meant for the son. The
  3969	son, as far as he knew, was in Bristol. It was mere chance that
  3970	he was within earshot. The 'Cooee!' was meant to attract the
  3971	attention of whoever it was that he had the appointment with. But
  3972	'Cooee' is a distinctly Australian cry, and one which is used
  3973	between Australians. There is a strong presumption that the
  3974	person whom McCarthy expected to meet him at Boscombe Pool was
  3975	someone who had been in Australia."
  3976	
  3977	"What of the rat, then?"
  3978	
  3979	Sherlock Holmes took a folded paper from his pocket and flattened
  3980	it out on the table. "This is a map of the Colony of Victoria,"
  3981	he said. "I wired to Bristol for it last night." He put his hand
  3982	over part of the map. "What do you read?"
  3983	
  3984	"ARAT," I read.
  3985	
  3986	"And now?" He raised his hand.
  3987	
  3988	"BALLARAT."
  3989	
  3990	"Quite so. That was the word the man uttered, and of which his
  3991	son only caught the last two syllables. He was trying to utter
  3992	the name of his murderer. So and so, of Ballarat."
  3993	
  3994	"It is wonderful!" I exclaimed.
  3995	
  3996	"It is obvious. And now, you see, I had narrowed the field down
  3997	considerably. The possession of a grey garment was a third point
  3998	which, granting the son's statement to be correct, was a
  3999	certainty. We have come now out of mere vagueness to the definite
  4000	conception of an Australian from Ballarat with a grey cloak."
  4001	
  4002	"Certainly."
  4003	
  4004	"And one who was at home in the district, for the pool can only
  4005	be approached by the farm or by the estate, where strangers could
  4006	hardly wander."
  4007	
  4008	"Quite so."
  4009	
  4010	"Then comes our expedition of to-day. By an examination of the
  4011	ground I gained the trifling details which I gave to that
  4012	imbecile Lestrade, as to the personality of the criminal."
  4013	
  4014	"But how did you gain them?"
  4015	
  4016	"You know my method. It is founded upon the observation of
  4017	trifles."
  4018	
  4019	"His height I know that you might roughly judge from the length
  4020	of his stride. His boots, too, might be told from their traces."
  4021	
  4022	"Yes, they were peculiar boots."
  4023	
  4024	"But his lameness?"
  4025	
  4026	"The impression of his right foot was always less distinct than
  4027	his left. He put less weight upon it. Why? Because he limped--he
  4028	was lame."
  4029	
  4030	"But his left-handedness."
  4031	
  4032	"You were yourself struck by the nature of the injury as recorded
  4033	by the surgeon at the inquest. The blow was struck from
  4034	immediately behind, and yet was upon the left side. Now, how can
  4035	that be unless it were by a left-handed man? He had stood behind
  4036	that tree during the interview between the father and son. He had
  4037	even smoked there. I found the ash of a cigar, which my special
  4038	knowledge of tobacco ashes enables me to pronounce as an Indian
  4039	cigar. I have, as you know, devoted some attention to this, and
  4040	written a little monograph on the ashes of 140 different
  4041	varieties of pipe, cigar, and cigarette tobacco. Having found the
  4042	ash, I then looked round and discovered the stump among the moss
  4043	where he had tossed it. It was an Indian cigar, of the variety
  4044	which are rolled in Rotterdam."
  4045	
  4046	"And the cigar-holder?"
  4047	
  4048	"I could see that the end had not been in his mouth. Therefore he
  4049	used a holder. The tip had been cut off, not bitten off, but the
  4050	cut was not a clean one, so I deduced a blunt pen-knife."
  4051	
  4052	"Holmes," I said, "you have drawn a net round this man from which
  4053	he cannot escape, and you have saved an innocent human life as
  4054	truly as if you had cut the cord which was hanging him. I see the
  4055	direction in which all this points. The culprit is--"
  4056	
  4057	"Mr. John Turner," cried the hotel waiter, opening the door of
  4058	our sitting-room, and ushering in a visitor.
  4059	
  4060	The man who entered was a strange and impressive figure. His
  4061	slow, limping step and bowed shoulders gave the appearance of
  4062	decrepitude, and yet his hard, deep-lined, craggy features, and
  4063	his enormous limbs showed that he was possessed of unusual
  4064	strength of body and of character. His tangled beard, grizzled
  4065	hair, and outstanding, drooping eyebrows combined to give an air
  4066	of dignity and power to his appearance, but his face was of an
  4067	ashen white, while his lips and the corners of his nostrils were
  4068	tinged with a shade of blue. It was clear to me at a glance that
  4069	he was in the grip of some deadly and chronic disease.
  4070	
  4071	"Pray sit down on the sofa," said Holmes gently. "You had my
  4072	note?"
  4073	
  4074	"Yes, the lodge-keeper brought it up. You said that you wished to
  4075	see me here to avoid scandal."
  4076	
  4077	"I thought people would talk if I went to the Hall."
  4078	
  4079	"And why did you wish to see me?" He looked across at my
  4080	companion with despair in his weary eyes, as though his question
  4081	was already answered.
  4082	
  4083	"Yes," said Holmes, answering the look rather than the words. "It
  4084	is so. I know all about McCarthy."
  4085	
  4086	The old man sank his face in his hands. "God help me!" he cried.
  4087	"But I would not have let the young man come to harm. I give you
  4088	my word that I would have spoken out if it went against him at
  4089	the Assizes."
  4090	
  4091	"I am glad to hear you say so," said Holmes gravely.
  4092	
  4093	"I would have spoken now had it not been for my dear girl. It
  4094	would break her heart--it will break her heart when she hears
  4095	that I am arrested."
  4096	
  4097	"It may not come to that," said Holmes.
  4098	
  4099	"What?"
  4100	
  4101	"I am no official agent. I understand that it was your daughter
  4102	who required my presence here, and I am acting in her interests.
  4103	Young McCarthy must be got off, however."
  4104	
  4105	"I am a dying man," said old Turner. "I have had diabetes for
  4106	years. My doctor says it is a question whether I shall live a
  4107	month. Yet I would rather die under my own roof than in a gaol."
  4108	
  4109	Holmes rose and sat down at the table with his pen in his hand
  4110	and a bundle of paper before him. "Just tell us the truth," he
  4111	said. "I shall jot down the facts. You will sign it, and Watson
  4112	here can witness it. Then I could produce your confession at the
  4113	last extremity to save young McCarthy. I promise you that I shall
  4114	not use it unless it is absolutely needed."
  4115	
  4116	"It's as well," said the old man; "it's a question whether I
  4117	shall live to the Assizes, so it matters little to me, but I
  4118	should wish to spare Alice the shock. And now I will make the
  4119	thing clear to you; it has been a long time in the acting, but
  4120	will not take me long to tell.
  4121	
  4122	"You didn't know this dead man, McCarthy. He was a devil
  4123	incarnate. I tell you that. God keep you out of the clutches of
  4124	such a man as he. His grip has been upon me these twenty years,
  4125	and he has blasted my life. I'll tell you first how I came to be
  4126	in his power.
  4127	
  4128	"It was in the early '60's at the diggings. I was a young chap
  4129	then, hot-blooded and reckless, ready to turn my hand at
  4130	anything; I got among bad companions, took to drink, had no luck
  4131	with my claim, took to the bush, and in a word became what you
  4132	would call over here a highway robber. There were six of us, and
  4133	we had a wild, free life of it, sticking up a station from time
  4134	to time, or stopping the wagons on the road to the diggings.
  4135	Black Jack of Ballarat was the name I went under, and our party
  4136	is still remembered in the colony as the Ballarat Gang.
  4137	
  4138	"One day a gold convoy came down from Ballarat to Melbourne, and
  4139	we lay in wait for it and attacked it. There were six troopers
  4140	and six of us, so it was a close thing, but we emptied four of
  4141	their saddles at the first volley. Three of our boys were killed,
  4142	however, before we got the swag. I put my pistol to the head of
  4143	the wagon-driver, who was this very man McCarthy. I wish to the
  4144	Lord that I had shot him then, but I spared him, though I saw his
  4145	wicked little eyes fixed on my face, as though to remember every
  4146	feature. We got away with the gold, became wealthy men, and made
  4147	our way over to England without being suspected. There I parted
  4148	from my old pals and determined to settle down to a quiet and
  4149	respectable life. I bought this estate, which chanced to be in
  4150	the market, and I set myself to do a little good with my money,
  4151	to make up for the way in which I had earned it. I married, too,
  4152	and though my wife died young she left me my dear little Alice.
  4153	Even when she was just a baby her wee hand seemed to lead me down
  4154	the right path as nothing else had ever done. In a word, I turned
  4155	over a new leaf and did my best to make up for the past. All was
  4156	going well when McCarthy laid his grip upon me.
  4157	
  4158	"I had gone up to town about an investment, and I met him in
  4159	Regent Street with hardly a coat to his back or a boot to his
  4160	foot.
  4161	
  4162	"'Here we are, Jack,' says he, touching me on the arm; 'we'll be
  4163	as good as a family to you. There's two of us, me and my son, and
  4164	you can have the keeping of us. If you don't--it's a fine,
  4165	law-abiding country is England, and there's always a policeman
  4166	within hail.'
  4167	
  4168	"Well, down they came to the west country, there was no shaking
  4169	them off, and there they have lived rent free on my best land
  4170	ever since. There was no rest for me, no peace, no forgetfulness;
  4171	turn where I would, there was his cunning, grinning face at my
  4172	elbow. It grew worse as Alice grew up, for he soon saw I was more
  4173	afraid of her knowing my past than of the police. Whatever he
  4174	wanted he must have, and whatever it was I gave him without
  4175	question, land, money, houses, until at last he asked a thing
  4176	which I could not give. He asked for Alice.
  4177	
  4178	"His son, you see, had grown up, and so had my girl, and as I was
  4179	known to be in weak health, it seemed a fine stroke to him that
  4180	his lad should step into the whole property. But there I was
  4181	firm. I would not have his cursed stock mixed with mine; not that
  4182	I had any dislike to the lad, but his blood was in him, and that
  4183	was enough. I stood firm. McCarthy threatened. I braved him to do
  4184	his worst. We were to meet at the pool midway between our houses
  4185	to talk it over.
  4186	
  4187	"When I went down there I found him talking with his son, so I
  4188	smoked a cigar and waited behind a tree until he should be alone.
  4189	But as I listened to his talk all that was black and bitter in
  4190	me seemed to come uppermost. He was urging his son to marry my
  4191	daughter with as little regard for what she might think as if she
  4192	were a slut from off the streets. It drove me mad to think that I
  4193	and all that I held most dear should be in the power of such a
  4194	man as this. Could I not snap the bond? I was already a dying and
  4195	a desperate man. Though clear of mind and fairly strong of limb,
  4196	I knew that my own fate was sealed. But my memory and my girl!
  4197	Both could be saved if I could but silence that foul tongue. I
  4198	did it, Mr. Holmes. I would do it again. Deeply as I have sinned,
  4199	I have led a life of martyrdom to atone for it. But that my girl
  4200	should be entangled in the same meshes which held me was more
  4201	than I could suffer. I struck him down with no more compunction
  4202	than if he had been some foul and venomous beast. His cry brought
  4203	back his son; but I had gained the cover of the wood, though I
  4204	was forced to go back to fetch the cloak which I had dropped in
  4205	my flight. That is the true story, gentlemen, of all that
  4206	occurred."
  4207	
  4208	"Well, it is not for me to judge you," said Holmes as the old man
  4209	signed the statement which had been drawn out. "I pray that we
  4210	may never be exposed to such a temptation."
  4211	
  4212	"I pray not, sir. And what do you intend to do?"
  4213	
  4214	"In view of your health, nothing. You are yourself aware that you
  4215	will soon have to answer for your deed at a higher court than the
  4216	Assizes. I will keep your confession, and if McCarthy is
  4217	condemned I shall be forced to use it. If not, it shall never be
  4218	seen by mortal eye; and your secret, whether you be alive or
  4219	dead, shall be safe with us."
  4220	
  4221	"Farewell, then," said the old man solemnly. "Your own deathbeds,
  4222	when they come, will be the easier for the thought of the peace
  4223	which you have given to mine." Tottering and shaking in all his
  4224	giant frame, he stumbled slowly from the room.
  4225	
  4226	"God help us!" said Holmes after a long silence. "Why does fate
  4227	play such tricks with poor, helpless worms? I never hear of such
  4228	a case as this that I do not think of Baxter's words, and say,
  4229	'There, but for the grace of God, goes Sherlock Holmes.'"
  4230	
  4231	James McCarthy was acquitted at the Assizes on the strength of a
  4232	number of objections which had been drawn out by Holmes and
  4233	submitted to the defending counsel. Old Turner lived for seven
  4234	months after our interview, but he is now dead; and there is
  4235	every prospect that the son and daughter may come to live happily
  4236	together in ignorance of the black cloud which rests upon their
  4237	past.
  4238	
  4239	
  4240	
  4241	ADVENTURE V. THE FIVE ORANGE PIPS
  4242	
  4243	When I glance over my notes and records of the Sherlock Holmes
  4244	cases between the years '82 and '90, I am faced by so many which
  4245	present strange and interesting features that it is no easy
  4246	matter to know which to choose and which to leave. Some, however,
  4247	have already gained publicity through the papers, and others have
  4248	not offered a field for those peculiar qualities which my friend
  4249	possessed in so high a degree, and which it is the object of
  4250	these papers to illustrate. Some, too, have baffled his
  4251	analytical skill, and would be, as narratives, beginnings without
  4252	an ending, while others have been but partially cleared up, and
  4253	have their explanations founded rather upon conjecture and
  4254	surmise than on that absolute logical proof which was so dear to
  4255	him. There is, however, one of these last which was so remarkable
  4256	in its details and so startling in its results that I am tempted
  4257	to give some account of it in spite of the fact that there are
  4258	points in connection with it which never have been, and probably
  4259	never will be, entirely cleared up.
  4260	
  4261	The year '87 furnished us with a long series of cases of greater
  4262	or less interest, of which I retain the records. Among my
  4263	headings under this one twelve months I find an account of the
  4264	adventure of the Paradol Chamber, of the Amateur Mendicant
  4265	Society, who held a luxurious club in the lower vault of a
  4266	furniture warehouse, of the facts connected with the loss of the
  4267	British barque "Sophy Anderson", of the singular adventures of the
  4268	Grice Patersons in the island of Uffa, and finally of the
  4269	Camberwell poisoning case. In the latter, as may be remembered,
  4270	Sherlock Holmes was able, by winding up the dead man's watch, to
  4271	prove that it had been wound up two hours before, and that
  4272	therefore the deceased had gone to bed within that time--a
  4273	deduction which was of the greatest importance in clearing up the
  4274	case. All these I may sketch out at some future date, but none of
  4275	them present such singular features as the strange train of
  4276	circumstances which I have now taken up my pen to describe.
  4277	
  4278	It was in the latter days of September, and the equinoctial gales
  4279	had set in with exceptional violence. All day the wind had
  4280	screamed and the rain had beaten against the windows, so that
  4281	even here in the heart of great, hand-made London we were forced
  4282	to raise our minds for the instant from the routine of life and
  4283	to recognise the presence of those great elemental forces which
  4284	shriek at mankind through the bars of his civilisation, like
  4285	untamed beasts in a cage. As evening drew in, the storm grew
  4286	higher and louder, and the wind cried and sobbed like a child in
  4287	the chimney. Sherlock Holmes sat moodily at one side of the
  4288	fireplace cross-indexing his records of crime, while I at the
  4289	other was deep in one of Clark Russell's fine sea-stories until
  4290	the howl of the gale from without seemed to blend with the text,
  4291	and the splash of the rain to lengthen out into the long swash of
  4292	the sea waves. My wife was on a visit to her mother's, and for a
  4293	few days I was a dweller once more in my old quarters at Baker
  4294	Street.
  4295	
  4296	"Why," said I, glancing up at my companion, "that was surely the
  4297	bell. Who could come to-night? Some friend of yours, perhaps?"
  4298	
  4299	"Except yourself I have none," he answered. "I do not encourage
  4300	visitors."
  4301	
  4302	"A client, then?"
  4303	
  4304	"If so, it is a serious case. Nothing less would bring a man out
  4305	on such a day and at such an hour. But I take it that it is more
  4306	likely to be some crony of the landlady's."
  4307	
  4308	Sherlock Holmes was wrong in his conjecture, however, for there
  4309	came a step in the passage and a tapping at the door. He
  4310	stretched out his long arm to turn the lamp away from himself and
  4311	towards the vacant chair upon which a newcomer must sit.
  4312	
  4313	"Come in!" said he.
  4314	
  4315	The man who entered was young, some two-and-twenty at the
  4316	outside, well-groomed and trimly clad, with something of
  4317	refinement and delicacy in his bearing. The streaming umbrella
  4318	which he held in his hand, and his long shining waterproof told
  4319	of the fierce weather through which he had come. He looked about
  4320	him anxiously in the glare of the lamp, and I could see that his
  4321	face was pale and his eyes heavy, like those of a man who is
  4322	weighed down with some great anxiety.
  4323	
  4324	"I owe you an apology," he said, raising his golden pince-nez to
  4325	his eyes. "I trust that I am not intruding. I fear that I have
  4326	brought some traces of the storm and rain into your snug
  4327	chamber."
  4328	
  4329	"Give me your coat and umbrella," said Holmes. "They may rest
  4330	here on the hook and will be dry presently. You have come up from
  4331	the south-west, I see."
  4332	
  4333	"Yes, from Horsham."
  4334	
  4335	"That clay and chalk mixture which I see upon your toe caps is
  4336	quite distinctive."
  4337	
  4338	"I have come for advice."
  4339	
  4340	"That is easily got."
  4341	
  4342	"And help."
  4343	
  4344	"That is not always so easy."
  4345	
  4346	"I have heard of you, Mr. Holmes. I heard from Major Prendergast
  4347	how you saved him in the Tankerville Club scandal."
  4348	
  4349	"Ah, of course. He was wrongfully accused of cheating at cards."
  4350	
  4351	"He said that you could solve anything."
  4352	
  4353	"He said too much."
  4354	
  4355	"That you are never beaten."
  4356	
  4357	"I have been beaten four times--three times by men, and once by a
  4358	woman."
  4359	
  4360	"But what is that compared with the number of your successes?"
  4361	
  4362	"It is true that I have been generally successful."
  4363	
  4364	"Then you may be so with me."
  4365	
  4366	"I beg that you will draw your chair up to the fire and favour me
  4367	with some details as to your case."
  4368	
  4369	"It is no ordinary one."
  4370	
  4371	"None of those which come to me are. I am the last court of
  4372	appeal."
  4373	
  4374	"And yet I question, sir, whether, in all your experience, you
  4375	have ever listened to a more mysterious and inexplicable chain of
  4376	events than those which have happened in my own family."
  4377	
  4378	"You fill me with interest," said Holmes. "Pray give us the
  4379	essential facts from the commencement, and I can afterwards
  4380	question you as to those details which seem to me to be most
  4381	important."
  4382	
  4383	The young man pulled his chair up and pushed his wet feet out
  4384	towards the blaze.
  4385	
  4386	"My name," said he, "is John Openshaw, but my own affairs have,
  4387	as far as I can understand, little to do with this awful
  4388	business. It is a hereditary matter; so in order to give you an
  4389	idea of the facts, I must go back to the commencement of the
  4390	affair.
  4391	
  4392	"You must know that my grandfather had two sons--my uncle Elias
  4393	and my father Joseph. My father had a small factory at Coventry,
  4394	which he enlarged at the time of the invention of bicycling. He
  4395	was a patentee of the Openshaw unbreakable tire, and his business
  4396	met with such success that he was able to sell it and to retire
  4397	upon a handsome competence.
  4398	
  4399	"My uncle Elias emigrated to America when he was a young man and
  4400	became a planter in Florida, where he was reported to have done
  4401	very well. At the time of the war he fought in Jackson's army,
  4402	and afterwards under Hood, where he rose to be a colonel. When
  4403	Lee laid down his arms my uncle returned to his plantation, where
  4404	he remained for three or four years. About 1869 or 1870 he came
  4405	back to Europe and took a small estate in Sussex, near Horsham.
  4406	He had made a very considerable fortune in the States, and his
  4407	reason for leaving them was his aversion to the negroes, and his
  4408	dislike of the Republican policy in extending the franchise to
  4409	them. He was a singular man, fierce and quick-tempered, very
  4410	foul-mouthed when he was angry, and of a most retiring
  4411	disposition. During all the years that he lived at Horsham, I
  4412	doubt if ever he set foot in the town. He had a garden and two or
  4413	three fields round his house, and there he would take his
  4414	exercise, though very often for weeks on end he would never leave
  4415	his room. He drank a great deal of brandy and smoked very
  4416	heavily, but he would see no society and did not want any
  4417	friends, not even his own brother.
  4418	
  4419	"He didn't mind me; in fact, he took a fancy to me, for at the
  4420	time when he saw me first I was a youngster of twelve or so. This
  4421	would be in the year 1878, after he had been eight or nine years
  4422	in England. He begged my father to let me live with him and he
  4423	was very kind to me in his way. When he was sober he used to be
  4424	fond of playing backgammon and draughts with me, and he would
  4425	make me his representative both with the servants and with the
  4426	tradespeople, so that by the time that I was sixteen I was quite
  4427	master of the house. I kept all the keys and could go where I
  4428	liked and do what I liked, so long as I did not disturb him in
  4429	his privacy. There was one singular exception, however, for he
  4430	had a single room, a lumber-room up among the attics, which was
  4431	invariably locked, and which he would never permit either me or
  4432	anyone else to enter. With a boy's curiosity I have peeped
  4433	through the keyhole, but I was never able to see more than such a
  4434	collection of old trunks and bundles as would be expected in such
  4435	a room.
  4436	
  4437	"One day--it was in March, 1883--a letter with a foreign stamp
  4438	lay upon the table in front of the colonel's plate. It was not a
  4439	common thing for him to receive letters, for his bills were all
  4440	paid in ready money, and he had no friends of any sort. 'From
  4441	India!' said he as he took it up, 'Pondicherry postmark! What can
  4442	this be?' Opening it hurriedly, out there jumped five little
  4443	dried orange pips, which pattered down upon his plate. I began to
  4444	laugh at this, but the laugh was struck from my lips at the sight
  4445	of his face. His lip had fallen, his eyes were protruding, his
  4446	skin the colour of putty, and he glared at the envelope which he
  4447	still held in his trembling hand, 'K. K. K.!' he shrieked, and
  4448	then, 'My God, my God, my sins have overtaken me!'
  4449	
  4450	"'What is it, uncle?' I cried.
  4451	
  4452	"'Death,' said he, and rising from the table he retired to his
  4453	room, leaving me palpitating with horror. I took up the envelope
  4454	and saw scrawled in red ink upon the inner flap, just above the
  4455	gum, the letter K three times repeated. There was nothing else
  4456	save the five dried pips. What could be the reason of his
  4457	overpowering terror? I left the breakfast-table, and as I
  4458	ascended the stair I met him coming down with an old rusty key,
  4459	which must have belonged to the attic, in one hand, and a small
  4460	brass box, like a cashbox, in the other.
  4461	
  4462	"'They may do what they like, but I'll checkmate them still,'
  4463	said he with an oath. 'Tell Mary that I shall want a fire in my
  4464	room to-day, and send down to Fordham, the Horsham lawyer.'
  4465	
  4466	"I did as he ordered, and when the lawyer arrived I was asked to
  4467	step up to the room. The fire was burning brightly, and in the
  4468	grate there was a mass of black, fluffy ashes, as of burned
  4469	paper, while the brass box stood open and empty beside it. As I
  4470	glanced at the box I noticed, with a start, that upon the lid was
  4471	printed the treble K which I had read in the morning upon the
  4472	envelope.
  4473	
  4474	"'I wish you, John,' said my uncle, 'to witness my will. I leave
  4475	my estate, with all its advantages and all its disadvantages, to
  4476	my brother, your father, whence it will, no doubt, descend to
  4477	you. If you can enjoy it in peace, well and good! If you find you
  4478	cannot, take my advice, my boy, and leave it to your deadliest
  4479	enemy. I am sorry to give you such a two-edged thing, but I can't
  4480	say what turn things are going to take. Kindly sign the paper
  4481	where Mr. Fordham shows you.'
  4482	
  4483	"I signed the paper as directed, and the lawyer took it away with
  4484	him. The singular incident made, as you may think, the deepest
  4485	impression upon me, and I pondered over it and turned it every
  4486	way in my mind without being able to make anything of it. Yet I
  4487	could not shake off the vague feeling of dread which it left
  4488	behind, though the sensation grew less keen as the weeks passed
  4489	and nothing happened to disturb the usual routine of our lives. I
  4490	could see a change in my uncle, however. He drank more than ever,
  4491	and he was less inclined for any sort of society. Most of his
  4492	time he would spend in his room, with the door locked upon the
  4493	inside, but sometimes he would emerge in a sort of drunken frenzy
  4494	and would burst out of the house and tear about the garden with a
  4495	revolver in his hand, screaming out that he was afraid of no man,
  4496	and that he was not to be cooped up, like a sheep in a pen, by
  4497	man or devil. When these hot fits were over, however, he would
  4498	rush tumultuously in at the door and lock and bar it behind him,
  4499	like a man who can brazen it out no longer against the terror
  4500	which lies at the roots of his soul. At such times I have seen
  4501	his face, even on a cold day, glisten with moisture, as though it
  4502	were new raised from a basin.
  4503	
  4504	"Well, to come to an end of the matter, Mr. Holmes, and not to
  4505	abuse your patience, there came a night when he made one of those
  4506	drunken sallies from which he never came back. We found him, when
  4507	we went to search for him, face downward in a little
  4508	green-scummed pool, which lay at the foot of the garden. There
  4509	was no sign of any violence, and the water was but two feet deep,
  4510	so that the jury, having regard to his known eccentricity,
  4511	brought in a verdict of 'suicide.' But I, who knew how he winced
  4512	from the very thought of death, had much ado to persuade myself
  4513	that he had gone out of his way to meet it. The matter passed,
  4514	however, and my father entered into possession of the estate, and
  4515	of some 14,000 pounds, which lay to his credit at the bank."
  4516	
  4517	"One moment," Holmes interposed, "your statement is, I foresee,
  4518	one of the most remarkable to which I have ever listened. Let me
  4519	have the date of the reception by your uncle of the letter, and
  4520	the date of his supposed suicide."
  4521	
  4522	"The letter arrived on March 10, 1883. His death was seven weeks
  4523	later, upon the night of May 2nd."
  4524	
  4525	"Thank you. Pray proceed."
  4526	
  4527	"When my father took over the Horsham property, he, at my
  4528	request, made a careful examination of the attic, which had been
  4529	always locked up. We found the brass box there, although its
  4530	contents had been destroyed. On the inside of the cover was a
  4531	paper label, with the initials of K. K. K. repeated upon it, and
  4532	'Letters, memoranda, receipts, and a register' written beneath.
  4533	These, we presume, indicated the nature of the papers which had
  4534	been destroyed by Colonel Openshaw. For the rest, there was
  4535	nothing of much importance in the attic save a great many
  4536	scattered papers and note-books bearing upon my uncle's life in
  4537	America. Some of them were of the war time and showed that he had
  4538	done his duty well and had borne the repute of a brave soldier.
  4539	Others were of a date during the reconstruction of the Southern
  4540	states, and were mostly concerned with politics, for he had
  4541	evidently taken a strong part in opposing the carpet-bag
  4542	politicians who had been sent down from the North.
  4543	
  4544	"Well, it was the beginning of '84 when my father came to live at
  4545	Horsham, and all went as well as possible with us until the
  4546	January of '85. On the fourth day after the new year I heard my
  4547	father give a sharp cry of surprise as we sat together at the
  4548	breakfast-table. There he was, sitting with a newly opened
  4549	envelope in one hand and five dried orange pips in the
  4550	outstretched palm of the other one. He had always laughed at what
  4551	he called my cock-and-bull story about the colonel, but he looked
  4552	very scared and puzzled now that the same thing had come upon
  4553	himself.
  4554	
  4555	"'Why, what on earth does this mean, John?' he stammered.
  4556	
  4557	"My heart had turned to lead. 'It is K. K. K.,' said I.
  4558	
  4559	"He looked inside the envelope. 'So it is,' he cried. 'Here are
  4560	the very letters. But what is this written above them?'
  4561	
  4562	"'Put the papers on the sundial,' I read, peeping over his
  4563	shoulder.
  4564	
  4565	"'What papers? What sundial?' he asked.
  4566	
  4567	"'The sundial in the garden. There is no other,' said I; 'but the
  4568	papers must be those that are destroyed.'
  4569	
  4570	"'Pooh!' said he, gripping hard at his courage. 'We are in a
  4571	civilised land here, and we can't have tomfoolery of this kind.
  4572	Where does the thing come from?'
  4573	
  4574	"'From Dundee,' I answered, glancing at the postmark.
  4575	
  4576	"'Some preposterous practical joke,' said he. 'What have I to do
  4577	with sundials and papers? I shall take no notice of such
  4578	nonsense.'
  4579	
  4580	"'I should certainly speak to the police,' I said.
  4581	
  4582	"'And be laughed at for my pains. Nothing of the sort.'
  4583	
  4584	"'Then let me do so?'
  4585	
  4586	"'No, I forbid you. I won't have a fuss made about such
  4587	nonsense.'
  4588	
  4589	"It was in vain to argue with him, for he was a very obstinate
  4590	man. I went about, however, with a heart which was full of
  4591	forebodings.
  4592	
  4593	"On the third day after the coming of the letter my father went
  4594	from home to visit an old friend of his, Major Freebody, who is
  4595	in command of one of the forts upon Portsdown Hill. I was glad
  4596	that he should go, for it seemed to me that he was farther from
  4597	danger when he was away from home. In that, however, I was in
  4598	error. Upon the second day of his absence I received a telegram
  4599	from the major, imploring me to come at once. My father had
  4600	fallen over one of the deep chalk-pits which abound in the
  4601	neighbourhood, and was lying senseless, with a shattered skull. I
  4602	hurried to him, but he passed away without having ever recovered
  4603	his consciousness. He had, as it appears, been returning from
  4604	Fareham in the twilight, and as the country was unknown to him,
  4605	and the chalk-pit unfenced, the jury had no hesitation in
  4606	bringing in a verdict of 'death from accidental causes.'
  4607	Carefully as I examined every fact connected with his death, I
  4608	was unable to find anything which could suggest the idea of
  4609	murder. There were no signs of violence, no footmarks, no
  4610	robbery, no record of strangers having been seen upon the roads.
  4611	And yet I need not tell you that my mind was far from at ease,
  4612	and that I was well-nigh certain that some foul plot had been
  4613	woven round him.
  4614	
  4615	"In this sinister way I came into my inheritance. You will ask me
  4616	why I did not dispose of it? I answer, because I was well
  4617	convinced that our troubles were in some way dependent upon an
  4618	incident in my uncle's life, and that the danger would be as
  4619	pressing in one house as in another.
  4620	
  4621	"It was in January, '85, that my poor father met his end, and two
  4622	years and eight months have elapsed since then. During that time
  4623	I have lived happily at Horsham, and I had begun to hope that
  4624	this curse had passed away from the family, and that it had ended
  4625	with the last generation. I had begun to take comfort too soon,
  4626	however; yesterday morning the blow fell in the very shape in
  4627	which it had come upon my father."
  4628	
  4629	The young man took from his waistcoat a crumpled envelope, and
