  4630	turning to the table he shook out upon it five little dried
  4631	orange pips.
  4632	
  4633	"This is the envelope," he continued. "The postmark is
  4634	London--eastern division. Within are the very words which were
  4635	upon my father's last message: 'K. K. K.'; and then 'Put the
  4636	papers on the sundial.'"
  4637	
  4638	"What have you done?" asked Holmes.
  4639	
  4640	"Nothing."
  4641	
  4642	"Nothing?"
  4643	
  4644	"To tell the truth"--he sank his face into his thin, white
  4645	hands--"I have felt helpless. I have felt like one of those poor
  4646	rabbits when the snake is writhing towards it. I seem to be in
  4647	the grasp of some resistless, inexorable evil, which no foresight
  4648	and no precautions can guard against."
  4649	
  4650	"Tut! tut!" cried Sherlock Holmes. "You must act, man, or you are
  4651	lost. Nothing but energy can save you. This is no time for
  4652	despair."
  4653	
  4654	"I have seen the police."
  4655	
  4656	"Ah!"
  4657	
  4658	"But they listened to my story with a smile. I am convinced that
  4659	the inspector has formed the opinion that the letters are all
  4660	practical jokes, and that the deaths of my relations were really
  4661	accidents, as the jury stated, and were not to be connected with
  4662	the warnings."
  4663	
  4664	Holmes shook his clenched hands in the air. "Incredible
  4665	imbecility!" he cried.
  4666	
  4667	"They have, however, allowed me a policeman, who may remain in
  4668	the house with me."
  4669	
  4670	"Has he come with you to-night?"
  4671	
  4672	"No. His orders were to stay in the house."
  4673	
  4674	Again Holmes raved in the air.
  4675	
  4676	"Why did you come to me," he cried, "and, above all, why did you
  4677	not come at once?"
  4678	
  4679	"I did not know. It was only to-day that I spoke to Major
  4680	Prendergast about my troubles and was advised by him to come to
  4681	you."
  4682	
  4683	"It is really two days since you had the letter. We should have
  4684	acted before this. You have no further evidence, I suppose, than
  4685	that which you have placed before us--no suggestive detail which
  4686	might help us?"
  4687	
  4688	"There is one thing," said John Openshaw. He rummaged in his coat
  4689	pocket, and, drawing out a piece of discoloured, blue-tinted
  4690	paper, he laid it out upon the table. "I have some remembrance,"
  4691	said he, "that on the day when my uncle burned the papers I
  4692	observed that the small, unburned margins which lay amid the
  4693	ashes were of this particular colour. I found this single sheet
  4694	upon the floor of his room, and I am inclined to think that it
  4695	may be one of the papers which has, perhaps, fluttered out from
  4696	among the others, and in that way has escaped destruction. Beyond
  4697	the mention of pips, I do not see that it helps us much. I think
  4698	myself that it is a page from some private diary. The writing is
  4699	undoubtedly my uncle's."
  4700	
  4701	Holmes moved the lamp, and we both bent over the sheet of paper,
  4702	which showed by its ragged edge that it had indeed been torn from
  4703	a book. It was headed, "March, 1869," and beneath were the
  4704	following enigmatical notices:
  4705	
  4706	"4th. Hudson came. Same old platform.
  4707	
  4708	"7th. Set the pips on McCauley, Paramore, and
  4709	      John Swain, of St. Augustine.
  4710	
  4711	"9th. McCauley cleared.
  4712	
  4713	"10th. John Swain cleared.
  4714	
  4715	"12th. Visited Paramore. All well."
  4716	
  4717	"Thank you!" said Holmes, folding up the paper and returning it
  4718	to our visitor. "And now you must on no account lose another
  4719	instant. We cannot spare time even to discuss what you have told
  4720	me. You must get home instantly and act."
  4721	
  4722	"What shall I do?"
  4723	
  4724	"There is but one thing to do. It must be done at once. You must
  4725	put this piece of paper which you have shown us into the brass
  4726	box which you have described. You must also put in a note to say
  4727	that all the other papers were burned by your uncle, and that
  4728	this is the only one which remains. You must assert that in such
  4729	words as will carry conviction with them. Having done this, you
  4730	must at once put the box out upon the sundial, as directed. Do
  4731	you understand?"
  4732	
  4733	"Entirely."
  4734	
  4735	"Do not think of revenge, or anything of the sort, at present. I
  4736	think that we may gain that by means of the law; but we have our
  4737	web to weave, while theirs is already woven. The first
  4738	consideration is to remove the pressing danger which threatens
  4739	you. The second is to clear up the mystery and to punish the
  4740	guilty parties."
  4741	
  4742	"I thank you," said the young man, rising and pulling on his
  4743	overcoat. "You have given me fresh life and hope. I shall
  4744	certainly do as you advise."
  4745	
  4746	"Do not lose an instant. And, above all, take care of yourself in
  4747	the meanwhile, for I do not think that there can be a doubt that
  4748	you are threatened by a very real and imminent danger. How do you
  4749	go back?"
  4750	
  4751	"By train from Waterloo."
  4752	
  4753	"It is not yet nine. The streets will be crowded, so I trust that
  4754	you may be in safety. And yet you cannot guard yourself too
  4755	closely."
  4756	
  4757	"I am armed."
  4758	
  4759	"That is well. To-morrow I shall set to work upon your case."
  4760	
  4761	"I shall see you at Horsham, then?"
  4762	
  4763	"No, your secret lies in London. It is there that I shall seek
  4764	it."
  4765	
  4766	"Then I shall call upon you in a day, or in two days, with news
  4767	as to the box and the papers. I shall take your advice in every
  4768	particular." He shook hands with us and took his leave. Outside
  4769	the wind still screamed and the rain splashed and pattered
  4770	against the windows. This strange, wild story seemed to have come
  4771	to us from amid the mad elements--blown in upon us like a sheet
  4772	of sea-weed in a gale--and now to have been reabsorbed by them
  4773	once more.
  4774	
  4775	Sherlock Holmes sat for some time in silence, with his head sunk
  4776	forward and his eyes bent upon the red glow of the fire. Then he
  4777	lit his pipe, and leaning back in his chair he watched the blue
  4778	smoke-rings as they chased each other up to the ceiling.
  4779	
  4780	"I think, Watson," he remarked at last, "that of all our cases we
  4781	have had none more fantastic than this."
  4782	
  4783	"Save, perhaps, the Sign of Four."
  4784	
  4785	"Well, yes. Save, perhaps, that. And yet this John Openshaw seems
  4786	to me to be walking amid even greater perils than did the
  4787	Sholtos."
  4788	
  4789	"But have you," I asked, "formed any definite conception as to
  4790	what these perils are?"
  4791	
  4792	"There can be no question as to their nature," he answered.
  4793	
  4794	"Then what are they? Who is this K. K. K., and why does he pursue
  4795	this unhappy family?"
  4796	
  4797	Sherlock Holmes closed his eyes and placed his elbows upon the
  4798	arms of his chair, with his finger-tips together. "The ideal
  4799	reasoner," he remarked, "would, when he had once been shown a
  4800	single fact in all its bearings, deduce from it not only all the
  4801	chain of events which led up to it but also all the results which
  4802	would follow from it. As Cuvier could correctly describe a whole
  4803	animal by the contemplation of a single bone, so the observer who
  4804	has thoroughly understood one link in a series of incidents
  4805	should be able to accurately state all the other ones, both
  4806	before and after. We have not yet grasped the results which the
  4807	reason alone can attain to. Problems may be solved in the study
  4808	which have baffled all those who have sought a solution by the
  4809	aid of their senses. To carry the art, however, to its highest
  4810	pitch, it is necessary that the reasoner should be able to
  4811	utilise all the facts which have come to his knowledge; and this
  4812	in itself implies, as you will readily see, a possession of all
  4813	knowledge, which, even in these days of free education and
  4814	encyclopaedias, is a somewhat rare accomplishment. It is not so
  4815	impossible, however, that a man should possess all knowledge
  4816	which is likely to be useful to him in his work, and this I have
  4817	endeavoured in my case to do. If I remember rightly, you on one
  4818	occasion, in the early days of our friendship, defined my limits
  4819	in a very precise fashion."
  4820	
  4821	"Yes," I answered, laughing. "It was a singular document.
  4822	Philosophy, astronomy, and politics were marked at zero, I
  4823	remember. Botany variable, geology profound as regards the
  4824	mud-stains from any region within fifty miles of town, chemistry
  4825	eccentric, anatomy unsystematic, sensational literature and crime
  4826	records unique, violin-player, boxer, swordsman, lawyer, and
  4827	self-poisoner by cocaine and tobacco. Those, I think, were the
  4828	main points of my analysis."
  4829	
  4830	Holmes grinned at the last item. "Well," he said, "I say now, as
  4831	I said then, that a man should keep his little brain-attic
  4832	stocked with all the furniture that he is likely to use, and the
  4833	rest he can put away in the lumber-room of his library, where he
  4834	can get it if he wants it. Now, for such a case as the one which
  4835	has been submitted to us to-night, we need certainly to muster
  4836	all our resources. Kindly hand me down the letter K of the
  4837	'American Encyclopaedia' which stands upon the shelf beside you.
  4838	Thank you. Now let us consider the situation and see what may be
  4839	deduced from it. In the first place, we may start with a strong
  4840	presumption that Colonel Openshaw had some very strong reason for
  4841	leaving America. Men at his time of life do not change all their
  4842	habits and exchange willingly the charming climate of Florida for
  4843	the lonely life of an English provincial town. His extreme love
  4844	of solitude in England suggests the idea that he was in fear of
  4845	someone or something, so we may assume as a working hypothesis
  4846	that it was fear of someone or something which drove him from
  4847	America. As to what it was he feared, we can only deduce that by
  4848	considering the formidable letters which were received by himself
  4849	and his successors. Did you remark the postmarks of those
  4850	letters?"
  4851	
  4852	"The first was from Pondicherry, the second from Dundee, and the
  4853	third from London."
  4854	
  4855	"From East London. What do you deduce from that?"
  4856	
  4857	"They are all seaports. That the writer was on board of a ship."
  4858	
  4859	"Excellent. We have already a clue. There can be no doubt that
  4860	the probability--the strong probability--is that the writer was
  4861	on board of a ship. And now let us consider another point. In the
  4862	case of Pondicherry, seven weeks elapsed between the threat and
  4863	its fulfilment, in Dundee it was only some three or four days.
  4864	Does that suggest anything?"
  4865	
  4866	"A greater distance to travel."
  4867	
  4868	"But the letter had also a greater distance to come."
  4869	
  4870	"Then I do not see the point."
  4871	
  4872	"There is at least a presumption that the vessel in which the man
  4873	or men are is a sailing-ship. It looks as if they always send
  4874	their singular warning or token before them when starting upon
  4875	their mission. You see how quickly the deed followed the sign
  4876	when it came from Dundee. If they had come from Pondicherry in a
  4877	steamer they would have arrived almost as soon as their letter.
  4878	But, as a matter of fact, seven weeks elapsed. I think that those
  4879	seven weeks represented the difference between the mail-boat which
  4880	brought the letter and the sailing vessel which brought the
  4881	writer."
  4882	
  4883	"It is possible."
  4884	
  4885	"More than that. It is probable. And now you see the deadly
  4886	urgency of this new case, and why I urged young Openshaw to
  4887	caution. The blow has always fallen at the end of the time which
  4888	it would take the senders to travel the distance. But this one
  4889	comes from London, and therefore we cannot count upon delay."
  4890	
  4891	"Good God!" I cried. "What can it mean, this relentless
  4892	persecution?"
  4893	
  4894	"The papers which Openshaw carried are obviously of vital
  4895	importance to the person or persons in the sailing-ship. I think
  4896	that it is quite clear that there must be more than one of them.
  4897	A single man could not have carried out two deaths in such a way
  4898	as to deceive a coroner's jury. There must have been several in
  4899	it, and they must have been men of resource and determination.
  4900	Their papers they mean to have, be the holder of them who it may.
  4901	In this way you see K. K. K. ceases to be the initials of an
  4902	individual and becomes the badge of a society."
  4903	
  4904	"But of what society?"
  4905	
  4906	"Have you never--" said Sherlock Holmes, bending forward and
  4907	sinking his voice--"have you never heard of the Ku Klux Klan?"
  4908	
  4909	"I never have."
  4910	
  4911	Holmes turned over the leaves of the book upon his knee. "Here it
  4912	is," said he presently:
  4913	
  4914	"'Ku Klux Klan. A name derived from the fanciful resemblance to
  4915	the sound produced by cocking a rifle. This terrible secret
  4916	society was formed by some ex-Confederate soldiers in the
  4917	Southern states after the Civil War, and it rapidly formed local
  4918	branches in different parts of the country, notably in Tennessee,
  4919	Louisiana, the Carolinas, Georgia, and Florida. Its power was
  4920	used for political purposes, principally for the terrorising of
  4921	the negro voters and the murdering and driving from the country
  4922	of those who were opposed to its views. Its outrages were usually
  4923	preceded by a warning sent to the marked man in some fantastic
  4924	but generally recognised shape--a sprig of oak-leaves in some
  4925	parts, melon seeds or orange pips in others. On receiving this
  4926	the victim might either openly abjure his former ways, or might
  4927	fly from the country. If he braved the matter out, death would
  4928	unfailingly come upon him, and usually in some strange and
  4929	unforeseen manner. So perfect was the organisation of the
  4930	society, and so systematic its methods, that there is hardly a
  4931	case upon record where any man succeeded in braving it with
  4932	impunity, or in which any of its outrages were traced home to the
  4933	perpetrators. For some years the organisation flourished in spite
  4934	of the efforts of the United States government and of the better
  4935	classes of the community in the South. Eventually, in the year
  4936	1869, the movement rather suddenly collapsed, although there have
  4937	been sporadic outbreaks of the same sort since that date.'
  4938	
  4939	"You will observe," said Holmes, laying down the volume, "that
  4940	the sudden breaking up of the society was coincident with the
  4941	disappearance of Openshaw from America with their papers. It may
  4942	well have been cause and effect. It is no wonder that he and his
  4943	family have some of the more implacable spirits upon their track.
  4944	You can understand that this register and diary may implicate
  4945	some of the first men in the South, and that there may be many
  4946	who will not sleep easy at night until it is recovered."
  4947	
  4948	"Then the page we have seen--"
  4949	
  4950	"Is such as we might expect. It ran, if I remember right, 'sent
  4951	the pips to A, B, and C'--that is, sent the society's warning to
  4952	them. Then there are successive entries that A and B cleared, or
  4953	left the country, and finally that C was visited, with, I fear, a
  4954	sinister result for C. Well, I think, Doctor, that we may let
  4955	some light into this dark place, and I believe that the only
  4956	chance young Openshaw has in the meantime is to do what I have
  4957	told him. There is nothing more to be said or to be done
  4958	to-night, so hand me over my violin and let us try to forget for
  4959	half an hour the miserable weather and the still more miserable
  4960	ways of our fellow-men."
  4961	
  4962	
  4963	It had cleared in the morning, and the sun was shining with a
  4964	subdued brightness through the dim veil which hangs over the
  4965	great city. Sherlock Holmes was already at breakfast when I came
  4966	down.
  4967	
  4968	"You will excuse me for not waiting for you," said he; "I have, I
  4969	foresee, a very busy day before me in looking into this case of
  4970	young Openshaw's."
  4971	
  4972	"What steps will you take?" I asked.
  4973	
  4974	"It will very much depend upon the results of my first inquiries.
  4975	I may have to go down to Horsham, after all."
  4976	
  4977	"You will not go there first?"
  4978	
  4979	"No, I shall commence with the City. Just ring the bell and the
  4980	maid will bring up your coffee."
  4981	
  4982	As I waited, I lifted the unopened newspaper from the table and
  4983	glanced my eye over it. It rested upon a heading which sent a
  4984	chill to my heart.
  4985	
  4986	"Holmes," I cried, "you are too late."
  4987	
  4988	"Ah!" said he, laying down his cup, "I feared as much. How was it
  4989	done?" He spoke calmly, but I could see that he was deeply moved.
  4990	
  4991	"My eye caught the name of Openshaw, and the heading 'Tragedy
  4992	Near Waterloo Bridge.' Here is the account:
  4993	
  4994	"Between nine and ten last night Police-Constable Cook, of the H
  4995	Division, on duty near Waterloo Bridge, heard a cry for help and
  4996	a splash in the water. The night, however, was extremely dark and
  4997	stormy, so that, in spite of the help of several passers-by, it
  4998	was quite impossible to effect a rescue. The alarm, however, was
  4999	given, and, by the aid of the water-police, the body was
  5000	eventually recovered. It proved to be that of a young gentleman
  5001	whose name, as it appears from an envelope which was found in his
  5002	pocket, was John Openshaw, and whose residence is near Horsham.
  5003	It is conjectured that he may have been hurrying down to catch
  5004	the last train from Waterloo Station, and that in his haste and
  5005	the extreme darkness he missed his path and walked over the edge
  5006	of one of the small landing-places for river steamboats. The body
  5007	exhibited no traces of violence, and there can be no doubt that
  5008	the deceased had been the victim of an unfortunate accident,
  5009	which should have the effect of calling the attention of the
  5010	authorities to the condition of the riverside landing-stages."
  5011	
  5012	We sat in silence for some minutes, Holmes more depressed and
  5013	shaken than I had ever seen him.
  5014	
  5015	"That hurts my pride, Watson," he said at last. "It is a petty
  5016	feeling, no doubt, but it hurts my pride. It becomes a personal
  5017	matter with me now, and, if God sends me health, I shall set my
  5018	hand upon this gang. That he should come to me for help, and that
  5019	I should send him away to his death--!" He sprang from his chair
  5020	and paced about the room in uncontrollable agitation, with a
  5021	flush upon his sallow cheeks and a nervous clasping and
  5022	unclasping of his long thin hands.
  5023	
  5024	"They must be cunning devils," he exclaimed at last. "How could
  5025	they have decoyed him down there? The Embankment is not on the
  5026	direct line to the station. The bridge, no doubt, was too
  5027	crowded, even on such a night, for their purpose. Well, Watson,
  5028	we shall see who will win in the long run. I am going out now!"
  5029	
  5030	"To the police?"
  5031	
  5032	"No; I shall be my own police. When I have spun the web they may
  5033	take the flies, but not before."
  5034	
  5035	All day I was engaged in my professional work, and it was late in
  5036	the evening before I returned to Baker Street. Sherlock Holmes
  5037	had not come back yet. It was nearly ten o'clock before he
  5038	entered, looking pale and worn. He walked up to the sideboard,
  5039	and tearing a piece from the loaf he devoured it voraciously,
  5040	washing it down with a long draught of water.
  5041	
  5042	"You are hungry," I remarked.
  5043	
  5044	"Starving. It had escaped my memory. I have had nothing since
  5045	breakfast."
  5046	
  5047	"Nothing?"
  5048	
  5049	"Not a bite. I had no time to think of it."
  5050	
  5051	"And how have you succeeded?"
  5052	
  5053	"Well."
  5054	
  5055	"You have a clue?"
  5056	
  5057	"I have them in the hollow of my hand. Young Openshaw shall not
  5058	long remain unavenged. Why, Watson, let us put their own devilish
  5059	trade-mark upon them. It is well thought of!"
  5060	
  5061	"What do you mean?"
  5062	
  5063	He took an orange from the cupboard, and tearing it to pieces he
  5064	squeezed out the pips upon the table. Of these he took five and
  5065	thrust them into an envelope. On the inside of the flap he wrote
  5066	"S. H. for J. O." Then he sealed it and addressed it to "Captain
  5067	James Calhoun, Barque 'Lone Star,' Savannah, Georgia."
  5068	
  5069	"That will await him when he enters port," said he, chuckling.
  5070	"It may give him a sleepless night. He will find it as sure a
  5071	precursor of his fate as Openshaw did before him."
  5072	
  5073	"And who is this Captain Calhoun?"
  5074	
  5075	"The leader of the gang. I shall have the others, but he first."
  5076	
  5077	"How did you trace it, then?"
  5078	
  5079	He took a large sheet of paper from his pocket, all covered with
  5080	dates and names.
  5081	
  5082	"I have spent the whole day," said he, "over Lloyd's registers
  5083	and files of the old papers, following the future career of every
  5084	vessel which touched at Pondicherry in January and February in
  5085	'83. There were thirty-six ships of fair tonnage which were
  5086	reported there during those months. Of these, one, the 'Lone Star,'
  5087	instantly attracted my attention, since, although it was reported
  5088	as having cleared from London, the name is that which is given to
  5089	one of the states of the Union."
  5090	
  5091	"Texas, I think."
  5092	
  5093	"I was not and am not sure which; but I knew that the ship must
  5094	have an American origin."
  5095	
  5096	"What then?"
  5097	
  5098	"I searched the Dundee records, and when I found that the barque
  5099	'Lone Star' was there in January, '85, my suspicion became a
  5100	certainty. I then inquired as to the vessels which lay at present
  5101	in the port of London."
  5102	
  5103	"Yes?"
  5104	
  5105	"The 'Lone Star' had arrived here last week. I went down to the
  5106	Albert Dock and found that she had been taken down the river by
  5107	the early tide this morning, homeward bound to Savannah. I wired
  5108	to Gravesend and learned that she had passed some time ago, and
  5109	as the wind is easterly I have no doubt that she is now past the
  5110	Goodwins and not very far from the Isle of Wight."
  5111	
  5112	"What will you do, then?"
  5113	
  5114	"Oh, I have my hand upon him. He and the two mates, are as I
  5115	learn, the only native-born Americans in the ship. The others are
  5116	Finns and Germans. I know, also, that they were all three away
  5117	from the ship last night. I had it from the stevedore who has
  5118	been loading their cargo. By the time that their sailing-ship
  5119	reaches Savannah the mail-boat will have carried this letter, and
  5120	the cable will have informed the police of Savannah that these
  5121	three gentlemen are badly wanted here upon a charge of murder."
  5122	
  5123	There is ever a flaw, however, in the best laid of human plans,
  5124	and the murderers of John Openshaw were never to receive the
  5125	orange pips which would show them that another, as cunning and as
  5126	resolute as themselves, was upon their track. Very long and very
  5127	severe were the equinoctial gales that year. We waited long for
  5128	news of the "Lone Star" of Savannah, but none ever reached us. We
  5129	did at last hear that somewhere far out in the Atlantic a
  5130	shattered stern-post of a boat was seen swinging in the trough
  5131	of a wave, with the letters "L. S." carved upon it, and that is
  5132	all which we shall ever know of the fate of the "Lone Star."
  5133	
  5134	
  5135	
  5136	ADVENTURE VI. THE MAN WITH THE TWISTED LIP
  5137	
  5138	Isa Whitney, brother of the late Elias Whitney, D.D., Principal
  5139	of the Theological College of St. George's, was much addicted to
  5140	opium. The habit grew upon him, as I understand, from some
  5141	foolish freak when he was at college; for having read De
  5142	Quincey's description of his dreams and sensations, he had
  5143	drenched his tobacco with laudanum in an attempt to produce the
  5144	same effects. He found, as so many more have done, that the
  5145	practice is easier to attain than to get rid of, and for many
  5146	years he continued to be a slave to the drug, an object of
  5147	mingled horror and pity to his friends and relatives. I can see
  5148	him now, with yellow, pasty face, drooping lids, and pin-point
  5149	pupils, all huddled in a chair, the wreck and ruin of a noble
  5150	man.
  5151	
  5152	One night--it was in June, '89--there came a ring to my bell,
  5153	about the hour when a man gives his first yawn and glances at the
  5154	clock. I sat up in my chair, and my wife laid her needle-work
  5155	down in her lap and made a little face of disappointment.
  5156	
  5157	"A patient!" said she. "You'll have to go out."
  5158	
  5159	I groaned, for I was newly come back from a weary day.
  5160	
  5161	We heard the door open, a few hurried words, and then quick steps
  5162	upon the linoleum. Our own door flew open, and a lady, clad in
  5163	some dark-coloured stuff, with a black veil, entered the room.
  5164	
  5165	"You will excuse my calling so late," she began, and then,
  5166	suddenly losing her self-control, she ran forward, threw her arms
  5167	about my wife's neck, and sobbed upon her shoulder. "Oh, I'm in
  5168	such trouble!" she cried; "I do so want a little help."
  5169	
  5170	"Why," said my wife, pulling up her veil, "it is Kate Whitney.
  5171	How you startled me, Kate! I had not an idea who you were when
  5172	you came in."
  5173	
  5174	"I didn't know what to do, so I came straight to you." That was
  5175	always the way. Folk who were in grief came to my wife like birds
  5176	to a light-house.
  5177	
  5178	"It was very sweet of you to come. Now, you must have some wine
  5179	and water, and sit here comfortably and tell us all about it. Or
  5180	should you rather that I sent James off to bed?"
  5181	
  5182	"Oh, no, no! I want the doctor's advice and help, too. It's about
  5183	Isa. He has not been home for two days. I am so frightened about
  5184	him!"
  5185	
  5186	It was not the first time that she had spoken to us of her
  5187	husband's trouble, to me as a doctor, to my wife as an old friend
  5188	and school companion. We soothed and comforted her by such words
  5189	as we could find. Did she know where her husband was? Was it
  5190	possible that we could bring him back to her?
  5191	
  5192	It seems that it was. She had the surest information that of late
  5193	he had, when the fit was on him, made use of an opium den in the
  5194	farthest east of the City. Hitherto his orgies had always been
  5195	confined to one day, and he had come back, twitching and
  5196	shattered, in the evening. But now the spell had been upon him
  5197	eight-and-forty hours, and he lay there, doubtless among the
  5198	dregs of the docks, breathing in the poison or sleeping off the
  5199	effects. There he was to be found, she was sure of it, at the Bar
  5200	of Gold, in Upper Swandam Lane. But what was she to do? How could
  5201	she, a young and timid woman, make her way into such a place and
  5202	pluck her husband out from among the ruffians who surrounded him?
  5203	
  5204	There was the case, and of course there was but one way out of
  5205	it. Might I not escort her to this place? And then, as a second
  5206	thought, why should she come at all? I was Isa Whitney's medical
  5207	adviser, and as such I had influence over him. I could manage it
  5208	better if I were alone. I promised her on my word that I would
  5209	send him home in a cab within two hours if he were indeed at the
  5210	address which she had given me. And so in ten minutes I had left
  5211	my armchair and cheery sitting-room behind me, and was speeding
  5212	eastward in a hansom on a strange errand, as it seemed to me at
  5213	the time, though the future only could show how strange it was to
  5214	be.
  5215	
  5216	But there was no great difficulty in the first stage of my
  5217	adventure. Upper Swandam Lane is a vile alley lurking behind the
  5218	high wharves which line the north side of the river to the east
  5219	of London Bridge. Between a slop-shop and a gin-shop, approached
  5220	by a steep flight of steps leading down to a black gap like the
  5221	mouth of a cave, I found the den of which I was in search.
  5222	Ordering my cab to wait, I passed down the steps, worn hollow in
  5223	the centre by the ceaseless tread of drunken feet; and by the
  5224	light of a flickering oil-lamp above the door I found the latch
  5225	and made my way into a long, low room, thick and heavy with the
  5226	brown opium smoke, and terraced with wooden berths, like the
  5227	forecastle of an emigrant ship.
  5228	
  5229	Through the gloom one could dimly catch a glimpse of bodies lying
  5230	in strange fantastic poses, bowed shoulders, bent knees, heads
  5231	thrown back, and chins pointing upward, with here and there a
  5232	dark, lack-lustre eye turned upon the newcomer. Out of the black
  5233	shadows there glimmered little red circles of light, now bright,
  5234	now faint, as the burning poison waxed or waned in the bowls of
  5235	the metal pipes. The most lay silent, but some muttered to
  5236	themselves, and others talked together in a strange, low,
  5237	monotonous voice, their conversation coming in gushes, and then
  5238	suddenly tailing off into silence, each mumbling out his own
  5239	thoughts and paying little heed to the words of his neighbour. At
  5240	the farther end was a small brazier of burning charcoal, beside
  5241	which on a three-legged wooden stool there sat a tall, thin old
  5242	man, with his jaw resting upon his two fists, and his elbows upon
  5243	his knees, staring into the fire.
  5244	
  5245	As I entered, a sallow Malay attendant had hurried up with a pipe
  5246	for me and a supply of the drug, beckoning me to an empty berth.
  5247	
  5248	"Thank you. I have not come to stay," said I. "There is a friend
  5249	of mine here, Mr. Isa Whitney, and I wish to speak with him."
  5250	
  5251	There was a movement and an exclamation from my right, and
  5252	peering through the gloom, I saw Whitney, pale, haggard, and
  5253	unkempt, staring out at me.
  5254	
  5255	"My God! It's Watson," said he. He was in a pitiable state of
  5256	reaction, with every nerve in a twitter. "I say, Watson, what
  5257	o'clock is it?"
  5258	
  5259	"Nearly eleven."
  5260	
  5261	"Of what day?"
  5262	
  5263	"Of Friday, June 19th."
  5264	
  5265	"Good heavens! I thought it was Wednesday. It is Wednesday. What
  5266	d'you want to frighten a chap for?" He sank his face onto his
  5267	arms and began to sob in a high treble key.
  5268	
  5269	"I tell you that it is Friday, man. Your wife has been waiting
  5270	this two days for you. You should be ashamed of yourself!"
  5271	
  5272	"So I am. But you've got mixed, Watson, for I have only been here
  5273	a few hours, three pipes, four pipes--I forget how many. But I'll
  5274	go home with you. I wouldn't frighten Kate--poor little Kate.
  5275	Give me your hand! Have you a cab?"
  5276	
  5277	"Yes, I have one waiting."
  5278	
  5279	"Then I shall go in it. But I must owe something. Find what I
  5280	owe, Watson. I am all off colour. I can do nothing for myself."
  5281	
  5282	I walked down the narrow passage between the double row of
  5283	sleepers, holding my breath to keep out the vile, stupefying
  5284	fumes of the drug, and looking about for the manager. As I passed
  5285	the tall man who sat by the brazier I felt a sudden pluck at my
  5286	skirt, and a low voice whispered, "Walk past me, and then look
  5287	back at me." The words fell quite distinctly upon my ear. I
  5288	glanced down. They could only have come from the old man at my
  5289	side, and yet he sat now as absorbed as ever, very thin, very
  5290	wrinkled, bent with age, an opium pipe dangling down from between
  5291	his knees, as though it had dropped in sheer lassitude from his
  5292	fingers. I took two steps forward and looked back. It took all my
  5293	self-control to prevent me from breaking out into a cry of
  5294	astonishment. He had turned his back so that none could see him
  5295	but I. His form had filled out, his wrinkles were gone, the dull
  5296	eyes had regained their fire, and there, sitting by the fire and
  5297	grinning at my surprise, was none other than Sherlock Holmes. He
  5298	made a slight motion to me to approach him, and instantly, as he
  5299	turned his face half round to the company once more, subsided
  5300	into a doddering, loose-lipped senility.
  5301	
  5302	"Holmes!" I whispered, "what on earth are you doing in this den?"
  5303	
  5304	"As low as you can," he answered; "I have excellent ears. If you
  5305	would have the great kindness to get rid of that sottish friend
  5306	of yours I should be exceedingly glad to have a little talk with
  5307	you."
  5308	
  5309	"I have a cab outside."
  5310	
  5311	"Then pray send him home in it. You may safely trust him, for he
  5312	appears to be too limp to get into any mischief. I should
  5313	recommend you also to send a note by the cabman to your wife to
  5314	say that you have thrown in your lot with me. If you will wait
  5315	outside, I shall be with you in five minutes."
  5316	
  5317	It was difficult to refuse any of Sherlock Holmes' requests, for
  5318	they were always so exceedingly definite, and put forward with
  5319	such a quiet air of mastery. I felt, however, that when Whitney
  5320	was once confined in the cab my mission was practically
  5321	accomplished; and for the rest, I could not wish anything better
  5322	than to be associated with my friend in one of those singular
  5323	adventures which were the normal condition of his existence. In a
  5324	few minutes I had written my note, paid Whitney's bill, led him
  5325	out to the cab, and seen him driven through the darkness. In a
  5326	very short time a decrepit figure had emerged from the opium den,
  5327	and I was walking down the street with Sherlock Holmes. For two
  5328	streets he shuffled along with a bent back and an uncertain foot.
  5329	Then, glancing quickly round, he straightened himself out and
  5330	burst into a hearty fit of laughter.
  5331	
  5332	"I suppose, Watson," said he, "that you imagine that I have added
  5333	opium-smoking to cocaine injections, and all the other little
  5334	weaknesses on which you have favoured me with your medical
  5335	views."
  5336	
  5337	"I was certainly surprised to find you there."
  5338	
  5339	"But not more so than I to find you."
  5340	
  5341	"I came to find a friend."
  5342	
  5343	"And I to find an enemy."
  5344	
  5345	"An enemy?"
  5346	
  5347	"Yes; one of my natural enemies, or, shall I say, my natural
  5348	prey. Briefly, Watson, I am in the midst of a very remarkable
  5349	inquiry, and I have hoped to find a clue in the incoherent
  5350	ramblings of these sots, as I have done before now. Had I been
  5351	recognised in that den my life would not have been worth an
  5352	hour's purchase; for I have used it before now for my own
  5353	purposes, and the rascally Lascar who runs it has sworn to have
  5354	vengeance upon me. There is a trap-door at the back of that
  5355	building, near the corner of Paul's Wharf, which could tell some
  5356	strange tales of what has passed through it upon the moonless
  5357	nights."
  5358	
  5359	"What! You do not mean bodies?"
  5360	
  5361	"Ay, bodies, Watson. We should be rich men if we had 1000 pounds
  5362	for every poor devil who has been done to death in that den. It
  5363	is the vilest murder-trap on the whole riverside, and I fear that
  5364	Neville St. Clair has entered it never to leave it more. But our
  5365	trap should be here." He put his two forefingers between his
  5366	teeth and whistled shrilly--a signal which was answered by a
  5367	similar whistle from the distance, followed shortly by the rattle
  5368	of wheels and the clink of horses' hoofs.
  5369	
  5370	"Now, Watson," said Holmes, as a tall dog-cart dashed up through
  5371	the gloom, throwing out two golden tunnels of yellow light from
  5372	its side lanterns. "You'll come with me, won't you?"
  5373	
  5374	"If I can be of use."
  5375	
  5376	"Oh, a trusty comrade is always of use; and a chronicler still
  5377	more so. My room at The Cedars is a double-bedded one."
  5378	
  5379	"The Cedars?"
  5380	
  5381	"Yes; that is Mr. St. Clair's house. I am staying there while I
  5382	conduct the inquiry."
  5383	
  5384	"Where is it, then?"
  5385	
  5386	"Near Lee, in Kent. We have a seven-mile drive before us."
  5387	
  5388	"But I am all in the dark."
  5389	
  5390	"Of course you are. You'll know all about it presently. Jump up
  5391	here. All right, John; we shall not need you. Here's half a
  5392	crown. Look out for me to-morrow, about eleven. Give her her
  5393	head. So long, then!"
  5394	
  5395	He flicked the horse with his whip, and we dashed away through
  5396	the endless succession of sombre and deserted streets, which
  5397	widened gradually, until we were flying across a broad
  5398	balustraded bridge, with the murky river flowing sluggishly
  5399	beneath us. Beyond lay another dull wilderness of bricks and
  5400	mortar, its silence broken only by the heavy, regular footfall of
  5401	the policeman, or the songs and shouts of some belated party of
  5402	revellers. A dull wrack was drifting slowly across the sky, and a
  5403	star or two twinkled dimly here and there through the rifts of
  5404	the clouds. Holmes drove in silence, with his head sunk upon his
  5405	breast, and the air of a man who is lost in thought, while I sat
  5406	beside him, curious to learn what this new quest might be which
  5407	seemed to tax his powers so sorely, and yet afraid to break in
  5408	upon the current of his thoughts. We had driven several miles,
  5409	and were beginning to get to the fringe of the belt of suburban
  5410	villas, when he shook himself, shrugged his shoulders, and lit up
  5411	his pipe with the air of a man who has satisfied himself that he
  5412	is acting for the best.
  5413	
  5414	"You have a grand gift of silence, Watson," said he. "It makes
  5415	you quite invaluable as a companion. 'Pon my word, it is a great
  5416	thing for me to have someone to talk to, for my own thoughts are
  5417	not over-pleasant. I was wondering what I should say to this dear
  5418	little woman to-night when she meets me at the door."
  5419	
  5420	"You forget that I know nothing about it."
  5421	
  5422	"I shall just have time to tell you the facts of the case before
  5423	we get to Lee. It seems absurdly simple, and yet, somehow I can
  5424	get nothing to go upon. There's plenty of thread, no doubt, but I
  5425	can't get the end of it into my hand. Now, I'll state the case
  5426	clearly and concisely to you, Watson, and maybe you can see a
  5427	spark where all is dark to me."
  5428	
  5429	"Proceed, then."
  5430	
  5431	"Some years ago--to be definite, in May, 1884--there came to Lee
  5432	a gentleman, Neville St. Clair by name, who appeared to have
  5433	plenty of money. He took a large villa, laid out the grounds very
  5434	nicely, and lived generally in good style. By degrees he made
  5435	friends in the neighbourhood, and in 1887 he married the daughter
  5436	of a local brewer, by whom he now has two children. He had no
  5437	occupation, but was interested in several companies and went into
  5438	town as a rule in the morning, returning by the 5:14 from Cannon
  5439	Street every night. Mr. St. Clair is now thirty-seven years of
  5440	age, is a man of temperate habits, a good husband, a very
  5441	affectionate father, and a man who is popular with all who know
  5442	him. I may add that his whole debts at the present moment, as far
  5443	as we have been able to ascertain, amount to 88 pounds 10s., while
  5444	he has 220 pounds standing to his credit in the Capital and
  5445	Counties Bank. There is no reason, therefore, to think that money
  5446	troubles have been weighing upon his mind.
  5447	
  5448	"Last Monday Mr. Neville St. Clair went into town rather earlier
  5449	than usual, remarking before he started that he had two important
  5450	commissions to perform, and that he would bring his little boy
  5451	home a box of bricks. Now, by the merest chance, his wife
  5452	received a telegram upon this same Monday, very shortly after his
  5453	departure, to the effect that a small parcel of considerable
  5454	value which she had been expecting was waiting for her at the
  5455	offices of the Aberdeen Shipping Company. Now, if you are well up
  5456	in your London, you will know that the office of the company is
  5457	in Fresno Street, which branches out of Upper Swandam Lane, where
  5458	you found me to-night. Mrs. St. Clair had her lunch, started for
  5459	the City, did some shopping, proceeded to the company's office,
  5460	got her packet, and found herself at exactly 4:35 walking through
  5461	Swandam Lane on her way back to the station. Have you followed me
  5462	so far?"
  5463	
  5464	"It is very clear."
  5465	
  5466	"If you remember, Monday was an exceedingly hot day, and Mrs. St.
  5467	Clair walked slowly, glancing about in the hope of seeing a cab,
  5468	as she did not like the neighbourhood in which she found herself.
  5469	While she was walking in this way down Swandam Lane, she suddenly
  5470	heard an ejaculation or cry, and was struck cold to see her
  5471	husband looking down at her and, as it seemed to her, beckoning
  5472	to her from a second-floor window. The window was open, and she
  5473	distinctly saw his face, which she describes as being terribly
  5474	agitated. He waved his hands frantically to her, and then
  5475	vanished from the window so suddenly that it seemed to her that
  5476	he had been plucked back by some irresistible force from behind.
  5477	One singular point which struck her quick feminine eye was that
  5478	although he wore some dark coat, such as he had started to town
  5479	in, he had on neither collar nor necktie.
  5480	
  5481	"Convinced that something was amiss with him, she rushed down the
  5482	steps--for the house was none other than the opium den in which
  5483	you found me to-night--and running through the front room she
  5484	attempted to ascend the stairs which led to the first floor. At
  5485	the foot of the stairs, however, she met this Lascar scoundrel of
  5486	whom I have spoken, who thrust her back and, aided by a Dane, who
  5487	acts as assistant there, pushed her out into the street. Filled
  5488	with the most maddening doubts and fears, she rushed down the
  5489	lane and, by rare good-fortune, met in Fresno Street a number of
  5490	constables with an inspector, all on their way to their beat. The
  5491	inspector and two men accompanied her back, and in spite of the
  5492	continued resistance of the proprietor, they made their way to
  5493	the room in which Mr. St. Clair had last been seen. There was no
  5494	sign of him there. In fact, in the whole of that floor there was
  5495	no one to be found save a crippled wretch of hideous aspect, who,
  5496	it seems, made his home there. Both he and the Lascar stoutly
  5497	swore that no one else had been in the front room during the
  5498	afternoon. So determined was their denial that the inspector was
  5499	staggered, and had almost come to believe that Mrs. St. Clair had
  5500	been deluded when, with a cry, she sprang at a small deal box
  5501	which lay upon the table and tore the lid from it. Out there fell
  5502	a cascade of children's bricks. It was the toy which he had
  5503	promised to bring home.
  5504	
  5505	"This discovery, and the evident confusion which the cripple
  5506	showed, made the inspector realise that the matter was serious.
  5507	The rooms were carefully examined, and results all pointed to an
  5508	abominable crime. The front room was plainly furnished as a
  5509	sitting-room and led into a small bedroom, which looked out upon
  5510	the back of one of the wharves. Between the wharf and the bedroom
  5511	window is a narrow strip, which is dry at low tide but is covered
  5512	at high tide with at least four and a half feet of water. The
  5513	bedroom window was a broad one and opened from below. On
  5514	examination traces of blood were to be seen upon the windowsill,
  5515	and several scattered drops were visible upon the wooden floor of
  5516	the bedroom. Thrust away behind a curtain in the front room were
  5517	all the clothes of Mr. Neville St. Clair, with the exception of
  5518	his coat. His boots, his socks, his hat, and his watch--all were
  5519	there. There were no signs of violence upon any of these
  5520	garments, and there were no other traces of Mr. Neville St.
  5521	Clair. Out of the window he must apparently have gone for no
  5522	other exit could be discovered, and the ominous bloodstains upon
  5523	the sill gave little promise that he could save himself by
  5524	swimming, for the tide was at its very highest at the moment of
  5525	the tragedy.
  5526	
  5527	"And now as to the villains who seemed to be immediately
  5528	implicated in the matter. The Lascar was known to be a man of the
  5529	vilest antecedents, but as, by Mrs. St. Clair's story, he was
  5530	known to have been at the foot of the stair within a very few
  5531	seconds of her husband's appearance at the window, he could
  5532	hardly have been more than an accessory to the crime. His defence
  5533	was one of absolute ignorance, and he protested that he had no
  5534	knowledge as to the doings of Hugh Boone, his lodger, and that he
  5535	could not account in any way for the presence of the missing
  5536	gentleman's clothes.
  5537	
  5538	"So much for the Lascar manager. Now for the sinister cripple who
  5539	lives upon the second floor of the opium den, and who was
  5540	certainly the last human being whose eyes rested upon Neville St.
  5541	Clair. His name is Hugh Boone, and his hideous face is one which
  5542	is familiar to every man who goes much to the City. He is a
  5543	professional beggar, though in order to avoid the police
  5544	regulations he pretends to a small trade in wax vestas. Some
  5545	little distance down Threadneedle Street, upon the left-hand
  5546	side, there is, as you may have remarked, a small angle in the
  5547	wall. Here it is that this creature takes his daily seat,
  5548	cross-legged with his tiny stock of matches on his lap, and as he
  5549	is a piteous spectacle a small rain of charity descends into the
  5550	greasy leather cap which lies upon the pavement beside him. I
  5551	have watched the fellow more than once before ever I thought of
  5552	making his professional acquaintance, and I have been surprised
  5553	at the harvest which he has reaped in a short time. His
  5554	appearance, you see, is so remarkable that no one can pass him
  5555	without observing him. A shock of orange hair, a pale face
  5556	disfigured by a horrible scar, which, by its contraction, has
  5557	turned up the outer edge of his upper lip, a bulldog chin, and a
  5558	pair of very penetrating dark eyes, which present a singular
  5559	contrast to the colour of his hair, all mark him out from amid
  5560	the common crowd of mendicants and so, too, does his wit, for he
  5561	is ever ready with a reply to any piece of chaff which may be
  5562	thrown at him by the passers-by. This is the man whom we now
  5563	learn to have been the lodger at the opium den, and to have been
  5564	the last man to see the gentleman of whom we are in quest."
  5565	
  5566	"But a cripple!" said I. "What could he have done single-handed
  5567	against a man in the prime of life?"
  5568	
  5569	"He is a cripple in the sense that he walks with a limp; but in
  5570	other respects he appears to be a powerful and well-nurtured man.
  5571	Surely your medical experience would tell you, Watson, that
  5572	weakness in one limb is often compensated for by exceptional
  5573	strength in the others."
  5574	
  5575	"Pray continue your narrative."
  5576	
  5577	"Mrs. St. Clair had fainted at the sight of the blood upon the
  5578	window, and she was escorted home in a cab by the police, as her
  5579	presence could be of no help to them in their investigations.
  5580	Inspector Barton, who had charge of the case, made a very careful
  5581	examination of the premises, but without finding anything which
  5582	threw any light upon the matter. One mistake had been made in not
  5583	arresting Boone instantly, as he was allowed some few minutes
  5584	during which he might have communicated with his friend the
  5585	Lascar, but this fault was soon remedied, and he was seized and
  5586	searched, without anything being found which could incriminate
  5587	him. There were, it is true, some blood-stains upon his right
  5588	shirt-sleeve, but he pointed to his ring-finger, which had been
  5589	cut near the nail, and explained that the bleeding came from
  5590	there, adding that he had been to the window not long before, and
  5591	that the stains which had been observed there came doubtless from
  5592	the same source. He denied strenuously having ever seen Mr.
  5593	Neville St. Clair and swore that the presence of the clothes in
  5594	his room was as much a mystery to him as to the police. As to
  5595	Mrs. St. Clair's assertion that she had actually seen her husband
  5596	at the window, he declared that she must have been either mad or
  5597	dreaming. He was removed, loudly protesting, to the
  5598	police-station, while the inspector remained upon the premises in
  5599	the hope that the ebbing tide might afford some fresh clue.
  5600	
  5601	"And it did, though they hardly found upon the mud-bank what they
  5602	had feared to find. It was Neville St. Clair's coat, and not
  5603	Neville St. Clair, which lay uncovered as the tide receded. And
  5604	what do you think they found in the pockets?"
  5605	
  5606	"I cannot imagine."
  5607	
  5608	"No, I don't think you would guess. Every pocket stuffed with
  5609	pennies and half-pennies--421 pennies and 270 half-pennies. It
  5610	was no wonder that it had not been swept away by the tide. But a
  5611	human body is a different matter. There is a fierce eddy between
  5612	the wharf and the house. It seemed likely enough that the
  5613	weighted coat had remained when the stripped body had been sucked
  5614	away into the river."
  5615	
  5616	"But I understand that all the other clothes were found in the
  5617	room. Would the body be dressed in a coat alone?"
  5618	
  5619	"No, sir, but the facts might be met speciously enough. Suppose
  5620	that this man Boone had thrust Neville St. Clair through the
  5621	window, there is no human eye which could have seen the deed.
  5622	What would he do then? It would of course instantly strike him
  5623	that he must get rid of the tell-tale garments. He would seize
  5624	the coat, then, and be in the act of throwing it out, when it
  5625	would occur to him that it would swim and not sink. He has little
  5626	time, for he has heard the scuffle downstairs when the wife tried
  5627	to force her way up, and perhaps he has already heard from his
  5628	Lascar confederate that the police are hurrying up the street.
  5629	There is not an instant to be lost. He rushes to some secret
  5630	hoard, where he has accumulated the fruits of his beggary, and he
  5631	stuffs all the coins upon which he can lay his hands into the
  5632	pockets to make sure of the coat's sinking. He throws it out, and
  5633	would have done the same with the other garments had not he heard
  5634	the rush of steps below, and only just had time to close the
  5635	window when the police appeared."
  5636	
  5637	"It certainly sounds feasible."
  5638	
  5639	"Well, we will take it as a working hypothesis for want of a
  5640	better. Boone, as I have told you, was arrested and taken to the
  5641	station, but it could not be shown that there had ever before
  5642	been anything against him. He had for years been known as a
  5643	professional beggar, but his life appeared to have been a very
  5644	quiet and innocent one. There the matter stands at present, and
  5645	the questions which have to be solved--what Neville St. Clair was
  5646	doing in the opium den, what happened to him when there, where is
  5647	he now, and what Hugh Boone had to do with his disappearance--are
  5648	all as far from a solution as ever. I confess that I cannot
  5649	recall any case within my experience which looked at the first
  5650	glance so simple and yet which presented such difficulties."
  5651	
  5652	While Sherlock Holmes had been detailing this singular series of
  5653	events, we had been whirling through the outskirts of the great
  5654	town until the last straggling houses had been left behind, and
  5655	we rattled along with a country hedge upon either side of us.
  5656	Just as he finished, however, we drove through two scattered
  5657	villages, where a few lights still glimmered in the windows.
  5658	
  5659	"We are on the outskirts of Lee," said my companion. "We have
  5660	touched on three English counties in our short drive, starting in
  5661	Middlesex, passing over an angle of Surrey, and ending in Kent.
  5662	See that light among the trees? That is The Cedars, and beside
  5663	that lamp sits a woman whose anxious ears have already, I have
  5664	little doubt, caught the clink of our horse's feet."
  5665	
  5666	"But why are you not conducting the case from Baker Street?" I
  5667	asked.
  5668	
  5669	"Because there are many inquiries which must be made out here.
  5670	Mrs. St. Clair has most kindly put two rooms at my disposal, and
  5671	you may rest assured that she will have nothing but a welcome for
  5672	my friend and colleague. I hate to meet her, Watson, when I have
  5673	no news of her husband. Here we are. Whoa, there, whoa!"
  5674	
  5675	We had pulled up in front of a large villa which stood within its
  5676	own grounds. A stable-boy had run out to the horse's head, and
  5677	springing down, I followed Holmes up the small, winding
  5678	gravel-drive which led to the house. As we approached, the door
  5679	flew open, and a little blonde woman stood in the opening, clad
  5680	in some sort of light mousseline de soie, with a touch of fluffy
  5681	pink chiffon at her neck and wrists. She stood with her figure
  5682	outlined against the flood of light, one hand upon the door, one
  5683	half-raised in her eagerness, her body slightly bent, her head
  5684	and face protruded, with eager eyes and parted lips, a standing
  5685	question.
  5686	
  5687	"Well?" she cried, "well?" And then, seeing that there were two
  5688	of us, she gave a cry of hope which sank into a groan as she saw
  5689	that my companion shook his head and shrugged his shoulders.
  5690	
  5691	"No good news?"
  5692	
  5693	"None."
  5694	
  5695	"No bad?"
  5696	
  5697	"No."
  5698	
  5699	"Thank God for that. But come in. You must be weary, for you have
  5700	had a long day."
  5701	
  5702	"This is my friend, Dr. Watson. He has been of most vital use to
  5703	me in several of my cases, and a lucky chance has made it
  5704	possible for me to bring him out and associate him with this
  5705	investigation."
  5706	
  5707	"I am delighted to see you," said she, pressing my hand warmly.
  5708	"You will, I am sure, forgive anything that may be wanting in our
  5709	arrangements, when you consider the blow which has come so
  5710	suddenly upon us."
  5711	
  5712	"My dear madam," said I, "I am an old campaigner, and if I were
  5713	not I can very well see that no apology is needed. If I can be of
  5714	any assistance, either to you or to my friend here, I shall be
  5715	indeed happy."
  5716	
  5717	"Now, Mr. Sherlock Holmes," said the lady as we entered a
  5718	well-lit dining-room, upon the table of which a cold supper had
  5719	been laid out, "I should very much like to ask you one or two
  5720	plain questions, to which I beg that you will give a plain
  5721	answer."
  5722	
  5723	"Certainly, madam."
  5724	
  5725	"Do not trouble about my feelings. I am not hysterical, nor given
  5726	to fainting. I simply wish to hear your real, real opinion."
  5727	
  5728	"Upon what point?"
  5729	
  5730	"In your heart of hearts, do you think that Neville is alive?"
  5731	
  5732	Sherlock Holmes seemed to be embarrassed by the question.
  5733	"Frankly, now!" she repeated, standing upon the rug and looking
  5734	keenly down at him as he leaned back in a basket-chair.
  5735	
  5736	"Frankly, then, madam, I do not."
  5737	
  5738	"You think that he is dead?"
  5739	
  5740	"I do."
  5741	
  5742	"Murdered?"
  5743	
  5744	"I don't say that. Perhaps."
  5745	
  5746	"And on what day did he meet his death?"
  5747	
  5748	"On Monday."
  5749	
  5750	"Then perhaps, Mr. Holmes, you will be good enough to explain how
  5751	it is that I have received a letter from him to-day."
  5752	
  5753	Sherlock Holmes sprang out of his chair as if he had been
  5754	galvanised.
  5755	
  5756	"What!" he roared.
  5757	
  5758	"Yes, to-day." She stood smiling, holding up a little slip of
  5759	paper in the air.
  5760	
  5761	"May I see it?"
  5762	
  5763	"Certainly."
  5764	
  5765	He snatched it from her in his eagerness, and smoothing it out
  5766	upon the table he drew over the lamp and examined it intently. I
  5767	had left my chair and was gazing at it over his shoulder. The
  5768	envelope was a very coarse one and was stamped with the Gravesend
  5769	postmark and with the date of that very day, or rather of the day
  5770	before, for it was considerably after midnight.
  5771	
  5772	"Coarse writing," murmured Holmes. "Surely this is not your
  5773	husband's writing, madam."
  5774	
  5775	"No, but the enclosure is."
  5776	
  5777	"I perceive also that whoever addressed the envelope had to go
  5778	and inquire as to the address."
  5779	
  5780	"How can you tell that?"
  5781	
  5782	"The name, you see, is in perfectly black ink, which has dried
  5783	itself. The rest is of the greyish colour, which shows that
  5784	blotting-paper has been used. If it had been written straight
  5785	off, and then blotted, none would be of a deep black shade. This
  5786	man has written the name, and there has then been a pause before
  5787	he wrote the address, which can only mean that he was not
  5788	familiar with it. It is, of course, a trifle, but there is
  5789	nothing so important as trifles. Let us now see the letter. Ha!
  5790	there has been an enclosure here!"
  5791	
  5792	"Yes, there was a ring. His signet-ring."
  5793	
  5794	"And you are sure that this is your husband's hand?"
  5795	
  5796	"One of his hands."
  5797	
  5798	"One?"
  5799	
  5800	"His hand when he wrote hurriedly. It is very unlike his usual
  5801	writing, and yet I know it well."
  5802	
  5803	"'Dearest do not be frightened. All will come well. There is a
  5804	huge error which it may take some little time to rectify.
  5805	Wait in patience.--NEVILLE.' Written in pencil upon the fly-leaf
  5806	of a book, octavo size, no water-mark. Hum! Posted to-day in
  5807	Gravesend by a man with a dirty thumb. Ha! And the flap has been
  5808	gummed, if I am not very much in error, by a person who had been
  5809	chewing tobacco. And you have no doubt that it is your husband's
  5810	hand, madam?"
  5811	
  5812	"None. Neville wrote those words."
  5813	
  5814	"And they were posted to-day at Gravesend. Well, Mrs. St. Clair,
  5815	the clouds lighten, though I should not venture to say that the
  5816	danger is over."
  5817	
  5818	"But he must be alive, Mr. Holmes."
  5819	
  5820	"Unless this is a clever forgery to put us on the wrong scent.
  5821	The ring, after all, proves nothing. It may have been taken from
  5822	him."
  5823	
  5824	"No, no; it is, it is his very own writing!"
  5825	
  5826	"Very well. It may, however, have been written on Monday and only
  5827	posted to-day."
  5828	
  5829	"That is possible."
  5830	
  5831	"If so, much may have happened between."
  5832	
  5833	"Oh, you must not discourage me, Mr. Holmes. I know that all is
  5834	well with him. There is so keen a sympathy between us that I
  5835	should know if evil came upon him. On the very day that I saw him
  5836	last he cut himself in the bedroom, and yet I in the dining-room
  5837	rushed upstairs instantly with the utmost certainty that
  5838	something had happened. Do you think that I would respond to such
  5839	a trifle and yet be ignorant of his death?"
  5840	
  5841	"I have seen too much not to know that the impression of a woman
  5842	may be more valuable than the conclusion of an analytical
  5843	reasoner. And in this letter you certainly have a very strong
  5844	piece of evidence to corroborate your view. But if your husband
  5845	is alive and able to write letters, why should he remain away
  5846	from you?"
  5847	
  5848	"I cannot imagine. It is unthinkable."
  5849	
  5850	"And on Monday he made no remarks before leaving you?"
  5851	
  5852	"No."
  5853	
  5854	"And you were surprised to see him in Swandam Lane?"
  5855	
  5856	"Very much so."
  5857	
  5858	"Was the window open?"
  5859	
  5860	"Yes."
  5861	
  5862	"Then he might have called to you?"
  5863	
  5864	"He might."
  5865	
  5866	"He only, as I understand, gave an inarticulate cry?"
  5867	
  5868	"Yes."
  5869	
  5870	"A call for help, you thought?"
  5871	
  5872	"Yes. He waved his hands."
  5873	
  5874	"But it might have been a cry of surprise. Astonishment at the
  5875	unexpected sight of you might cause him to throw up his hands?"
  5876	
  5877	"It is possible."
  5878	
  5879	"And you thought he was pulled back?"
  5880	
  5881	"He disappeared so suddenly."
  5882	
  5883	"He might have leaped back. You did not see anyone else in the
  5884	room?"
  5885	
  5886	"No, but this horrible man confessed to having been there, and
  5887	the Lascar was at the foot of the stairs."
  5888	
  5889	"Quite so. Your husband, as far as you could see, had his
  5890	ordinary clothes on?"
  5891	
  5892	"But without his collar or tie. I distinctly saw his bare
  5893	throat."
  5894	
  5895	"Had he ever spoken of Swandam Lane?"
  5896	
  5897	"Never."
  5898	
  5899	"Had he ever showed any signs of having taken opium?"
  5900	
  5901	"Never."
  5902	
  5903	"Thank you, Mrs. St. Clair. Those are the principal points about
  5904	which I wished to be absolutely clear. We shall now have a little
  5905	supper and then retire, for we may have a very busy day
  5906	to-morrow."
  5907	
  5908	A large and comfortable double-bedded room had been placed at our
  5909	disposal, and I was quickly between the sheets, for I was weary
  5910	after my night of adventure. Sherlock Holmes was a man, however,
  5911	who, when he had an unsolved problem upon his mind, would go for
  5912	days, and even for a week, without rest, turning it over,
  5913	rearranging his facts, looking at it from every point of view
  5914	until he had either fathomed it or convinced himself that his
  5915	data were insufficient. It was soon evident to me that he was now
  5916	preparing for an all-night sitting. He took off his coat and
  5917	waistcoat, put on a large blue dressing-gown, and then wandered
  5918	about the room collecting pillows from his bed and cushions from
  5919	the sofa and armchairs. With these he constructed a sort of
  5920	Eastern divan, upon which he perched himself cross-legged, with
  5921	an ounce of shag tobacco and a box of matches laid out in front
  5922	of him. In the dim light of the lamp I saw him sitting there, an
  5923	old briar pipe between his lips, his eyes fixed vacantly upon the
  5924	corner of the ceiling, the blue smoke curling up from him,
  5925	silent, motionless, with the light shining upon his strong-set
  5926	aquiline features. So he sat as I dropped off to sleep, and so he
  5927	sat when a sudden ejaculation caused me to wake up, and I found
  5928	the summer sun shining into the apartment. The pipe was still
  5929	between his lips, the smoke still curled upward, and the room was
  5930	full of a dense tobacco haze, but nothing remained of the heap of
  5931	shag which I had seen upon the previous night.
  5932	
  5933	"Awake, Watson?" he asked.
  5934	
  5935	"Yes."
  5936	
  5937	"Game for a morning drive?"
  5938	
  5939	"Certainly."
  5940	
  5941	"Then dress. No one is stirring yet, but I know where the
  5942	stable-boy sleeps, and we shall soon have the trap out." He
  5943	chuckled to himself as he spoke, his eyes twinkled, and he seemed
  5944	a different man to the sombre thinker of the previous night.
  5945	
  5946	As I dressed I glanced at my watch. It was no wonder that no one
  5947	was stirring. It was twenty-five minutes past four. I had hardly
  5948	finished when Holmes returned with the news that the boy was
  5949	putting in the horse.
  5950	
  5951	"I want to test a little theory of mine," said he, pulling on his
  5952	boots. "I think, Watson, that you are now standing in the
  5953	presence of one of the most absolute fools in Europe. I deserve
  5954	to be kicked from here to Charing Cross. But I think I have the
  5955	key of the affair now."
  5956	
  5957	"And where is it?" I asked, smiling.
  5958	
  5959	"In the bathroom," he answered. "Oh, yes, I am not joking," he
  5960	continued, seeing my look of incredulity. "I have just been
  5961	there, and I have taken it out, and I have got it in this
  5962	Gladstone bag. Come on, my boy, and we shall see whether it will
  5963	not fit the lock."
  5964	
  5965	We made our way downstairs as quietly as possible, and out into
  5966	the bright morning sunshine. In the road stood our horse and
  5967	trap, with the half-clad stable-boy waiting at the head. We both
  5968	sprang in, and away we dashed down the London Road. A few country
  5969	carts were stirring, bearing in vegetables to the metropolis, but
  5970	the lines of villas on either side were as silent and lifeless as
  5971	some city in a dream.
  5972	
  5973	"It has been in some points a singular case," said Holmes,
  5974	flicking the horse on into a gallop. "I confess that I have been
  5975	as blind as a mole, but it is better to learn wisdom late than
  5976	never to learn it at all."
  5977	
  5978	In town the earliest risers were just beginning to look sleepily
  5979	from their windows as we drove through the streets of the Surrey
  5980	side. Passing down the Waterloo Bridge Road we crossed over the
  5981	river, and dashing up Wellington Street wheeled sharply to the
  5982	right and found ourselves in Bow Street. Sherlock Holmes was well
  5983	known to the force, and the two constables at the door saluted
  5984	him. One of them held the horse's head while the other led us in.
  5985	
  5986	"Who is on duty?" asked Holmes.
  5987	
  5988	"Inspector Bradstreet, sir."
  5989	
  5990	"Ah, Bradstreet, how are you?" A tall, stout official had come
  5991	down the stone-flagged passage, in a peaked cap and frogged
  5992	jacket. "I wish to have a quiet word with you, Bradstreet."
  5993	"Certainly, Mr. Holmes. Step into my room here." It was a small,
  5994	office-like room, with a huge ledger upon the table, and a
  5995	telephone projecting from the wall. The inspector sat down at his
  5996	desk.
  5997	
  5998	"What can I do for you, Mr. Holmes?"
  5999	
  6000	"I called about that beggarman, Boone--the one who was charged
  6001	with being concerned in the disappearance of Mr. Neville St.
  6002	Clair, of Lee."
  6003	
  6004	"Yes. He was brought up and remanded for further inquiries."
  6005	
  6006	"So I heard. You have him here?"
  6007	
  6008	"In the cells."
  6009	
  6010	"Is he quiet?"
  6011	
  6012	"Oh, he gives no trouble. But he is a dirty scoundrel."
  6013	
  6014	"Dirty?"
  6015	
  6016	"Yes, it is all we can do to make him wash his hands, and his
  6017	face is as black as a tinker's. Well, when once his case has been
  6018	settled, he will have a regular prison bath; and I think, if you
  6019	saw him, you would agree with me that he needed it."
  6020	
  6021	"I should like to see him very much."
  6022	
  6023	"Would you? That is easily done. Come this way. You can leave
  6024	your bag."
  6025	
  6026	"No, I think that I'll take it."
  6027	
  6028	"Very good. Come this way, if you please." He led us down a
  6029	passage, opened a barred door, passed down a winding stair, and
  6030	brought us to a whitewashed corridor with a line of doors on each
  6031	side.
  6032	
  6033	"The third on the right is his," said the inspector. "Here it
  6034	is!" He quietly shot back a panel in the upper part of the door
  6035	and glanced through.
  6036	
  6037	"He is asleep," said he. "You can see him very well."
  6038	
  6039	We both put our eyes to the grating. The prisoner lay with his
  6040	face towards us, in a very deep sleep, breathing slowly and
  6041	heavily. He was a middle-sized man, coarsely clad as became his
  6042	calling, with a coloured shirt protruding through the rent in his
  6043	tattered coat. He was, as the inspector had said, extremely
  6044	dirty, but the grime which covered his face could not conceal its
  6045	repulsive ugliness. A broad wheal from an old scar ran right
  6046	across it from eye to chin, and by its contraction had turned up
  6047	one side of the upper lip, so that three teeth were exposed in a
  6048	perpetual snarl. A shock of very bright red hair grew low over
  6049	his eyes and forehead.
  6050	
  6051	"He's a beauty, isn't he?" said the inspector.
  6052	
  6053	"He certainly needs a wash," remarked Holmes. "I had an idea that
  6054	he might, and I took the liberty of bringing the tools with me."
  6055	He opened the Gladstone bag as he spoke, and took out, to my
  6056	astonishment, a very large bath-sponge.
  6057	
  6058	"He! he! You are a funny one," chuckled the inspector.
  6059	
  6060	"Now, if you will have the great goodness to open that door very
  6061	quietly, we will soon make him cut a much more respectable
  6062	figure."
  6063	
  6064	"Well, I don't know why not," said the inspector. "He doesn't
  6065	look a credit to the Bow Street cells, does he?" He slipped his
  6066	key into the lock, and we all very quietly entered the cell. The
  6067	sleeper half turned, and then settled down once more into a deep
  6068	slumber. Holmes stooped to the water-jug, moistened his sponge,
  6069	and then rubbed it twice vigorously across and down the
  6070	prisoner's face.
  6071	
  6072	"Let me introduce you," he shouted, "to Mr. Neville St. Clair, of
  6073	Lee, in the county of Kent."
  6074	
  6075	Never in my life have I seen such a sight. The man's face peeled
  6076	off under the sponge like the bark from a tree. Gone was the
  6077	coarse brown tint! Gone, too, was the horrid scar which had
  6078	seamed it across, and the twisted lip which had given the
  6079	repulsive sneer to the face! A twitch brought away the tangled
  6080	red hair, and there, sitting up in his bed, was a pale,
  6081	sad-faced, refined-looking man, black-haired and smooth-skinned,
  6082	rubbing his eyes and staring about him with sleepy bewilderment.
  6083	Then suddenly realising the exposure, he broke into a scream and
  6084	threw himself down with his face to the pillow.
  6085	
  6086	"Great heavens!" cried the inspector, "it is, indeed, the missing
  6087	man. I know him from the photograph."
  6088	
  6089	The prisoner turned with the reckless air of a man who abandons
  6090	himself to his destiny. "Be it so," said he. "And pray what am I
  6091	charged with?"
  6092	
  6093	"With making away with Mr. Neville St.-- Oh, come, you can't be
  6094	charged with that unless they make a case of attempted suicide of
  6095	it," said the inspector with a grin. "Well, I have been
  6096	twenty-seven years in the force, but this really takes the cake."
  6097	
  6098	"If I am Mr. Neville St. Clair, then it is obvious that no crime
  6099	has been committed, and that, therefore, I am illegally
  6100	detained."
  6101	
  6102	"No crime, but a very great error has been committed," said
  6103	Holmes. "You would have done better to have trusted you wife."
  6104	
  6105	"It was not the wife; it was the children," groaned the prisoner.
  6106	"God help me, I would not have them ashamed of their father. My
  6107	God! What an exposure! What can I do?"
  6108	
  6109	Sherlock Holmes sat down beside him on the couch and patted him
  6110	kindly on the shoulder.
  6111	
  6112	"If you leave it to a court of law to clear the matter up," said
  6113	he, "of course you can hardly avoid publicity. On the other hand,
  6114	if you convince the police authorities that there is no possible
  6115	case against you, I do not know that there is any reason that the
  6116	details should find their way into the papers. Inspector
  6117	Bradstreet would, I am sure, make notes upon anything which you
  6118	might tell us and submit it to the proper authorities. The case
  6119	would then never go into court at all."
  6120	
  6121	"God bless you!" cried the prisoner passionately. "I would have
  6122	endured imprisonment, ay, even execution, rather than have left
  6123	my miserable secret as a family blot to my children.
  6124	
  6125	"You are the first who have ever heard my story. My father was a
  6126	schoolmaster in Chesterfield, where I received an excellent
  6127	education. I travelled in my youth, took to the stage, and
  6128	finally became a reporter on an evening paper in London. One day
  6129	my editor wished to have a series of articles upon begging in the
  6130	metropolis, and I volunteered to supply them. There was the point
  6131	from which all my adventures started. It was only by trying
  6132	begging as an amateur that I could get the facts upon which to
  6133	base my articles. When an actor I had, of course, learned all the
  6134	secrets of making up, and had been famous in the green-room for
  6135	my skill. I took advantage now of my attainments. I painted my
  6136	face, and to make myself as pitiable as possible I made a good
  6137	scar and fixed one side of my lip in a twist by the aid of a
  6138	small slip of flesh-coloured plaster. Then with a red head of
  6139	hair, and an appropriate dress, I took my station in the business
  6140	part of the city, ostensibly as a match-seller but really as a
  6141	beggar. For seven hours I plied my trade, and when I returned
  6142	home in the evening I found to my surprise that I had received no
  6143	less than 26s. 4d.
  6144	
  6145	"I wrote my articles and thought little more of the matter until,
  6146	some time later, I backed a bill for a friend and had a writ
  6147	served upon me for 25 pounds. I was at my wit's end where to get
  6148	the money, but a sudden idea came to me. I begged a fortnight's
  6149	grace from the creditor, asked for a holiday from my employers,
  6150	and spent the time in begging in the City under my disguise. In
  6151	ten days I had the money and had paid the debt.
  6152	
  6153	"Well, you can imagine how hard it was to settle down to arduous
  6154	work at 2 pounds a week when I knew that I could earn as much in
  6155	a day by smearing my face with a little paint, laying my cap on
  6156	the ground, and sitting still. It was a long fight between my
  6157	pride and the money, but the dollars won at last, and I threw up
  6158	reporting and sat day after day in the corner which I had first
  6159	chosen, inspiring pity by my ghastly face and filling my pockets
  6160	with coppers. Only one man knew my secret. He was the keeper of a
  6161	low den in which I used to lodge in Swandam Lane, where I could
  6162	every morning emerge as a squalid beggar and in the evenings
  6163	transform myself into a well-dressed man about town. This fellow,
  6164	a Lascar, was well paid by me for his rooms, so that I knew that
  6165	my secret was safe in his possession.
  6166	
  6167	"Well, very soon I found that I was saving considerable sums of
  6168	money. I do not mean that any beggar in the streets of London
  6169	could earn 700 pounds a year--which is less than my average
  6170	takings--but I had exceptional advantages in my power of making
  6171	up, and also in a facility of repartee, which improved by
  6172	practice and made me quite a recognised character in the City.
  6173	All day a stream of pennies, varied by silver, poured in upon me,
  6174	and it was a very bad day in which I failed to take 2 pounds.
  6175	
  6176	"As I grew richer I grew more ambitious, took a house in the
  6177	country, and eventually married, without anyone having a
  6178	suspicion as to my real occupation. My dear wife knew that I had
