  6179	business in the City. She little knew what.
  6180	
  6181	"Last Monday I had finished for the day and was dressing in my
  6182	room above the opium den when I looked out of my window and saw,
  6183	to my horror and astonishment, that my wife was standing in the
  6184	street, with her eyes fixed full upon me. I gave a cry of
  6185	surprise, threw up my arms to cover my face, and, rushing to my
  6186	confidant, the Lascar, entreated him to prevent anyone from
  6187	coming up to me. I heard her voice downstairs, but I knew that
  6188	she could not ascend. Swiftly I threw off my clothes, pulled on
  6189	those of a beggar, and put on my pigments and wig. Even a wife's
  6190	eyes could not pierce so complete a disguise. But then it
  6191	occurred to me that there might be a search in the room, and that
  6192	the clothes might betray me. I threw open the window, reopening
  6193	by my violence a small cut which I had inflicted upon myself in
  6194	the bedroom that morning. Then I seized my coat, which was
  6195	weighted by the coppers which I had just transferred to it from
  6196	the leather bag in which I carried my takings. I hurled it out of
  6197	the window, and it disappeared into the Thames. The other clothes
  6198	would have followed, but at that moment there was a rush of
  6199	constables up the stair, and a few minutes after I found, rather,
  6200	I confess, to my relief, that instead of being identified as Mr.
  6201	Neville St. Clair, I was arrested as his murderer.
  6202	
  6203	"I do not know that there is anything else for me to explain. I
  6204	was determined to preserve my disguise as long as possible, and
  6205	hence my preference for a dirty face. Knowing that my wife would
  6206	be terribly anxious, I slipped off my ring and confided it to the
  6207	Lascar at a moment when no constable was watching me, together
  6208	with a hurried scrawl, telling her that she had no cause to
  6209	fear."
  6210	
  6211	"That note only reached her yesterday," said Holmes.
  6212	
  6213	"Good God! What a week she must have spent!"
  6214	
  6215	"The police have watched this Lascar," said Inspector Bradstreet,
  6216	"and I can quite understand that he might find it difficult to
  6217	post a letter unobserved. Probably he handed it to some sailor
  6218	customer of his, who forgot all about it for some days."
  6219	
  6220	"That was it," said Holmes, nodding approvingly; "I have no doubt
  6221	of it. But have you never been prosecuted for begging?"
  6222	
  6223	"Many times; but what was a fine to me?"
  6224	
  6225	"It must stop here, however," said Bradstreet. "If the police are
  6226	to hush this thing up, there must be no more of Hugh Boone."
  6227	
  6228	"I have sworn it by the most solemn oaths which a man can take."
  6229	
  6230	"In that case I think that it is probable that no further steps
  6231	may be taken. But if you are found again, then all must come out.
  6232	I am sure, Mr. Holmes, that we are very much indebted to you for
  6233	having cleared the matter up. I wish I knew how you reach your
  6234	results."
  6235	
  6236	"I reached this one," said my friend, "by sitting upon five
  6237	pillows and consuming an ounce of shag. I think, Watson, that if
  6238	we drive to Baker Street we shall just be in time for breakfast."
  6239	
  6240	
  6241	
  6242	VII. THE ADVENTURE OF THE BLUE CARBUNCLE
  6243	
  6244	I had called upon my friend Sherlock Holmes upon the second
  6245	morning after Christmas, with the intention of wishing him the
  6246	compliments of the season. He was lounging upon the sofa in a
  6247	purple dressing-gown, a pipe-rack within his reach upon the
  6248	right, and a pile of crumpled morning papers, evidently newly
  6249	studied, near at hand. Beside the couch was a wooden chair, and
  6250	on the angle of the back hung a very seedy and disreputable
  6251	hard-felt hat, much the worse for wear, and cracked in several
  6252	places. A lens and a forceps lying upon the seat of the chair
  6253	suggested that the hat had been suspended in this manner for the
  6254	purpose of examination.
  6255	
  6256	"You are engaged," said I; "perhaps I interrupt you."
  6257	
  6258	"Not at all. I am glad to have a friend with whom I can discuss
  6259	my results. The matter is a perfectly trivial one"--he jerked his
  6260	thumb in the direction of the old hat--"but there are points in
  6261	connection with it which are not entirely devoid of interest and
  6262	even of instruction."
  6263	
  6264	I seated myself in his armchair and warmed my hands before his
  6265	crackling fire, for a sharp frost had set in, and the windows
  6266	were thick with the ice crystals. "I suppose," I remarked, "that,
  6267	homely as it looks, this thing has some deadly story linked on to
  6268	it--that it is the clue which will guide you in the solution of
  6269	some mystery and the punishment of some crime."
  6270	
  6271	"No, no. No crime," said Sherlock Holmes, laughing. "Only one of
  6272	those whimsical little incidents which will happen when you have
  6273	four million human beings all jostling each other within the
  6274	space of a few square miles. Amid the action and reaction of so
  6275	dense a swarm of humanity, every possible combination of events
  6276	may be expected to take place, and many a little problem will be
  6277	presented which may be striking and bizarre without being
  6278	criminal. We have already had experience of such."
  6279	
  6280	"So much so," I remarked, "that of the last six cases which I
  6281	have added to my notes, three have been entirely free of any
  6282	legal crime."
  6283	
  6284	"Precisely. You allude to my attempt to recover the Irene Adler
  6285	papers, to the singular case of Miss Mary Sutherland, and to the
  6286	adventure of the man with the twisted lip. Well, I have no doubt
  6287	that this small matter will fall into the same innocent category.
  6288	You know Peterson, the commissionaire?"
  6289	
  6290	"Yes."
  6291	
  6292	"It is to him that this trophy belongs."
  6293	
  6294	"It is his hat."
  6295	
  6296	"No, no, he found it. Its owner is unknown. I beg that you will
  6297	look upon it not as a battered billycock but as an intellectual
  6298	problem. And, first, as to how it came here. It arrived upon
  6299	Christmas morning, in company with a good fat goose, which is, I
  6300	have no doubt, roasting at this moment in front of Peterson's
  6301	fire. The facts are these: about four o'clock on Christmas
  6302	morning, Peterson, who, as you know, is a very honest fellow, was
  6303	returning from some small jollification and was making his way
  6304	homeward down Tottenham Court Road. In front of him he saw, in
  6305	the gaslight, a tallish man, walking with a slight stagger, and
  6306	carrying a white goose slung over his shoulder. As he reached the
  6307	corner of Goodge Street, a row broke out between this stranger
  6308	and a little knot of roughs. One of the latter knocked off the
  6309	man's hat, on which he raised his stick to defend himself and,
  6310	swinging it over his head, smashed the shop window behind him.
  6311	Peterson had rushed forward to protect the stranger from his
  6312	assailants; but the man, shocked at having broken the window, and
  6313	seeing an official-looking person in uniform rushing towards him,
  6314	dropped his goose, took to his heels, and vanished amid the
  6315	labyrinth of small streets which lie at the back of Tottenham
  6316	Court Road. The roughs had also fled at the appearance of
  6317	Peterson, so that he was left in possession of the field of
  6318	battle, and also of the spoils of victory in the shape of this
  6319	battered hat and a most unimpeachable Christmas goose."
  6320	
  6321	"Which surely he restored to their owner?"
  6322	
  6323	"My dear fellow, there lies the problem. It is true that 'For
  6324	Mrs. Henry Baker' was printed upon a small card which was tied to
  6325	the bird's left leg, and it is also true that the initials 'H.
  6326	B.' are legible upon the lining of this hat, but as there are
  6327	some thousands of Bakers, and some hundreds of Henry Bakers in
  6328	this city of ours, it is not easy to restore lost property to any
  6329	one of them."
  6330	
  6331	"What, then, did Peterson do?"
  6332	
  6333	"He brought round both hat and goose to me on Christmas morning,
  6334	knowing that even the smallest problems are of interest to me.
  6335	The goose we retained until this morning, when there were signs
  6336	that, in spite of the slight frost, it would be well that it
  6337	should be eaten without unnecessary delay. Its finder has carried
  6338	it off, therefore, to fulfil the ultimate destiny of a goose,
  6339	while I continue to retain the hat of the unknown gentleman who
  6340	lost his Christmas dinner."
  6341	
  6342	"Did he not advertise?"
  6343	
  6344	"No."
  6345	
  6346	"Then, what clue could you have as to his identity?"
  6347	
  6348	"Only as much as we can deduce."
  6349	
  6350	"From his hat?"
  6351	
  6352	"Precisely."
  6353	
  6354	"But you are joking. What can you gather from this old battered
  6355	felt?"
  6356	
  6357	"Here is my lens. You know my methods. What can you gather
  6358	yourself as to the individuality of the man who has worn this
  6359	article?"
  6360	
  6361	I took the tattered object in my hands and turned it over rather
  6362	ruefully. It was a very ordinary black hat of the usual round
  6363	shape, hard and much the worse for wear. The lining had been of
  6364	red silk, but was a good deal discoloured. There was no maker's
  6365	name; but, as Holmes had remarked, the initials "H. B." were
  6366	scrawled upon one side. It was pierced in the brim for a
  6367	hat-securer, but the elastic was missing. For the rest, it was
  6368	cracked, exceedingly dusty, and spotted in several places,
  6369	although there seemed to have been some attempt to hide the
  6370	discoloured patches by smearing them with ink.
  6371	
  6372	"I can see nothing," said I, handing it back to my friend.
  6373	
  6374	"On the contrary, Watson, you can see everything. You fail,
  6375	however, to reason from what you see. You are too timid in
  6376	drawing your inferences."
  6377	
  6378	"Then, pray tell me what it is that you can infer from this hat?"
  6379	
  6380	He picked it up and gazed at it in the peculiar introspective
  6381	fashion which was characteristic of him. "It is perhaps less
  6382	suggestive than it might have been," he remarked, "and yet there
  6383	are a few inferences which are very distinct, and a few others
  6384	which represent at least a strong balance of probability. That
  6385	the man was highly intellectual is of course obvious upon the
  6386	face of it, and also that he was fairly well-to-do within the
  6387	last three years, although he has now fallen upon evil days. He
  6388	had foresight, but has less now than formerly, pointing to a
  6389	moral retrogression, which, when taken with the decline of his
  6390	fortunes, seems to indicate some evil influence, probably drink,
  6391	at work upon him. This may account also for the obvious fact that
  6392	his wife has ceased to love him."
  6393	
  6394	"My dear Holmes!"
  6395	
  6396	"He has, however, retained some degree of self-respect," he
  6397	continued, disregarding my remonstrance. "He is a man who leads a
  6398	sedentary life, goes out little, is out of training entirely, is
  6399	middle-aged, has grizzled hair which he has had cut within the
  6400	last few days, and which he anoints with lime-cream. These are
  6401	the more patent facts which are to be deduced from his hat. Also,
  6402	by the way, that it is extremely improbable that he has gas laid
  6403	on in his house."
  6404	
  6405	"You are certainly joking, Holmes."
  6406	
  6407	"Not in the least. Is it possible that even now, when I give you
  6408	these results, you are unable to see how they are attained?"
  6409	
  6410	"I have no doubt that I am very stupid, but I must confess that I
  6411	am unable to follow you. For example, how did you deduce that
  6412	this man was intellectual?"
  6413	
  6414	For answer Holmes clapped the hat upon his head. It came right
  6415	over the forehead and settled upon the bridge of his nose. "It is
  6416	a question of cubic capacity," said he; "a man with so large a
  6417	brain must have something in it."
  6418	
  6419	"The decline of his fortunes, then?"
  6420	
  6421	"This hat is three years old. These flat brims curled at the edge
  6422	came in then. It is a hat of the very best quality. Look at the
  6423	band of ribbed silk and the excellent lining. If this man could
  6424	afford to buy so expensive a hat three years ago, and has had no
  6425	hat since, then he has assuredly gone down in the world."
  6426	
  6427	"Well, that is clear enough, certainly. But how about the
  6428	foresight and the moral retrogression?"
  6429	
  6430	Sherlock Holmes laughed. "Here is the foresight," said he putting
  6431	his finger upon the little disc and loop of the hat-securer.
  6432	"They are never sold upon hats. If this man ordered one, it is a
  6433	sign of a certain amount of foresight, since he went out of his
  6434	way to take this precaution against the wind. But since we see
  6435	that he has broken the elastic and has not troubled to replace
  6436	it, it is obvious that he has less foresight now than formerly,
  6437	which is a distinct proof of a weakening nature. On the other
  6438	hand, he has endeavoured to conceal some of these stains upon the
  6439	felt by daubing them with ink, which is a sign that he has not
  6440	entirely lost his self-respect."
  6441	
  6442	"Your reasoning is certainly plausible."
  6443	
  6444	"The further points, that he is middle-aged, that his hair is
  6445	grizzled, that it has been recently cut, and that he uses
  6446	lime-cream, are all to be gathered from a close examination of the
  6447	lower part of the lining. The lens discloses a large number of
  6448	hair-ends, clean cut by the scissors of the barber. They all
  6449	appear to be adhesive, and there is a distinct odour of
  6450	lime-cream. This dust, you will observe, is not the gritty, grey
  6451	dust of the street but the fluffy brown dust of the house,
  6452	showing that it has been hung up indoors most of the time, while
  6453	the marks of moisture upon the inside are proof positive that the
  6454	wearer perspired very freely, and could therefore, hardly be in
  6455	the best of training."
  6456	
  6457	"But his wife--you said that she had ceased to love him."
  6458	
  6459	"This hat has not been brushed for weeks. When I see you, my dear
  6460	Watson, with a week's accumulation of dust upon your hat, and
  6461	when your wife allows you to go out in such a state, I shall fear
  6462	that you also have been unfortunate enough to lose your wife's
  6463	affection."
  6464	
  6465	"But he might be a bachelor."
  6466	
  6467	"Nay, he was bringing home the goose as a peace-offering to his
  6468	wife. Remember the card upon the bird's leg."
  6469	
  6470	"You have an answer to everything. But how on earth do you deduce
  6471	that the gas is not laid on in his house?"
  6472	
  6473	"One tallow stain, or even two, might come by chance; but when I
  6474	see no less than five, I think that there can be little doubt
  6475	that the individual must be brought into frequent contact with
  6476	burning tallow--walks upstairs at night probably with his hat in
  6477	one hand and a guttering candle in the other. Anyhow, he never
  6478	got tallow-stains from a gas-jet. Are you satisfied?"
  6479	
  6480	"Well, it is very ingenious," said I, laughing; "but since, as
  6481	you said just now, there has been no crime committed, and no harm
  6482	done save the loss of a goose, all this seems to be rather a
  6483	waste of energy."
  6484	
  6485	Sherlock Holmes had opened his mouth to reply, when the door flew
  6486	open, and Peterson, the commissionaire, rushed into the apartment
  6487	with flushed cheeks and the face of a man who is dazed with
  6488	astonishment.
  6489	
  6490	"The goose, Mr. Holmes! The goose, sir!" he gasped.
  6491	
  6492	"Eh? What of it, then? Has it returned to life and flapped off
  6493	through the kitchen window?" Holmes twisted himself round upon
  6494	the sofa to get a fairer view of the man's excited face.
  6495	
  6496	"See here, sir! See what my wife found in its crop!" He held out
  6497	his hand and displayed upon the centre of the palm a brilliantly
  6498	scintillating blue stone, rather smaller than a bean in size, but
  6499	of such purity and radiance that it twinkled like an electric
  6500	point in the dark hollow of his hand.
  6501	
  6502	Sherlock Holmes sat up with a whistle. "By Jove, Peterson!" said
  6503	he, "this is treasure trove indeed. I suppose you know what you
  6504	have got?"
  6505	
  6506	"A diamond, sir? A precious stone. It cuts into glass as though
  6507	it were putty."
  6508	
  6509	"It's more than a precious stone. It is the precious stone."
  6510	
  6511	"Not the Countess of Morcar's blue carbuncle!" I ejaculated.
  6512	
  6513	"Precisely so. I ought to know its size and shape, seeing that I
  6514	have read the advertisement about it in The Times every day
  6515	lately. It is absolutely unique, and its value can only be
  6516	conjectured, but the reward offered of 1000 pounds is certainly
  6517	not within a twentieth part of the market price."
  6518	
  6519	"A thousand pounds! Great Lord of mercy!" The commissionaire
  6520	plumped down into a chair and stared from one to the other of us.
  6521	
  6522	"That is the reward, and I have reason to know that there are
  6523	sentimental considerations in the background which would induce
  6524	the Countess to part with half her fortune if she could but
  6525	recover the gem."
  6526	
  6527	"It was lost, if I remember aright, at the Hotel Cosmopolitan," I
  6528	remarked.
  6529	
  6530	"Precisely so, on December 22nd, just five days ago. John Horner,
  6531	a plumber, was accused of having abstracted it from the lady's
  6532	jewel-case. The evidence against him was so strong that the case
  6533	has been referred to the Assizes. I have some account of the
  6534	matter here, I believe." He rummaged amid his newspapers,
  6535	glancing over the dates, until at last he smoothed one out,
  6536	doubled it over, and read the following paragraph:
  6537	
  6538	"Hotel Cosmopolitan Jewel Robbery. John Horner, 26, plumber, was
  6539	brought up upon the charge of having upon the 22nd inst.,
  6540	abstracted from the jewel-case of the Countess of Morcar the
  6541	valuable gem known as the blue carbuncle. James Ryder,
  6542	upper-attendant at the hotel, gave his evidence to the effect
  6543	that he had shown Horner up to the dressing-room of the Countess
  6544	of Morcar upon the day of the robbery in order that he might
  6545	solder the second bar of the grate, which was loose. He had
  6546	remained with Horner some little time, but had finally been
  6547	called away. On returning, he found that Horner had disappeared,
  6548	that the bureau had been forced open, and that the small morocco
  6549	casket in which, as it afterwards transpired, the Countess was
  6550	accustomed to keep her jewel, was lying empty upon the
  6551	dressing-table. Ryder instantly gave the alarm, and Horner was
  6552	arrested the same evening; but the stone could not be found
  6553	either upon his person or in his rooms. Catherine Cusack, maid to
  6554	the Countess, deposed to having heard Ryder's cry of dismay on
  6555	discovering the robbery, and to having rushed into the room,
  6556	where she found matters as described by the last witness.
  6557	Inspector Bradstreet, B division, gave evidence as to the arrest
  6558	of Horner, who struggled frantically, and protested his innocence
  6559	in the strongest terms. Evidence of a previous conviction for
  6560	robbery having been given against the prisoner, the magistrate
  6561	refused to deal summarily with the offence, but referred it to
  6562	the Assizes. Horner, who had shown signs of intense emotion
  6563	during the proceedings, fainted away at the conclusion and was
  6564	carried out of court."
  6565	
  6566	"Hum! So much for the police-court," said Holmes thoughtfully,
  6567	tossing aside the paper. "The question for us now to solve is the
  6568	sequence of events leading from a rifled jewel-case at one end to
  6569	the crop of a goose in Tottenham Court Road at the other. You
  6570	see, Watson, our little deductions have suddenly assumed a much
  6571	more important and less innocent aspect. Here is the stone; the
  6572	stone came from the goose, and the goose came from Mr. Henry
  6573	Baker, the gentleman with the bad hat and all the other
  6574	characteristics with which I have bored you. So now we must set
  6575	ourselves very seriously to finding this gentleman and
  6576	ascertaining what part he has played in this little mystery. To
  6577	do this, we must try the simplest means first, and these lie
  6578	undoubtedly in an advertisement in all the evening papers. If
  6579	this fail, I shall have recourse to other methods."
  6580	
  6581	"What will you say?"
  6582	
  6583	"Give me a pencil and that slip of paper. Now, then: 'Found at
  6584	the corner of Goodge Street, a goose and a black felt hat. Mr.
  6585	Henry Baker can have the same by applying at 6:30 this evening at
  6586	221B, Baker Street.' That is clear and concise."
  6587	
  6588	"Very. But will he see it?"
  6589	
  6590	"Well, he is sure to keep an eye on the papers, since, to a poor
  6591	man, the loss was a heavy one. He was clearly so scared by his
  6592	mischance in breaking the window and by the approach of Peterson
  6593	that he thought of nothing but flight, but since then he must
  6594	have bitterly regretted the impulse which caused him to drop his
  6595	bird. Then, again, the introduction of his name will cause him to
  6596	see it, for everyone who knows him will direct his attention to
  6597	it. Here you are, Peterson, run down to the advertising agency
  6598	and have this put in the evening papers."
  6599	
  6600	"In which, sir?"
  6601	
  6602	"Oh, in the Globe, Star, Pall Mall, St. James's, Evening News,
  6603	Standard, Echo, and any others that occur to you."
  6604	
  6605	"Very well, sir. And this stone?"
  6606	
  6607	"Ah, yes, I shall keep the stone. Thank you. And, I say,
  6608	Peterson, just buy a goose on your way back and leave it here
  6609	with me, for we must have one to give to this gentleman in place
  6610	of the one which your family is now devouring."
  6611	
  6612	When the commissionaire had gone, Holmes took up the stone and
  6613	held it against the light. "It's a bonny thing," said he. "Just
  6614	see how it glints and sparkles. Of course it is a nucleus and
  6615	focus of crime. Every good stone is. They are the devil's pet
  6616	baits. In the larger and older jewels every facet may stand for a
  6617	bloody deed. This stone is not yet twenty years old. It was found
  6618	in the banks of the Amoy River in southern China and is remarkable
  6619	in having every characteristic of the carbuncle, save that it is
  6620	blue in shade instead of ruby red. In spite of its youth, it has
  6621	already a sinister history. There have been two murders, a
  6622	vitriol-throwing, a suicide, and several robberies brought about
  6623	for the sake of this forty-grain weight of crystallised charcoal.
  6624	Who would think that so pretty a toy would be a purveyor to the
  6625	gallows and the prison? I'll lock it up in my strong box now and
  6626	drop a line to the Countess to say that we have it."
  6627	
  6628	"Do you think that this man Horner is innocent?"
  6629	
  6630	"I cannot tell."
  6631	
  6632	"Well, then, do you imagine that this other one, Henry Baker, had
  6633	anything to do with the matter?"
  6634	
  6635	"It is, I think, much more likely that Henry Baker is an
  6636	absolutely innocent man, who had no idea that the bird which he
  6637	was carrying was of considerably more value than if it were made
  6638	of solid gold. That, however, I shall determine by a very simple
  6639	test if we have an answer to our advertisement."
  6640	
  6641	"And you can do nothing until then?"
  6642	
  6643	"Nothing."
  6644	
  6645	"In that case I shall continue my professional round. But I shall
  6646	come back in the evening at the hour you have mentioned, for I
  6647	should like to see the solution of so tangled a business."
  6648	
  6649	"Very glad to see you. I dine at seven. There is a woodcock, I
  6650	believe. By the way, in view of recent occurrences, perhaps I
  6651	ought to ask Mrs. Hudson to examine its crop."
  6652	
  6653	I had been delayed at a case, and it was a little after half-past
  6654	six when I found myself in Baker Street once more. As I
  6655	approached the house I saw a tall man in a Scotch bonnet with a
  6656	coat which was buttoned up to his chin waiting outside in the
  6657	bright semicircle which was thrown from the fanlight. Just as I
  6658	arrived the door was opened, and we were shown up together to
  6659	Holmes' room.
  6660	
  6661	"Mr. Henry Baker, I believe," said he, rising from his armchair
  6662	and greeting his visitor with the easy air of geniality which he
  6663	could so readily assume. "Pray take this chair by the fire, Mr.
  6664	Baker. It is a cold night, and I observe that your circulation is
  6665	more adapted for summer than for winter. Ah, Watson, you have
  6666	just come at the right time. Is that your hat, Mr. Baker?"
  6667	
  6668	"Yes, sir, that is undoubtedly my hat."
  6669	
  6670	He was a large man with rounded shoulders, a massive head, and a
  6671	broad, intelligent face, sloping down to a pointed beard of
  6672	grizzled brown. A touch of red in nose and cheeks, with a slight
  6673	tremor of his extended hand, recalled Holmes' surmise as to his
  6674	habits. His rusty black frock-coat was buttoned right up in
  6675	front, with the collar turned up, and his lank wrists protruded
  6676	from his sleeves without a sign of cuff or shirt. He spoke in a
  6677	slow staccato fashion, choosing his words with care, and gave the
  6678	impression generally of a man of learning and letters who had had
  6679	ill-usage at the hands of fortune.
  6680	
  6681	"We have retained these things for some days," said Holmes,
  6682	"because we expected to see an advertisement from you giving your
  6683	address. I am at a loss to know now why you did not advertise."
  6684	
  6685	Our visitor gave a rather shamefaced laugh. "Shillings have not
  6686	been so plentiful with me as they once were," he remarked. "I had
  6687	no doubt that the gang of roughs who assaulted me had carried off
  6688	both my hat and the bird. I did not care to spend more money in a
  6689	hopeless attempt at recovering them."
  6690	
  6691	"Very naturally. By the way, about the bird, we were compelled to
  6692	eat it."
  6693	
  6694	"To eat it!" Our visitor half rose from his chair in his
  6695	excitement.
  6696	
  6697	"Yes, it would have been of no use to anyone had we not done so.
  6698	But I presume that this other goose upon the sideboard, which is
  6699	about the same weight and perfectly fresh, will answer your
  6700	purpose equally well?"
  6701	
  6702	"Oh, certainly, certainly," answered Mr. Baker with a sigh of
  6703	relief.
  6704	
  6705	"Of course, we still have the feathers, legs, crop, and so on of
  6706	your own bird, so if you wish--"
  6707	
  6708	The man burst into a hearty laugh. "They might be useful to me as
  6709	relics of my adventure," said he, "but beyond that I can hardly
  6710	see what use the disjecta membra of my late acquaintance are
  6711	going to be to me. No, sir, I think that, with your permission, I
  6712	will confine my attentions to the excellent bird which I perceive
  6713	upon the sideboard."
  6714	
  6715	Sherlock Holmes glanced sharply across at me with a slight shrug
  6716	of his shoulders.
  6717	
  6718	"There is your hat, then, and there your bird," said he. "By the
  6719	way, would it bore you to tell me where you got the other one
  6720	from? I am somewhat of a fowl fancier, and I have seldom seen a
  6721	better grown goose."
  6722	
  6723	"Certainly, sir," said Baker, who had risen and tucked his newly
  6724	gained property under his arm. "There are a few of us who
  6725	frequent the Alpha Inn, near the Museum--we are to be found in
  6726	the Museum itself during the day, you understand. This year our
  6727	good host, Windigate by name, instituted a goose club, by which,
  6728	on consideration of some few pence every week, we were each to
  6729	receive a bird at Christmas. My pence were duly paid, and the
  6730	rest is familiar to you. I am much indebted to you, sir, for a
  6731	Scotch bonnet is fitted neither to my years nor my gravity." With
  6732	a comical pomposity of manner he bowed solemnly to both of us and
  6733	strode off upon his way.
  6734	
  6735	"So much for Mr. Henry Baker," said Holmes when he had closed the
  6736	door behind him. "It is quite certain that he knows nothing
  6737	whatever about the matter. Are you hungry, Watson?"
  6738	
  6739	"Not particularly."
  6740	
  6741	"Then I suggest that we turn our dinner into a supper and follow
  6742	up this clue while it is still hot."
  6743	
  6744	"By all means."
  6745	
  6746	It was a bitter night, so we drew on our ulsters and wrapped
  6747	cravats about our throats. Outside, the stars were shining coldly
  6748	in a cloudless sky, and the breath of the passers-by blew out
  6749	into smoke like so many pistol shots. Our footfalls rang out
  6750	crisply and loudly as we swung through the doctors' quarter,
  6751	Wimpole Street, Harley Street, and so through Wigmore Street into
  6752	Oxford Street. In a quarter of an hour we were in Bloomsbury at
  6753	the Alpha Inn, which is a small public-house at the corner of one
  6754	of the streets which runs down into Holborn. Holmes pushed open
  6755	the door of the private bar and ordered two glasses of beer from
  6756	the ruddy-faced, white-aproned landlord.
  6757	
  6758	"Your beer should be excellent if it is as good as your geese,"
  6759	said he.
  6760	
  6761	"My geese!" The man seemed surprised.
  6762	
  6763	"Yes. I was speaking only half an hour ago to Mr. Henry Baker,
  6764	who was a member of your goose club."
  6765	
  6766	"Ah! yes, I see. But you see, sir, them's not our geese."
  6767	
  6768	"Indeed! Whose, then?"
  6769	
  6770	"Well, I got the two dozen from a salesman in Covent Garden."
  6771	
  6772	"Indeed? I know some of them. Which was it?"
  6773	
  6774	"Breckinridge is his name."
  6775	
  6776	"Ah! I don't know him. Well, here's your good health landlord,
  6777	and prosperity to your house. Good-night."
  6778	
  6779	"Now for Mr. Breckinridge," he continued, buttoning up his coat
  6780	as we came out into the frosty air. "Remember, Watson that though
  6781	we have so homely a thing as a goose at one end of this chain, we
  6782	have at the other a man who will certainly get seven years' penal
  6783	servitude unless we can establish his innocence. It is possible
  6784	that our inquiry may but confirm his guilt; but, in any case, we
  6785	have a line of investigation which has been missed by the police,
  6786	and which a singular chance has placed in our hands. Let us
  6787	follow it out to the bitter end. Faces to the south, then, and
  6788	quick march!"
  6789	
  6790	We passed across Holborn, down Endell Street, and so through a
  6791	zigzag of slums to Covent Garden Market. One of the largest
  6792	stalls bore the name of Breckinridge upon it, and the proprietor
  6793	a horsey-looking man, with a sharp face and trim side-whiskers was
  6794	helping a boy to put up the shutters.
  6795	
  6796	"Good-evening. It's a cold night," said Holmes.
  6797	
  6798	The salesman nodded and shot a questioning glance at my
  6799	companion.
  6800	
  6801	"Sold out of geese, I see," continued Holmes, pointing at the
  6802	bare slabs of marble.
  6803	
  6804	"Let you have five hundred to-morrow morning."
  6805	
  6806	"That's no good."
  6807	
  6808	"Well, there are some on the stall with the gas-flare."
  6809	
  6810	"Ah, but I was recommended to you."
  6811	
  6812	"Who by?"
  6813	
  6814	"The landlord of the Alpha."
  6815	
  6816	"Oh, yes; I sent him a couple of dozen."
  6817	
  6818	"Fine birds they were, too. Now where did you get them from?"
  6819	
  6820	To my surprise the question provoked a burst of anger from the
  6821	salesman.
  6822	
  6823	"Now, then, mister," said he, with his head cocked and his arms
  6824	akimbo, "what are you driving at? Let's have it straight, now."
  6825	
  6826	"It is straight enough. I should like to know who sold you the
  6827	geese which you supplied to the Alpha."
  6828	
  6829	"Well then, I shan't tell you. So now!"
  6830	
  6831	"Oh, it is a matter of no importance; but I don't know why you
  6832	should be so warm over such a trifle."
  6833	
  6834	"Warm! You'd be as warm, maybe, if you were as pestered as I am.
  6835	When I pay good money for a good article there should be an end
  6836	of the business; but it's 'Where are the geese?' and 'Who did you
  6837	sell the geese to?' and 'What will you take for the geese?' One
  6838	would think they were the only geese in the world, to hear the
  6839	fuss that is made over them."
  6840	
  6841	"Well, I have no connection with any other people who have been
  6842	making inquiries," said Holmes carelessly. "If you won't tell us
  6843	the bet is off, that is all. But I'm always ready to back my
  6844	opinion on a matter of fowls, and I have a fiver on it that the
  6845	bird I ate is country bred."
  6846	
  6847	"Well, then, you've lost your fiver, for it's town bred," snapped
  6848	the salesman.
  6849	
  6850	"It's nothing of the kind."
  6851	
  6852	"I say it is."
  6853	
  6854	"I don't believe it."
  6855	
  6856	"D'you think you know more about fowls than I, who have handled
  6857	them ever since I was a nipper? I tell you, all those birds that
  6858	went to the Alpha were town bred."
  6859	
  6860	"You'll never persuade me to believe that."
  6861	
  6862	"Will you bet, then?"
  6863	
  6864	"It's merely taking your money, for I know that I am right. But
  6865	I'll have a sovereign on with you, just to teach you not to be
  6866	obstinate."
  6867	
  6868	The salesman chuckled grimly. "Bring me the books, Bill," said
  6869	he.
  6870	
  6871	The small boy brought round a small thin volume and a great
  6872	greasy-backed one, laying them out together beneath the hanging
  6873	lamp.
  6874	
  6875	"Now then, Mr. Cocksure," said the salesman, "I thought that I
  6876	was out of geese, but before I finish you'll find that there is
  6877	still one left in my shop. You see this little book?"
  6878	
  6879	"Well?"
  6880	
  6881	"That's the list of the folk from whom I buy. D'you see? Well,
  6882	then, here on this page are the country folk, and the numbers
  6883	after their names are where their accounts are in the big ledger.
  6884	Now, then! You see this other page in red ink? Well, that is a
  6885	list of my town suppliers. Now, look at that third name. Just
  6886	read it out to me."
  6887	
  6888	"Mrs. Oakshott, 117, Brixton Road--249," read Holmes.
  6889	
  6890	"Quite so. Now turn that up in the ledger."
  6891	
  6892	Holmes turned to the page indicated. "Here you are, 'Mrs.
  6893	Oakshott, 117, Brixton Road, egg and poultry supplier.'"
  6894	
  6895	"Now, then, what's the last entry?"
  6896	
  6897	"'December 22nd. Twenty-four geese at 7s. 6d.'"
  6898	
  6899	"Quite so. There you are. And underneath?"
  6900	
  6901	"'Sold to Mr. Windigate of the Alpha, at 12s.'"
  6902	
  6903	"What have you to say now?"
  6904	
  6905	Sherlock Holmes looked deeply chagrined. He drew a sovereign from
  6906	his pocket and threw it down upon the slab, turning away with the
  6907	air of a man whose disgust is too deep for words. A few yards off
  6908	he stopped under a lamp-post and laughed in the hearty, noiseless
  6909	fashion which was peculiar to him.
  6910	
  6911	"When you see a man with whiskers of that cut and the 'Pink 'un'
  6912	protruding out of his pocket, you can always draw him by a bet,"
  6913	said he. "I daresay that if I had put 100 pounds down in front of
  6914	him, that man would not have given me such complete information
  6915	as was drawn from him by the idea that he was doing me on a
  6916	wager. Well, Watson, we are, I fancy, nearing the end of our
  6917	quest, and the only point which remains to be determined is
  6918	whether we should go on to this Mrs. Oakshott to-night, or
  6919	whether we should reserve it for to-morrow. It is clear from what
  6920	that surly fellow said that there are others besides ourselves
  6921	who are anxious about the matter, and I should--"
  6922	
  6923	His remarks were suddenly cut short by a loud hubbub which broke
  6924	out from the stall which we had just left. Turning round we saw a
  6925	little rat-faced fellow standing in the centre of the circle of
  6926	yellow light which was thrown by the swinging lamp, while
  6927	Breckinridge, the salesman, framed in the door of his stall, was
  6928	shaking his fists fiercely at the cringing figure.
  6929	
  6930	"I've had enough of you and your geese," he shouted. "I wish you
  6931	were all at the devil together. If you come pestering me any more
  6932	with your silly talk I'll set the dog at you. You bring Mrs.
  6933	Oakshott here and I'll answer her, but what have you to do with
  6934	it? Did I buy the geese off you?"
  6935	
  6936	"No; but one of them was mine all the same," whined the little
  6937	man.
  6938	
  6939	"Well, then, ask Mrs. Oakshott for it."
  6940	
  6941	"She told me to ask you."
  6942	
  6943	"Well, you can ask the King of Proosia, for all I care. I've had
  6944	enough of it. Get out of this!" He rushed fiercely forward, and
  6945	the inquirer flitted away into the darkness.
  6946	
  6947	"Ha! this may save us a visit to Brixton Road," whispered Holmes.
  6948	"Come with me, and we will see what is to be made of this
  6949	fellow." Striding through the scattered knots of people who
  6950	lounged round the flaring stalls, my companion speedily overtook
  6951	the little man and touched him upon the shoulder. He sprang
  6952	round, and I could see in the gas-light that every vestige of
  6953	colour had been driven from his face.
  6954	
  6955	"Who are you, then? What do you want?" he asked in a quavering
  6956	voice.
  6957	
  6958	"You will excuse me," said Holmes blandly, "but I could not help
  6959	overhearing the questions which you put to the salesman just now.
  6960	I think that I could be of assistance to you."
  6961	
  6962	"You? Who are you? How could you know anything of the matter?"
  6963	
  6964	"My name is Sherlock Holmes. It is my business to know what other
  6965	people don't know."
  6966	
  6967	"But you can know nothing of this?"
  6968	
  6969	"Excuse me, I know everything of it. You are endeavouring to
  6970	trace some geese which were sold by Mrs. Oakshott, of Brixton
  6971	Road, to a salesman named Breckinridge, by him in turn to Mr.
  6972	Windigate, of the Alpha, and by him to his club, of which Mr.
  6973	Henry Baker is a member."
  6974	
  6975	"Oh, sir, you are the very man whom I have longed to meet," cried
  6976	the little fellow with outstretched hands and quivering fingers.
  6977	"I can hardly explain to you how interested I am in this matter."
  6978	
  6979	Sherlock Holmes hailed a four-wheeler which was passing. "In that
  6980	case we had better discuss it in a cosy room rather than in this
  6981	wind-swept market-place," said he. "But pray tell me, before we
  6982	go farther, who it is that I have the pleasure of assisting."
  6983	
  6984	The man hesitated for an instant. "My name is John Robinson," he
  6985	answered with a sidelong glance.
  6986	
  6987	"No, no; the real name," said Holmes sweetly. "It is always
  6988	awkward doing business with an alias."
  6989	
  6990	A flush sprang to the white cheeks of the stranger. "Well then,"
  6991	said he, "my real name is James Ryder."
  6992	
  6993	"Precisely so. Head attendant at the Hotel Cosmopolitan. Pray
  6994	step into the cab, and I shall soon be able to tell you
  6995	everything which you would wish to know."
  6996	
  6997	The little man stood glancing from one to the other of us with
  6998	half-frightened, half-hopeful eyes, as one who is not sure
  6999	whether he is on the verge of a windfall or of a catastrophe.
  7000	Then he stepped into the cab, and in half an hour we were back in
  7001	the sitting-room at Baker Street. Nothing had been said during
  7002	our drive, but the high, thin breathing of our new companion, and
  7003	the claspings and unclaspings of his hands, spoke of the nervous
  7004	tension within him.
  7005	
  7006	"Here we are!" said Holmes cheerily as we filed into the room.
  7007	"The fire looks very seasonable in this weather. You look cold,
  7008	Mr. Ryder. Pray take the basket-chair. I will just put on my
  7009	slippers before we settle this little matter of yours. Now, then!
  7010	You want to know what became of those geese?"
  7011	
  7012	"Yes, sir."
  7013	
  7014	"Or rather, I fancy, of that goose. It was one bird, I imagine in
  7015	which you were interested--white, with a black bar across the
  7016	tail."
  7017	
  7018	Ryder quivered with emotion. "Oh, sir," he cried, "can you tell
  7019	me where it went to?"
  7020	
  7021	"It came here."
  7022	
  7023	"Here?"
  7024	
  7025	"Yes, and a most remarkable bird it proved. I don't wonder that
  7026	you should take an interest in it. It laid an egg after it was
  7027	dead--the bonniest, brightest little blue egg that ever was seen.
  7028	I have it here in my museum."
  7029	
  7030	Our visitor staggered to his feet and clutched the mantelpiece
  7031	with his right hand. Holmes unlocked his strong-box and held up
  7032	the blue carbuncle, which shone out like a star, with a cold,
  7033	brilliant, many-pointed radiance. Ryder stood glaring with a
  7034	drawn face, uncertain whether to claim or to disown it.
  7035	
  7036	"The game's up, Ryder," said Holmes quietly. "Hold up, man, or
  7037	you'll be into the fire! Give him an arm back into his chair,
  7038	Watson. He's not got blood enough to go in for felony with
  7039	impunity. Give him a dash of brandy. So! Now he looks a little
  7040	more human. What a shrimp it is, to be sure!"
  7041	
  7042	For a moment he had staggered and nearly fallen, but the brandy
  7043	brought a tinge of colour into his cheeks, and he sat staring
  7044	with frightened eyes at his accuser.
  7045	
  7046	"I have almost every link in my hands, and all the proofs which I
  7047	could possibly need, so there is little which you need tell me.
  7048	Still, that little may as well be cleared up to make the case
  7049	complete. You had heard, Ryder, of this blue stone of the
  7050	Countess of Morcar's?"
  7051	
  7052	"It was Catherine Cusack who told me of it," said he in a
  7053	crackling voice.
  7054	
  7055	"I see--her ladyship's waiting-maid. Well, the temptation of
  7056	sudden wealth so easily acquired was too much for you, as it has
  7057	been for better men before you; but you were not very scrupulous
  7058	in the means you used. It seems to me, Ryder, that there is the
  7059	making of a very pretty villain in you. You knew that this man
  7060	Horner, the plumber, had been concerned in some such matter
  7061	before, and that suspicion would rest the more readily upon him.
  7062	What did you do, then? You made some small job in my lady's
  7063	room--you and your confederate Cusack--and you managed that he
  7064	should be the man sent for. Then, when he had left, you rifled
  7065	the jewel-case, raised the alarm, and had this unfortunate man
  7066	arrested. You then--"
  7067	
  7068	Ryder threw himself down suddenly upon the rug and clutched at my
  7069	companion's knees. "For God's sake, have mercy!" he shrieked.
  7070	"Think of my father! Of my mother! It would break their hearts. I
  7071	never went wrong before! I never will again. I swear it. I'll
  7072	swear it on a Bible. Oh, don't bring it into court! For Christ's
  7073	sake, don't!"
  7074	
  7075	"Get back into your chair!" said Holmes sternly. "It is very well
  7076	to cringe and crawl now, but you thought little enough of this
  7077	poor Horner in the dock for a crime of which he knew nothing."
  7078	
  7079	"I will fly, Mr. Holmes. I will leave the country, sir. Then the
  7080	charge against him will break down."
  7081	
  7082	"Hum! We will talk about that. And now let us hear a true account
  7083	of the next act. How came the stone into the goose, and how came
  7084	the goose into the open market? Tell us the truth, for there lies
  7085	your only hope of safety."
  7086	
  7087	Ryder passed his tongue over his parched lips. "I will tell you
  7088	it just as it happened, sir," said he. "When Horner had been
  7089	arrested, it seemed to me that it would be best for me to get
  7090	away with the stone at once, for I did not know at what moment
  7091	the police might not take it into their heads to search me and my
  7092	room. There was no place about the hotel where it would be safe.
  7093	I went out, as if on some commission, and I made for my sister's
  7094	house. She had married a man named Oakshott, and lived in Brixton
  7095	Road, where she fattened fowls for the market. All the way there
  7096	every man I met seemed to me to be a policeman or a detective;
  7097	and, for all that it was a cold night, the sweat was pouring down
  7098	my face before I came to the Brixton Road. My sister asked me
  7099	what was the matter, and why I was so pale; but I told her that I
  7100	had been upset by the jewel robbery at the hotel. Then I went
  7101	into the back yard and smoked a pipe and wondered what it would
  7102	be best to do.
  7103	
  7104	"I had a friend once called Maudsley, who went to the bad, and
  7105	has just been serving his time in Pentonville. One day he had met
  7106	me, and fell into talk about the ways of thieves, and how they
  7107	could get rid of what they stole. I knew that he would be true to
  7108	me, for I knew one or two things about him; so I made up my mind
  7109	to go right on to Kilburn, where he lived, and take him into my
  7110	confidence. He would show me how to turn the stone into money.
  7111	But how to get to him in safety? I thought of the agonies I had
  7112	gone through in coming from the hotel. I might at any moment be
  7113	seized and searched, and there would be the stone in my waistcoat
  7114	pocket. I was leaning against the wall at the time and looking at
  7115	the geese which were waddling about round my feet, and suddenly
  7116	an idea came into my head which showed me how I could beat the
  7117	best detective that ever lived.
  7118	
  7119	"My sister had told me some weeks before that I might have the
  7120	pick of her geese for a Christmas present, and I knew that she
  7121	was always as good as her word. I would take my goose now, and in
  7122	it I would carry my stone to Kilburn. There was a little shed in
  7123	the yard, and behind this I drove one of the birds--a fine big
  7124	one, white, with a barred tail. I caught it, and prying its bill
  7125	open, I thrust the stone down its throat as far as my finger
  7126	could reach. The bird gave a gulp, and I felt the stone pass
  7127	along its gullet and down into its crop. But the creature flapped
  7128	and struggled, and out came my sister to know what was the
  7129	matter. As I turned to speak to her the brute broke loose and
  7130	fluttered off among the others.
  7131	
  7132	"'Whatever were you doing with that bird, Jem?' says she.
  7133	
  7134	"'Well,' said I, 'you said you'd give me one for Christmas, and I
  7135	was feeling which was the fattest.'
  7136	
  7137	"'Oh,' says she, 'we've set yours aside for you--Jem's bird, we
  7138	call it. It's the big white one over yonder. There's twenty-six
  7139	of them, which makes one for you, and one for us, and two dozen
  7140	for the market.'
  7141	
  7142	"'Thank you, Maggie,' says I; 'but if it is all the same to you,
  7143	I'd rather have that one I was handling just now.'
  7144	
  7145	"'The other is a good three pound heavier,' said she, 'and we
  7146	fattened it expressly for you.'
  7147	
  7148	"'Never mind. I'll have the other, and I'll take it now,' said I.
  7149	
  7150	"'Oh, just as you like,' said she, a little huffed. 'Which is it
  7151	you want, then?'
  7152	
  7153	"'That white one with the barred tail, right in the middle of the
  7154	flock.'
  7155	
  7156	"'Oh, very well. Kill it and take it with you.'
  7157	
  7158	"Well, I did what she said, Mr. Holmes, and I carried the bird
  7159	all the way to Kilburn. I told my pal what I had done, for he was
  7160	a man that it was easy to tell a thing like that to. He laughed
  7161	until he choked, and we got a knife and opened the goose. My
  7162	heart turned to water, for there was no sign of the stone, and I
  7163	knew that some terrible mistake had occurred. I left the bird,
  7164	rushed back to my sister's, and hurried into the back yard. There
  7165	was not a bird to be seen there.
  7166	
  7167	"'Where are they all, Maggie?' I cried.
  7168	
  7169	"'Gone to the dealer's, Jem.'
  7170	
  7171	"'Which dealer's?'
  7172	
  7173	"'Breckinridge, of Covent Garden.'
  7174	
  7175	"'But was there another with a barred tail?' I asked, 'the same
  7176	as the one I chose?'
  7177	
  7178	"'Yes, Jem; there were two barred-tailed ones, and I could never
  7179	tell them apart.'
  7180	
  7181	"Well, then, of course I saw it all, and I ran off as hard as my
  7182	feet would carry me to this man Breckinridge; but he had sold the
  7183	lot at once, and not one word would he tell me as to where they
  7184	had gone. You heard him yourselves to-night. Well, he has always
  7185	answered me like that. My sister thinks that I am going mad.
  7186	Sometimes I think that I am myself. And now--and now I am myself
  7187	a branded thief, without ever having touched the wealth for which
  7188	I sold my character. God help me! God help me!" He burst into
  7189	convulsive sobbing, with his face buried in his hands.
  7190	
  7191	There was a long silence, broken only by his heavy breathing and
  7192	by the measured tapping of Sherlock Holmes' finger-tips upon the
  7193	edge of the table. Then my friend rose and threw open the door.
  7194	
  7195	"Get out!" said he.
  7196	
  7197	"What, sir! Oh, Heaven bless you!"
  7198	
  7199	"No more words. Get out!"
  7200	
  7201	And no more words were needed. There was a rush, a clatter upon
  7202	the stairs, the bang of a door, and the crisp rattle of running
  7203	footfalls from the street.
  7204	
  7205	"After all, Watson," said Holmes, reaching up his hand for his
  7206	clay pipe, "I am not retained by the police to supply their
  7207	deficiencies. If Horner were in danger it would be another thing;
  7208	but this fellow will not appear against him, and the case must
  7209	collapse. I suppose that I am commuting a felony, but it is just
  7210	possible that I am saving a soul. This fellow will not go wrong
  7211	again; he is too terribly frightened. Send him to gaol now, and
  7212	you make him a gaol-bird for life. Besides, it is the season of
  7213	forgiveness. Chance has put in our way a most singular and
  7214	whimsical problem, and its solution is its own reward. If you
  7215	will have the goodness to touch the bell, Doctor, we will begin
  7216	another investigation, in which, also a bird will be the chief
  7217	feature."
  7218	
  7219	
  7220	
  7221	VIII. THE ADVENTURE OF THE SPECKLED BAND
  7222	
  7223	On glancing over my notes of the seventy odd cases in which I
  7224	have during the last eight years studied the methods of my friend
  7225	Sherlock Holmes, I find many tragic, some comic, a large number
  7226	merely strange, but none commonplace; for, working as he did
  7227	rather for the love of his art than for the acquirement of
  7228	wealth, he refused to associate himself with any investigation
  7229	which did not tend towards the unusual, and even the fantastic.
  7230	Of all these varied cases, however, I cannot recall any which
  7231	presented more singular features than that which was associated
  7232	with the well-known Surrey family of the Roylotts of Stoke Moran.
  7233	The events in question occurred in the early days of my
  7234	association with Holmes, when we were sharing rooms as bachelors
  7235	in Baker Street. It is possible that I might have placed them
  7236	upon record before, but a promise of secrecy was made at the
  7237	time, from which I have only been freed during the last month by
  7238	the untimely death of the lady to whom the pledge was given. It
  7239	is perhaps as well that the facts should now come to light, for I
  7240	have reasons to know that there are widespread rumours as to the
  7241	death of Dr. Grimesby Roylott which tend to make the matter even
  7242	more terrible than the truth.
  7243	
  7244	It was early in April in the year '83 that I woke one morning to
  7245	find Sherlock Holmes standing, fully dressed, by the side of my
  7246	bed. He was a late riser, as a rule, and as the clock on the
  7247	mantelpiece showed me that it was only a quarter-past seven, I
  7248	blinked up at him in some surprise, and perhaps just a little
  7249	resentment, for I was myself regular in my habits.
  7250	
  7251	"Very sorry to knock you up, Watson," said he, "but it's the
  7252	common lot this morning. Mrs. Hudson has been knocked up, she
  7253	retorted upon me, and I on you."
  7254	
  7255	"What is it, then--a fire?"
  7256	
  7257	"No; a client. It seems that a young lady has arrived in a
  7258	considerable state of excitement, who insists upon seeing me. She
  7259	is waiting now in the sitting-room. Now, when young ladies wander
  7260	about the metropolis at this hour of the morning, and knock
  7261	sleepy people up out of their beds, I presume that it is
  7262	something very pressing which they have to communicate. Should it
  7263	prove to be an interesting case, you would, I am sure, wish to
  7264	follow it from the outset. I thought, at any rate, that I should
  7265	call you and give you the chance."
  7266	
  7267	"My dear fellow, I would not miss it for anything."
  7268	
  7269	I had no keener pleasure than in following Holmes in his
  7270	professional investigations, and in admiring the rapid
  7271	deductions, as swift as intuitions, and yet always founded on a
  7272	logical basis with which he unravelled the problems which were
  7273	submitted to him. I rapidly threw on my clothes and was ready in
  7274	a few minutes to accompany my friend down to the sitting-room. A
  7275	lady dressed in black and heavily veiled, who had been sitting in
  7276	the window, rose as we entered.
  7277	
  7278	"Good-morning, madam," said Holmes cheerily. "My name is Sherlock
  7279	Holmes. This is my intimate friend and associate, Dr. Watson,
  7280	before whom you can speak as freely as before myself. Ha! I am
  7281	glad to see that Mrs. Hudson has had the good sense to light the
  7282	fire. Pray draw up to it, and I shall order you a cup of hot
  7283	coffee, for I observe that you are shivering."
  7284	
  7285	"It is not cold which makes me shiver," said the woman in a low
  7286	voice, changing her seat as requested.
  7287	
  7288	"What, then?"
  7289	
  7290	"It is fear, Mr. Holmes. It is terror." She raised her veil as
  7291	she spoke, and we could see that she was indeed in a pitiable
  7292	state of agitation, her face all drawn and grey, with restless
  7293	frightened eyes, like those of some hunted animal. Her features
  7294	and figure were those of a woman of thirty, but her hair was shot
  7295	with premature grey, and her expression was weary and haggard.
  7296	Sherlock Holmes ran her over with one of his quick,
  7297	all-comprehensive glances.
  7298	
  7299	"You must not fear," said he soothingly, bending forward and
  7300	patting her forearm. "We shall soon set matters right, I have no
  7301	doubt. You have come in by train this morning, I see."
  7302	
  7303	"You know me, then?"
  7304	
  7305	"No, but I observe the second half of a return ticket in the palm
  7306	of your left glove. You must have started early, and yet you had
  7307	a good drive in a dog-cart, along heavy roads, before you reached
  7308	the station."
  7309	
  7310	The lady gave a violent start and stared in bewilderment at my
  7311	companion.
  7312	
  7313	"There is no mystery, my dear madam," said he, smiling. "The left
  7314	arm of your jacket is spattered with mud in no less than seven
  7315	places. The marks are perfectly fresh. There is no vehicle save a
  7316	dog-cart which throws up mud in that way, and then only when you
  7317	sit on the left-hand side of the driver."
  7318	
  7319	"Whatever your reasons may be, you are perfectly correct," said
  7320	she. "I started from home before six, reached Leatherhead at
  7321	twenty past, and came in by the first train to Waterloo. Sir, I
  7322	can stand this strain no longer; I shall go mad if it continues.
  7323	I have no one to turn to--none, save only one, who cares for me,
  7324	and he, poor fellow, can be of little aid. I have heard of you,
  7325	Mr. Holmes; I have heard of you from Mrs. Farintosh, whom you
  7326	helped in the hour of her sore need. It was from her that I had
  7327	your address. Oh, sir, do you not think that you could help me,
  7328	too, and at least throw a little light through the dense darkness
  7329	which surrounds me? At present it is out of my power to reward
  7330	you for your services, but in a month or six weeks I shall be
  7331	married, with the control of my own income, and then at least you
  7332	shall not find me ungrateful."
  7333	
  7334	Holmes turned to his desk and, unlocking it, drew out a small
  7335	case-book, which he consulted.
  7336	
  7337	"Farintosh," said he. "Ah yes, I recall the case; it was
  7338	concerned with an opal tiara. I think it was before your time,
  7339	Watson. I can only say, madam, that I shall be happy to devote
  7340	the same care to your case as I did to that of your friend. As to
  7341	reward, my profession is its own reward; but you are at liberty
  7342	to defray whatever expenses I may be put to, at the time which
  7343	suits you best. And now I beg that you will lay before us
  7344	everything that may help us in forming an opinion upon the
  7345	matter."
  7346	
  7347	"Alas!" replied our visitor, "the very horror of my situation
  7348	lies in the fact that my fears are so vague, and my suspicions
  7349	depend so entirely upon small points, which might seem trivial to
  7350	another, that even he to whom of all others I have a right to
  7351	look for help and advice looks upon all that I tell him about it
  7352	as the fancies of a nervous woman. He does not say so, but I can
  7353	read it from his soothing answers and averted eyes. But I have
  7354	heard, Mr. Holmes, that you can see deeply into the manifold
  7355	wickedness of the human heart. You may advise me how to walk amid
  7356	the dangers which encompass me."
  7357	
  7358	"I am all attention, madam."
  7359	
  7360	"My name is Helen Stoner, and I am living with my stepfather, who
  7361	is the last survivor of one of the oldest Saxon families in
  7362	England, the Roylotts of Stoke Moran, on the western border of
  7363	Surrey."
  7364	
  7365	Holmes nodded his head. "The name is familiar to me," said he.
  7366	
  7367	"The family was at one time among the richest in England, and the
  7368	estates extended over the borders into Berkshire in the north,
  7369	and Hampshire in the west. In the last century, however, four
  7370	successive heirs were of a dissolute and wasteful disposition,
  7371	and the family ruin was eventually completed by a gambler in the
  7372	days of the Regency. Nothing was left save a few acres of ground,
  7373	and the two-hundred-year-old house, which is itself crushed under
  7374	a heavy mortgage. The last squire dragged out his existence
  7375	there, living the horrible life of an aristocratic pauper; but
  7376	his only son, my stepfather, seeing that he must adapt himself to
  7377	the new conditions, obtained an advance from a relative, which
  7378	enabled him to take a medical degree and went out to Calcutta,
  7379	where, by his professional skill and his force of character, he
  7380	established a large practice. In a fit of anger, however, caused
  7381	by some robberies which had been perpetrated in the house, he
  7382	beat his native butler to death and narrowly escaped a capital
  7383	sentence. As it was, he suffered a long term of imprisonment and
  7384	afterwards returned to England a morose and disappointed man.
  7385	
  7386	"When Dr. Roylott was in India he married my mother, Mrs. Stoner,
  7387	the young widow of Major-General Stoner, of the Bengal Artillery.
  7388	My sister Julia and I were twins, and we were only two years old
  7389	at the time of my mother's re-marriage. She had a considerable
  7390	sum of money--not less than 1000 pounds a year--and this she
  7391	bequeathed to Dr. Roylott entirely while we resided with him,
  7392	with a provision that a certain annual sum should be allowed to
  7393	each of us in the event of our marriage. Shortly after our return
  7394	to England my mother died--she was killed eight years ago in a
  7395	railway accident near Crewe. Dr. Roylott then abandoned his
  7396	attempts to establish himself in practice in London and took us
  7397	to live with him in the old ancestral house at Stoke Moran. The
  7398	money which my mother had left was enough for all our wants, and
  7399	there seemed to be no obstacle to our happiness.
  7400	
  7401	"But a terrible change came over our stepfather about this time.
  7402	Instead of making friends and exchanging visits with our
  7403	neighbours, who had at first been overjoyed to see a Roylott of
  7404	Stoke Moran back in the old family seat, he shut himself up in
  7405	his house and seldom came out save to indulge in ferocious
  7406	quarrels with whoever might cross his path. Violence of temper
  7407	approaching to mania has been hereditary in the men of the
  7408	family, and in my stepfather's case it had, I believe, been
  7409	intensified by his long residence in the tropics. A series of
  7410	disgraceful brawls took place, two of which ended in the
  7411	police-court, until at last he became the terror of the village,
  7412	and the folks would fly at his approach, for he is a man of
  7413	immense strength, and absolutely uncontrollable in his anger.
  7414	
  7415	"Last week he hurled the local blacksmith over a parapet into a
  7416	stream, and it was only by paying over all the money which I
  7417	could gather together that I was able to avert another public
  7418	exposure. He had no friends at all save the wandering gipsies,
  7419	and he would give these vagabonds leave to encamp upon the few
  7420	acres of bramble-covered land which represent the family estate,
  7421	and would accept in return the hospitality of their tents,
  7422	wandering away with them sometimes for weeks on end. He has a
  7423	passion also for Indian animals, which are sent over to him by a
  7424	correspondent, and he has at this moment a cheetah and a baboon,
  7425	which wander freely over his grounds and are feared by the
  7426	villagers almost as much as their master.
  7427	
  7428	"You can imagine from what I say that my poor sister Julia and I
  7429	had no great pleasure in our lives. No servant would stay with
  7430	us, and for a long time we did all the work of the house. She was
  7431	but thirty at the time of her death, and yet her hair had already
  7432	begun to whiten, even as mine has."
  7433	
  7434	"Your sister is dead, then?"
  7435	
  7436	"She died just two years ago, and it is of her death that I wish
  7437	to speak to you. You can understand that, living the life which I
  7438	have described, we were little likely to see anyone of our own
  7439	age and position. We had, however, an aunt, my mother's maiden
  7440	sister, Miss Honoria Westphail, who lives near Harrow, and we
  7441	were occasionally allowed to pay short visits at this lady's
  7442	house. Julia went there at Christmas two years ago, and met there
  7443	a half-pay major of marines, to whom she became engaged. My
  7444	stepfather learned of the engagement when my sister returned and
  7445	offered no objection to the marriage; but within a fortnight of
  7446	the day which had been fixed for the wedding, the terrible event
  7447	occurred which has deprived me of my only companion."
  7448	
  7449	Sherlock Holmes had been leaning back in his chair with his eyes
  7450	closed and his head sunk in a cushion, but he half opened his
  7451	lids now and glanced across at his visitor.
  7452	
  7453	"Pray be precise as to details," said he.
  7454	
  7455	"It is easy for me to be so, for every event of that dreadful
  7456	time is seared into my memory. The manor-house is, as I have
  7457	already said, very old, and only one wing is now inhabited. The
  7458	bedrooms in this wing are on the ground floor, the sitting-rooms
  7459	being in the central block of the buildings. Of these bedrooms
  7460	the first is Dr. Roylott's, the second my sister's, and the third
  7461	my own. There is no communication between them, but they all open
  7462	out into the same corridor. Do I make myself plain?"
  7463	
  7464	"Perfectly so."
  7465	
  7466	"The windows of the three rooms open out upon the lawn. That
  7467	fatal night Dr. Roylott had gone to his room early, though we
  7468	knew that he had not retired to rest, for my sister was troubled
  7469	by the smell of the strong Indian cigars which it was his custom
  7470	to smoke. She left her room, therefore, and came into mine, where
  7471	she sat for some time, chatting about her approaching wedding. At
  7472	eleven o'clock she rose to leave me, but she paused at the door
  7473	and looked back.
  7474	
  7475	"'Tell me, Helen,' said she, 'have you ever heard anyone whistle
  7476	in the dead of the night?'
  7477	
  7478	"'Never,' said I.
  7479	
  7480	"'I suppose that you could not possibly whistle, yourself, in
  7481	your sleep?'
  7482	
  7483	"'Certainly not. But why?'
  7484	
  7485	"'Because during the last few nights I have always, about three
  7486	in the morning, heard a low, clear whistle. I am a light sleeper,
  7487	and it has awakened me. I cannot tell where it came from--perhaps
  7488	from the next room, perhaps from the lawn. I thought that I would
  7489	just ask you whether you had heard it.'
  7490	
  7491	"'No, I have not. It must be those wretched gipsies in the
  7492	plantation.'
  7493	
  7494	"'Very likely. And yet if it were on the lawn, I wonder that you
  7495	did not hear it also.'
  7496	
  7497	"'Ah, but I sleep more heavily than you.'
  7498	
  7499	"'Well, it is of no great consequence, at any rate.' She smiled
  7500	back at me, closed my door, and a few moments later I heard her
  7501	key turn in the lock."
  7502	
  7503	"Indeed," said Holmes. "Was it your custom always to lock
  7504	yourselves in at night?"
  7505	
  7506	"Always."
  7507	
  7508	"And why?"
  7509	
  7510	"I think that I mentioned to you that the doctor kept a cheetah
  7511	and a baboon. We had no feeling of security unless our doors were
  7512	locked."
  7513	
  7514	"Quite so. Pray proceed with your statement."
  7515	
  7516	"I could not sleep that night. A vague feeling of impending
  7517	misfortune impressed me. My sister and I, you will recollect,
  7518	were twins, and you know how subtle are the links which bind two
  7519	souls which are so closely allied. It was a wild night. The wind
  7520	was howling outside, and the rain was beating and splashing
  7521	against the windows. Suddenly, amid all the hubbub of the gale,
  7522	there burst forth the wild scream of a terrified woman. I knew
  7523	that it was my sister's voice. I sprang from my bed, wrapped a
  7524	shawl round me, and rushed into the corridor. As I opened my door
  7525	I seemed to hear a low whistle, such as my sister described, and
  7526	a few moments later a clanging sound, as if a mass of metal had
  7527	fallen. As I ran down the passage, my sister's door was unlocked,
  7528	and revolved slowly upon its hinges. I stared at it
  7529	horror-stricken, not knowing what was about to issue from it. By
  7530	the light of the corridor-lamp I saw my sister appear at the
  7531	opening, her face blanched with terror, her hands groping for
  7532	help, her whole figure swaying to and fro like that of a
  7533	drunkard. I ran to her and threw my arms round her, but at that
  7534	moment her knees seemed to give way and she fell to the ground.
  7535	She writhed as one who is in terrible pain, and her limbs were
  7536	dreadfully convulsed. At first I thought that she had not
  7537	recognised me, but as I bent over her she suddenly shrieked out
  7538	in a voice which I shall never forget, 'Oh, my God! Helen! It was
  7539	the band! The speckled band!' There was something else which she
  7540	would fain have said, and she stabbed with her finger into the
  7541	air in the direction of the doctor's room, but a fresh convulsion
  7542	seized her and choked her words. I rushed out, calling loudly for
  7543	my stepfather, and I met him hastening from his room in his
  7544	dressing-gown. When he reached my sister's side she was
  7545	unconscious, and though he poured brandy down her throat and sent
  7546	for medical aid from the village, all efforts were in vain, for
  7547	she slowly sank and died without having recovered her
  7548	consciousness. Such was the dreadful end of my beloved sister."
  7549	
  7550	"One moment," said Holmes, "are you sure about this whistle and
  7551	metallic sound? Could you swear to it?"
  7552	
  7553	"That was what the county coroner asked me at the inquiry. It is
  7554	my strong impression that I heard it, and yet, among the crash of
  7555	the gale and the creaking of an old house, I may possibly have
  7556	been deceived."
  7557	
  7558	"Was your sister dressed?"
  7559	
  7560	"No, she was in her night-dress. In her right hand was found the
  7561	charred stump of a match, and in her left a match-box."
  7562	
  7563	"Showing that she had struck a light and looked about her when
  7564	the alarm took place. That is important. And what conclusions did
  7565	the coroner come to?"
  7566	
  7567	"He investigated the case with great care, for Dr. Roylott's
  7568	conduct had long been notorious in the county, but he was unable
  7569	to find any satisfactory cause of death. My evidence showed that
  7570	the door had been fastened upon the inner side, and the windows
  7571	were blocked by old-fashioned shutters with broad iron bars,
  7572	which were secured every night. The walls were carefully sounded,
  7573	and were shown to be quite solid all round, and the flooring was
  7574	also thoroughly examined, with the same result. The chimney is
  7575	wide, but is barred up by four large staples. It is certain,
  7576	therefore, that my sister was quite alone when she met her end.
  7577	Besides, there were no marks of any violence upon her."
  7578	
  7579	"How about poison?"
  7580	
  7581	"The doctors examined her for it, but without success."
  7582	
  7583	"What do you think that this unfortunate lady died of, then?"
  7584	
  7585	"It is my belief that she died of pure fear and nervous shock,
  7586	though what it was that frightened her I cannot imagine."
  7587	
  7588	"Were there gipsies in the plantation at the time?"
  7589	
  7590	"Yes, there are nearly always some there."
  7591	
  7592	"Ah, and what did you gather from this allusion to a band--a
  7593	speckled band?"
  7594	
  7595	"Sometimes I have thought that it was merely the wild talk of
  7596	delirium, sometimes that it may have referred to some band of
  7597	people, perhaps to these very gipsies in the plantation. I do not
  7598	know whether the spotted handkerchiefs which so many of them wear
  7599	over their heads might have suggested the strange adjective which
  7600	she used."
  7601	
  7602	Holmes shook his head like a man who is far from being satisfied.
  7603	
  7604	"These are very deep waters," said he; "pray go on with your
  7605	narrative."
  7606	
  7607	"Two years have passed since then, and my life has been until
  7608	lately lonelier than ever. A month ago, however, a dear friend,
  7609	whom I have known for many years, has done me the honour to ask
  7610	my hand in marriage. His name is Armitage--Percy Armitage--the
  7611	second son of Mr. Armitage, of Crane Water, near Reading. My
  7612	stepfather has offered no opposition to the match, and we are to
  7613	be married in the course of the spring. Two days ago some repairs
  7614	were started in the west wing of the building, and my bedroom
  7615	wall has been pierced, so that I have had to move into the
  7616	chamber in which my sister died, and to sleep in the very bed in
  7617	which she slept. Imagine, then, my thrill of terror when last
  7618	night, as I lay awake, thinking over her terrible fate, I
  7619	suddenly heard in the silence of the night the low whistle which
  7620	had been the herald of her own death. I sprang up and lit the
  7621	lamp, but nothing was to be seen in the room. I was too shaken to
  7622	go to bed again, however, so I dressed, and as soon as it was
  7623	daylight I slipped down, got a dog-cart at the Crown Inn, which
  7624	is opposite, and drove to Leatherhead, from whence I have come on
  7625	this morning with the one object of seeing you and asking your
  7626	advice."
  7627	
  7628	"You have done wisely," said my friend. "But have you told me
  7629	all?"
  7630	
  7631	"Yes, all."
  7632	
  7633	"Miss Roylott, you have not. You are screening your stepfather."
  7634	
  7635	"Why, what do you mean?"
  7636	
  7637	For answer Holmes pushed back the frill of black lace which
  7638	fringed the hand that lay upon our visitor's knee. Five little
  7639	livid spots, the marks of four fingers and a thumb, were printed
  7640	upon the white wrist.
  7641	
  7642	"You have been cruelly used," said Holmes.
  7643	
  7644	The lady coloured deeply and covered over her injured wrist. "He
  7645	is a hard man," she said, "and perhaps he hardly knows his own
  7646	strength."
  7647	
  7648	There was a long silence, during which Holmes leaned his chin
  7649	upon his hands and stared into the crackling fire.
  7650	
  7651	"This is a very deep business," he said at last. "There are a
  7652	thousand details which I should desire to know before I decide
  7653	upon our course of action. Yet we have not a moment to lose. If
  7654	we were to come to Stoke Moran to-day, would it be possible for
  7655	us to see over these rooms without the knowledge of your
  7656	stepfather?"
  7657	
  7658	"As it happens, he spoke of coming into town to-day upon some
  7659	most important business. It is probable that he will be away all
  7660	day, and that there would be nothing to disturb you. We have a
  7661	housekeeper now, but she is old and foolish, and I could easily
  7662	get her out of the way."
  7663	
  7664	"Excellent. You are not averse to this trip, Watson?"
  7665	
  7666	"By no means."
  7667	
  7668	"Then we shall both come. What are you going to do yourself?"
  7669	
  7670	"I have one or two things which I would wish to do now that I am
  7671	in town. But I shall return by the twelve o'clock train, so as to
  7672	be there in time for your coming."
  7673	
  7674	"And you may expect us early in the afternoon. I have myself some
  7675	small business matters to attend to. Will you not wait and
  7676	breakfast?"
  7677	
  7678	"No, I must go. My heart is lightened already since I have
  7679	confided my trouble to you. I shall look forward to seeing you
  7680	again this afternoon." She dropped her thick black veil over her
  7681	face and glided from the room.
  7682	
  7683	"And what do you think of it all, Watson?" asked Sherlock Holmes,
  7684	leaning back in his chair.
  7685	
  7686	"It seems to me to be a most dark and sinister business."
  7687	
  7688	"Dark enough and sinister enough."
  7689	
  7690	"Yet if the lady is correct in saying that the flooring and walls
  7691	are sound, and that the door, window, and chimney are impassable,
  7692	then her sister must have been undoubtedly alone when she met her
  7693	mysterious end."
  7694	
  7695	"What becomes, then, of these nocturnal whistles, and what of the
  7696	very peculiar words of the dying woman?"
  7697	
  7698	"I cannot think."
  7699	
  7700	"When you combine the ideas of whistles at night, the presence of
  7701	a band of gipsies who are on intimate terms with this old doctor,
  7702	the fact that we have every reason to believe that the doctor has
  7703	an interest in preventing his stepdaughter's marriage, the dying
  7704	allusion to a band, and, finally, the fact that Miss Helen Stoner
  7705	heard a metallic clang, which might have been caused by one of
  7706	those metal bars that secured the shutters falling back into its
  7707	place, I think that there is good ground to think that the
  7708	mystery may be cleared along those lines."
  7709	
  7710	"But what, then, did the gipsies do?"
  7711	
  7712	"I cannot imagine."
  7713	
  7714	"I see many objections to any such theory."
  7715
