  7716	"And so do I. It is precisely for that reason that we are going
  7717	to Stoke Moran this day. I want to see whether the objections are
  7718	fatal, or if they may be explained away. But what in the name of
  7719	the devil!"
  7720	
  7721	The ejaculation had been drawn from my companion by the fact that
  7722	our door had been suddenly dashed open, and that a huge man had
  7723	framed himself in the aperture. His costume was a peculiar
  7724	mixture of the professional and of the agricultural, having a
  7725	black top-hat, a long frock-coat, and a pair of high gaiters,
  7726	with a hunting-crop swinging in his hand. So tall was he that his
  7727	hat actually brushed the cross bar of the doorway, and his
  7728	breadth seemed to span it across from side to side. A large face,
  7729	seared with a thousand wrinkles, burned yellow with the sun, and
  7730	marked with every evil passion, was turned from one to the other
  7731	of us, while his deep-set, bile-shot eyes, and his high, thin,
  7732	fleshless nose, gave him somewhat the resemblance to a fierce old
  7733	bird of prey.
  7734	
  7735	"Which of you is Holmes?" asked this apparition.
  7736	
  7737	"My name, sir; but you have the advantage of me," said my
  7738	companion quietly.
  7739	
  7740	"I am Dr. Grimesby Roylott, of Stoke Moran."
  7741	
  7742	"Indeed, Doctor," said Holmes blandly. "Pray take a seat."
  7743	
  7744	"I will do nothing of the kind. My stepdaughter has been here. I
  7745	have traced her. What has she been saying to you?"
  7746	
  7747	"It is a little cold for the time of the year," said Holmes.
  7748	
  7749	"What has she been saying to you?" screamed the old man
  7750	furiously.
  7751	
  7752	"But I have heard that the crocuses promise well," continued my
  7753	companion imperturbably.
  7754	
  7755	"Ha! You put me off, do you?" said our new visitor, taking a step
  7756	forward and shaking his hunting-crop. "I know you, you scoundrel!
  7757	I have heard of you before. You are Holmes, the meddler."
  7758	
  7759	My friend smiled.
  7760	
  7761	"Holmes, the busybody!"
  7762	
  7763	His smile broadened.
  7764	
  7765	"Holmes, the Scotland Yard Jack-in-office!"
  7766	
  7767	Holmes chuckled heartily. "Your conversation is most
  7768	entertaining," said he. "When you go out close the door, for
  7769	there is a decided draught."
  7770	
  7771	"I will go when I have said my say. Don't you dare to meddle with
  7772	my affairs. I know that Miss Stoner has been here. I traced her!
  7773	I am a dangerous man to fall foul of! See here." He stepped
  7774	swiftly forward, seized the poker, and bent it into a curve with
  7775	his huge brown hands.
  7776	
  7777	"See that you keep yourself out of my grip," he snarled, and
  7778	hurling the twisted poker into the fireplace he strode out of the
  7779	room.
  7780	
  7781	"He seems a very amiable person," said Holmes, laughing. "I am
  7782	not quite so bulky, but if he had remained I might have shown him
  7783	that my grip was not much more feeble than his own." As he spoke
  7784	he picked up the steel poker and, with a sudden effort,
  7785	straightened it out again.
  7786	
  7787	"Fancy his having the insolence to confound me with the official
  7788	detective force! This incident gives zest to our investigation,
  7789	however, and I only trust that our little friend will not suffer
  7790	from her imprudence in allowing this brute to trace her. And now,
  7791	Watson, we shall order breakfast, and afterwards I shall walk
  7792	down to Doctors' Commons, where I hope to get some data which may
  7793	help us in this matter."
  7794	
  7795	
  7796	It was nearly one o'clock when Sherlock Holmes returned from his
  7797	excursion. He held in his hand a sheet of blue paper, scrawled
  7798	over with notes and figures.
  7799	
  7800	"I have seen the will of the deceased wife," said he. "To
  7801	determine its exact meaning I have been obliged to work out the
  7802	present prices of the investments with which it is concerned. The
  7803	total income, which at the time of the wife's death was little
  7804	short of 1100 pounds, is now, through the fall in agricultural
  7805	prices, not more than 750 pounds. Each daughter can claim an
  7806	income of 250 pounds, in case of marriage. It is evident,
  7807	therefore, that if both girls had married, this beauty would have
  7808	had a mere pittance, while even one of them would cripple him to
  7809	a very serious extent. My morning's work has not been wasted,
  7810	since it has proved that he has the very strongest motives for
  7811	standing in the way of anything of the sort. And now, Watson,
  7812	this is too serious for dawdling, especially as the old man is
  7813	aware that we are interesting ourselves in his affairs; so if you
  7814	are ready, we shall call a cab and drive to Waterloo. I should be
  7815	very much obliged if you would slip your revolver into your
  7816	pocket. An Eley's No. 2 is an excellent argument with gentlemen
  7817	who can twist steel pokers into knots. That and a tooth-brush
  7818	are, I think, all that we need."
  7819	
  7820	At Waterloo we were fortunate in catching a train for
  7821	Leatherhead, where we hired a trap at the station inn and drove
  7822	for four or five miles through the lovely Surrey lanes. It was a
  7823	perfect day, with a bright sun and a few fleecy clouds in the
  7824	heavens. The trees and wayside hedges were just throwing out
  7825	their first green shoots, and the air was full of the pleasant
  7826	smell of the moist earth. To me at least there was a strange
  7827	contrast between the sweet promise of the spring and this
  7828	sinister quest upon which we were engaged. My companion sat in
  7829	the front of the trap, his arms folded, his hat pulled down over
  7830	his eyes, and his chin sunk upon his breast, buried in the
  7831	deepest thought. Suddenly, however, he started, tapped me on the
  7832	shoulder, and pointed over the meadows.
  7833	
  7834	"Look there!" said he.
  7835	
  7836	A heavily timbered park stretched up in a gentle slope,
  7837	thickening into a grove at the highest point. From amid the
  7838	branches there jutted out the grey gables and high roof-tree of a
  7839	very old mansion.
  7840	
  7841	"Stoke Moran?" said he.
  7842	
  7843	"Yes, sir, that be the house of Dr. Grimesby Roylott," remarked
  7844	the driver.
  7845	
  7846	"There is some building going on there," said Holmes; "that is
  7847	where we are going."
  7848	
  7849	"There's the village," said the driver, pointing to a cluster of
  7850	roofs some distance to the left; "but if you want to get to the
  7851	house, you'll find it shorter to get over this stile, and so by
  7852	the foot-path over the fields. There it is, where the lady is
  7853	walking."
  7854	
  7855	"And the lady, I fancy, is Miss Stoner," observed Holmes, shading
  7856	his eyes. "Yes, I think we had better do as you suggest."
  7857	
  7858	We got off, paid our fare, and the trap rattled back on its way
  7859	to Leatherhead.
  7860	
  7861	"I thought it as well," said Holmes as we climbed the stile,
  7862	"that this fellow should think we had come here as architects, or
  7863	on some definite business. It may stop his gossip.
  7864	Good-afternoon, Miss Stoner. You see that we have been as good as
  7865	our word."
  7866	
  7867	Our client of the morning had hurried forward to meet us with a
  7868	face which spoke her joy. "I have been waiting so eagerly for
  7869	you," she cried, shaking hands with us warmly. "All has turned
  7870	out splendidly. Dr. Roylott has gone to town, and it is unlikely
  7871	that he will be back before evening."
  7872	
  7873	"We have had the pleasure of making the doctor's acquaintance,"
  7874	said Holmes, and in a few words he sketched out what had
  7875	occurred. Miss Stoner turned white to the lips as she listened.
  7876	
  7877	"Good heavens!" she cried, "he has followed me, then."
  7878	
  7879	"So it appears."
  7880	
  7881	"He is so cunning that I never know when I am safe from him. What
  7882	will he say when he returns?"
  7883	
  7884	"He must guard himself, for he may find that there is someone
  7885	more cunning than himself upon his track. You must lock yourself
  7886	up from him to-night. If he is violent, we shall take you away to
  7887	your aunt's at Harrow. Now, we must make the best use of our
  7888	time, so kindly take us at once to the rooms which we are to
  7889	examine."
  7890	
  7891	The building was of grey, lichen-blotched stone, with a high
  7892	central portion and two curving wings, like the claws of a crab,
  7893	thrown out on each side. In one of these wings the windows were
  7894	broken and blocked with wooden boards, while the roof was partly
  7895	caved in, a picture of ruin. The central portion was in little
  7896	better repair, but the right-hand block was comparatively modern,
  7897	and the blinds in the windows, with the blue smoke curling up
  7898	from the chimneys, showed that this was where the family resided.
  7899	Some scaffolding had been erected against the end wall, and the
  7900	stone-work had been broken into, but there were no signs of any
  7901	workmen at the moment of our visit. Holmes walked slowly up and
  7902	down the ill-trimmed lawn and examined with deep attention the
  7903	outsides of the windows.
  7904	
  7905	"This, I take it, belongs to the room in which you used to sleep,
  7906	the centre one to your sister's, and the one next to the main
  7907	building to Dr. Roylott's chamber?"
  7908	
  7909	"Exactly so. But I am now sleeping in the middle one."
  7910	
  7911	"Pending the alterations, as I understand. By the way, there does
  7912	not seem to be any very pressing need for repairs at that end
  7913	wall."
  7914	
  7915	"There were none. I believe that it was an excuse to move me from
  7916	my room."
  7917	
  7918	"Ah! that is suggestive. Now, on the other side of this narrow
  7919	wing runs the corridor from which these three rooms open. There
  7920	are windows in it, of course?"
  7921	
  7922	"Yes, but very small ones. Too narrow for anyone to pass
  7923	through."
  7924	
  7925	"As you both locked your doors at night, your rooms were
  7926	unapproachable from that side. Now, would you have the kindness
  7927	to go into your room and bar your shutters?"
  7928	
  7929	Miss Stoner did so, and Holmes, after a careful examination
  7930	through the open window, endeavoured in every way to force the
  7931	shutter open, but without success. There was no slit through
  7932	which a knife could be passed to raise the bar. Then with his
  7933	lens he tested the hinges, but they were of solid iron, built
  7934	firmly into the massive masonry. "Hum!" said he, scratching his
  7935	chin in some perplexity, "my theory certainly presents some
  7936	difficulties. No one could pass these shutters if they were
  7937	bolted. Well, we shall see if the inside throws any light upon
  7938	the matter."
  7939	
  7940	A small side door led into the whitewashed corridor from which
  7941	the three bedrooms opened. Holmes refused to examine the third
  7942	chamber, so we passed at once to the second, that in which Miss
  7943	Stoner was now sleeping, and in which her sister had met with her
  7944	fate. It was a homely little room, with a low ceiling and a
  7945	gaping fireplace, after the fashion of old country-houses. A
  7946	brown chest of drawers stood in one corner, a narrow
  7947	white-counterpaned bed in another, and a dressing-table on the
  7948	left-hand side of the window. These articles, with two small
  7949	wicker-work chairs, made up all the furniture in the room save
  7950	for a square of Wilton carpet in the centre. The boards round and
  7951	the panelling of the walls were of brown, worm-eaten oak, so old
  7952	and discoloured that it may have dated from the original building
  7953	of the house. Holmes drew one of the chairs into a corner and sat
  7954	silent, while his eyes travelled round and round and up and down,
  7955	taking in every detail of the apartment.
  7956	
  7957	"Where does that bell communicate with?" he asked at last
  7958	pointing to a thick bell-rope which hung down beside the bed, the
  7959	tassel actually lying upon the pillow.
  7960	
  7961	"It goes to the housekeeper's room."
  7962	
  7963	"It looks newer than the other things?"
  7964	
  7965	"Yes, it was only put there a couple of years ago."
  7966	
  7967	"Your sister asked for it, I suppose?"
  7968	
  7969	"No, I never heard of her using it. We used always to get what we
  7970	wanted for ourselves."
  7971	
  7972	"Indeed, it seemed unnecessary to put so nice a bell-pull there.
  7973	You will excuse me for a few minutes while I satisfy myself as to
  7974	this floor." He threw himself down upon his face with his lens in
  7975	his hand and crawled swiftly backward and forward, examining
  7976	minutely the cracks between the boards. Then he did the same with
  7977	the wood-work with which the chamber was panelled. Finally he
  7978	walked over to the bed and spent some time in staring at it and
  7979	in running his eye up and down the wall. Finally he took the
  7980	bell-rope in his hand and gave it a brisk tug.
  7981	
  7982	"Why, it's a dummy," said he.
  7983	
  7984	"Won't it ring?"
  7985	
  7986	"No, it is not even attached to a wire. This is very interesting.
  7987	You can see now that it is fastened to a hook just above where
  7988	the little opening for the ventilator is."
  7989	
  7990	"How very absurd! I never noticed that before."
  7991	
  7992	"Very strange!" muttered Holmes, pulling at the rope. "There are
  7993	one or two very singular points about this room. For example,
  7994	what a fool a builder must be to open a ventilator into another
  7995	room, when, with the same trouble, he might have communicated
  7996	with the outside air!"
  7997	
  7998	"That is also quite modern," said the lady.
  7999	
  8000	"Done about the same time as the bell-rope?" remarked Holmes.
  8001	
  8002	"Yes, there were several little changes carried out about that
  8003	time."
  8004	
  8005	"They seem to have been of a most interesting character--dummy
  8006	bell-ropes, and ventilators which do not ventilate. With your
  8007	permission, Miss Stoner, we shall now carry our researches into
  8008	the inner apartment."
  8009	
  8010	Dr. Grimesby Roylott's chamber was larger than that of his
  8011	step-daughter, but was as plainly furnished. A camp-bed, a small
  8012	wooden shelf full of books, mostly of a technical character, an
  8013	armchair beside the bed, a plain wooden chair against the wall, a
  8014	round table, and a large iron safe were the principal things
  8015	which met the eye. Holmes walked slowly round and examined each
  8016	and all of them with the keenest interest.
  8017	
  8018	"What's in here?" he asked, tapping the safe.
  8019	
  8020	"My stepfather's business papers."
  8021	
  8022	"Oh! you have seen inside, then?"
  8023	
  8024	"Only once, some years ago. I remember that it was full of
  8025	papers."
  8026	
  8027	"There isn't a cat in it, for example?"
  8028	
  8029	"No. What a strange idea!"
  8030	
  8031	"Well, look at this!" He took up a small saucer of milk which
  8032	stood on the top of it.
  8033	
  8034	"No; we don't keep a cat. But there is a cheetah and a baboon."
  8035	
  8036	"Ah, yes, of course! Well, a cheetah is just a big cat, and yet a
  8037	saucer of milk does not go very far in satisfying its wants, I
  8038	daresay. There is one point which I should wish to determine." He
  8039	squatted down in front of the wooden chair and examined the seat
  8040	of it with the greatest attention.
  8041	
  8042	"Thank you. That is quite settled," said he, rising and putting
  8043	his lens in his pocket. "Hullo! Here is something interesting!"
  8044	
  8045	The object which had caught his eye was a small dog lash hung on
  8046	one corner of the bed. The lash, however, was curled upon itself
  8047	and tied so as to make a loop of whipcord.
  8048	
  8049	"What do you make of that, Watson?"
  8050	
  8051	"It's a common enough lash. But I don't know why it should be
  8052	tied."
  8053	
  8054	"That is not quite so common, is it? Ah, me! it's a wicked world,
  8055	and when a clever man turns his brains to crime it is the worst
  8056	of all. I think that I have seen enough now, Miss Stoner, and
  8057	with your permission we shall walk out upon the lawn."
  8058	
  8059	I had never seen my friend's face so grim or his brow so dark as
  8060	it was when we turned from the scene of this investigation. We
  8061	had walked several times up and down the lawn, neither Miss
  8062	Stoner nor myself liking to break in upon his thoughts before he
  8063	roused himself from his reverie.
  8064	
  8065	"It is very essential, Miss Stoner," said he, "that you should
  8066	absolutely follow my advice in every respect."
  8067	
  8068	"I shall most certainly do so."
  8069	
  8070	"The matter is too serious for any hesitation. Your life may
  8071	depend upon your compliance."
  8072	
  8073	"I assure you that I am in your hands."
  8074	
  8075	"In the first place, both my friend and I must spend the night in
  8076	your room."
  8077	
  8078	Both Miss Stoner and I gazed at him in astonishment.
  8079	
  8080	"Yes, it must be so. Let me explain. I believe that that is the
  8081	village inn over there?"
  8082	
  8083	"Yes, that is the Crown."
  8084	
  8085	"Very good. Your windows would be visible from there?"
  8086	
  8087	"Certainly."
  8088	
  8089	"You must confine yourself to your room, on pretence of a
  8090	headache, when your stepfather comes back. Then when you hear him
  8091	retire for the night, you must open the shutters of your window,
  8092	undo the hasp, put your lamp there as a signal to us, and then
  8093	withdraw quietly with everything which you are likely to want
  8094	into the room which you used to occupy. I have no doubt that, in
  8095	spite of the repairs, you could manage there for one night."
  8096	
  8097	"Oh, yes, easily."
  8098	
  8099	"The rest you will leave in our hands."
  8100	
  8101	"But what will you do?"
  8102	
  8103	"We shall spend the night in your room, and we shall investigate
  8104	the cause of this noise which has disturbed you."
  8105	
  8106	"I believe, Mr. Holmes, that you have already made up your mind,"
  8107	said Miss Stoner, laying her hand upon my companion's sleeve.
  8108	
  8109	"Perhaps I have."
  8110	
  8111	"Then, for pity's sake, tell me what was the cause of my sister's
  8112	death."
  8113	
  8114	"I should prefer to have clearer proofs before I speak."
  8115	
  8116	"You can at least tell me whether my own thought is correct, and
  8117	if she died from some sudden fright."
  8118	
  8119	"No, I do not think so. I think that there was probably some more
  8120	tangible cause. And now, Miss Stoner, we must leave you for if
  8121	Dr. Roylott returned and saw us our journey would be in vain.
  8122	Good-bye, and be brave, for if you will do what I have told you,
  8123	you may rest assured that we shall soon drive away the dangers
  8124	that threaten you."
  8125	
  8126	Sherlock Holmes and I had no difficulty in engaging a bedroom and
  8127	sitting-room at the Crown Inn. They were on the upper floor, and
  8128	from our window we could command a view of the avenue gate, and
  8129	of the inhabited wing of Stoke Moran Manor House. At dusk we saw
  8130	Dr. Grimesby Roylott drive past, his huge form looming up beside
  8131	the little figure of the lad who drove him. The boy had some
  8132	slight difficulty in undoing the heavy iron gates, and we heard
  8133	the hoarse roar of the doctor's voice and saw the fury with which
  8134	he shook his clinched fists at him. The trap drove on, and a few
  8135	minutes later we saw a sudden light spring up among the trees as
  8136	the lamp was lit in one of the sitting-rooms.
  8137	
  8138	"Do you know, Watson," said Holmes as we sat together in the
  8139	gathering darkness, "I have really some scruples as to taking you
  8140	to-night. There is a distinct element of danger."
  8141	
  8142	"Can I be of assistance?"
  8143	
  8144	"Your presence might be invaluable."
  8145	
  8146	"Then I shall certainly come."
  8147	
  8148	"It is very kind of you."
  8149	
  8150	"You speak of danger. You have evidently seen more in these rooms
  8151	than was visible to me."
  8152	
  8153	"No, but I fancy that I may have deduced a little more. I imagine
  8154	that you saw all that I did."
  8155	
  8156	"I saw nothing remarkable save the bell-rope, and what purpose
  8157	that could answer I confess is more than I can imagine."
  8158	
  8159	"You saw the ventilator, too?"
  8160	
  8161	"Yes, but I do not think that it is such a very unusual thing to
  8162	have a small opening between two rooms. It was so small that a
  8163	rat could hardly pass through."
  8164	
  8165	"I knew that we should find a ventilator before ever we came to
  8166	Stoke Moran."
  8167	
  8168	"My dear Holmes!"
  8169	
  8170	"Oh, yes, I did. You remember in her statement she said that her
  8171	sister could smell Dr. Roylott's cigar. Now, of course that
  8172	suggested at once that there must be a communication between the
  8173	two rooms. It could only be a small one, or it would have been
  8174	remarked upon at the coroner's inquiry. I deduced a ventilator."
  8175	
  8176	"But what harm can there be in that?"
  8177	
  8178	"Well, there is at least a curious coincidence of dates. A
  8179	ventilator is made, a cord is hung, and a lady who sleeps in the
  8180	bed dies. Does not that strike you?"
  8181	
  8182	"I cannot as yet see any connection."
  8183	
  8184	"Did you observe anything very peculiar about that bed?"
  8185	
  8186	"No."
  8187	
  8188	"It was clamped to the floor. Did you ever see a bed fastened
  8189	like that before?"
  8190	
  8191	"I cannot say that I have."
  8192	
  8193	"The lady could not move her bed. It must always be in the same
  8194	relative position to the ventilator and to the rope--or so we may
  8195	call it, since it was clearly never meant for a bell-pull."
  8196	
  8197	"Holmes," I cried, "I seem to see dimly what you are hinting at.
  8198	We are only just in time to prevent some subtle and horrible
  8199	crime."
  8200	
  8201	"Subtle enough and horrible enough. When a doctor does go wrong
  8202	he is the first of criminals. He has nerve and he has knowledge.
  8203	Palmer and Pritchard were among the heads of their profession.
  8204	This man strikes even deeper, but I think, Watson, that we shall
  8205	be able to strike deeper still. But we shall have horrors enough
  8206	before the night is over; for goodness' sake let us have a quiet
  8207	pipe and turn our minds for a few hours to something more
  8208	cheerful."
  8209	
  8210	
  8211	About nine o'clock the light among the trees was extinguished,
  8212	and all was dark in the direction of the Manor House. Two hours
  8213	passed slowly away, and then, suddenly, just at the stroke of
  8214	eleven, a single bright light shone out right in front of us.
  8215	
  8216	"That is our signal," said Holmes, springing to his feet; "it
  8217	comes from the middle window."
  8218	
  8219	As we passed out he exchanged a few words with the landlord,
  8220	explaining that we were going on a late visit to an acquaintance,
  8221	and that it was possible that we might spend the night there. A
  8222	moment later we were out on the dark road, a chill wind blowing
  8223	in our faces, and one yellow light twinkling in front of us
  8224	through the gloom to guide us on our sombre errand.
  8225	
  8226	There was little difficulty in entering the grounds, for
  8227	unrepaired breaches gaped in the old park wall. Making our way
  8228	among the trees, we reached the lawn, crossed it, and were about
  8229	to enter through the window when out from a clump of laurel
  8230	bushes there darted what seemed to be a hideous and distorted
  8231	child, who threw itself upon the grass with writhing limbs and
  8232	then ran swiftly across the lawn into the darkness.
  8233	
  8234	"My God!" I whispered; "did you see it?"
  8235	
  8236	Holmes was for the moment as startled as I. His hand closed like
  8237	a vice upon my wrist in his agitation. Then he broke into a low
  8238	laugh and put his lips to my ear.
  8239	
  8240	"It is a nice household," he murmured. "That is the baboon."
  8241	
  8242	I had forgotten the strange pets which the doctor affected. There
  8243	was a cheetah, too; perhaps we might find it upon our shoulders
  8244	at any moment. I confess that I felt easier in my mind when,
  8245	after following Holmes' example and slipping off my shoes, I
  8246	found myself inside the bedroom. My companion noiselessly closed
  8247	the shutters, moved the lamp onto the table, and cast his eyes
  8248	round the room. All was as we had seen it in the daytime. Then
  8249	creeping up to me and making a trumpet of his hand, he whispered
  8250	into my ear again so gently that it was all that I could do to
  8251	distinguish the words:
  8252	
  8253	"The least sound would be fatal to our plans."
  8254	
  8255	I nodded to show that I had heard.
  8256	
  8257	"We must sit without light. He would see it through the
  8258	ventilator."
  8259	
  8260	I nodded again.
  8261	
  8262	"Do not go asleep; your very life may depend upon it. Have your
  8263	pistol ready in case we should need it. I will sit on the side of
  8264	the bed, and you in that chair."
  8265	
  8266	I took out my revolver and laid it on the corner of the table.
  8267	
  8268	Holmes had brought up a long thin cane, and this he placed upon
  8269	the bed beside him. By it he laid the box of matches and the
  8270	stump of a candle. Then he turned down the lamp, and we were left
  8271	in darkness.
  8272	
  8273	How shall I ever forget that dreadful vigil? I could not hear a
  8274	sound, not even the drawing of a breath, and yet I knew that my
  8275	companion sat open-eyed, within a few feet of me, in the same
  8276	state of nervous tension in which I was myself. The shutters cut
  8277	off the least ray of light, and we waited in absolute darkness.
  8278	
  8279	From outside came the occasional cry of a night-bird, and once at
  8280	our very window a long drawn catlike whine, which told us that
  8281	the cheetah was indeed at liberty. Far away we could hear the
  8282	deep tones of the parish clock, which boomed out every quarter of
  8283	an hour. How long they seemed, those quarters! Twelve struck, and
  8284	one and two and three, and still we sat waiting silently for
  8285	whatever might befall.
  8286	
  8287	Suddenly there was the momentary gleam of a light up in the
  8288	direction of the ventilator, which vanished immediately, but was
  8289	succeeded by a strong smell of burning oil and heated metal.
  8290	Someone in the next room had lit a dark-lantern. I heard a gentle
  8291	sound of movement, and then all was silent once more, though the
  8292	smell grew stronger. For half an hour I sat with straining ears.
  8293	Then suddenly another sound became audible--a very gentle,
  8294	soothing sound, like that of a small jet of steam escaping
  8295	continually from a kettle. The instant that we heard it, Holmes
  8296	sprang from the bed, struck a match, and lashed furiously with
  8297	his cane at the bell-pull.
  8298	
  8299	"You see it, Watson?" he yelled. "You see it?"
  8300	
  8301	But I saw nothing. At the moment when Holmes struck the light I
  8302	heard a low, clear whistle, but the sudden glare flashing into my
  8303	weary eyes made it impossible for me to tell what it was at which
  8304	my friend lashed so savagely. I could, however, see that his face
  8305	was deadly pale and filled with horror and loathing. He had
  8306	ceased to strike and was gazing up at the ventilator when
  8307	suddenly there broke from the silence of the night the most
  8308	horrible cry to which I have ever listened. It swelled up louder
  8309	and louder, a hoarse yell of pain and fear and anger all mingled
  8310	in the one dreadful shriek. They say that away down in the
  8311	village, and even in the distant parsonage, that cry raised the
  8312	sleepers from their beds. It struck cold to our hearts, and I
  8313	stood gazing at Holmes, and he at me, until the last echoes of it
  8314	had died away into the silence from which it rose.
  8315	
  8316	"What can it mean?" I gasped.
  8317	
  8318	"It means that it is all over," Holmes answered. "And perhaps,
  8319	after all, it is for the best. Take your pistol, and we will
  8320	enter Dr. Roylott's room."
  8321	
  8322	With a grave face he lit the lamp and led the way down the
  8323	corridor. Twice he struck at the chamber door without any reply
  8324	from within. Then he turned the handle and entered, I at his
  8325	heels, with the cocked pistol in my hand.
  8326	
  8327	It was a singular sight which met our eyes. On the table stood a
  8328	dark-lantern with the shutter half open, throwing a brilliant
  8329	beam of light upon the iron safe, the door of which was ajar.
  8330	Beside this table, on the wooden chair, sat Dr. Grimesby Roylott
  8331	clad in a long grey dressing-gown, his bare ankles protruding
  8332	beneath, and his feet thrust into red heelless Turkish slippers.
  8333	Across his lap lay the short stock with the long lash which we
  8334	had noticed during the day. His chin was cocked upward and his
  8335	eyes were fixed in a dreadful, rigid stare at the corner of the
  8336	ceiling. Round his brow he had a peculiar yellow band, with
  8337	brownish speckles, which seemed to be bound tightly round his
  8338	head. As we entered he made neither sound nor motion.
  8339	
  8340	"The band! the speckled band!" whispered Holmes.
  8341	
  8342	I took a step forward. In an instant his strange headgear began
  8343	to move, and there reared itself from among his hair the squat
  8344	diamond-shaped head and puffed neck of a loathsome serpent.
  8345	
  8346	"It is a swamp adder!" cried Holmes; "the deadliest snake in
  8347	India. He has died within ten seconds of being bitten. Violence
  8348	does, in truth, recoil upon the violent, and the schemer falls
  8349	into the pit which he digs for another. Let us thrust this
  8350	creature back into its den, and we can then remove Miss Stoner to
  8351	some place of shelter and let the county police know what has
  8352	happened."
  8353	
  8354	As he spoke he drew the dog-whip swiftly from the dead man's lap,
  8355	and throwing the noose round the reptile's neck he drew it from
  8356	its horrid perch and, carrying it at arm's length, threw it into
  8357	the iron safe, which he closed upon it.
  8358	
  8359	Such are the true facts of the death of Dr. Grimesby Roylott, of
  8360	Stoke Moran. It is not necessary that I should prolong a
  8361	narrative which has already run to too great a length by telling
  8362	how we broke the sad news to the terrified girl, how we conveyed
  8363	her by the morning train to the care of her good aunt at Harrow,
  8364	of how the slow process of official inquiry came to the
  8365	conclusion that the doctor met his fate while indiscreetly
  8366	playing with a dangerous pet. The little which I had yet to learn
  8367	of the case was told me by Sherlock Holmes as we travelled back
  8368	next day.
  8369	
  8370	"I had," said he, "come to an entirely erroneous conclusion which
  8371	shows, my dear Watson, how dangerous it always is to reason from
  8372	insufficient data. The presence of the gipsies, and the use of
  8373	the word 'band,' which was used by the poor girl, no doubt, to
  8374	explain the appearance which she had caught a hurried glimpse of
  8375	by the light of her match, were sufficient to put me upon an
  8376	entirely wrong scent. I can only claim the merit that I instantly
  8377	reconsidered my position when, however, it became clear to me
  8378	that whatever danger threatened an occupant of the room could not
  8379	come either from the window or the door. My attention was
  8380	speedily drawn, as I have already remarked to you, to this
  8381	ventilator, and to the bell-rope which hung down to the bed. The
  8382	discovery that this was a dummy, and that the bed was clamped to
  8383	the floor, instantly gave rise to the suspicion that the rope was
  8384	there as a bridge for something passing through the hole and
  8385	coming to the bed. The idea of a snake instantly occurred to me,
  8386	and when I coupled it with my knowledge that the doctor was
  8387	furnished with a supply of creatures from India, I felt that I
  8388	was probably on the right track. The idea of using a form of
  8389	poison which could not possibly be discovered by any chemical
  8390	test was just such a one as would occur to a clever and ruthless
  8391	man who had had an Eastern training. The rapidity with which such
  8392	a poison would take effect would also, from his point of view, be
  8393	an advantage. It would be a sharp-eyed coroner, indeed, who could
  8394	distinguish the two little dark punctures which would show where
  8395	the poison fangs had done their work. Then I thought of the
  8396	whistle. Of course he must recall the snake before the morning
  8397	light revealed it to the victim. He had trained it, probably by
  8398	the use of the milk which we saw, to return to him when summoned.
  8399	He would put it through this ventilator at the hour that he
  8400	thought best, with the certainty that it would crawl down the
  8401	rope and land on the bed. It might or might not bite the
  8402	occupant, perhaps she might escape every night for a week, but
  8403	sooner or later she must fall a victim.
  8404	
  8405	"I had come to these conclusions before ever I had entered his
  8406	room. An inspection of his chair showed me that he had been in
  8407	the habit of standing on it, which of course would be necessary
  8408	in order that he should reach the ventilator. The sight of the
  8409	safe, the saucer of milk, and the loop of whipcord were enough to
  8410	finally dispel any doubts which may have remained. The metallic
  8411	clang heard by Miss Stoner was obviously caused by her stepfather
  8412	hastily closing the door of his safe upon its terrible occupant.
  8413	Having once made up my mind, you know the steps which I took in
  8414	order to put the matter to the proof. I heard the creature hiss
  8415	as I have no doubt that you did also, and I instantly lit the
  8416	light and attacked it."
  8417	
  8418	"With the result of driving it through the ventilator."
  8419	
  8420	"And also with the result of causing it to turn upon its master
  8421	at the other side. Some of the blows of my cane came home and
  8422	roused its snakish temper, so that it flew upon the first person
  8423	it saw. In this way I am no doubt indirectly responsible for Dr.
  8424	Grimesby Roylott's death, and I cannot say that it is likely to
  8425	weigh very heavily upon my conscience."
  8426	
  8427	
  8428	
  8429	IX. THE ADVENTURE OF THE ENGINEER'S THUMB
  8430	
  8431	Of all the problems which have been submitted to my friend, Mr.
  8432	Sherlock Holmes, for solution during the years of our intimacy,
  8433	there were only two which I was the means of introducing to his
  8434	notice--that of Mr. Hatherley's thumb, and that of Colonel
  8435	Warburton's madness. Of these the latter may have afforded a
  8436	finer field for an acute and original observer, but the other was
  8437	so strange in its inception and so dramatic in its details that
  8438	it may be the more worthy of being placed upon record, even if it
  8439	gave my friend fewer openings for those deductive methods of
  8440	reasoning by which he achieved such remarkable results. The story
  8441	has, I believe, been told more than once in the newspapers, but,
  8442	like all such narratives, its effect is much less striking when
  8443	set forth en bloc in a single half-column of print than when the
  8444	facts slowly evolve before your own eyes, and the mystery clears
  8445	gradually away as each new discovery furnishes a step which leads
  8446	on to the complete truth. At the time the circumstances made a
  8447	deep impression upon me, and the lapse of two years has hardly
  8448	served to weaken the effect.
  8449	
  8450	It was in the summer of '89, not long after my marriage, that the
  8451	events occurred which I am now about to summarise. I had returned
  8452	to civil practice and had finally abandoned Holmes in his Baker
  8453	Street rooms, although I continually visited him and occasionally
  8454	even persuaded him to forgo his Bohemian habits so far as to come
  8455	and visit us. My practice had steadily increased, and as I
  8456	happened to live at no very great distance from Paddington
  8457	Station, I got a few patients from among the officials. One of
  8458	these, whom I had cured of a painful and lingering disease, was
  8459	never weary of advertising my virtues and of endeavouring to send
  8460	me on every sufferer over whom he might have any influence.
  8461	
  8462	One morning, at a little before seven o'clock, I was awakened by
  8463	the maid tapping at the door to announce that two men had come
  8464	from Paddington and were waiting in the consulting-room. I
  8465	dressed hurriedly, for I knew by experience that railway cases
  8466	were seldom trivial, and hastened downstairs. As I descended, my
  8467	old ally, the guard, came out of the room and closed the door
  8468	tightly behind him.
  8469	
  8470	"I've got him here," he whispered, jerking his thumb over his
  8471	shoulder; "he's all right."
  8472	
  8473	"What is it, then?" I asked, for his manner suggested that it was
  8474	some strange creature which he had caged up in my room.
  8475	
  8476	"It's a new patient," he whispered. "I thought I'd bring him
  8477	round myself; then he couldn't slip away. There he is, all safe
  8478	and sound. I must go now, Doctor; I have my dooties, just the
  8479	same as you." And off he went, this trusty tout, without even
  8480	giving me time to thank him.
  8481	
  8482	I entered my consulting-room and found a gentleman seated by the
  8483	table. He was quietly dressed in a suit of heather tweed with a
  8484	soft cloth cap which he had laid down upon my books. Round one of
  8485	his hands he had a handkerchief wrapped, which was mottled all
  8486	over with bloodstains. He was young, not more than
  8487	five-and-twenty, I should say, with a strong, masculine face; but
  8488	he was exceedingly pale and gave me the impression of a man who
  8489	was suffering from some strong agitation, which it took all his
  8490	strength of mind to control.
  8491	
  8492	"I am sorry to knock you up so early, Doctor," said he, "but I
  8493	have had a very serious accident during the night. I came in by
  8494	train this morning, and on inquiring at Paddington as to where I
  8495	might find a doctor, a worthy fellow very kindly escorted me
  8496	here. I gave the maid a card, but I see that she has left it upon
  8497	the side-table."
  8498	
  8499	I took it up and glanced at it. "Mr. Victor Hatherley, hydraulic
  8500	engineer, 16A, Victoria Street (3rd floor)." That was the name,
  8501	style, and abode of my morning visitor. "I regret that I have
  8502	kept you waiting," said I, sitting down in my library-chair. "You
  8503	are fresh from a night journey, I understand, which is in itself
  8504	a monotonous occupation."
  8505	
  8506	"Oh, my night could not be called monotonous," said he, and
  8507	laughed. He laughed very heartily, with a high, ringing note,
  8508	leaning back in his chair and shaking his sides. All my medical
  8509	instincts rose up against that laugh.
  8510	
  8511	"Stop it!" I cried; "pull yourself together!" and I poured out
  8512	some water from a caraffe.
  8513	
  8514	It was useless, however. He was off in one of those hysterical
  8515	outbursts which come upon a strong nature when some great crisis
  8516	is over and gone. Presently he came to himself once more, very
  8517	weary and pale-looking.
  8518	
  8519	"I have been making a fool of myself," he gasped.
  8520	
  8521	"Not at all. Drink this." I dashed some brandy into the water,
  8522	and the colour began to come back to his bloodless cheeks.
  8523	
  8524	"That's better!" said he. "And now, Doctor, perhaps you would
  8525	kindly attend to my thumb, or rather to the place where my thumb
  8526	used to be."
  8527	
  8528	He unwound the handkerchief and held out his hand. It gave even
  8529	my hardened nerves a shudder to look at it. There were four
  8530	protruding fingers and a horrid red, spongy surface where the
  8531	thumb should have been. It had been hacked or torn right out from
  8532	the roots.
  8533	
  8534	"Good heavens!" I cried, "this is a terrible injury. It must have
  8535	bled considerably."
  8536	
  8537	"Yes, it did. I fainted when it was done, and I think that I must
  8538	have been senseless for a long time. When I came to I found that
  8539	it was still bleeding, so I tied one end of my handkerchief very
  8540	tightly round the wrist and braced it up with a twig."
  8541	
  8542	"Excellent! You should have been a surgeon."
  8543	
  8544	"It is a question of hydraulics, you see, and came within my own
  8545	province."
  8546	
  8547	"This has been done," said I, examining the wound, "by a very
  8548	heavy and sharp instrument."
  8549	
  8550	"A thing like a cleaver," said he.
  8551	
  8552	"An accident, I presume?"
  8553	
  8554	"By no means."
  8555	
  8556	"What! a murderous attack?"
  8557	
  8558	"Very murderous indeed."
  8559	
  8560	"You horrify me."
  8561	
  8562	I sponged the wound, cleaned it, dressed it, and finally covered
  8563	it over with cotton wadding and carbolised bandages. He lay back
  8564	without wincing, though he bit his lip from time to time.
  8565	
  8566	"How is that?" I asked when I had finished.
  8567	
  8568	"Capital! Between your brandy and your bandage, I feel a new man.
  8569	I was very weak, but I have had a good deal to go through."
  8570	
  8571	"Perhaps you had better not speak of the matter. It is evidently
  8572	trying to your nerves."
  8573	
  8574	"Oh, no, not now. I shall have to tell my tale to the police;
  8575	but, between ourselves, if it were not for the convincing
  8576	evidence of this wound of mine, I should be surprised if they
  8577	believed my statement, for it is a very extraordinary one, and I
  8578	have not much in the way of proof with which to back it up; and,
  8579	even if they believe me, the clues which I can give them are so
  8580	vague that it is a question whether justice will be done."
  8581	
  8582	"Ha!" cried I, "if it is anything in the nature of a problem
  8583	which you desire to see solved, I should strongly recommend you
  8584	to come to my friend, Mr. Sherlock Holmes, before you go to the
  8585	official police."
  8586	
  8587	"Oh, I have heard of that fellow," answered my visitor, "and I
  8588	should be very glad if he would take the matter up, though of
  8589	course I must use the official police as well. Would you give me
  8590	an introduction to him?"
  8591	
  8592	"I'll do better. I'll take you round to him myself."
  8593	
  8594	"I should be immensely obliged to you."
  8595	
  8596	"We'll call a cab and go together. We shall just be in time to
  8597	have a little breakfast with him. Do you feel equal to it?"
  8598	
  8599	"Yes; I shall not feel easy until I have told my story."
  8600	
  8601	"Then my servant will call a cab, and I shall be with you in an
  8602	instant." I rushed upstairs, explained the matter shortly to my
  8603	wife, and in five minutes was inside a hansom, driving with my
  8604	new acquaintance to Baker Street.
  8605	
  8606	Sherlock Holmes was, as I expected, lounging about his
  8607	sitting-room in his dressing-gown, reading the agony column of The
  8608	Times and smoking his before-breakfast pipe, which was composed
  8609	of all the plugs and dottles left from his smokes of the day
  8610	before, all carefully dried and collected on the corner of the
  8611	mantelpiece. He received us in his quietly genial fashion,
  8612	ordered fresh rashers and eggs, and joined us in a hearty meal.
  8613	When it was concluded he settled our new acquaintance upon the
  8614	sofa, placed a pillow beneath his head, and laid a glass of
  8615	brandy and water within his reach.
  8616	
  8617	"It is easy to see that your experience has been no common one,
  8618	Mr. Hatherley," said he. "Pray, lie down there and make yourself
  8619	absolutely at home. Tell us what you can, but stop when you are
  8620	tired and keep up your strength with a little stimulant."
  8621	
  8622	"Thank you," said my patient. "but I have felt another man since
  8623	the doctor bandaged me, and I think that your breakfast has
  8624	completed the cure. I shall take up as little of your valuable
  8625	time as possible, so I shall start at once upon my peculiar
  8626	experiences."
  8627	
  8628	Holmes sat in his big armchair with the weary, heavy-lidded
  8629	expression which veiled his keen and eager nature, while I sat
  8630	opposite to him, and we listened in silence to the strange story
  8631	which our visitor detailed to us.
  8632	
  8633	"You must know," said he, "that I am an orphan and a bachelor,
  8634	residing alone in lodgings in London. By profession I am a
  8635	hydraulic engineer, and I have had considerable experience of my
  8636	work during the seven years that I was apprenticed to Venner &
  8637	Matheson, the well-known firm, of Greenwich. Two years ago,
  8638	having served my time, and having also come into a fair sum of
  8639	money through my poor father's death, I determined to start in
  8640	business for myself and took professional chambers in Victoria
  8641	Street.
  8642	
  8643	"I suppose that everyone finds his first independent start in
  8644	business a dreary experience. To me it has been exceptionally so.
  8645	During two years I have had three consultations and one small
  8646	job, and that is absolutely all that my profession has brought
  8647	me. My gross takings amount to 27 pounds 10s. Every day, from
  8648	nine in the morning until four in the afternoon, I waited in my
  8649	little den, until at last my heart began to sink, and I came to
  8650	believe that I should never have any practice at all.
  8651	
  8652	"Yesterday, however, just as I was thinking of leaving the
  8653	office, my clerk entered to say there was a gentleman waiting who
  8654	wished to see me upon business. He brought up a card, too, with
  8655	the name of 'Colonel Lysander Stark' engraved upon it. Close at
  8656	his heels came the colonel himself, a man rather over the middle
  8657	size, but of an exceeding thinness. I do not think that I have
  8658	ever seen so thin a man. His whole face sharpened away into nose
  8659	and chin, and the skin of his cheeks was drawn quite tense over
  8660	his outstanding bones. Yet this emaciation seemed to be his
  8661	natural habit, and due to no disease, for his eye was bright, his
  8662	step brisk, and his bearing assured. He was plainly but neatly
  8663	dressed, and his age, I should judge, would be nearer forty than
  8664	thirty.
  8665	
  8666	"'Mr. Hatherley?' said he, with something of a German accent.
  8667	'You have been recommended to me, Mr. Hatherley, as being a man
  8668	who is not only proficient in his profession but is also discreet
  8669	and capable of preserving a secret.'
  8670	
  8671	"I bowed, feeling as flattered as any young man would at such an
  8672	address. 'May I ask who it was who gave me so good a character?'
  8673	
  8674	"'Well, perhaps it is better that I should not tell you that just
  8675	at this moment. I have it from the same source that you are both
  8676	an orphan and a bachelor and are residing alone in London.'
  8677	
  8678	"'That is quite correct,' I answered; 'but you will excuse me if
  8679	I say that I cannot see how all this bears upon my professional
  8680	qualifications. I understand that it was on a professional matter
  8681	that you wished to speak to me?'
  8682	
  8683	"'Undoubtedly so. But you will find that all I say is really to
  8684	the point. I have a professional commission for you, but absolute
  8685	secrecy is quite essential--absolute secrecy, you understand, and
  8686	of course we may expect that more from a man who is alone than
  8687	from one who lives in the bosom of his family.'
  8688	
  8689	"'If I promise to keep a secret,' said I, 'you may absolutely
  8690	depend upon my doing so.'
  8691	
  8692	"He looked very hard at me as I spoke, and it seemed to me that I
  8693	had never seen so suspicious and questioning an eye.
  8694	
  8695	"'Do you promise, then?' said he at last.
  8696	
  8697	"'Yes, I promise.'
  8698	
  8699	"'Absolute and complete silence before, during, and after? No
  8700	reference to the matter at all, either in word or writing?'
  8701	
  8702	"'I have already given you my word.'
  8703	
  8704	"'Very good.' He suddenly sprang up, and darting like lightning
  8705	across the room he flung open the door. The passage outside was
  8706	empty.
  8707	
  8708	"'That's all right,' said he, coming back. 'I know that clerks are
  8709	sometimes curious as to their master's affairs. Now we can talk
  8710	in safety.' He drew up his chair very close to mine and began to
  8711	stare at me again with the same questioning and thoughtful look.
  8712	
  8713	"A feeling of repulsion, and of something akin to fear had begun
  8714	to rise within me at the strange antics of this fleshless man.
  8715	Even my dread of losing a client could not restrain me from
  8716	showing my impatience.
  8717	
  8718	"'I beg that you will state your business, sir,' said I; 'my time
  8719	is of value.' Heaven forgive me for that last sentence, but the
  8720	words came to my lips.
  8721	
  8722	"'How would fifty guineas for a night's work suit you?' he asked.
  8723	
  8724	"'Most admirably.'
  8725	
  8726	"'I say a night's work, but an hour's would be nearer the mark. I
  8727	simply want your opinion about a hydraulic stamping machine which
  8728	has got out of gear. If you show us what is wrong we shall soon
  8729	set it right ourselves. What do you think of such a commission as
  8730	that?'
  8731	
  8732	"'The work appears to be light and the pay munificent.'
  8733	
  8734	"'Precisely so. We shall want you to come to-night by the last
  8735	train.'
  8736	
  8737	"'Where to?'
  8738	
  8739	"'To Eyford, in Berkshire. It is a little place near the borders
  8740	of Oxfordshire, and within seven miles of Reading. There is a
  8741	train from Paddington which would bring you there at about
  8742	11:15.'
  8743	
  8744	"'Very good.'
  8745	
  8746	"'I shall come down in a carriage to meet you.'
  8747	
  8748	"'There is a drive, then?'
  8749	
  8750	"'Yes, our little place is quite out in the country. It is a good
  8751	seven miles from Eyford Station.'
  8752	
  8753	"'Then we can hardly get there before midnight. I suppose there
  8754	would be no chance of a train back. I should be compelled to stop
  8755	the night.'
  8756	
  8757	"'Yes, we could easily give you a shake-down.'
  8758	
  8759	"'That is very awkward. Could I not come at some more convenient
  8760	hour?'
  8761	
  8762	"'We have judged it best that you should come late. It is to
  8763	recompense you for any inconvenience that we are paying to you, a
  8764	young and unknown man, a fee which would buy an opinion from the
  8765	very heads of your profession. Still, of course, if you would
  8766	like to draw out of the business, there is plenty of time to do
  8767	so.'
  8768	
  8769	"I thought of the fifty guineas, and of how very useful they
  8770	would be to me. 'Not at all,' said I, 'I shall be very happy to
  8771	accommodate myself to your wishes. I should like, however, to
  8772	understand a little more clearly what it is that you wish me to
  8773	do.'
  8774	
  8775	"'Quite so. It is very natural that the pledge of secrecy which
  8776	we have exacted from you should have aroused your curiosity. I
  8777	have no wish to commit you to anything without your having it all
  8778	laid before you. I suppose that we are absolutely safe from
  8779	eavesdroppers?'
  8780	
  8781	"'Entirely.'
  8782	
  8783	"'Then the matter stands thus. You are probably aware that
  8784	fuller's-earth is a valuable product, and that it is only found
  8785	in one or two places in England?'
  8786	
  8787	"'I have heard so.'
  8788	
  8789	"'Some little time ago I bought a small place--a very small
  8790	place--within ten miles of Reading. I was fortunate enough to
  8791	discover that there was a deposit of fuller's-earth in one of my
  8792	fields. On examining it, however, I found that this deposit was a
  8793	comparatively small one, and that it formed a link between two
  8794	very much larger ones upon the right and left--both of them,
  8795	however, in the grounds of my neighbours. These good people were
  8796	absolutely ignorant that their land contained that which was
  8797	quite as valuable as a gold-mine. Naturally, it was to my
  8798	interest to buy their land before they discovered its true value,
  8799	but unfortunately I had no capital by which I could do this. I
  8800	took a few of my friends into the secret, however, and they
  8801	suggested that we should quietly and secretly work our own little
  8802	deposit and that in this way we should earn the money which would
  8803	enable us to buy the neighbouring fields. This we have now been
  8804	doing for some time, and in order to help us in our operations we
  8805	erected a hydraulic press. This press, as I have already
  8806	explained, has got out of order, and we wish your advice upon the
  8807	subject. We guard our secret very jealously, however, and if it
  8808	once became known that we had hydraulic engineers coming to our
  8809	little house, it would soon rouse inquiry, and then, if the facts
  8810	came out, it would be good-bye to any chance of getting these
  8811	fields and carrying out our plans. That is why I have made you
  8812	promise me that you will not tell a human being that you are
  8813	going to Eyford to-night. I hope that I make it all plain?'
  8814	
  8815	"'I quite follow you,' said I. 'The only point which I could not
  8816	quite understand was what use you could make of a hydraulic press
  8817	in excavating fuller's-earth, which, as I understand, is dug out
  8818	like gravel from a pit.'
  8819	
  8820	"'Ah!' said he carelessly, 'we have our own process. We compress
  8821	the earth into bricks, so as to remove them without revealing
  8822	what they are. But that is a mere detail. I have taken you fully
  8823	into my confidence now, Mr. Hatherley, and I have shown you how I
  8824	trust you.' He rose as he spoke. 'I shall expect you, then, at
  8825	Eyford at 11:15.'
  8826	
  8827	"'I shall certainly be there.'
  8828	
  8829	"'And not a word to a soul.' He looked at me with a last long,
  8830	questioning gaze, and then, pressing my hand in a cold, dank
  8831	grasp, he hurried from the room.
  8832	
  8833	"Well, when I came to think it all over in cool blood I was very
  8834	much astonished, as you may both think, at this sudden commission
  8835	which had been intrusted to me. On the one hand, of course, I was
  8836	glad, for the fee was at least tenfold what I should have asked
  8837	had I set a price upon my own services, and it was possible that
  8838	this order might lead to other ones. On the other hand, the face
  8839	and manner of my patron had made an unpleasant impression upon
  8840	me, and I could not think that his explanation of the
  8841	fuller's-earth was sufficient to explain the necessity for my
  8842	coming at midnight, and his extreme anxiety lest I should tell
  8843	anyone of my errand. However, I threw all fears to the winds, ate
  8844	a hearty supper, drove to Paddington, and started off, having
  8845	obeyed to the letter the injunction as to holding my tongue.
  8846	
  8847	"At Reading I had to change not only my carriage but my station.
  8848	However, I was in time for the last train to Eyford, and I
  8849	reached the little dim-lit station after eleven o'clock. I was the
  8850	only passenger who got out there, and there was no one upon the
  8851	platform save a single sleepy porter with a lantern. As I passed
  8852	out through the wicket gate, however, I found my acquaintance of
  8853	the morning waiting in the shadow upon the other side. Without a
  8854	word he grasped my arm and hurried me into a carriage, the door
  8855	of which was standing open. He drew up the windows on either
  8856	side, tapped on the wood-work, and away we went as fast as the
  8857	horse could go."
  8858	
  8859	"One horse?" interjected Holmes.
  8860	
  8861	"Yes, only one."
  8862	
  8863	"Did you observe the colour?"
  8864	
  8865	"Yes, I saw it by the side-lights when I was stepping into the
  8866	carriage. It was a chestnut."
  8867	
  8868	"Tired-looking or fresh?"
  8869	
  8870	"Oh, fresh and glossy."
  8871	
  8872	"Thank you. I am sorry to have interrupted you. Pray continue
  8873	your most interesting statement."
  8874	
  8875	"Away we went then, and we drove for at least an hour. Colonel
  8876	Lysander Stark had said that it was only seven miles, but I
  8877	should think, from the rate that we seemed to go, and from the
  8878	time that we took, that it must have been nearer twelve. He sat
  8879	at my side in silence all the time, and I was aware, more than
  8880	once when I glanced in his direction, that he was looking at me
  8881	with great intensity. The country roads seem to be not very good
  8882	in that part of the world, for we lurched and jolted terribly. I
  8883	tried to look out of the windows to see something of where we
  8884	were, but they were made of frosted glass, and I could make out
  8885	nothing save the occasional bright blur of a passing light. Now
  8886	and then I hazarded some remark to break the monotony of the
  8887	journey, but the colonel answered only in monosyllables, and the
  8888	conversation soon flagged. At last, however, the bumping of the
  8889	road was exchanged for the crisp smoothness of a gravel-drive,
  8890	and the carriage came to a stand. Colonel Lysander Stark sprang
  8891	out, and, as I followed after him, pulled me swiftly into a porch
  8892	which gaped in front of us. We stepped, as it were, right out of
  8893	the carriage and into the hall, so that I failed to catch the
  8894	most fleeting glance of the front of the house. The instant that
  8895	I had crossed the threshold the door slammed heavily behind us,
  8896	and I heard faintly the rattle of the wheels as the carriage
  8897	drove away.
  8898	
  8899	"It was pitch dark inside the house, and the colonel fumbled
  8900	about looking for matches and muttering under his breath.
  8901	Suddenly a door opened at the other end of the passage, and a
  8902	long, golden bar of light shot out in our direction. It grew
  8903	broader, and a woman appeared with a lamp in her hand, which she
  8904	held above her head, pushing her face forward and peering at us.
  8905	I could see that she was pretty, and from the gloss with which
  8906	the light shone upon her dark dress I knew that it was a rich
  8907	material. She spoke a few words in a foreign tongue in a tone as
  8908	though asking a question, and when my companion answered in a
  8909	gruff monosyllable she gave such a start that the lamp nearly
  8910	fell from her hand. Colonel Stark went up to her, whispered
  8911	something in her ear, and then, pushing her back into the room
  8912	from whence she had come, he walked towards me again with the
  8913	lamp in his hand.
  8914	
  8915	"'Perhaps you will have the kindness to wait in this room for a
  8916	few minutes,' said he, throwing open another door. It was a
  8917	quiet, little, plainly furnished room, with a round table in the
  8918	centre, on which several German books were scattered. Colonel
  8919	Stark laid down the lamp on the top of a harmonium beside the
  8920	door. 'I shall not keep you waiting an instant,' said he, and
  8921	vanished into the darkness.
  8922	
  8923	"I glanced at the books upon the table, and in spite of my
  8924	ignorance of German I could see that two of them were treatises
  8925	on science, the others being volumes of poetry. Then I walked
  8926	across to the window, hoping that I might catch some glimpse of
  8927	the country-side, but an oak shutter, heavily barred, was folded
  8928	across it. It was a wonderfully silent house. There was an old
  8929	clock ticking loudly somewhere in the passage, but otherwise
  8930	everything was deadly still. A vague feeling of uneasiness began
  8931	to steal over me. Who were these German people, and what were
  8932	they doing living in this strange, out-of-the-way place? And
  8933	where was the place? I was ten miles or so from Eyford, that was
  8934	all I knew, but whether north, south, east, or west I had no
  8935	idea. For that matter, Reading, and possibly other large towns,
  8936	were within that radius, so the place might not be so secluded,
  8937	after all. Yet it was quite certain, from the absolute stillness,
  8938	that we were in the country. I paced up and down the room,
  8939	humming a tune under my breath to keep up my spirits and feeling
  8940	that I was thoroughly earning my fifty-guinea fee.
  8941	
  8942	"Suddenly, without any preliminary sound in the midst of the
  8943	utter stillness, the door of my room swung slowly open. The woman
  8944	was standing in the aperture, the darkness of the hall behind
  8945	her, the yellow light from my lamp beating upon her eager and
  8946	beautiful face. I could see at a glance that she was sick with
  8947	fear, and the sight sent a chill to my own heart. She held up one
  8948	shaking finger to warn me to be silent, and she shot a few
  8949	whispered words of broken English at me, her eyes glancing back,
  8950	like those of a frightened horse, into the gloom behind her.
  8951	
  8952	"'I would go,' said she, trying hard, as it seemed to me, to
  8953	speak calmly; 'I would go. I should not stay here. There is no
  8954	good for you to do.'
  8955	
  8956	"'But, madam,' said I, 'I have not yet done what I came for. I
  8957	cannot possibly leave until I have seen the machine.'
  8958	
  8959	"'It is not worth your while to wait,' she went on. 'You can pass
  8960	through the door; no one hinders.' And then, seeing that I smiled
  8961	and shook my head, she suddenly threw aside her constraint and
  8962	made a step forward, with her hands wrung together. 'For the love
  8963	of Heaven!' she whispered, 'get away from here before it is too
  8964	late!'
  8965	
  8966	"But I am somewhat headstrong by nature, and the more ready to
  8967	engage in an affair when there is some obstacle in the way. I
  8968	thought of my fifty-guinea fee, of my wearisome journey, and of
  8969	the unpleasant night which seemed to be before me. Was it all to
  8970	go for nothing? Why should I slink away without having carried
  8971	out my commission, and without the payment which was my due? This
  8972	woman might, for all I knew, be a monomaniac. With a stout
  8973	bearing, therefore, though her manner had shaken me more than I
  8974	cared to confess, I still shook my head and declared my intention
  8975	of remaining where I was. She was about to renew her entreaties
  8976	when a door slammed overhead, and the sound of several footsteps
  8977	was heard upon the stairs. She listened for an instant, threw up
  8978	her hands with a despairing gesture, and vanished as suddenly and
  8979	as noiselessly as she had come.
  8980	
  8981	"The newcomers were Colonel Lysander Stark and a short thick man
  8982	with a chinchilla beard growing out of the creases of his double
  8983	chin, who was introduced to me as Mr. Ferguson.
  8984	
  8985	"'This is my secretary and manager,' said the colonel. 'By the
  8986	way, I was under the impression that I left this door shut just
  8987	now. I fear that you have felt the draught.'
  8988	
  8989	"'On the contrary,' said I, 'I opened the door myself because I
  8990	felt the room to be a little close.'
  8991	
  8992	"He shot one of his suspicious looks at me. 'Perhaps we had
  8993	better proceed to business, then,' said he. 'Mr. Ferguson and I
  8994	will take you up to see the machine.'
  8995	
  8996	"'I had better put my hat on, I suppose.'
  8997	
  8998	"'Oh, no, it is in the house.'
  8999	
  9000	"'What, you dig fuller's-earth in the house?'
  9001	
  9002	"'No, no. This is only where we compress it. But never mind that.
  9003	All we wish you to do is to examine the machine and to let us
  9004	know what is wrong with it.'
  9005	
  9006	"We went upstairs together, the colonel first with the lamp, the
  9007	fat manager and I behind him. It was a labyrinth of an old house,
  9008	with corridors, passages, narrow winding staircases, and little
  9009	low doors, the thresholds of which were hollowed out by the
  9010	generations who had crossed them. There were no carpets and no
  9011	signs of any furniture above the ground floor, while the plaster
  9012	was peeling off the walls, and the damp was breaking through in
  9013	green, unhealthy blotches. I tried to put on as unconcerned an
  9014	air as possible, but I had not forgotten the warnings of the
  9015	lady, even though I disregarded them, and I kept a keen eye upon
  9016	my two companions. Ferguson appeared to be a morose and silent
  9017	man, but I could see from the little that he said that he was at
  9018	least a fellow-countryman.
  9019	
  9020	"Colonel Lysander Stark stopped at last before a low door, which
  9021	he unlocked. Within was a small, square room, in which the three
  9022	of us could hardly get at one time. Ferguson remained outside,
  9023	and the colonel ushered me in.
  9024	
  9025	"'We are now,' said he, 'actually within the hydraulic press, and
  9026	it would be a particularly unpleasant thing for us if anyone were
  9027	to turn it on. The ceiling of this small chamber is really the
  9028	end of the descending piston, and it comes down with the force of
  9029	many tons upon this metal floor. There are small lateral columns
  9030	of water outside which receive the force, and which transmit and
  9031	multiply it in the manner which is familiar to you. The machine
  9032	goes readily enough, but there is some stiffness in the working
  9033	of it, and it has lost a little of its force. Perhaps you will
  9034	have the goodness to look it over and to show us how we can set
  9035	it right.'
  9036	
  9037	"I took the lamp from him, and I examined the machine very
  9038	thoroughly. It was indeed a gigantic one, and capable of
  9039	exercising enormous pressure. When I passed outside, however, and
  9040	pressed down the levers which controlled it, I knew at once by
  9041	the whishing sound that there was a slight leakage, which allowed
  9042	a regurgitation of water through one of the side cylinders. An
  9043	examination showed that one of the india-rubber bands which was
  9044	round the head of a driving-rod had shrunk so as not quite to
  9045	fill the socket along which it worked. This was clearly the cause
  9046	of the loss of power, and I pointed it out to my companions, who
  9047	followed my remarks very carefully and asked several practical
  9048	questions as to how they should proceed to set it right. When I
  9049	had made it clear to them, I returned to the main chamber of the
  9050	machine and took a good look at it to satisfy my own curiosity.
  9051	It was obvious at a glance that the story of the fuller's-earth
  9052	was the merest fabrication, for it would be absurd to suppose
  9053	that so powerful an engine could be designed for so inadequate a
  9054	purpose. The walls were of wood, but the floor consisted of a
  9055	large iron trough, and when I came to examine it I could see a
  9056	crust of metallic deposit all over it. I had stooped and was
  9057	scraping at this to see exactly what it was when I heard a
  9058	muttered exclamation in German and saw the cadaverous face of the
  9059	colonel looking down at me.
  9060	
  9061	"'What are you doing there?' he asked.
  9062	
  9063	"I felt angry at having been tricked by so elaborate a story as
  9064	that which he had told me. 'I was admiring your fuller's-earth,'
  9065	said I; 'I think that I should be better able to advise you as to
  9066	your machine if I knew what the exact purpose was for which it
  9067	was used.'
  9068	
  9069	"The instant that I uttered the words I regretted the rashness of
  9070	my speech. His face set hard, and a baleful light sprang up in
  9071	his grey eyes.
  9072	
  9073	"'Very well,' said he, 'you shall know all about the machine.' He
  9074	took a step backward, slammed the little door, and turned the key
  9075	in the lock. I rushed towards it and pulled at the handle, but it
  9076	was quite secure, and did not give in the least to my kicks and
  9077	shoves. 'Hullo!' I yelled. 'Hullo! Colonel! Let me out!'
  9078	
  9079	"And then suddenly in the silence I heard a sound which sent my
  9080	heart into my mouth. It was the clank of the levers and the swish
  9081	of the leaking cylinder. He had set the engine at work. The lamp
  9082	still stood upon the floor where I had placed it when examining
  9083	the trough. By its light I saw that the black ceiling was coming
  9084	down upon me, slowly, jerkily, but, as none knew better than
  9085	myself, with a force which must within a minute grind me to a
  9086	shapeless pulp. I threw myself, screaming, against the door, and
  9087	dragged with my nails at the lock. I implored the colonel to let
  9088	me out, but the remorseless clanking of the levers drowned my
  9089	cries. The ceiling was only a foot or two above my head, and with
  9090	my hand upraised I could feel its hard, rough surface. Then it
  9091	flashed through my mind that the pain of my death would depend
  9092	very much upon the position in which I met it. If I lay on my
  9093	face the weight would come upon my spine, and I shuddered to
  9094	think of that dreadful snap. Easier the other way, perhaps; and
  9095	yet, had I the nerve to lie and look up at that deadly black
  9096	shadow wavering down upon me? Already I was unable to stand
  9097	erect, when my eye caught something which brought a gush of hope
  9098	back to my heart.
  9099	
  9100	"I have said that though the floor and ceiling were of iron, the
  9101	walls were of wood. As I gave a last hurried glance around, I saw
  9102	a thin line of yellow light between two of the boards, which
  9103	broadened and broadened as a small panel was pushed backward. For
  9104	an instant I could hardly believe that here was indeed a door
  9105	which led away from death. The next instant I threw myself
  9106	through, and lay half-fainting upon the other side. The panel had
  9107	closed again behind me, but the crash of the lamp, and a few
  9108	moments afterwards the clang of the two slabs of metal, told me
  9109	how narrow had been my escape.
  9110	
  9111	"I was recalled to myself by a frantic plucking at my wrist, and
  9112	I found myself lying upon the stone floor of a narrow corridor,
  9113	while a woman bent over me and tugged at me with her left hand,
  9114	while she held a candle in her right. It was the same good friend
  9115	whose warning I had so foolishly rejected.
  9116	
  9117	"'Come! come!' she cried breathlessly. 'They will be here in a
  9118	moment. They will see that you are not there. Oh, do not waste
  9119	the so-precious time, but come!'
  9120	
  9121	"This time, at least, I did not scorn her advice. I staggered to
  9122	my feet and ran with her along the corridor and down a winding
  9123	stair. The latter led to another broad passage, and just as we
  9124	reached it we heard the sound of running feet and the shouting of
  9125	two voices, one answering the other from the floor on which  we
  9126	were and from the one beneath. My guide stopped and looked about
  9127	her like one  who is at her wit's end. Then she threw open a door
  9128	which led into a bedroom, through the window of which the moon
  9129	was shining brightly.
  9130	
  9131	"'It is your only chance,' said she. 'It is high, but it may be
  9132	that you can jump it.'
  9133	
  9134	"As she spoke a light sprang into view at the further end of the
  9135	passage, and I saw the lean figure of Colonel Lysander Stark
  9136	rushing forward with a lantern in one hand and a weapon like a
  9137	butcher's cleaver in the other. I rushed across the bedroom,
  9138	flung open the window, and looked out. How quiet and sweet and
  9139	wholesome the garden looked in the moonlight, and it could not be
  9140	more than thirty feet down. I clambered out upon the sill, but I
  9141	hesitated to jump until I should have heard what passed between
  9142	my saviour and the ruffian who pursued me. If she were ill-used,
  9143	then at any risks I was determined to go back to her assistance.
  9144	The thought had hardly flashed through my mind before he was at
  9145	the door, pushing his way past her; but she threw her arms round
  9146	him and tried to hold him back.
  9147	
  9148	"'Fritz! Fritz!' she cried in English, 'remember your promise
  9149	after the last time. You said it should not be again. He will be
  9150	silent! Oh, he will be silent!'
  9151	
  9152	"'You are mad, Elise!' he shouted, struggling to break away from
  9153	her. 'You will be the ruin of us. He has seen too much. Let me
  9154	pass, I say!' He dashed her to one side, and, rushing to the
  9155	window, cut at me with his heavy weapon. I had let myself go, and
  9156	was hanging by the hands to the sill, when his blow fell. I was
  9157	conscious of a dull pain, my grip loosened, and I fell into the
  9158	garden below.
  9159	
  9160	"I was shaken but not hurt by the fall; so I picked myself up and
  9161	rushed off among the bushes as hard as I could run, for I
  9162	understood that I was far from being out of danger yet. Suddenly,
  9163	however, as I ran, a deadly dizziness and sickness came over me.
  9164	I glanced down at my hand, which was throbbing painfully, and
  9165	then, for the first time, saw that my thumb had been cut off and
  9166	that the blood was pouring from my wound. I endeavoured to tie my
  9167	handkerchief round it, but there came a sudden buzzing in my
  9168	ears, and next moment I fell in a dead faint among the
  9169	rose-bushes.
  9170	
  9171	"How long I remained unconscious I cannot tell. It must have been
  9172	a very long time, for the moon had sunk, and a bright morning was
  9173	breaking when I came to myself. My clothes were all sodden with
  9174	dew, and my coat-sleeve was drenched with blood from my wounded
  9175	thumb. The smarting of it recalled in an instant all the
  9176	particulars of my night's adventure, and I sprang to my feet with
  9177	the feeling that I might hardly yet be safe from my pursuers. But
  9178	to my astonishment, when I came to look round me, neither house
  9179	nor garden were to be seen. I had been lying in an angle of the
  9180	hedge close by the highroad, and just a little lower down was a
  9181	long building, which proved, upon my approaching it, to be the
  9182	very station at which I had arrived upon the previous night. Were
  9183	it not for the ugly wound upon my hand, all that had passed
  9184	during those dreadful hours might have been an evil dream.
  9185	
  9186	"Half dazed, I went into the station and asked about the morning
  9187	train. There would be one to Reading in less than an hour. The
  9188	same porter was on duty, I found, as had been there when I
  9189	arrived. I inquired of him whether he had ever heard of Colonel
  9190	Lysander Stark. The name was strange to him. Had he observed a
  9191	carriage the night before waiting for me? No, he had not. Was
  9192	there a police-station anywhere near? There was one about three
  9193	miles off.
  9194	
  9195	"It was too far for me to go, weak and ill as I was. I determined
  9196	to wait until I got back to town before telling my story to the
  9197	police. It was a little past six when I arrived, so I went first
  9198	to have my wound dressed, and then the doctor was kind enough to
  9199	bring me along here. I put the case into your hands and shall do
  9200	exactly what you advise."
  9201	
  9202	We both sat in silence for some little time after listening to
  9203	this extraordinary narrative. Then Sherlock Holmes pulled down
  9204	from the shelf one of the ponderous commonplace books in which he
  9205	placed his cuttings.
  9206	
  9207	"Here is an advertisement which will interest you," said he. "It
  9208	appeared in all the papers about a year ago. Listen to this:
  9209	'Lost, on the 9th inst., Mr. Jeremiah Hayling, aged
  9210	twenty-six, a hydraulic engineer. Left his lodgings at ten
  9211	o'clock at night, and has not been heard of since. Was
  9212	dressed in,' etc., etc. Ha! That represents the last time that
  9213	the colonel needed to have his machine overhauled, I fancy."
  9214	
  9215	"Good heavens!" cried my patient. "Then that explains what the
  9216	girl said."
  9217	
  9218	"Undoubtedly. It is quite clear that the colonel was a cool and
  9219	desperate man, who was absolutely determined that nothing should
  9220	stand in the way of his little game, like those out-and-out
  9221	pirates who will leave no survivor from a captured ship. Well,
  9222	every moment now is precious, so if you feel equal to it we shall
