  9223	go down to Scotland Yard at once as a preliminary to starting for
  9224	Eyford."
  9225	
  9226	Some three hours or so afterwards we were all in the train
  9227	together, bound from Reading to the little Berkshire village.
  9228	There were Sherlock Holmes, the hydraulic engineer, Inspector
  9229	Bradstreet, of Scotland Yard, a plain-clothes man, and myself.
  9230	Bradstreet had spread an ordnance map of the county out upon the
  9231	seat and was busy with his compasses drawing a circle with Eyford
  9232	for its centre.
  9233	
  9234	"There you are," said he. "That circle is drawn at a radius of
  9235	ten miles from the village. The place we want must be somewhere
  9236	near that line. You said ten miles, I think, sir."
  9237	
  9238	"It was an hour's good drive."
  9239	
  9240	"And you think that they brought you back all that way when you
  9241	were unconscious?"
  9242	
  9243	"They must have done so. I have a confused memory, too, of having
  9244	been lifted and conveyed somewhere."
  9245	
  9246	"What I cannot understand," said I, "is why they should have
  9247	spared you when they found you lying fainting in the garden.
  9248	Perhaps the villain was softened by the woman's entreaties."
  9249	
  9250	"I hardly think that likely. I never saw a more inexorable face
  9251	in my life."
  9252	
  9253	"Oh, we shall soon clear up all that," said Bradstreet. "Well, I
  9254	have drawn my circle, and I only wish I knew at what point upon
  9255	it the folk that we are in search of are to be found."
  9256	
  9257	"I think I could lay my finger on it," said Holmes quietly.
  9258	
  9259	"Really, now!" cried the inspector, "you have formed your
  9260	opinion! Come, now, we shall see who agrees with you. I say it is
  9261	south, for the country is more deserted there."
  9262	
  9263	"And I say east," said my patient.
  9264	
  9265	"I am for west," remarked the plain-clothes man. "There are
  9266	several quiet little villages up there."
  9267	
  9268	"And I am for north," said I, "because there are no hills there,
  9269	and our friend says that he did not notice the carriage go up
  9270	any."
  9271	
  9272	"Come," cried the inspector, laughing; "it's a very pretty
  9273	diversity of opinion. We have boxed the compass among us. Who do
  9274	you give your casting vote to?"
  9275	
  9276	"You are all wrong."
  9277	
  9278	"But we can't all be."
  9279	
  9280	"Oh, yes, you can. This is my point." He placed his finger in the
  9281	centre of the circle. "This is where we shall find them."
  9282	
  9283	"But the twelve-mile drive?" gasped Hatherley.
  9284	
  9285	"Six out and six back. Nothing simpler. You say yourself that the
  9286	horse was fresh and glossy when you got in. How could it be that
  9287	if it had gone twelve miles over heavy roads?"
  9288	
  9289	"Indeed, it is a likely ruse enough," observed Bradstreet
  9290	thoughtfully. "Of course there can be no doubt as to the nature
  9291	of this gang."
  9292	
  9293	"None at all," said Holmes. "They are coiners on a large scale,
  9294	and have used the machine to form the amalgam which has taken the
  9295	place of silver."
  9296	
  9297	"We have known for some time that a clever gang was at work,"
  9298	said the inspector. "They have been turning out half-crowns by
  9299	the thousand. We even traced them as far as Reading, but could
  9300	get no farther, for they had covered their traces in a way that
  9301	showed that they were very old hands. But now, thanks to this
  9302	lucky chance, I think that we have got them right enough."
  9303	
  9304	But the inspector was mistaken, for those criminals were not
  9305	destined to fall into the hands of justice. As we rolled into
  9306	Eyford Station we saw a gigantic column of smoke which streamed
  9307	up from behind a small clump of trees in the neighbourhood and
  9308	hung like an immense ostrich feather over the landscape.
  9309	
  9310	"A house on fire?" asked Bradstreet as the train steamed off
  9311	again on its way.
  9312	
  9313	"Yes, sir!" said the station-master.
  9314	
  9315	"When did it break out?"
  9316	
  9317	"I hear that it was during the night, sir, but it has got worse,
  9318	and the whole place is in a blaze."
  9319	
  9320	"Whose house is it?"
  9321	
  9322	"Dr. Becher's."
  9323	
  9324	"Tell me," broke in the engineer, "is Dr. Becher a German, very
  9325	thin, with a long, sharp nose?"
  9326	
  9327	The station-master laughed heartily. "No, sir, Dr. Becher is an
  9328	Englishman, and there isn't a man in the parish who has a
  9329	better-lined waistcoat. But he has a gentleman staying with him,
  9330	a patient, as I understand, who is a foreigner, and he looks as
  9331	if a little good Berkshire beef would do him no harm."
  9332	
  9333	The station-master had not finished his speech before we were all
  9334	hastening in the direction of the fire. The road topped a low
  9335	hill, and there was a great widespread whitewashed building in
  9336	front of us, spouting fire at every chink and window, while in
  9337	the garden in front three fire-engines were vainly striving to
  9338	keep the flames under.
  9339	
  9340	"That's it!" cried Hatherley, in intense excitement. "There is
  9341	the gravel-drive, and there are the rose-bushes where I lay. That
  9342	second window is the one that I jumped from."
  9343	
  9344	"Well, at least," said Holmes, "you have had your revenge upon
  9345	them. There can be no question that it was your oil-lamp which,
  9346	when it was crushed in the press, set fire to the wooden walls,
  9347	though no doubt they were too excited in the chase after you to
  9348	observe it at the time. Now keep your eyes open in this crowd for
  9349	your friends of last night, though I very much fear that they are
  9350	a good hundred miles off by now."
  9351	
  9352	And Holmes' fears came to be realised, for from that day to this
  9353	no word has ever been heard either of the beautiful woman, the
  9354	sinister German, or the morose Englishman. Early that morning a
  9355	peasant had met a cart containing several people and some very
  9356	bulky boxes driving rapidly in the direction of Reading, but
  9357	there all traces of the fugitives disappeared, and even Holmes'
  9358	ingenuity failed ever to discover the least clue as to their
  9359	whereabouts.
  9360	
  9361	The firemen had been much perturbed at the strange arrangements
  9362	which they had found within, and still more so by discovering a
  9363	newly severed human thumb upon a window-sill of the second floor.
  9364	About sunset, however, their efforts were at last successful, and
  9365	they subdued the flames, but not before the roof had fallen in,
  9366	and the whole place been reduced to such absolute ruin that, save
  9367	some twisted cylinders and iron piping, not a trace remained of
  9368	the machinery which had cost our unfortunate acquaintance so
  9369	dearly. Large masses of nickel and of tin were discovered stored
  9370	in an out-house, but no coins were to be found, which may have
  9371	explained the presence of those bulky boxes which have been
  9372	already referred to.
  9373	
  9374	How our hydraulic engineer had been conveyed from the garden to
  9375	the spot where he recovered his senses might have remained
  9376	forever a mystery were it not for the soft mould, which told us a
  9377	very plain tale. He had evidently been carried down by two
  9378	persons, one of whom had remarkably small feet and the other
  9379	unusually large ones. On the whole, it was most probable that the
  9380	silent Englishman, being less bold or less murderous than his
  9381	companion, had assisted the woman to bear the unconscious man out
  9382	of the way of danger.
  9383	
  9384	"Well," said our engineer ruefully as we took our seats to return
  9385	once more to London, "it has been a pretty business for me! I
  9386	have lost my thumb and I have lost a fifty-guinea fee, and what
  9387	have I gained?"
  9388	
  9389	"Experience," said Holmes, laughing. "Indirectly it may be of
  9390	value, you know; you have only to put it into words to gain the
  9391	reputation of being excellent company for the remainder of your
  9392	existence."
  9393	
  9394	
  9395	
  9396	X. THE ADVENTURE OF THE NOBLE BACHELOR
  9397	
  9398	The Lord St. Simon marriage, and its curious termination, have
  9399	long ceased to be a subject of interest in those exalted circles
  9400	in which the unfortunate bridegroom moves. Fresh scandals have
  9401	eclipsed it, and their more piquant details have drawn the
  9402	gossips away from this four-year-old drama. As I have reason to
  9403	believe, however, that the full facts have never been revealed to
  9404	the general public, and as my friend Sherlock Holmes had a
  9405	considerable share in clearing the matter up, I feel that no
  9406	memoir of him would be complete without some little sketch of
  9407	this remarkable episode.
  9408	
  9409	It was a few weeks before my own marriage, during the days when I
  9410	was still sharing rooms with Holmes in Baker Street, that he came
  9411	home from an afternoon stroll to find a letter on the table
  9412	waiting for him. I had remained indoors all day, for the weather
  9413	had taken a sudden turn to rain, with high autumnal winds, and
  9414	the Jezail bullet which I had brought back in one of my limbs as
  9415	a relic of my Afghan campaign throbbed with dull persistence.
  9416	With my body in one easy-chair and my legs upon another, I had
  9417	surrounded myself with a cloud of newspapers until at last,
  9418	saturated with the news of the day, I tossed them all aside and
  9419	lay listless, watching the huge crest and monogram upon the
  9420	envelope upon the table and wondering lazily who my friend's
  9421	noble correspondent could be.
  9422	
  9423	"Here is a very fashionable epistle," I remarked as he entered.
  9424	"Your morning letters, if I remember right, were from a
  9425	fish-monger and a tide-waiter."
  9426	
  9427	"Yes, my correspondence has certainly the charm of variety," he
  9428	answered, smiling, "and the humbler are usually the more
  9429	interesting. This looks like one of those unwelcome social
  9430	summonses which call upon a man either to be bored or to lie."
  9431	
  9432	He broke the seal and glanced over the contents.
  9433	
  9434	"Oh, come, it may prove to be something of interest, after all."
  9435	
  9436	"Not social, then?"
  9437	
  9438	"No, distinctly professional."
  9439	
  9440	"And from a noble client?"
  9441	
  9442	"One of the highest in England."
  9443	
  9444	"My dear fellow, I congratulate you."
  9445	
  9446	"I assure you, Watson, without affectation, that the status of my
  9447	client is a matter of less moment to me than the interest of his
  9448	case. It is just possible, however, that that also may not be
  9449	wanting in this new investigation. You have been reading the
  9450	papers diligently of late, have you not?"
  9451	
  9452	"It looks like it," said I ruefully, pointing to a huge bundle in
  9453	the corner. "I have had nothing else to do."
  9454	
  9455	"It is fortunate, for you will perhaps be able to post me up. I
  9456	read nothing except the criminal news and the agony column. The
  9457	latter is always instructive. But if you have followed recent
  9458	events so closely you must have read about Lord St. Simon and his
  9459	wedding?"
  9460	
  9461	"Oh, yes, with the deepest interest."
  9462	
  9463	"That is well. The letter which I hold in my hand is from Lord
  9464	St. Simon. I will read it to you, and in return you must turn
  9465	over these papers and let me have whatever bears upon the matter.
  9466	This is what he says:
  9467	
  9468	"'MY DEAR MR. SHERLOCK HOLMES:--Lord Backwater tells me that I
  9469	may place implicit reliance upon your judgment and discretion. I
  9470	have determined, therefore, to call upon you and to consult you
  9471	in reference to the very painful event which has occurred in
  9472	connection with my wedding. Mr. Lestrade, of Scotland Yard, is
  9473	acting already in the matter, but he assures me that he sees no
  9474	objection to your co-operation, and that he even thinks that
  9475	it might be of some assistance. I will call at four o'clock in
  9476	the afternoon, and, should you have any other engagement at that
  9477	time, I hope that you will postpone it, as this matter is of
  9478	paramount importance. Yours faithfully, ST. SIMON.'
  9479	
  9480	"It is dated from Grosvenor Mansions, written with a quill pen,
  9481	and the noble lord has had the misfortune to get a smear of ink
  9482	upon the outer side of his right little finger," remarked Holmes
  9483	as he folded up the epistle.
  9484	
  9485	"He says four o'clock. It is three now. He will be here in an
  9486	hour."
  9487	
  9488	"Then I have just time, with your assistance, to get clear upon
  9489	the subject. Turn over those papers and arrange the extracts in
  9490	their order of time, while I take a glance as to who our client
  9491	is." He picked a red-covered volume from a line of books of
  9492	reference beside the mantelpiece. "Here he is," said he, sitting
  9493	down and flattening it out upon his knee. "'Lord Robert Walsingham
  9494	de Vere St. Simon, second son of the Duke of Balmoral.' Hum! 'Arms:
  9495	Azure, three caltrops in chief over a fess sable. Born in 1846.'
  9496	He's forty-one years of age, which is mature for marriage. Was
  9497	Under-Secretary for the colonies in a late administration. The
  9498	Duke, his father, was at one time Secretary for Foreign Affairs.
  9499	They inherit Plantagenet blood by direct descent, and Tudor on
  9500	the distaff side. Ha! Well, there is nothing very instructive in
  9501	all this. I think that I must turn to you Watson, for something
  9502	more solid."
  9503	
  9504	"I have very little difficulty in finding what I want," said I,
  9505	"for the facts are quite recent, and the matter struck me as
  9506	remarkable. I feared to refer them to you, however, as I knew
  9507	that you had an inquiry on hand and that you disliked the
  9508	intrusion of other matters."
  9509	
  9510	"Oh, you mean the little problem of the Grosvenor Square
  9511	furniture van. That is quite cleared up now--though, indeed, it
  9512	was obvious from the first. Pray give me the results of your
  9513	newspaper selections."
  9514	
  9515	"Here is the first notice which I can find. It is in the personal
  9516	column of the Morning Post, and dates, as you see, some weeks
  9517	back: 'A marriage has been arranged,' it says, 'and will, if
  9518	rumour is correct, very shortly take place, between Lord Robert
  9519	St. Simon, second son of the Duke of Balmoral, and Miss Hatty
  9520	Doran, the only daughter of Aloysius Doran. Esq., of San
  9521	Francisco, Cal., U.S.A.' That is all."
  9522	
  9523	"Terse and to the point," remarked Holmes, stretching his long,
  9524	thin legs towards the fire.
  9525	
  9526	"There was a paragraph amplifying this in one of the society
  9527	papers of the same week. Ah, here it is: 'There will soon be a
  9528	call for protection in the marriage market, for the present
  9529	free-trade principle appears to tell heavily against our home
  9530	product. One by one the management of the noble houses of Great
  9531	Britain is passing into the hands of our fair cousins from across
  9532	the Atlantic. An important addition has been made during the last
  9533	week to the list of the prizes which have been borne away by
  9534	these charming invaders. Lord St. Simon, who has shown himself
  9535	for over twenty years proof against the little god's arrows, has
  9536	now definitely announced his approaching marriage with Miss Hatty
  9537	Doran, the fascinating daughter of a California millionaire. Miss
  9538	Doran, whose graceful figure and striking face attracted much
  9539	attention at the Westbury House festivities, is an only child,
  9540	and it is currently reported that her dowry will run to
  9541	considerably over the six figures, with expectancies for the
  9542	future. As it is an open secret that the Duke of Balmoral has
  9543	been compelled to sell his pictures within the last few years,
  9544	and as Lord St. Simon has no property of his own save the small
  9545	estate of Birchmoor, it is obvious that the Californian heiress
  9546	is not the only gainer by an alliance which will enable her to
  9547	make the easy and common transition from a Republican lady to a
  9548	British peeress.'"
  9549	
  9550	"Anything else?" asked Holmes, yawning.
  9551	
  9552	"Oh, yes; plenty. Then there is another note in the Morning Post
  9553	to say that the marriage would be an absolutely quiet one, that it
  9554	would be at St. George's, Hanover Square, that only half a dozen
  9555	intimate friends would be invited, and that the party would
  9556	return to the furnished house at Lancaster Gate which has been
  9557	taken by Mr. Aloysius Doran. Two days later--that is, on
  9558	Wednesday last--there is a curt announcement that the wedding had
  9559	taken place, and that the honeymoon would be passed at Lord
  9560	Backwater's place, near Petersfield. Those are all the notices
  9561	which appeared before the disappearance of the bride."
  9562	
  9563	"Before the what?" asked Holmes with a start.
  9564	
  9565	"The vanishing of the lady."
  9566	
  9567	"When did she vanish, then?"
  9568	
  9569	"At the wedding breakfast."
  9570	
  9571	"Indeed. This is more interesting than it promised to be; quite
  9572	dramatic, in fact."
  9573	
  9574	"Yes; it struck me as being a little out of the common."
  9575	
  9576	"They often vanish before the ceremony, and occasionally during
  9577	the honeymoon; but I cannot call to mind anything quite so prompt
  9578	as this. Pray let me have the details."
  9579	
  9580	"I warn you that they are very incomplete."
  9581	
  9582	"Perhaps we may make them less so."
  9583	
  9584	"Such as they are, they are set forth in a single article of a
  9585	morning paper of yesterday, which I will read to you. It is
  9586	headed, 'Singular Occurrence at a Fashionable Wedding':
  9587	
  9588	"'The family of Lord Robert St. Simon has been thrown into the
  9589	greatest consternation by the strange and painful episodes which
  9590	have taken place in connection with his wedding. The ceremony, as
  9591	shortly announced in the papers of yesterday, occurred on the
  9592	previous morning; but it is only now that it has been possible to
  9593	confirm the strange rumours which have been so persistently
  9594	floating about. In spite of the attempts of the friends to hush
  9595	the matter up, so much public attention has now been drawn to it
  9596	that no good purpose can be served by affecting to disregard what
  9597	is a common subject for conversation.
  9598	
  9599	"'The ceremony, which was performed at St. George's, Hanover
  9600	Square, was a very quiet one, no one being present save the
  9601	father of the bride, Mr. Aloysius Doran, the Duchess of Balmoral,
  9602	Lord Backwater, Lord Eustace and Lady Clara St. Simon (the
  9603	younger brother and sister of the bridegroom), and Lady Alicia
  9604	Whittington. The whole party proceeded afterwards to the house of
  9605	Mr. Aloysius Doran, at Lancaster Gate, where breakfast had been
  9606	prepared. It appears that some little trouble was caused by a
  9607	woman, whose name has not been ascertained, who endeavoured to
  9608	force her way into the house after the bridal party, alleging
  9609	that she had some claim upon Lord St. Simon. It was only after a
  9610	painful and prolonged scene that she was ejected by the butler
  9611	and the footman. The bride, who had fortunately entered the house
  9612	before this unpleasant interruption, had sat down to breakfast
  9613	with the rest, when she complained of a sudden indisposition and
  9614	retired to her room. Her prolonged absence having caused some
  9615	comment, her father followed her, but learned from her maid that
  9616	she had only come up to her chamber for an instant, caught up an
  9617	ulster and bonnet, and hurried down to the passage. One of the
  9618	footmen declared that he had seen a lady leave the house thus
  9619	apparelled, but had refused to credit that it was his mistress,
  9620	believing her to be with the company. On ascertaining that his
  9621	daughter had disappeared, Mr. Aloysius Doran, in conjunction with
  9622	the bridegroom, instantly put themselves in communication with
  9623	the police, and very energetic inquiries are being made, which
  9624	will probably result in a speedy clearing up of this very
  9625	singular business. Up to a late hour last night, however, nothing
  9626	had transpired as to the whereabouts of the missing lady. There
  9627	are rumours of foul play in the matter, and it is said that the
  9628	police have caused the arrest of the woman who had caused the
  9629	original disturbance, in the belief that, from jealousy or some
  9630	other motive, she may have been concerned in the strange
  9631	disappearance of the bride.'"
  9632	
  9633	"And is that all?"
  9634	
  9635	"Only one little item in another of the morning papers, but it is
  9636	a suggestive one."
  9637	
  9638	"And it is--"
  9639	
  9640	"That Miss Flora Millar, the lady who had caused the disturbance,
  9641	has actually been arrested. It appears that she was formerly a
  9642	danseuse at the Allegro, and that she has known the bridegroom
  9643	for some years. There are no further particulars, and the whole
  9644	case is in your hands now--so far as it has been set forth in the
  9645	public press."
  9646	
  9647	"And an exceedingly interesting case it appears to be. I would
  9648	not have missed it for worlds. But there is a ring at the bell,
  9649	Watson, and as the clock makes it a few minutes after four, I
  9650	have no doubt that this will prove to be our noble client. Do not
  9651	dream of going, Watson, for I very much prefer having a witness,
  9652	if only as a check to my own memory."
  9653	
  9654	"Lord Robert St. Simon," announced our page-boy, throwing open
  9655	the door. A gentleman entered, with a pleasant, cultured face,
  9656	high-nosed and pale, with something perhaps of petulance about
  9657	the mouth, and with the steady, well-opened eye of a man whose
  9658	pleasant lot it had ever been to command and to be obeyed. His
  9659	manner was brisk, and yet his general appearance gave an undue
  9660	impression of age, for he had a slight forward stoop and a little
  9661	bend of the knees as he walked. His hair, too, as he swept off
  9662	his very curly-brimmed hat, was grizzled round the edges and thin
  9663	upon the top. As to his dress, it was careful to the verge of
  9664	foppishness, with high collar, black frock-coat, white waistcoat,
  9665	yellow gloves, patent-leather shoes, and light-coloured gaiters.
  9666	He advanced slowly into the room, turning his head from left to
  9667	right, and swinging in his right hand the cord which held his
  9668	golden eyeglasses.
  9669	
  9670	"Good-day, Lord St. Simon," said Holmes, rising and bowing. "Pray
  9671	take the basket-chair. This is my friend and colleague, Dr.
  9672	Watson. Draw up a little to the fire, and we will talk this
  9673	matter over."
  9674	
  9675	"A most painful matter to me, as you can most readily imagine,
  9676	Mr. Holmes. I have been cut to the quick. I understand that you
  9677	have already managed several delicate cases of this sort, sir,
  9678	though I presume that they were hardly from the same class of
  9679	society."
  9680	
  9681	"No, I am descending."
  9682	
  9683	"I beg pardon."
  9684	
  9685	"My last client of the sort was a king."
  9686	
  9687	"Oh, really! I had no idea. And which king?"
  9688	
  9689	"The King of Scandinavia."
  9690	
  9691	"What! Had he lost his wife?"
  9692	
  9693	"You can understand," said Holmes suavely, "that I extend to the
  9694	affairs of my other clients the same secrecy which I promise to
  9695	you in yours."
  9696	
  9697	"Of course! Very right! very right! I'm sure I beg pardon. As to
  9698	my own case, I am ready to give you any information which may
  9699	assist you in forming an opinion."
  9700	
  9701	"Thank you. I have already learned all that is in the public
  9702	prints, nothing more. I presume that I may take it as correct--
  9703	this article, for example, as to the disappearance of the bride."
  9704	
  9705	Lord St. Simon glanced over it. "Yes, it is correct, as far as it
  9706	goes."
  9707	
  9708	"But it needs a great deal of supplementing before anyone could
  9709	offer an opinion. I think that I may arrive at my facts most
  9710	directly by questioning you."
  9711	
  9712	"Pray do so."
  9713	
  9714	"When did you first meet Miss Hatty Doran?"
  9715	
  9716	"In San Francisco, a year ago."
  9717	
  9718	"You were travelling in the States?"
  9719	
  9720	"Yes."
  9721	
  9722	"Did you become engaged then?"
  9723	
  9724	"No."
  9725	
  9726	"But you were on a friendly footing?"
  9727	
  9728	"I was amused by her society, and she could see that I was
  9729	amused."
  9730	
  9731	"Her father is very rich?"
  9732	
  9733	"He is said to be the richest man on the Pacific slope."
  9734	
  9735	"And how did he make his money?"
  9736	
  9737	"In mining. He had nothing a few years ago. Then he struck gold,
  9738	invested it, and came up by leaps and bounds."
  9739	
  9740	"Now, what is your own impression as to the young lady's--your
  9741	wife's character?"
  9742	
  9743	The nobleman swung his glasses a little faster and stared down
  9744	into the fire. "You see, Mr. Holmes," said he, "my wife was
  9745	twenty before her father became a rich man. During that time she
  9746	ran free in a mining camp and wandered through woods or
  9747	mountains, so that her education has come from Nature rather than
  9748	from the schoolmaster. She is what we call in England a tomboy,
  9749	with a strong nature, wild and free, unfettered by any sort of
  9750	traditions. She is impetuous--volcanic, I was about to say. She
  9751	is swift in making up her mind and fearless in carrying out her
  9752	resolutions. On the other hand, I would not have given her the
  9753	name which I have the honour to bear"--he gave a little stately
  9754	cough--"had not I thought her to be at bottom a noble woman. I
  9755	believe that she is capable of heroic self-sacrifice and that
  9756	anything dishonourable would be repugnant to her."
  9757	
  9758	"Have you her photograph?"
  9759	
  9760	"I brought this with me." He opened a locket and showed us the
  9761	full face of a very lovely woman. It was not a photograph but an
  9762	ivory miniature, and the artist had brought out the full effect
  9763	of the lustrous black hair, the large dark eyes, and the
  9764	exquisite mouth. Holmes gazed long and earnestly at it. Then he
  9765	closed the locket and handed it back to Lord St. Simon.
  9766	
  9767	"The young lady came to London, then, and you renewed your
  9768	acquaintance?"
  9769	
  9770	"Yes, her father brought her over for this last London season. I
  9771	met her several times, became engaged to her, and have now
  9772	married her."
  9773	
  9774	"She brought, I understand, a considerable dowry?"
  9775	
  9776	"A fair dowry. Not more than is usual in my family."
  9777	
  9778	"And this, of course, remains to you, since the marriage is a
  9779	fait accompli?"
  9780	
  9781	"I really have made no inquiries on the subject."
  9782	
  9783	"Very naturally not. Did you see Miss Doran on the day before the
  9784	wedding?"
  9785	
  9786	"Yes."
  9787	
  9788	"Was she in good spirits?"
  9789	
  9790	"Never better. She kept talking of what we should do in our
  9791	future lives."
  9792	
  9793	"Indeed! That is very interesting. And on the morning of the
  9794	wedding?"
  9795	
  9796	"She was as bright as possible--at least until after the
  9797	ceremony."
  9798	
  9799	"And did you observe any change in her then?"
  9800	
  9801	"Well, to tell the truth, I saw then the first signs that I had
  9802	ever seen that her temper was just a little sharp. The incident
  9803	however, was too trivial to relate and can have no possible
  9804	bearing upon the case."
  9805	
  9806	"Pray let us have it, for all that."
  9807	
  9808	"Oh, it is childish. She dropped her bouquet as we went towards
  9809	the vestry. She was passing the front pew at the time, and it
  9810	fell over into the pew. There was a moment's delay, but the
  9811	gentleman in the pew handed it up to her again, and it did not
  9812	appear to be the worse for the fall. Yet when I spoke to her of
  9813	the matter, she answered me abruptly; and in the carriage, on our
  9814	way home, she seemed absurdly agitated over this trifling cause."
  9815	
  9816	"Indeed! You say that there was a gentleman in the pew. Some of
  9817	the general public were present, then?"
  9818	
  9819	"Oh, yes. It is impossible to exclude them when the church is
  9820	open."
  9821	
  9822	"This gentleman was not one of your wife's friends?"
  9823	
  9824	"No, no; I call him a gentleman by courtesy, but he was quite a
  9825	common-looking person. I hardly noticed his appearance. But
  9826	really I think that we are wandering rather far from the point."
  9827	
  9828	"Lady St. Simon, then, returned from the wedding in a less
  9829	cheerful frame of mind than she had gone to it. What did she do
  9830	on re-entering her father's house?"
  9831	
  9832	"I saw her in conversation with her maid."
  9833	
  9834	"And who is her maid?"
  9835	
  9836	"Alice is her name. She is an American and came from California
  9837	with her."
  9838	
  9839	"A confidential servant?"
  9840	
  9841	"A little too much so. It seemed to me that her mistress allowed
  9842	her to take great liberties. Still, of course, in America they
  9843	look upon these things in a different way."
  9844	
  9845	"How long did she speak to this Alice?"
  9846	
  9847	"Oh, a few minutes. I had something else to think of."
  9848	
  9849	"You did not overhear what they said?"
  9850	
  9851	"Lady St. Simon said something about 'jumping a claim.' She was
  9852	accustomed to use slang of the kind. I have no idea what she
  9853	meant."
  9854	
  9855	"American slang is very expressive sometimes. And what did your
  9856	wife do when she finished speaking to her maid?"
  9857	
  9858	"She walked into the breakfast-room."
  9859	
  9860	"On your arm?"
  9861	
  9862	"No, alone. She was very independent in little matters like that.
  9863	Then, after we had sat down for ten minutes or so, she rose
  9864	hurriedly, muttered some words of apology, and left the room. She
  9865	never came back."
  9866	
  9867	"But this maid, Alice, as I understand, deposes that she went to
  9868	her room, covered her bride's dress with a long ulster, put on a
  9869	bonnet, and went out."
  9870	
  9871	"Quite so. And she was afterwards seen walking into Hyde Park in
  9872	company with Flora Millar, a woman who is now in custody, and who
  9873	had already made a disturbance at Mr. Doran's house that
  9874	morning."
  9875	
  9876	"Ah, yes. I should like a few particulars as to this young lady,
  9877	and your relations to her."
  9878	
  9879	Lord St. Simon shrugged his shoulders and raised his eyebrows.
  9880	"We have been on a friendly footing for some years--I may say on
  9881	a very friendly footing. She used to be at the Allegro. I have
  9882	not treated her ungenerously, and she had no just cause of
  9883	complaint against me, but you know what women are, Mr. Holmes.
  9884	Flora was a dear little thing, but exceedingly hot-headed and
  9885	devotedly attached to me. She wrote me dreadful letters when she
  9886	heard that I was about to be married, and, to tell the truth, the
  9887	reason why I had the marriage celebrated so quietly was that I
  9888	feared lest there might be a scandal in the church. She came to
  9889	Mr. Doran's door just after we returned, and she endeavoured to
  9890	push her way in, uttering very abusive expressions towards my
  9891	wife, and even threatening her, but I had foreseen the
  9892	possibility of something of the sort, and I had two police
  9893	fellows there in private clothes, who soon pushed her out again.
  9894	She was quiet when she saw that there was no good in making a
  9895	row."
  9896	
  9897	"Did your wife hear all this?"
  9898	
  9899	"No, thank goodness, she did not."
  9900	
  9901	"And she was seen walking with this very woman afterwards?"
  9902	
  9903	"Yes. That is what Mr. Lestrade, of Scotland Yard, looks upon as
  9904	so serious. It is thought that Flora decoyed my wife out and laid
  9905	some terrible trap for her."
  9906	
  9907	"Well, it is a possible supposition."
  9908	
  9909	"You think so, too?"
  9910	
  9911	"I did not say a probable one. But you do not yourself look upon
  9912	this as likely?"
  9913	
  9914	"I do not think Flora would hurt a fly."
  9915	
  9916	"Still, jealousy is a strange transformer of characters. Pray
  9917	what is your own theory as to what took place?"
  9918	
  9919	"Well, really, I came to seek a theory, not to propound one. I
  9920	have given you all the facts. Since you ask me, however, I may
  9921	say that it has occurred to me as possible that the excitement of
  9922	this affair, the consciousness that she had made so immense a
  9923	social stride, had the effect of causing some little nervous
  9924	disturbance in my wife."
  9925	
  9926	"In short, that she had become suddenly deranged?"
  9927	
  9928	"Well, really, when I consider that she has turned her back--I
  9929	will not say upon me, but upon so much that many have aspired to
  9930	without success--I can hardly explain it in any other fashion."
  9931	
  9932	"Well, certainly that is also a conceivable hypothesis," said
  9933	Holmes, smiling. "And now, Lord St. Simon, I think that I have
  9934	nearly all my data. May I ask whether you were seated at the
  9935	breakfast-table so that you could see out of the window?"
  9936	
  9937	"We could see the other side of the road and the Park."
  9938	
  9939	"Quite so. Then I do not think that I need to detain you longer.
  9940	I shall communicate with you."
  9941	
  9942	"Should you be fortunate enough to solve this problem," said our
  9943	client, rising.
  9944	
  9945	"I have solved it."
  9946	
  9947	"Eh? What was that?"
  9948	
  9949	"I say that I have solved it."
  9950	
  9951	"Where, then, is my wife?"
  9952	
  9953	"That is a detail which I shall speedily supply."
  9954	
  9955	Lord St. Simon shook his head. "I am afraid that it will take
  9956	wiser heads than yours or mine," he remarked, and bowing in a
  9957	stately, old-fashioned manner he departed.
  9958	
  9959	"It is very good of Lord St. Simon to honour my head by putting
  9960	it on a level with his own," said Sherlock Holmes, laughing. "I
  9961	think that I shall have a whisky and soda and a cigar after all
  9962	this cross-questioning. I had formed my conclusions as to the
  9963	case before our client came into the room."
  9964	
  9965	"My dear Holmes!"
  9966	
  9967	"I have notes of several similar cases, though none, as I
  9968	remarked before, which were quite as prompt. My whole examination
  9969	served to turn my conjecture into a certainty. Circumstantial
  9970	evidence is occasionally very convincing, as when you find a
  9971	trout in the milk, to quote Thoreau's example."
  9972	
  9973	"But I have heard all that you have heard."
  9974	
  9975	"Without, however, the knowledge of pre-existing cases which
  9976	serves me so well. There was a parallel instance in Aberdeen some
  9977	years back, and something on very much the same lines at Munich
  9978	the year after the Franco-Prussian War. It is one of these
  9979	cases--but, hullo, here is Lestrade! Good-afternoon, Lestrade!
  9980	You will find an extra tumbler upon the sideboard, and there are
  9981	cigars in the box."
  9982	
  9983	The official detective was attired in a pea-jacket and cravat,
  9984	which gave him a decidedly nautical appearance, and he carried a
  9985	black canvas bag in his hand. With a short greeting he seated
  9986	himself and lit the cigar which had been offered to him.
  9987	
  9988	"What's up, then?" asked Holmes with a twinkle in his eye. "You
  9989	look dissatisfied."
  9990	
  9991	"And I feel dissatisfied. It is this infernal St. Simon marriage
  9992	case. I can make neither head nor tail of the business."
  9993	
  9994	"Really! You surprise me."
  9995	
  9996	"Who ever heard of such a mixed affair? Every clue seems to slip
  9997	through my fingers. I have been at work upon it all day."
  9998	
  9999	"And very wet it seems to have made you," said Holmes laying his
 10000	hand upon the arm of the pea-jacket.
 10001	
 10002	"Yes, I have been dragging the Serpentine."
 10003	
 10004	"In heaven's name, what for?"
 10005	
 10006	"In search of the body of Lady St. Simon."
 10007	
 10008	Sherlock Holmes leaned back in his chair and laughed heartily.
 10009	
 10010	"Have you dragged the basin of Trafalgar Square fountain?" he
 10011	asked.
 10012	
 10013	"Why? What do you mean?"
 10014	
 10015	"Because you have just as good a chance of finding this lady in
 10016	the one as in the other."
 10017	
 10018	Lestrade shot an angry glance at my companion. "I suppose you
 10019	know all about it," he snarled.
 10020	
 10021	"Well, I have only just heard the facts, but my mind is made up."
 10022	
 10023	"Oh, indeed! Then you think that the Serpentine plays no part in
 10024	the matter?"
 10025	
 10026	"I think it very unlikely."
 10027	
 10028	"Then perhaps you will kindly explain how it is that we found
 10029	this in it?" He opened his bag as he spoke, and tumbled onto the
 10030	floor a wedding-dress of watered silk, a pair of white satin
 10031	shoes and a bride's wreath and veil, all discoloured and soaked
 10032	in water. "There," said he, putting a new wedding-ring upon the
 10033	top of the pile. "There is a little nut for you to crack, Master
 10034	Holmes."
 10035	
 10036	"Oh, indeed!" said my friend, blowing blue rings into the air.
 10037	"You dragged them from the Serpentine?"
 10038	
 10039	"No. They were found floating near the margin by a park-keeper.
 10040	They have been identified as her clothes, and it seemed to me
 10041	that if the clothes were there the body would not be far off."
 10042	
 10043	"By the same brilliant reasoning, every man's body is to be found
 10044	in the neighbourhood of his wardrobe. And pray what did you hope
 10045	to arrive at through this?"
 10046	
 10047	"At some evidence implicating Flora Millar in the disappearance."
 10048	
 10049	"I am afraid that you will find it difficult."
 10050	
 10051	"Are you, indeed, now?" cried Lestrade with some bitterness. "I
 10052	am afraid, Holmes, that you are not very practical with your
 10053	deductions and your inferences. You have made two blunders in as
 10054	many minutes. This dress does implicate Miss Flora Millar."
 10055	
 10056	"And how?"
 10057	
 10058	"In the dress is a pocket. In the pocket is a card-case. In the
 10059	card-case is a note. And here is the very note." He slapped it
 10060	down upon the table in front of him. "Listen to this: 'You will
 10061	see me when all is ready. Come at once. F.H.M.' Now my theory all
 10062	along has been that Lady St. Simon was decoyed away by Flora
 10063	Millar, and that she, with confederates, no doubt, was
 10064	responsible for her disappearance. Here, signed with her
 10065	initials, is the very note which was no doubt quietly slipped
 10066	into her hand at the door and which lured her within their
 10067	reach."
 10068	
 10069	"Very good, Lestrade," said Holmes, laughing. "You really are
 10070	very fine indeed. Let me see it." He took up the paper in a
 10071	listless way, but his attention instantly became riveted, and he
 10072	gave a little cry of satisfaction. "This is indeed important,"
 10073	said he.
 10074	
 10075	"Ha! you find it so?"
 10076	
 10077	"Extremely so. I congratulate you warmly."
 10078	
 10079	Lestrade rose in his triumph and bent his head to look. "Why," he
 10080	shrieked, "you're looking at the wrong side!"
 10081	
 10082	"On the contrary, this is the right side."
 10083	
 10084	"The right side? You're mad! Here is the note written in pencil
 10085	over here."
 10086	
 10087	"And over here is what appears to be the fragment of a hotel
 10088	bill, which interests me deeply."
 10089	
 10090	"There's nothing in it. I looked at it before," said Lestrade.
 10091	"'Oct. 4th, rooms 8s., breakfast 2s. 6d., cocktail 1s., lunch 2s.
 10092	6d., glass sherry, 8d.' I see nothing in that."
 10093	
 10094	"Very likely not. It is most important, all the same. As to the
 10095	note, it is important also, or at least the initials are, so I
 10096	congratulate you again."
 10097	
 10098	"I've wasted time enough," said Lestrade, rising. "I believe in
 10099	hard work and not in sitting by the fire spinning fine theories.
 10100	Good-day, Mr. Holmes, and we shall see which gets to the bottom
 10101	of the matter first." He gathered up the garments, thrust them
 10102	into the bag, and made for the door.
 10103	
 10104	"Just one hint to you, Lestrade," drawled Holmes before his rival
 10105	vanished; "I will tell you the true solution of the matter. Lady
 10106	St. Simon is a myth. There is not, and there never has been, any
 10107	such person."
 10108	
 10109	Lestrade looked sadly at my companion. Then he turned to me,
 10110	tapped his forehead three times, shook his head solemnly, and
 10111	hurried away.
 10112	
 10113	He had hardly shut the door behind him when Holmes rose to put on
 10114	his overcoat. "There is something in what the fellow says about
 10115	outdoor work," he remarked, "so I think, Watson, that I must
 10116	leave you to your papers for a little."
 10117	
 10118	It was after five o'clock when Sherlock Holmes left me, but I had
 10119	no time to be lonely, for within an hour there arrived a
 10120	confectioner's man with a very large flat box. This he unpacked
 10121	with the help of a youth whom he had brought with him, and
 10122	presently, to my very great astonishment, a quite epicurean
 10123	little cold supper began to be laid out upon our humble
 10124	lodging-house mahogany. There were a couple of brace of cold
 10125	woodcock, a pheasant, a p?t? de foie gras pie with a group of
 10126	ancient and cobwebby bottles. Having laid out all these luxuries,
 10127	my two visitors vanished away, like the genii of the Arabian
 10128	Nights, with no explanation save that the things had been paid
 10129	for and were ordered to this address.
 10130	
 10131	Just before nine o'clock Sherlock Holmes stepped briskly into the
 10132	room. His features were gravely set, but there was a light in his
 10133	eye which made me think that he had not been disappointed in his
 10134	conclusions.
 10135	
 10136	"They have laid the supper, then," he said, rubbing his hands.
 10137	
 10138	"You seem to expect company. They have laid for five."
 10139	
 10140	"Yes, I fancy we may have some company dropping in," said he. "I
 10141	am surprised that Lord St. Simon has not already arrived. Ha! I
 10142	fancy that I hear his step now upon the stairs."
 10143	
 10144	It was indeed our visitor of the afternoon who came bustling in,
 10145	dangling his glasses more vigorously than ever, and with a very
 10146	perturbed expression upon his aristocratic features.
 10147	
 10148	"My messenger reached you, then?" asked Holmes.
 10149	
 10150	"Yes, and I confess that the contents startled me beyond measure.
 10151	Have you good authority for what you say?"
 10152	
 10153	"The best possible."
 10154	
 10155	Lord St. Simon sank into a chair and passed his hand over his
 10156	forehead.
 10157	
 10158	"What will the Duke say," he murmured, "when he hears that one of
 10159	the family has been subjected to such humiliation?"
 10160	
 10161	"It is the purest accident. I cannot allow that there is any
 10162	humiliation."
 10163	
 10164	"Ah, you look on these things from another standpoint."
 10165	
 10166	"I fail to see that anyone is to blame. I can hardly see how the
 10167	lady could have acted otherwise, though her abrupt method of
 10168	doing it was undoubtedly to be regretted. Having no mother, she
 10169	had no one to advise her at such a crisis."
 10170	
 10171	"It was a slight, sir, a public slight," said Lord St. Simon,
 10172	tapping his fingers upon the table.
 10173	
 10174	"You must make allowance for this poor girl, placed in so
 10175	unprecedented a position."
 10176	
 10177	"I will make no allowance. I am very angry indeed, and I have
 10178	been shamefully used."
 10179	
 10180	"I think that I heard a ring," said Holmes. "Yes, there are steps
 10181	on the landing. If I cannot persuade you to take a lenient view
 10182	of the matter, Lord St. Simon, I have brought an advocate here
 10183	who may be more successful." He opened the door and ushered in a
 10184	lady and gentleman. "Lord St. Simon," said he "allow me to
 10185	introduce you to Mr. and Mrs. Francis Hay Moulton. The lady, I
 10186	think, you have already met."
 10187	
 10188	At the sight of these newcomers our client had sprung from his
 10189	seat and stood very erect, with his eyes cast down and his hand
 10190	thrust into the breast of his frock-coat, a picture of offended
 10191	dignity. The lady had taken a quick step forward and had held out
 10192	her hand to him, but he still refused to raise his eyes. It was
 10193	as well for his resolution, perhaps, for her pleading face was
 10194	one which it was hard to resist.
 10195	
 10196	"You're angry, Robert," said she. "Well, I guess you have every
 10197	cause to be."
 10198	
 10199	"Pray make no apology to me," said Lord St. Simon bitterly.
 10200	
 10201	"Oh, yes, I know that I have treated you real bad and that I
 10202	should have spoken to you before I went; but I was kind of
 10203	rattled, and from the time when I saw Frank here again I just
 10204	didn't know what I was doing or saying. I only wonder I didn't
 10205	fall down and do a faint right there before the altar."
 10206	
 10207	"Perhaps, Mrs. Moulton, you would like my friend and me to leave
 10208	the room while you explain this matter?"
 10209	
 10210	"If I may give an opinion," remarked the strange gentleman,
 10211	"we've had just a little too much secrecy over this business
 10212	already. For my part, I should like all Europe and America to
 10213	hear the rights of it." He was a small, wiry, sunburnt man,
 10214	clean-shaven, with a sharp face and alert manner.
 10215	
 10216	"Then I'll tell our story right away," said the lady. "Frank here
 10217	and I met in '84, in McQuire's camp, near the Rockies, where pa
 10218	was working a claim. We were engaged to each other, Frank and I;
 10219	but then one day father struck a rich pocket and made a pile,
 10220	while poor Frank here had a claim that petered out and came to
 10221	nothing. The richer pa grew the poorer was Frank; so at last pa
 10222	wouldn't hear of our engagement lasting any longer, and he took
 10223	me away to 'Frisco. Frank wouldn't throw up his hand, though; so
 10224	he followed me there, and he saw me without pa knowing anything
 10225	about it. It would only have made him mad to know, so we just
 10226	fixed it all up for ourselves. Frank said that he would go and
 10227	make his pile, too, and never come back to claim me until he had
 10228	as much as pa. So then I promised to wait for him to the end of
 10229	time and pledged myself not to marry anyone else while he lived.
 10230	'Why shouldn't we be married right away, then,' said he, 'and
 10231	then I will feel sure of you; and I won't claim to be your
 10232	husband until I come back?' Well, we talked it over, and he had
 10233	fixed it all up so nicely, with a clergyman all ready in waiting,
 10234	that we just did it right there; and then Frank went off to seek
 10235	his fortune, and I went back to pa.
 10236	
 10237	"The next I heard of Frank was that he was in Montana, and then
 10238	he went prospecting in Arizona, and then I heard of him from New
 10239	Mexico. After that came a long newspaper story about how a
 10240	miners' camp had been attacked by Apache Indians, and there was
 10241	my Frank's name among the killed. I fainted dead away, and I was
 10242	very sick for months after. Pa thought I had a decline and took
 10243	me to half the doctors in 'Frisco. Not a word of news came for a
 10244	year and more, so that I never doubted that Frank was really
 10245	dead. Then Lord St. Simon came to 'Frisco, and we came to London,
 10246	and a marriage was arranged, and pa was very pleased, but I felt
 10247	all the time that no man on this earth would ever take the place
 10248	in my heart that had been given to my poor Frank.
 10249	
 10250	"Still, if I had married Lord St. Simon, of course I'd have done
 10251	my duty by him. We can't command our love, but we can our
 10252	actions. I went to the altar with him with the intention to make
 10253	him just as good a wife as it was in me to be. But you may
 10254	imagine what I felt when, just as I came to the altar rails, I
 10255	glanced back and saw Frank standing and looking at me out of the
 10256	first pew. I thought it was his ghost at first; but when I looked
 10257	again there he was still, with a kind of question in his eyes, as
 10258	if to ask me whether I were glad or sorry to see him. I wonder I
 10259	didn't drop. I know that everything was turning round, and the
 10260	words of the clergyman were just like the buzz of a bee in my
 10261	ear. I didn't know what to do. Should I stop the service and make
 10262	a scene in the church? I glanced at him again, and he seemed to
 10263	know what I was thinking, for he raised his finger to his lips to
 10264	tell me to be still. Then I saw him scribble on a piece of paper,
 10265	and I knew that he was writing me a note. As I passed his pew on
 10266	the way out I dropped my bouquet over to him, and he slipped the
 10267	note into my hand when he returned me the flowers. It was only a
 10268	line asking me to join him when he made the sign to me to do so.
 10269	Of course I never doubted for a moment that my first duty was now
 10270	to him, and I determined to do just whatever he might direct.
 10271	
 10272	"When I got back I told my maid, who had known him in California,
 10273	and had always been his friend. I ordered her to say nothing, but
 10274	to get a few things packed and my ulster ready. I know I ought to
 10275	have spoken to Lord St. Simon, but it was dreadful hard before
 10276	his mother and all those great people. I just made up my mind to
 10277	run away and explain afterwards. I hadn't been at the table ten
 10278	minutes before I saw Frank out of the window at the other side of
 10279	the road. He beckoned to me and then began walking into the Park.
 10280	I slipped out, put on my things, and followed him. Some woman
 10281	came talking something or other about Lord St. Simon to
 10282	me--seemed to me from the little I heard as if he had a little
 10283	secret of his own before marriage also--but I managed to get away
 10284	from her and soon overtook Frank. We got into a cab together, and
 10285	away we drove to some lodgings he had taken in Gordon Square, and
 10286	that was my true wedding after all those years of waiting. Frank
 10287	had been a prisoner among the Apaches, had escaped, came on to
 10288	'Frisco, found that I had given him up for dead and had gone to
 10289	England, followed me there, and had come upon me at last on the
 10290	very morning of my second wedding."
 10291	
 10292	"I saw it in a paper," explained the American. "It gave the name
 10293	and the church but not where the lady lived."
 10294	
 10295	"Then we had a talk as to what we should do, and Frank was all
 10296	for openness, but I was so ashamed of it all that I felt as if I
 10297	should like to vanish away and never see any of them again--just
 10298	sending a line to pa, perhaps, to show him that I was alive. It
 10299	was awful to me to think of all those lords and ladies sitting
 10300	round that breakfast-table and waiting for me to come back. So
 10301	Frank took my wedding-clothes and things and made a bundle of
 10302	them, so that I should not be traced, and dropped them away
 10303	somewhere where no one could find them. It is likely that we
 10304	should have gone on to Paris to-morrow, only that this good
 10305	gentleman, Mr. Holmes, came round to us this evening, though how
 10306	he found us is more than I can think, and he showed us very
 10307	clearly and kindly that I was wrong and that Frank was right, and
 10308	that we should be putting ourselves in the wrong if we were so
 10309	secret. Then he offered to give us a chance of talking to Lord
 10310	St. Simon alone, and so we came right away round to his rooms at
 10311	once. Now, Robert, you have heard it all, and I am very sorry if
 10312	I have given you pain, and I hope that you do not think very
 10313	meanly of me."
 10314	
 10315	Lord St. Simon had by no means relaxed his rigid attitude, but
 10316	had listened with a frowning brow and a compressed lip to this
 10317	long narrative.
 10318	
 10319	"Excuse me," he said, "but it is not my custom to discuss my most
 10320	intimate personal affairs in this public manner."
 10321	
 10322	"Then you won't forgive me? You won't shake hands before I go?"
 10323	
 10324	"Oh, certainly, if it would give you any pleasure." He put out
 10325	his hand and coldly grasped that which she extended to him.
 10326	
 10327	"I had hoped," suggested Holmes, "that you would have joined us
 10328	in a friendly supper."
 10329	
 10330	"I think that there you ask a little too much," responded his
 10331	Lordship. "I may be forced to acquiesce in these recent
 10332	developments, but I can hardly be expected to make merry over
 10333	them. I think that with your permission I will now wish you all a
 10334	very good-night." He included us all in a sweeping bow and
 10335	stalked out of the room.
 10336	
 10337	"Then I trust that you at least will honour me with your
 10338	company," said Sherlock Holmes. "It is always a joy to meet an
 10339	American, Mr. Moulton, for I am one of those who believe that the
 10340	folly of a monarch and the blundering of a minister in far-gone
 10341	years will not prevent our children from being some day citizens
 10342	of the same world-wide country under a flag which shall be a
 10343	quartering of the Union Jack with the Stars and Stripes."
 10344	
 10345	"The case has been an interesting one," remarked Holmes when our
 10346	visitors had left us, "because it serves to show very clearly how
 10347	simple the explanation may be of an affair which at first sight
 10348	seems to be almost inexplicable. Nothing could be more natural
 10349	than the sequence of events as narrated by this lady, and nothing
 10350	stranger than the result when viewed, for instance, by Mr.
 10351	Lestrade of Scotland Yard."
 10352	
 10353	"You were not yourself at fault at all, then?"
 10354	
 10355	"From the first, two facts were very obvious to me, the one that
 10356	the lady had been quite willing to undergo the wedding ceremony,
 10357	the other that she had repented of it within a few minutes of
 10358	returning home. Obviously something had occurred during the
 10359	morning, then, to cause her to change her mind. What could that
 10360	something be? She could not have spoken to anyone when she was
 10361	out, for she had been in the company of the bridegroom. Had she
 10362	seen someone, then? If she had, it must be someone from America
 10363	because she had spent so short a time in this country that she
 10364	could hardly have allowed anyone to acquire so deep an influence
 10365	over her that the mere sight of him would induce her to change
 10366	her plans so completely. You see we have already arrived, by a
 10367	process of exclusion, at the idea that she might have seen an
 10368	American. Then who could this American be, and why should he
 10369	possess so much influence over her? It might be a lover; it might
 10370	be a husband. Her young womanhood had, I knew, been spent in
 10371	rough scenes and under strange conditions. So far I had got
 10372	before I ever heard Lord St. Simon's narrative. When he told us
 10373	of a man in a pew, of the change in the bride's manner, of so
 10374	transparent a device for obtaining a note as the dropping of a
 10375	bouquet, of her resort to her confidential maid, and of her very
 10376	significant allusion to claim-jumping--which in miners' parlance
 10377	means taking possession of that which another person has a prior
 10378	claim to--the whole situation became absolutely clear. She had
 10379	gone off with a man, and the man was either a lover or was a
 10380	previous husband--the chances being in favour of the latter."
 10381	
 10382	"And how in the world did you find them?"
 10383	
 10384	"It might have been difficult, but friend Lestrade held
 10385	information in his hands the value of which he did not himself
 10386	know. The initials were, of course, of the highest importance,
 10387	but more valuable still was it to know that within a week he had
 10388	settled his bill at one of the most select London hotels."
 10389	
 10390	"How did you deduce the select?"
 10391	
 10392	"By the select prices. Eight shillings for a bed and eightpence
 10393	for a glass of sherry pointed to one of the most expensive
 10394	hotels. There are not many in London which charge at that rate.
 10395	In the second one which I visited in Northumberland Avenue, I
 10396	learned by an inspection of the book that Francis H. Moulton, an
 10397	American gentleman, had left only the day before, and on looking
 10398	over the entries against him, I came upon the very items which I
 10399	had seen in the duplicate bill. His letters were to be forwarded
 10400	to 226 Gordon Square; so thither I travelled, and being fortunate
 10401	enough to find the loving couple at home, I ventured to give them
 10402	some paternal advice and to point out to them that it would be
 10403	better in every way that they should make their position a little
 10404	clearer both to the general public and to Lord St. Simon in
 10405	particular. I invited them to meet him here, and, as you see, I
 10406	made him keep the appointment."
 10407	
 10408	"But with no very good result," I remarked. "His conduct was
 10409	certainly not very gracious."
 10410	
 10411	"Ah, Watson," said Holmes, smiling, "perhaps you would not be
 10412	very gracious either, if, after all the trouble of wooing and
 10413	wedding, you found yourself deprived in an instant of wife and of
 10414	fortune. I think that we may judge Lord St. Simon very mercifully
 10415	and thank our stars that we are never likely to find ourselves in
 10416	the same position. Draw your chair up and hand me my violin, for
 10417	the only problem we have still to solve is how to while away
 10418	these bleak autumnal evenings."
 10419	
 10420	
 10421	
 10422	XI. THE ADVENTURE OF THE BERYL CORONET
 10423	
 10424	"Holmes," said I as I stood one morning in our bow-window looking
 10425	down the street, "here is a madman coming along. It seems rather
 10426	sad that his relatives should allow him to come out alone."
 10427	
 10428	My friend rose lazily from his armchair and stood with his hands
 10429	in the pockets of his dressing-gown, looking over my shoulder. It
 10430	was a bright, crisp February morning, and the snow of the day
 10431	before still lay deep upon the ground, shimmering brightly in the
 10432	wintry sun. Down the centre of Baker Street it had been ploughed
 10433	into a brown crumbly band by the traffic, but at either side and
 10434	on the heaped-up edges of the foot-paths it still lay as white as
 10435	when it fell. The grey pavement had been cleaned and scraped, but
 10436	was still dangerously slippery, so that there were fewer
 10437	passengers than usual. Indeed, from the direction of the
 10438	Metropolitan Station no one was coming save the single gentleman
 10439	whose eccentric conduct had drawn my attention.
 10440	
 10441	He was a man of about fifty, tall, portly, and imposing, with a
 10442	massive, strongly marked face and a commanding figure. He was
 10443	dressed in a sombre yet rich style, in black frock-coat, shining
 10444	hat, neat brown gaiters, and well-cut pearl-grey trousers. Yet
 10445	his actions were in absurd contrast to the dignity of his dress
 10446	and features, for he was running hard, with occasional little
 10447	springs, such as a weary man gives who is little accustomed to
 10448	set any tax upon his legs. As he ran he jerked his hands up and
 10449	down, waggled his head, and writhed his face into the most
 10450	extraordinary contortions.
 10451	
 10452	"What on earth can be the matter with him?" I asked. "He is
 10453	looking up at the numbers of the houses."
 10454	
 10455	"I believe that he is coming here," said Holmes, rubbing his
 10456	hands.
 10457	
 10458	"Here?"
 10459	
 10460	"Yes; I rather think he is coming to consult me professionally. I
 10461	think that I recognise the symptoms. Ha! did I not tell you?" As
 10462	he spoke, the man, puffing and blowing, rushed at our door and
 10463	pulled at our bell until the whole house resounded with the
 10464	clanging.
 10465	
 10466	A few moments later he was in our room, still puffing, still
 10467	gesticulating, but with so fixed a look of grief and despair in
 10468	his eyes that our smiles were turned in an instant to horror and
 10469	pity. For a while he could not get his words out, but swayed his
 10470	body and plucked at his hair like one who has been driven to the
 10471	extreme limits of his reason. Then, suddenly springing to his
 10472	feet, he beat his head against the wall with such force that we
 10473	both rushed upon him and tore him away to the centre of the room.
 10474	Sherlock Holmes pushed him down into the easy-chair and, sitting
 10475	beside him, patted his hand and chatted with him in the easy,
 10476	soothing tones which he knew so well how to employ.
 10477	
 10478	"You have come to me to tell your story, have you not?" said he.
 10479	"You are fatigued with your haste. Pray wait until you have
 10480	recovered yourself, and then I shall be most happy to look into
 10481	any little problem which you may submit to me."
 10482	
 10483	The man sat for a minute or more with a heaving chest, fighting
 10484	against his emotion. Then he passed his handkerchief over his
 10485	brow, set his lips tight, and turned his face towards us.
 10486	
 10487	"No doubt you think me mad?" said he.
 10488	
 10489	"I see that you have had some great trouble," responded Holmes.
 10490	
 10491	"God knows I have!--a trouble which is enough to unseat my
 10492	reason, so sudden and so terrible is it. Public disgrace I might
 10493	have faced, although I am a man whose character has never yet
 10494	borne a stain. Private affliction also is the lot of every man;
 10495	but the two coming together, and in so frightful a form, have
 10496	been enough to shake my very soul. Besides, it is not I alone.
 10497	The very noblest in the land may suffer unless some way be found
 10498	out of this horrible affair."
 10499	
 10500	"Pray compose yourself, sir," said Holmes, "and let me have a
 10501	clear account of who you are and what it is that has befallen
 10502	you."
 10503	
 10504	"My name," answered our visitor, "is probably familiar to your
 10505	ears. I am Alexander Holder, of the banking firm of Holder &
 10506	Stevenson, of Threadneedle Street."
 10507	
 10508	The name was indeed well known to us as belonging to the senior
 10509	partner in the second largest private banking concern in the City
 10510	of London. What could have happened, then, to bring one of the
 10511	foremost citizens of London to this most pitiable pass? We
 10512	waited, all curiosity, until with another effort he braced
 10513	himself to tell his story.
 10514	
 10515	"I feel that time is of value," said he; "that is why I hastened
 10516	here when the police inspector suggested that I should secure
 10517	your co-operation. I came to Baker Street by the Underground and
 10518	hurried from there on foot, for the cabs go slowly through this
 10519	snow. That is why I was so out of breath, for I am a man who
 10520	takes very little exercise. I feel better now, and I will put the
 10521	facts before you as shortly and yet as clearly as I can.
 10522	
 10523	"It is, of course, well known to you that in a successful banking
 10524	business as much depends upon our being able to find remunerative
 10525	investments for our funds as upon our increasing our connection
 10526	and the number of our depositors. One of our most lucrative means
 10527	of laying out money is in the shape of loans, where the security
 10528	is unimpeachable. We have done a good deal in this direction
 10529	during the last few years, and there are many noble families to
 10530	whom we have advanced large sums upon the security of their
 10531	pictures, libraries, or plate.
 10532	
 10533	"Yesterday morning I was seated in my office at the bank when a
 10534	card was brought in to me by one of the clerks. I started when I
 10535	saw the name, for it was that of none other than--well, perhaps
 10536	even to you I had better say no more than that it was a name
 10537	which is a household word all over the earth--one of the highest,
 10538	noblest, most exalted names in England. I was overwhelmed by the
 10539	honour and attempted, when he entered, to say so, but he plunged
 10540	at once into business with the air of a man who wishes to hurry
 10541	quickly through a disagreeable task.
 10542	
 10543	"'Mr. Holder,' said he, 'I have been informed that you are in the
 10544	habit of advancing money.'
 10545	
 10546	"'The firm does so when the security is good.' I answered.
 10547	
 10548	"'It is absolutely essential to me,' said he, 'that I should have
 10549	50,000 pounds at once. I could, of course, borrow so trifling a
 10550	sum ten times over from my friends, but I much prefer to make it
 10551	a matter of business and to carry out that business myself. In my
 10552	position you can readily understand that it is unwise to place
 10553	one's self under obligations.'
 10554	
 10555	"'For how long, may I ask, do you want this sum?' I asked.
 10556	
 10557	"'Next Monday I have a large sum due to me, and I shall then most
 10558	certainly repay what you advance, with whatever interest you
 10559	think it right to charge. But it is very essential to me that the
 10560	money should be paid at once.'
 10561	
 10562	"'I should be happy to advance it without further parley from my
 10563	own private purse,' said I, 'were it not that the strain would be
 10564	rather more than it could bear. If, on the other hand, I am to do
 10565	it in the name of the firm, then in justice to my partner I must
 10566	insist that, even in your case, every businesslike precaution
 10567	should be taken.'
 10568	
 10569	"'I should much prefer to have it so,' said he, raising up a
 10570	square, black morocco case which he had laid beside his chair.
 10571	'You have doubtless heard of the Beryl Coronet?'
 10572	
 10573	"'One of the most precious public possessions of the empire,'
 10574	said I.
 10575	
 10576	"'Precisely.' He opened the case, and there, imbedded in soft,
 10577	flesh-coloured velvet, lay the magnificent piece of jewellery
 10578	which he had named. 'There are thirty-nine enormous beryls,' said
 10579	he, 'and the price of the gold chasing is incalculable. The
 10580	lowest estimate would put the worth of the coronet at double the
 10581	sum which I have asked. I am prepared to leave it with you as my
 10582	security.'
 10583	
 10584	"I took the precious case into my hands and looked in some
 10585	perplexity from it to my illustrious client.
 10586	
 10587	"'You doubt its value?' he asked.
 10588	
 10589	"'Not at all. I only doubt--'
 10590	
 10591	"'The propriety of my leaving it. You may set your mind at rest
 10592	about that. I should not dream of doing so were it not absolutely
 10593	certain that I should be able in four days to reclaim it. It is a
 10594	pure matter of form. Is the security sufficient?'
 10595	
 10596	"'Ample.'
 10597	
 10598	"'You understand, Mr. Holder, that I am giving you a strong proof
 10599	of the confidence which I have in you, founded upon all that I
 10600	have heard of you. I rely upon you not only to be discreet and to
 10601	refrain from all gossip upon the matter but, above all, to
 10602	preserve this coronet with every possible precaution because I
 10603	need not say that a great public scandal would be caused if any
 10604	harm were to befall it. Any injury to it would be almost as
 10605	serious as its complete loss, for there are no beryls in the
 10606	world to match these, and it would be impossible to replace them.
 10607	I leave it with you, however, with every confidence, and I shall
 10608	call for it in person on Monday morning.'
 10609	
 10610	"Seeing that my client was anxious to leave, I said no more but,
 10611	calling for my cashier, I ordered him to pay over fifty 1000
 10612	pound notes. When I was alone once more, however, with the
 10613	precious case lying upon the table in front of me, I could not
 10614	but think with some misgivings of the immense responsibility
 10615	which it entailed upon me. There could be no doubt that, as it
 10616	was a national possession, a horrible scandal would ensue if any
 10617	misfortune should occur to it. I already regretted having ever
 10618	consented to take charge of it. However, it was too late to alter
 10619	the matter now, so I locked it up in my private safe and turned
 10620	once more to my work.
 10621	
 10622	"When evening came I felt that it would be an imprudence to leave
 10623	so precious a thing in the office behind me. Bankers' safes had
 10624	been forced before now, and why should not mine be? If so, how
 10625	terrible would be the position in which I should find myself! I
 10626	determined, therefore, that for the next few days I would always
 10627	carry the case backward and forward with me, so that it might
 10628	never be really out of my reach. With this intention, I called a
 10629	cab and drove out to my house at Streatham, carrying the jewel
 10630	with me. I did not breathe freely until I had taken it upstairs
 10631	and locked it in the bureau of my dressing-room.
 10632	
 10633	"And now a word as to my household, Mr. Holmes, for I wish you to
 10634	thoroughly understand the situation. My groom and my page sleep
 10635	out of the house, and may be set aside altogether. I have three
 10636	maid-servants who have been with me a number of years and whose
 10637	absolute reliability is quite above suspicion. Another, Lucy
 10638	Parr, the second waiting-maid, has only been in my service a few
 10639	months. She came with an excellent character, however, and has
 10640	always given me satisfaction. She is a very pretty girl and has
 10641	attracted admirers who have occasionally hung about the place.
 10642	That is the only drawback which we have found to her, but we
 10643	believe her to be a thoroughly good girl in every way.
 10644	
 10645	"So much for the servants. My family itself is so small that it
 10646	will not take me long to describe it. I am a widower and have an
 10647	only son, Arthur. He has been a disappointment to me, Mr.
 10648	Holmes--a grievous disappointment. I have no doubt that I am
 10649	myself to blame. People tell me that I have spoiled him. Very
 10650	likely I have. When my dear wife died I felt that he was all I
 10651	had to love. I could not bear to see the smile fade even for a
 10652	moment from his face. I have never denied him a wish. Perhaps it
 10653	would have been better for both of us had I been sterner, but I
 10654	meant it for the best.
 10655	
 10656	"It was naturally my intention that he should succeed me in my
 10657	business, but he was not of a business turn. He was wild,
 10658	wayward, and, to speak the truth, I could not trust him in the
 10659	handling of large sums of money. When he was young he became a
 10660	member of an aristocratic club, and there, having charming
 10661	manners, he was soon the intimate of a number of men with long
 10662	purses and expensive habits. He learned to play heavily at cards
 10663	and to squander money on the turf, until he had again and again
 10664	to come to me and implore me to give him an advance upon his
 10665	allowance, that he might settle his debts of honour. He tried
 10666	more than once to break away from the dangerous company which he
 10667	was keeping, but each time the influence of his friend, Sir
 10668	George Burnwell, was enough to draw him back again.
 10669	
 10670	"And, indeed, I could not wonder that such a man as Sir George
 10671	Burnwell should gain an influence over him, for he has frequently
 10672	brought him to my house, and I have found myself that I could
 10673	hardly resist the fascination of his manner. He is older than
 10674	Arthur, a man of the world to his finger-tips, one who had been
 10675	everywhere, seen everything, a brilliant talker, and a man of
 10676	great personal beauty. Yet when I think of him in cold blood, far
 10677	away from the glamour of his presence, I am convinced from his
 10678	cynical speech and the look which I have caught in his eyes that
 10679	he is one who should be deeply distrusted. So I think, and so,
 10680	too, thinks my little Mary, who has a woman's quick insight into
 10681	character.
 10682	
 10683	"And now there is only she to be described. She is my niece; but
 10684	when my brother died five years ago and left her alone in the
 10685	world I adopted her, and have looked upon her ever since as my
 10686	daughter. She is a sunbeam in my house--sweet, loving, beautiful,
 10687	a wonderful manager and housekeeper, yet as tender and quiet and
 10688	gentle as a woman could be. She is my right hand. I do not know
 10689	what I could do without her. In only one matter has she ever gone
 10690	against my wishes. Twice my boy has asked her to marry him, for
 10691	he loves her devotedly, but each time she has refused him. I
 10692	think that if anyone could have drawn him into the right path it
 10693	would have been she, and that his marriage might have changed his
 10694	whole life; but now, alas! it is too late--forever too late!
 10695	
 10696	"Now, Mr. Holmes, you know the people who live under my roof, and
 10697	I shall continue with my miserable story.
 10698	
 10699	"When we were taking coffee in the drawing-room that night after
 10700	dinner, I told Arthur and Mary my experience, and of the precious
 10701	treasure which we had under our roof, suppressing only the name
 10702	of my client. Lucy Parr, who had brought in the coffee, had, I am
 10703	sure, left the room; but I cannot swear that the door was closed.
 10704	Mary and Arthur were much interested and wished to see the famous
 10705	coronet, but I thought it better not to disturb it.
 10706	
 10707	"'Where have you put it?' asked Arthur.
 10708	
 10709	"'In my own bureau.'
 10710	
 10711	"'Well, I hope to goodness the house won't be burgled during the
 10712	night.' said he.
 10713	
 10714	"'It is locked up,' I answered.
 10715	
 10716	"'Oh, any old key will fit that bureau. When I was a youngster I
 10717	have opened it myself with the key of the box-room cupboard.'
 10718	
 10719	"He often had a wild way of talking, so that I thought little of
 10720	what he said. He followed me to my room, however, that night with
 10721	a very grave face.
 10722	
 10723	"'Look here, dad,' said he with his eyes cast down, 'can you let
 10724	me have 200 pounds?'
 10725	
 10726	"'No, I cannot!' I answered sharply. 'I have been far too
 10727	generous with you in money matters.'
 10728	
 10729	"'You have been very kind,' said he, 'but I must have this money,
 10730	or else I can never show my face inside the club again.'
 10731	
 10732	"'And a very good thing, too!' I cried.
 10733	
 10734	"'Yes, but you would not have me leave it a dishonoured man,'
 10735	said he. 'I could not bear the disgrace. I must raise the money
 10736	in some way, and if you will not let me have it, then I must try
 10737	other means.'
 10738	
 10739	"I was very angry, for this was the third demand during the
 10740	month. 'You shall not have a farthing from me,' I cried, on which
 10741	he bowed and left the room without another word.
 10742	
 10743	"When he was gone I unlocked my bureau, made sure that my
 10744	treasure was safe, and locked it again. Then I started to go
 10745	round the house to see that all was secure--a duty which I
 10746	usually leave to Mary but which I thought it well to perform
 10747	myself that night. As I came down the stairs I saw Mary herself
 10748	at the side window of the hall, which she closed and fastened as
 10749	I approached.
 10750	
 10751	"'Tell me, dad,' said she, looking, I thought, a little
 10752	disturbed, 'did you give Lucy, the maid, leave to go out
 10753	to-night?'
 10754	
 10755	"'Certainly not.'
 10756	
 10757	"'She came in just now by the back door. I have no doubt that she
 10758	has only been to the side gate to see someone, but I think that
 10759	it is hardly safe and should be stopped.'
 10760	
 10761	"'You must speak to her in the morning, or I will if you prefer
 10762	it. Are you sure that everything is fastened?'
 10763	
 10764	"'Quite sure, dad.'
 10765	
 10766	"'Then, good-night.' I kissed her and went up to my bedroom
 10767	again, where I was soon asleep.
 10768	
 10769	"I am endeavouring to tell you everything, Mr. Holmes, which may
 10770	have any bearing upon the case, but I beg that you will question
