 10771	me upon any point which I do not make clear."
 10772	
 10773	"On the contrary, your statement is singularly lucid."
 10774	
 10775	"I come to a part of my story now in which I should wish to be
 10776	particularly so. I am not a very heavy sleeper, and the anxiety
 10777	in my mind tended, no doubt, to make me even less so than usual.
 10778	About two in the morning, then, I was awakened by some sound in
 10779	the house. It had ceased ere I was wide awake, but it had left an
 10780	impression behind it as though a window had gently closed
 10781	somewhere. I lay listening with all my ears. Suddenly, to my
 10782	horror, there was a distinct sound of footsteps moving softly in
 10783	the next room. I slipped out of bed, all palpitating with fear,
 10784	and peeped round the corner of my dressing-room door.
 10785	
 10786	"'Arthur!' I screamed, 'you villain! you thief! How dare you
 10787	touch that coronet?'
 10788	
 10789	"The gas was half up, as I had left it, and my unhappy boy,
 10790	dressed only in his shirt and trousers, was standing beside the
 10791	light, holding the coronet in his hands. He appeared to be
 10792	wrenching at it, or bending it with all his strength. At my cry
 10793	he dropped it from his grasp and turned as pale as death. I
 10794	snatched it up and examined it. One of the gold corners, with
 10795	three of the beryls in it, was missing.
 10796	
 10797	"'You blackguard!' I shouted, beside myself with rage. 'You have
 10798	destroyed it! You have dishonoured me forever! Where are the
 10799	jewels which you have stolen?'
 10800	
 10801	"'Stolen!' he cried.
 10802	
 10803	"'Yes, thief!' I roared, shaking him by the shoulder.
 10804	
 10805	"'There are none missing. There cannot be any missing,' said he.
 10806	
 10807	"'There are three missing. And you know where they are. Must I
 10808	call you a liar as well as a thief? Did I not see you trying to
 10809	tear off another piece?'
 10810	
 10811	"'You have called me names enough,' said he, 'I will not stand it
 10812	any longer. I shall not say another word about this business,
 10813	since you have chosen to insult me. I will leave your house in
 10814	the morning and make my own way in the world.'
 10815	
 10816	"'You shall leave it in the hands of the police!' I cried
 10817	half-mad with grief and rage. 'I shall have this matter probed to
 10818	the bottom.'
 10819	
 10820	"'You shall learn nothing from me,' said he with a passion such
 10821	as I should not have thought was in his nature. 'If you choose to
 10822	call the police, let the police find what they can.'
 10823	
 10824	"By this time the whole house was astir, for I had raised my
 10825	voice in my anger. Mary was the first to rush into my room, and,
 10826	at the sight of the coronet and of Arthur's face, she read the
 10827	whole story and, with a scream, fell down senseless on the
 10828	ground. I sent the house-maid for the police and put the
 10829	investigation into their hands at once. When the inspector and a
 10830	constable entered the house, Arthur, who had stood sullenly with
 10831	his arms folded, asked me whether it was my intention to charge
 10832	him with theft. I answered that it had ceased to be a private
 10833	matter, but had become a public one, since the ruined coronet was
 10834	national property. I was determined that the law should have its
 10835	way in everything.
 10836	
 10837	"'At least,' said he, 'you will not have me arrested at once. It
 10838	would be to your advantage as well as mine if I might leave the
 10839	house for five minutes.'
 10840	
 10841	"'That you may get away, or perhaps that you may conceal what you
 10842	have stolen,' said I. And then, realising the dreadful position
 10843	in which I was placed, I implored him to remember that not only
 10844	my honour but that of one who was far greater than I was at
 10845	stake; and that he threatened to raise a scandal which would
 10846	convulse the nation. He might avert it all if he would but tell
 10847	me what he had done with the three missing stones.
 10848	
 10849	"'You may as well face the matter,' said I; 'you have been caught
 10850	in the act, and no confession could make your guilt more heinous.
 10851	If you but make such reparation as is in your power, by telling
 10852	us where the beryls are, all shall be forgiven and forgotten.'
 10853	
 10854	"'Keep your forgiveness for those who ask for it,' he answered,
 10855	turning away from me with a sneer. I saw that he was too hardened
 10856	for any words of mine to influence him. There was but one way for
 10857	it. I called in the inspector and gave him into custody. A search
 10858	was made at once not only of his person but of his room and of
 10859	every portion of the house where he could possibly have concealed
 10860	the gems; but no trace of them could be found, nor would the
 10861	wretched boy open his mouth for all our persuasions and our
 10862	threats. This morning he was removed to a cell, and I, after
 10863	going through all the police formalities, have hurried round to
 10864	you to implore you to use your skill in unravelling the matter.
 10865	The police have openly confessed that they can at present make
 10866	nothing of it. You may go to any expense which you think
 10867	necessary. I have already offered a reward of 1000 pounds. My
 10868	God, what shall I do! I have lost my honour, my gems, and my son
 10869	in one night. Oh, what shall I do!"
 10870	
 10871	He put a hand on either side of his head and rocked himself to
 10872	and fro, droning to himself like a child whose grief has got
 10873	beyond words.
 10874	
 10875	Sherlock Holmes sat silent for some few minutes, with his brows
 10876	knitted and his eyes fixed upon the fire.
 10877	
 10878	"Do you receive much company?" he asked.
 10879	
 10880	"None save my partner with his family and an occasional friend of
 10881	Arthur's. Sir George Burnwell has been several times lately. No
 10882	one else, I think."
 10883	
 10884	"Do you go out much in society?"
 10885	
 10886	"Arthur does. Mary and I stay at home. We neither of us care for
 10887	it."
 10888	
 10889	"That is unusual in a young girl."
 10890	
 10891	"She is of a quiet nature. Besides, she is not so very young. She
 10892	is four-and-twenty."
 10893	
 10894	"This matter, from what you say, seems to have been a shock to
 10895	her also."
 10896	
 10897	"Terrible! She is even more affected than I."
 10898	
 10899	"You have neither of you any doubt as to your son's guilt?"
 10900	
 10901	"How can we have when I saw him with my own eyes with the coronet
 10902	in his hands."
 10903	
 10904	"I hardly consider that a conclusive proof. Was the remainder of
 10905	the coronet at all injured?"
 10906	
 10907	"Yes, it was twisted."
 10908	
 10909	"Do you not think, then, that he might have been trying to
 10910	straighten it?"
 10911	
 10912	"God bless you! You are doing what you can for him and for me.
 10913	But it is too heavy a task. What was he doing there at all? If
 10914	his purpose were innocent, why did he not say so?"
 10915	
 10916	"Precisely. And if it were guilty, why did he not invent a lie?
 10917	His silence appears to me to cut both ways. There are several
 10918	singular points about the case. What did the police think of the
 10919	noise which awoke you from your sleep?"
 10920	
 10921	"They considered that it might be caused by Arthur's closing his
 10922	bedroom door."
 10923	
 10924	"A likely story! As if a man bent on felony would slam his door
 10925	so as to wake a household. What did they say, then, of the
 10926	disappearance of these gems?"
 10927	
 10928	"They are still sounding the planking and probing the furniture
 10929	in the hope of finding them."
 10930	
 10931	"Have they thought of looking outside the house?"
 10932	
 10933	"Yes, they have shown extraordinary energy. The whole garden has
 10934	already been minutely examined."
 10935	
 10936	"Now, my dear sir," said Holmes. "is it not obvious to you now
 10937	that this matter really strikes very much deeper than either you
 10938	or the police were at first inclined to think? It appeared to you
 10939	to be a simple case; to me it seems exceedingly complex. Consider
 10940	what is involved by your theory. You suppose that your son came
 10941	down from his bed, went, at great risk, to your dressing-room,
 10942	opened your bureau, took out your coronet, broke off by main
 10943	force a small portion of it, went off to some other place,
 10944	concealed three gems out of the thirty-nine, with such skill that
 10945	nobody can find them, and then returned with the other thirty-six
 10946	into the room in which he exposed himself to the greatest danger
 10947	of being discovered. I ask you now, is such a theory tenable?"
 10948	
 10949	"But what other is there?" cried the banker with a gesture of
 10950	despair. "If his motives were innocent, why does he not explain
 10951	them?"
 10952	
 10953	"It is our task to find that out," replied Holmes; "so now, if
 10954	you please, Mr. Holder, we will set off for Streatham together,
 10955	and devote an hour to glancing a little more closely into
 10956	details."
 10957	
 10958	My friend insisted upon my accompanying them in their expedition,
 10959	which I was eager enough to do, for my curiosity and sympathy
 10960	were deeply stirred by the story to which we had listened. I
 10961	confess that the guilt of the banker's son appeared to me to be
 10962	as obvious as it did to his unhappy father, but still I had such
 10963	faith in Holmes' judgment that I felt that there must be some
 10964	grounds for hope as long as he was dissatisfied with the accepted
 10965	explanation. He hardly spoke a word the whole way out to the
 10966	southern suburb, but sat with his chin upon his breast and his
 10967	hat drawn over his eyes, sunk in the deepest thought. Our client
 10968	appeared to have taken fresh heart at the little glimpse of hope
 10969	which had been presented to him, and he even broke into a
 10970	desultory chat with me over his business affairs. A short railway
 10971	journey and a shorter walk brought us to Fairbank, the modest
 10972	residence of the great financier.
 10973	
 10974	Fairbank was a good-sized square house of white stone, standing
 10975	back a little from the road. A double carriage-sweep, with a
 10976	snow-clad lawn, stretched down in front to two large iron gates
 10977	which closed the entrance. On the right side was a small wooden
 10978	thicket, which led into a narrow path between two neat hedges
 10979	stretching from the road to the kitchen door, and forming the
 10980	tradesmen's entrance. On the left ran a lane which led to the
 10981	stables, and was not itself within the grounds at all, being a
 10982	public, though little used, thoroughfare. Holmes left us standing
 10983	at the door and walked slowly all round the house, across the
 10984	front, down the tradesmen's path, and so round by the garden
 10985	behind into the stable lane. So long was he that Mr. Holder and I
 10986	went into the dining-room and waited by the fire until he should
 10987	return. We were sitting there in silence when the door opened and
 10988	a young lady came in. She was rather above the middle height,
 10989	slim, with dark hair and eyes, which seemed the darker against
 10990	the absolute pallor of her skin. I do not think that I have ever
 10991	seen such deadly paleness in a woman's face. Her lips, too, were
 10992	bloodless, but her eyes were flushed with crying. As she swept
 10993	silently into the room she impressed me with a greater sense of
 10994	grief than the banker had done in the morning, and it was the
 10995	more striking in her as she was evidently a woman of strong
 10996	character, with immense capacity for self-restraint. Disregarding
 10997	my presence, she went straight to her uncle and passed her hand
 10998	over his head with a sweet womanly caress.
 10999	
 11000	"You have given orders that Arthur should be liberated, have you
 11001	not, dad?" she asked.
 11002	
 11003	"No, no, my girl, the matter must be probed to the bottom."
 11004	
 11005	"But I am so sure that he is innocent. You know what woman's
 11006	instincts are. I know that he has done no harm and that you will
 11007	be sorry for having acted so harshly."
 11008	
 11009	"Why is he silent, then, if he is innocent?"
 11010	
 11011	"Who knows? Perhaps because he was so angry that you should
 11012	suspect him."
 11013	
 11014	"How could I help suspecting him, when I actually saw him with
 11015	the coronet in his hand?"
 11016	
 11017	"Oh, but he had only picked it up to look at it. Oh, do, do take
 11018	my word for it that he is innocent. Let the matter drop and say
 11019	no more. It is so dreadful to think of our dear Arthur in
 11020	prison!"
 11021	
 11022	"I shall never let it drop until the gems are found--never, Mary!
 11023	Your affection for Arthur blinds you as to the awful consequences
 11024	to me. Far from hushing the thing up, I have brought a gentleman
 11025	down from London to inquire more deeply into it."
 11026	
 11027	"This gentleman?" she asked, facing round to me.
 11028	
 11029	"No, his friend. He wished us to leave him alone. He is round in
 11030	the stable lane now."
 11031	
 11032	"The stable lane?" She raised her dark eyebrows. "What can he
 11033	hope to find there? Ah! this, I suppose, is he. I trust, sir,
 11034	that you will succeed in proving, what I feel sure is the truth,
 11035	that my cousin Arthur is innocent of this crime."
 11036	
 11037	"I fully share your opinion, and I trust, with you, that we may
 11038	prove it," returned Holmes, going back to the mat to knock the
 11039	snow from his shoes. "I believe I have the honour of addressing
 11040	Miss Mary Holder. Might I ask you a question or two?"
 11041	
 11042	"Pray do, sir, if it may help to clear this horrible affair up."
 11043	
 11044	"You heard nothing yourself last night?"
 11045	
 11046	"Nothing, until my uncle here began to speak loudly. I heard
 11047	that, and I came down."
 11048	
 11049	"You shut up the windows and doors the night before. Did you
 11050	fasten all the windows?"
 11051	
 11052	"Yes."
 11053	
 11054	"Were they all fastened this morning?"
 11055	
 11056	"Yes."
 11057	
 11058	"You have a maid who has a sweetheart? I think that you remarked
 11059	to your uncle last night that she had been out to see him?"
 11060	
 11061	"Yes, and she was the girl who waited in the drawing-room, and
 11062	who may have heard uncle's remarks about the coronet."
 11063	
 11064	"I see. You infer that she may have gone out to tell her
 11065	sweetheart, and that the two may have planned the robbery."
 11066	
 11067	"But what is the good of all these vague theories," cried the
 11068	banker impatiently, "when I have told you that I saw Arthur with
 11069	the coronet in his hands?"
 11070	
 11071	"Wait a little, Mr. Holder. We must come back to that. About this
 11072	girl, Miss Holder. You saw her return by the kitchen door, I
 11073	presume?"
 11074	
 11075	"Yes; when I went to see if the door was fastened for the night I
 11076	met her slipping in. I saw the man, too, in the gloom."
 11077	
 11078	"Do you know him?"
 11079	
 11080	"Oh, yes! he is the green-grocer who brings our vegetables round.
 11081	His name is Francis Prosper."
 11082	
 11083	"He stood," said Holmes, "to the left of the door--that is to
 11084	say, farther up the path than is necessary to reach the door?"
 11085	
 11086	"Yes, he did."
 11087	
 11088	"And he is a man with a wooden leg?"
 11089	
 11090	Something like fear sprang up in the young lady's expressive
 11091	black eyes. "Why, you are like a magician," said she. "How do you
 11092	know that?" She smiled, but there was no answering smile in
 11093	Holmes' thin, eager face.
 11094	
 11095	"I should be very glad now to go upstairs," said he. "I shall
 11096	probably wish to go over the outside of the house again. Perhaps
 11097	I had better take a look at the lower windows before I go up."
 11098	
 11099	He walked swiftly round from one to the other, pausing only at
 11100	the large one which looked from the hall onto the stable lane.
 11101	This he opened and made a very careful examination of the sill
 11102	with his powerful magnifying lens. "Now we shall go upstairs,"
 11103	said he at last.
 11104	
 11105	The banker's dressing-room was a plainly furnished little
 11106	chamber, with a grey carpet, a large bureau, and a long mirror.
 11107	Holmes went to the bureau first and looked hard at the lock.
 11108	
 11109	"Which key was used to open it?" he asked.
 11110	
 11111	"That which my son himself indicated--that of the cupboard of the
 11112	lumber-room."
 11113	
 11114	"Have you it here?"
 11115	
 11116	"That is it on the dressing-table."
 11117	
 11118	Sherlock Holmes took it up and opened the bureau.
 11119	
 11120	"It is a noiseless lock," said he. "It is no wonder that it did
 11121	not wake you. This case, I presume, contains the coronet. We must
 11122	have a look at it." He opened the case, and taking out the diadem
 11123	he laid it upon the table. It was a magnificent specimen of the
 11124	jeweller's art, and the thirty-six stones were the finest that I
 11125	have ever seen. At one side of the coronet was a cracked edge,
 11126	where a corner holding three gems had been torn away.
 11127	
 11128	"Now, Mr. Holder," said Holmes, "here is the corner which
 11129	corresponds to that which has been so unfortunately lost. Might I
 11130	beg that you will break it off."
 11131	
 11132	The banker recoiled in horror. "I should not dream of trying,"
 11133	said he.
 11134	
 11135	"Then I will." Holmes suddenly bent his strength upon it, but
 11136	without result. "I feel it give a little," said he; "but, though
 11137	I am exceptionally strong in the fingers, it would take me all my
 11138	time to break it. An ordinary man could not do it. Now, what do
 11139	you think would happen if I did break it, Mr. Holder? There would
 11140	be a noise like a pistol shot. Do you tell me that all this
 11141	happened within a few yards of your bed and that you heard
 11142	nothing of it?"
 11143	
 11144	"I do not know what to think. It is all dark to me."
 11145	
 11146	"But perhaps it may grow lighter as we go. What do you think,
 11147	Miss Holder?"
 11148	
 11149	"I confess that I still share my uncle's perplexity."
 11150	
 11151	"Your son had no shoes or slippers on when you saw him?"
 11152	
 11153	"He had nothing on save only his trousers and shirt."
 11154	
 11155	"Thank you. We have certainly been favoured with extraordinary
 11156	luck during this inquiry, and it will be entirely our own fault
 11157	if we do not succeed in clearing the matter up. With your
 11158	permission, Mr. Holder, I shall now continue my investigations
 11159	outside."
 11160	
 11161	He went alone, at his own request, for he explained that any
 11162	unnecessary footmarks might make his task more difficult. For an
 11163	hour or more he was at work, returning at last with his feet
 11164	heavy with snow and his features as inscrutable as ever.
 11165	
 11166	"I think that I have seen now all that there is to see, Mr.
 11167	Holder," said he; "I can serve you best by returning to my
 11168	rooms."
 11169	
 11170	"But the gems, Mr. Holmes. Where are they?"
 11171	
 11172	"I cannot tell."
 11173	
 11174	The banker wrung his hands. "I shall never see them again!" he
 11175	cried. "And my son? You give me hopes?"
 11176	
 11177	"My opinion is in no way altered."
 11178	
 11179	"Then, for God's sake, what was this dark business which was
 11180	acted in my house last night?"
 11181	
 11182	"If you can call upon me at my Baker Street rooms to-morrow
 11183	morning between nine and ten I shall be happy to do what I can to
 11184	make it clearer. I understand that you give me carte blanche to
 11185	act for you, provided only that I get back the gems, and that you
 11186	place no limit on the sum I may draw."
 11187	
 11188	"I would give my fortune to have them back."
 11189	
 11190	"Very good. I shall look into the matter between this and then.
 11191	Good-bye; it is just possible that I may have to come over here
 11192	again before evening."
 11193	
 11194	It was obvious to me that my companion's mind was now made up
 11195	about the case, although what his conclusions were was more than
 11196	I could even dimly imagine. Several times during our homeward
 11197	journey I endeavoured to sound him upon the point, but he always
 11198	glided away to some other topic, until at last I gave it over in
 11199	despair. It was not yet three when we found ourselves in our
 11200	rooms once more. He hurried to his chamber and was down again in
 11201	a few minutes dressed as a common loafer. With his collar turned
 11202	up, his shiny, seedy coat, his red cravat, and his worn boots, he
 11203	was a perfect sample of the class.
 11204	
 11205	"I think that this should do," said he, glancing into the glass
 11206	above the fireplace. "I only wish that you could come with me,
 11207	Watson, but I fear that it won't do. I may be on the trail in
 11208	this matter, or I may be following a will-o'-the-wisp, but I
 11209	shall soon know which it is. I hope that I may be back in a few
 11210	hours." He cut a slice of beef from the joint upon the sideboard,
 11211	sandwiched it between two rounds of bread, and thrusting this
 11212	rude meal into his pocket he started off upon his expedition.
 11213	
 11214	I had just finished my tea when he returned, evidently in
 11215	excellent spirits, swinging an old elastic-sided boot in his
 11216	hand. He chucked it down into a corner and helped himself to a
 11217	cup of tea.
 11218	
 11219	"I only looked in as I passed," said he. "I am going right on."
 11220	
 11221	"Where to?"
 11222	
 11223	"Oh, to the other side of the West End. It may be some time
 11224	before I get back. Don't wait up for me in case I should be
 11225	late."
 11226	
 11227	"How are you getting on?"
 11228	
 11229	"Oh, so so. Nothing to complain of. I have been out to Streatham
 11230	since I saw you last, but I did not call at the house. It is a
 11231	very sweet little problem, and I would not have missed it for a
 11232	good deal. However, I must not sit gossiping here, but must get
 11233	these disreputable clothes off and return to my highly
 11234	respectable self."
 11235	
 11236	I could see by his manner that he had stronger reasons for
 11237	satisfaction than his words alone would imply. His eyes twinkled,
 11238	and there was even a touch of colour upon his sallow cheeks. He
 11239	hastened upstairs, and a few minutes later I heard the slam of
 11240	the hall door, which told me that he was off once more upon his
 11241	congenial hunt.
 11242	
 11243	I waited until midnight, but there was no sign of his return, so
 11244	I retired to my room. It was no uncommon thing for him to be away
 11245	for days and nights on end when he was hot upon a scent, so that
 11246	his lateness caused me no surprise. I do not know at what hour he
 11247	came in, but when I came down to breakfast in the morning there
 11248	he was with a cup of coffee in one hand and the paper in the
 11249	other, as fresh and trim as possible.
 11250	
 11251	"You will excuse my beginning without you, Watson," said he, "but
 11252	you remember that our client has rather an early appointment this
 11253	morning."
 11254	
 11255	"Why, it is after nine now," I answered. "I should not be
 11256	surprised if that were he. I thought I heard a ring."
 11257	
 11258	It was, indeed, our friend the financier. I was shocked by the
 11259	change which had come over him, for his face which was naturally
 11260	of a broad and massive mould, was now pinched and fallen in,
 11261	while his hair seemed to me at least a shade whiter. He entered
 11262	with a weariness and lethargy which was even more painful than
 11263	his violence of the morning before, and he dropped heavily into
 11264	the armchair which I pushed forward for him.
 11265	
 11266	"I do not know what I have done to be so severely tried," said
 11267	he. "Only two days ago I was a happy and prosperous man, without
 11268	a care in the world. Now I am left to a lonely and dishonoured
 11269	age. One sorrow comes close upon the heels of another. My niece,
 11270	Mary, has deserted me."
 11271	
 11272	"Deserted you?"
 11273	
 11274	"Yes. Her bed this morning had not been slept in, her room was
 11275	empty, and a note for me lay upon the hall table. I had said to
 11276	her last night, in sorrow and not in anger, that if she had
 11277	married my boy all might have been well with him. Perhaps it was
 11278	thoughtless of me to say so. It is to that remark that she refers
 11279	in this note:
 11280	
 11281	"'MY DEAREST UNCLE:--I feel that I have brought trouble upon you,
 11282	and that if I had acted differently this terrible misfortune
 11283	might never have occurred. I cannot, with this thought in my
 11284	mind, ever again be happy under your roof, and I feel that I must
 11285	leave you forever. Do not worry about my future, for that is
 11286	provided for; and, above all, do not search for me, for it will
 11287	be fruitless labour and an ill-service to me. In life or in       
 11288	death, I am ever your loving,--MARY.'
 11289	
 11290	"What could she mean by that note, Mr. Holmes? Do you think it
 11291	points to suicide?"
 11292	
 11293	"No, no, nothing of the kind. It is perhaps the best possible
 11294	solution. I trust, Mr. Holder, that you are nearing the end of
 11295	your troubles."
 11296	
 11297	"Ha! You say so! You have heard something, Mr. Holmes; you have
 11298	learned something! Where are the gems?"
 11299	
 11300	"You would not think 1000 pounds apiece an excessive sum for
 11301	them?"
 11302	
 11303	"I would pay ten."
 11304	
 11305	"That would be unnecessary. Three thousand will cover the matter.
 11306	And there is a little reward, I fancy. Have you your check-book?
 11307	Here is a pen. Better make it out for 4000 pounds."
 11308	
 11309	With a dazed face the banker made out the required check. Holmes
 11310	walked over to his desk, took out a little triangular piece of
 11311	gold with three gems in it, and threw it down upon the table.
 11312	
 11313	With a shriek of joy our client clutched it up.
 11314	
 11315	"You have it!" he gasped. "I am saved! I am saved!"
 11316	
 11317	The reaction of joy was as passionate as his grief had been, and
 11318	he hugged his recovered gems to his bosom.
 11319	
 11320	"There is one other thing you owe, Mr. Holder," said Sherlock
 11321	Holmes rather sternly.
 11322	
 11323	"Owe!" He caught up a pen. "Name the sum, and I will pay it."
 11324	
 11325	"No, the debt is not to me. You owe a very humble apology to that
 11326	noble lad, your son, who has carried himself in this matter as I
 11327	should be proud to see my own son do, should I ever chance to
 11328	have one."
 11329	
 11330	"Then it was not Arthur who took them?"
 11331	
 11332	"I told you yesterday, and I repeat to-day, that it was not."
 11333	
 11334	"You are sure of it! Then let us hurry to him at once to let him
 11335	know that the truth is known."
 11336	
 11337	"He knows it already. When I had cleared it all up I had an
 11338	interview with him, and finding that he would not tell me the
 11339	story, I told it to him, on which he had to confess that I was
 11340	right and to add the very few details which were not yet quite
 11341	clear to me. Your news of this morning, however, may open his
 11342	lips."
 11343	
 11344	"For heaven's sake, tell me, then, what is this extraordinary
 11345	mystery!"
 11346	
 11347	"I will do so, and I will show you the steps by which I reached
 11348	it. And let me say to you, first, that which it is hardest for me
 11349	to say and for you to hear: there has been an understanding
 11350	between Sir George Burnwell and your niece Mary. They have now
 11351	fled together."
 11352	
 11353	"My Mary? Impossible!"
 11354	
 11355	"It is unfortunately more than possible; it is certain. Neither
 11356	you nor your son knew the true character of this man when you
 11357	admitted him into your family circle. He is one of the most
 11358	dangerous men in England--a ruined gambler, an absolutely
 11359	desperate villain, a man without heart or conscience. Your niece
 11360	knew nothing of such men. When he breathed his vows to her, as he
 11361	had done to a hundred before her, she flattered herself that she
 11362	alone had touched his heart. The devil knows best what he said,
 11363	but at least she became his tool and was in the habit of seeing
 11364	him nearly every evening."
 11365	
 11366	"I cannot, and I will not, believe it!" cried the banker with an
 11367	ashen face.
 11368	
 11369	"I will tell you, then, what occurred in your house last night.
 11370	Your niece, when you had, as she thought, gone to your room,
 11371	slipped down and talked to her lover through the window which
 11372	leads into the stable lane. His footmarks had pressed right
 11373	through the snow, so long had he stood there. She told him of the
 11374	coronet. His wicked lust for gold kindled at the news, and he
 11375	bent her to his will. I have no doubt that she loved you, but
 11376	there are women in whom the love of a lover extinguishes all
 11377	other loves, and I think that she must have been one. She had
 11378	hardly listened to his instructions when she saw you coming
 11379	downstairs, on which she closed the window rapidly and told you
 11380	about one of the servants' escapade with her wooden-legged lover,
 11381	which was all perfectly true.
 11382	
 11383	"Your boy, Arthur, went to bed after his interview with you but
 11384	he slept badly on account of his uneasiness about his club debts.
 11385	In the middle of the night he heard a soft tread pass his door,
 11386	so he rose and, looking out, was surprised to see his cousin
 11387	walking very stealthily along the passage until she disappeared
 11388	into your dressing-room. Petrified with astonishment, the lad
 11389	slipped on some clothes and waited there in the dark to see what
 11390	would come of this strange affair. Presently she emerged from the
 11391	room again, and in the light of the passage-lamp your son saw
 11392	that she carried the precious coronet in her hands. She passed
 11393	down the stairs, and he, thrilling with horror, ran along and
 11394	slipped behind the curtain near your door, whence he could see
 11395	what passed in the hall beneath. He saw her stealthily open the
 11396	window, hand out the coronet to someone in the gloom, and then
 11397	closing it once more hurry back to her room, passing quite close
 11398	to where he stood hid behind the curtain.
 11399	
 11400	"As long as she was on the scene he could not take any action
 11401	without a horrible exposure of the woman whom he loved. But the
 11402	instant that she was gone he realised how crushing a misfortune
 11403	this would be for you, and how all-important it was to set it
 11404	right. He rushed down, just as he was, in his bare feet, opened
 11405	the window, sprang out into the snow, and ran down the lane,
 11406	where he could see a dark figure in the moonlight. Sir George
 11407	Burnwell tried to get away, but Arthur caught him, and there was
 11408	a struggle between them, your lad tugging at one side of the
 11409	coronet, and his opponent at the other. In the scuffle, your son
 11410	struck Sir George and cut him over the eye. Then something
 11411	suddenly snapped, and your son, finding that he had the coronet
 11412	in his hands, rushed back, closed the window, ascended to your
 11413	room, and had just observed that the coronet had been twisted in
 11414	the struggle and was endeavouring to straighten it when you
 11415	appeared upon the scene."
 11416	
 11417	"Is it possible?" gasped the banker.
 11418	
 11419	"You then roused his anger by calling him names at a moment when
 11420	he felt that he had deserved your warmest thanks. He could not
 11421	explain the true state of affairs without betraying one who
 11422	certainly deserved little enough consideration at his hands. He
 11423	took the more chivalrous view, however, and preserved her
 11424	secret."
 11425	
 11426	"And that was why she shrieked and fainted when she saw the
 11427	coronet," cried Mr. Holder. "Oh, my God! what a blind fool I have
 11428	been! And his asking to be allowed to go out for five minutes!
 11429	The dear fellow wanted to see if the missing piece were at the
 11430	scene of the struggle. How cruelly I have misjudged him!"
 11431	
 11432	"When I arrived at the house," continued Holmes, "I at once went
 11433	very carefully round it to observe if there were any traces in
 11434	the snow which might help me. I knew that none had fallen since
 11435	the evening before, and also that there had been a strong frost
 11436	to preserve impressions. I passed along the tradesmen's path, but
 11437	found it all trampled down and indistinguishable. Just beyond it,
 11438	however, at the far side of the kitchen door, a woman had stood
 11439	and talked with a man, whose round impressions on one side showed
 11440	that he had a wooden leg. I could even tell that they had been
 11441	disturbed, for the woman had run back swiftly to the door, as was
 11442	shown by the deep toe and light heel marks, while Wooden-leg had
 11443	waited a little, and then had gone away. I thought at the time
 11444	that this might be the maid and her sweetheart, of whom you had
 11445	already spoken to me, and inquiry showed it was so. I passed
 11446	round the garden without seeing anything more than random tracks,
 11447	which I took to be the police; but when I got into the stable
 11448	lane a very long and complex story was written in the snow in
 11449	front of me.
 11450	
 11451	"There was a double line of tracks of a booted man, and a second
 11452	double line which I saw with delight belonged to a man with naked
 11453	feet. I was at once convinced from what you had told me that the
 11454	latter was your son. The first had walked both ways, but the
 11455	other had run swiftly, and as his tread was marked in places over
 11456	the depression of the boot, it was obvious that he had passed
 11457	after the other. I followed them up and found they led to the
 11458	hall window, where Boots had worn all the snow away while
 11459	waiting. Then I walked to the other end, which was a hundred
 11460	yards or more down the lane. I saw where Boots had faced round,
 11461	where the snow was cut up as though there had been a struggle,
 11462	and, finally, where a few drops of blood had fallen, to show me
 11463	that I was not mistaken. Boots had then run down the lane, and
 11464	another little smudge of blood showed that it was he who had been
 11465	hurt. When he came to the highroad at the other end, I found that
 11466	the pavement had been cleared, so there was an end to that clue.
 11467	
 11468	"On entering the house, however, I examined, as you remember, the
 11469	sill and framework of the hall window with my lens, and I could
 11470	at once see that someone had passed out. I could distinguish the
 11471	outline of an instep where the wet foot had been placed in coming
 11472	in. I was then beginning to be able to form an opinion as to what
 11473	had occurred. A man had waited outside the window; someone had
 11474	brought the gems; the deed had been overseen by your son; he had
 11475	pursued the thief; had struggled with him; they had each tugged
 11476	at the coronet, their united strength causing injuries which
 11477	neither alone could have effected. He had returned with the
 11478	prize, but had left a fragment in the grasp of his opponent. So
 11479	far I was clear. The question now was, who was the man and who
 11480	was it brought him the coronet?
 11481	
 11482	"It is an old maxim of mine that when you have excluded the
 11483	impossible, whatever remains, however improbable, must be the
 11484	truth. Now, I knew that it was not you who had brought it down,
 11485	so there only remained your niece and the maids. But if it were
 11486	the maids, why should your son allow himself to be accused in
 11487	their place? There could be no possible reason. As he loved his
 11488	cousin, however, there was an excellent explanation why he should
 11489	retain her secret--the more so as the secret was a disgraceful
 11490	one. When I remembered that you had seen her at that window, and
 11491	how she had fainted on seeing the coronet again, my conjecture
 11492	became a certainty.
 11493	
 11494	"And who could it be who was her confederate? A lover evidently,
 11495	for who else could outweigh the love and gratitude which she must
 11496	feel to you? I knew that you went out little, and that your
 11497	circle of friends was a very limited one. But among them was Sir
 11498	George Burnwell. I had heard of him before as being a man of evil
 11499	reputation among women. It must have been he who wore those boots
 11500	and retained the missing gems. Even though he knew that Arthur
 11501	had discovered him, he might still flatter himself that he was
 11502	safe, for the lad could not say a word without compromising his
 11503	own family.
 11504	
 11505	"Well, your own good sense will suggest what measures I took
 11506	next. I went in the shape of a loafer to Sir George's house,
 11507	managed to pick up an acquaintance with his valet, learned that
 11508	his master had cut his head the night before, and, finally, at
 11509	the expense of six shillings, made all sure by buying a pair of
 11510	his cast-off shoes. With these I journeyed down to Streatham and
 11511	saw that they exactly fitted the tracks."
 11512	
 11513	"I saw an ill-dressed vagabond in the lane yesterday evening,"
 11514	said Mr. Holder.
 11515	
 11516	"Precisely. It was I. I found that I had my man, so I came home
 11517	and changed my clothes. It was a delicate part which I had to
 11518	play then, for I saw that a prosecution must be avoided to avert
 11519	scandal, and I knew that so astute a villain would see that our
 11520	hands were tied in the matter. I went and saw him. At first, of
 11521	course, he denied everything. But when I gave him every
 11522	particular that had occurred, he tried to bluster and took down a
 11523	life-preserver from the wall. I knew my man, however, and I
 11524	clapped a pistol to his head before he could strike. Then he
 11525	became a little more reasonable. I told him that we would give
 11526	him a price for the stones he held--1000 pounds apiece. That
 11527	brought out the first signs of grief that he had shown. 'Why,
 11528	dash it all!' said he, 'I've let them go at six hundred for the
 11529	three!' I soon managed to get the address of the receiver who had
 11530	them, on promising him that there would be no prosecution. Off I
 11531	set to him, and after much chaffering I got our stones at 1000
 11532	pounds apiece. Then I looked in upon your son, told him that all
 11533	was right, and eventually got to my bed about two o'clock, after
 11534	what I may call a really hard day's work."
 11535	
 11536	"A day which has saved England from a great public scandal," said
 11537	the banker, rising. "Sir, I cannot find words to thank you, but
 11538	you shall not find me ungrateful for what you have done. Your
 11539	skill has indeed exceeded all that I have heard of it. And now I
 11540	must fly to my dear boy to apologise to him for the wrong which I
 11541	have done him. As to what you tell me of poor Mary, it goes to my
 11542	very heart. Not even your skill can inform me where she is now."
 11543	
 11544	"I think that we may safely say," returned Holmes, "that she is
 11545	wherever Sir George Burnwell is. It is equally certain, too, that
 11546	whatever her sins are, they will soon receive a more than
 11547	sufficient punishment."
 11548	
 11549	
 11550	
 11551	XII. THE ADVENTURE OF THE COPPER BEECHES
 11552	
 11553	"To the man who loves art for its own sake," remarked Sherlock
 11554	Holmes, tossing aside the advertisement sheet of the Daily
 11555	Telegraph, "it is frequently in its least important and lowliest
 11556	manifestations that the keenest pleasure is to be derived. It is
 11557	pleasant to me to observe, Watson, that you have so far grasped
 11558	this truth that in these little records of our cases which you
 11559	have been good enough to draw up, and, I am bound to say,
 11560	occasionally to embellish, you have given prominence not so much
 11561	to the many causes c?l?bres and sensational trials in which I
 11562	have figured but rather to those incidents which may have been
 11563	trivial in themselves, but which have given room for those
 11564	faculties of deduction and of logical synthesis which I have made
 11565	my special province."
 11566	
 11567	"And yet," said I, smiling, "I cannot quite hold myself absolved
 11568	from the charge of sensationalism which has been urged against my
 11569	records."
 11570	
 11571	"You have erred, perhaps," he observed, taking up a glowing
 11572	cinder with the tongs and lighting with it the long cherry-wood
 11573	pipe which was wont to replace his clay when he was in a
 11574	disputatious rather than a meditative mood--"you have erred
 11575	perhaps in attempting to put colour and life into each of your
 11576	statements instead of confining yourself to the task of placing
 11577	upon record that severe reasoning from cause to effect which is
 11578	really the only notable feature about the thing."
 11579	
 11580	"It seems to me that I have done you full justice in the matter,"
 11581	I remarked with some coldness, for I was repelled by the egotism
 11582	which I had more than once observed to be a strong factor in my
 11583	friend's singular character.
 11584	
 11585	"No, it is not selfishness or conceit," said he, answering, as
 11586	was his wont, my thoughts rather than my words. "If I claim full
 11587	justice for my art, it is because it is an impersonal thing--a
 11588	thing beyond myself. Crime is common. Logic is rare. Therefore it
 11589	is upon the logic rather than upon the crime that you should
 11590	dwell. You have degraded what should have been a course of
 11591	lectures into a series of tales."
 11592	
 11593	It was a cold morning of the early spring, and we sat after
 11594	breakfast on either side of a cheery fire in the old room at
 11595	Baker Street. A thick fog rolled down between the lines of
 11596	dun-coloured houses, and the opposing windows loomed like dark,
 11597	shapeless blurs through the heavy yellow wreaths. Our gas was lit
 11598	and shone on the white cloth and glimmer of china and metal, for
 11599	the table had not been cleared yet. Sherlock Holmes had been
 11600	silent all the morning, dipping continuously into the
 11601	advertisement columns of a succession of papers until at last,
 11602	having apparently given up his search, he had emerged in no very
 11603	sweet temper to lecture me upon my literary shortcomings.
 11604	
 11605	"At the same time," he remarked after a pause, during which he
 11606	had sat puffing at his long pipe and gazing down into the fire,
 11607	"you can hardly be open to a charge of sensationalism, for out of
 11608	these cases which you have been so kind as to interest yourself
 11609	in, a fair proportion do not treat of crime, in its legal sense,
 11610	at all. The small matter in which I endeavoured to help the King
 11611	of Bohemia, the singular experience of Miss Mary Sutherland, the
 11612	problem connected with the man with the twisted lip, and the
 11613	incident of the noble bachelor, were all matters which are
 11614	outside the pale of the law. But in avoiding the sensational, I
 11615	fear that you may have bordered on the trivial."
 11616	
 11617	"The end may have been so," I answered, "but the methods I hold
 11618	to have been novel and of interest."
 11619	
 11620	"Pshaw, my dear fellow, what do the public, the great unobservant
 11621	public, who could hardly tell a weaver by his tooth or a
 11622	compositor by his left thumb, care about the finer shades of
 11623	analysis and deduction! But, indeed, if you are trivial. I cannot
 11624	blame you, for the days of the great cases are past. Man, or at
 11625	least criminal man, has lost all enterprise and originality. As
 11626	to my own little practice, it seems to be degenerating into an
 11627	agency for recovering lost lead pencils and giving advice to
 11628	young ladies from boarding-schools. I think that I have touched
 11629	bottom at last, however. This note I had this morning marks my
 11630	zero-point, I fancy. Read it!" He tossed a crumpled letter across
 11631	to me.
 11632	
 11633	It was dated from Montague Place upon the preceding evening, and
 11634	ran thus:
 11635	
 11636	"DEAR MR. HOLMES:--I am very anxious to consult you as to whether
 11637	I should or should not accept a situation which has been offered
 11638	to me as governess. I shall call at half-past ten to-morrow if I
 11639	do not inconvenience you. Yours faithfully,
 11640	                                               "VIOLET HUNTER."
 11641	
 11642	"Do you know the young lady?" I asked.
 11643	
 11644	"Not I."
 11645	
 11646	"It is half-past ten now."
 11647	
 11648	"Yes, and I have no doubt that is her ring."
 11649	
 11650	"It may turn out to be of more interest than you think. You
 11651	remember that the affair of the blue carbuncle, which appeared to
 11652	be a mere whim at first, developed into a serious investigation.
 11653	It may be so in this case, also."
 11654	
 11655	"Well, let us hope so. But our doubts will very soon be solved,
 11656	for here, unless I am much mistaken, is the person in question."
 11657	
 11658	As he spoke the door opened and a young lady entered the room.
 11659	She was plainly but neatly dressed, with a bright, quick face,
 11660	freckled like a plover's egg, and with the brisk manner of a
 11661	woman who has had her own way to make in the world.
 11662	
 11663	"You will excuse my troubling you, I am sure," said she, as my
 11664	companion rose to greet her, "but I have had a very strange
 11665	experience, and as I have no parents or relations of any sort
 11666	from whom I could ask advice, I thought that perhaps you would be
 11667	kind enough to tell me what I should do."
 11668	
 11669	"Pray take a seat, Miss Hunter. I shall be happy to do anything
 11670	that I can to serve you."
 11671	
 11672	I could see that Holmes was favourably impressed by the manner
 11673	and speech of his new client. He looked her over in his searching
 11674	fashion, and then composed himself, with his lids drooping and
 11675	his finger-tips together, to listen to her story.
 11676	
 11677	"I have been a governess for five years," said she, "in the
 11678	family of Colonel Spence Munro, but two months ago the colonel
 11679	received an appointment at Halifax, in Nova Scotia, and took his
 11680	children over to America with him, so that I found myself without
 11681	a situation. I advertised, and I answered advertisements, but
 11682	without success. At last the little money which I had saved began
 11683	to run short, and I was at my wit's end as to what I should do.
 11684	
 11685	"There is a well-known agency for governesses in the West End
 11686	called Westaway's, and there I used to call about once a week in
 11687	order to see whether anything had turned up which might suit me.
 11688	Westaway was the name of the founder of the business, but it is
 11689	really managed by Miss Stoper. She sits in her own little office,
 11690	and the ladies who are seeking employment wait in an anteroom,
 11691	and are then shown in one by one, when she consults her ledgers
 11692	and sees whether she has anything which would suit them.
 11693	
 11694	"Well, when I called last week I was shown into the little office
 11695	as usual, but I found that Miss Stoper was not alone. A
 11696	prodigiously stout man with a very smiling face and a great heavy
 11697	chin which rolled down in fold upon fold over his throat sat at
 11698	her elbow with a pair of glasses on his nose, looking very
 11699	earnestly at the ladies who entered. As I came in he gave quite a
 11700	jump in his chair and turned quickly to Miss Stoper.
 11701	
 11702	"'That will do,' said he; 'I could not ask for anything better.
 11703	Capital! capital!' He seemed quite enthusiastic and rubbed his
 11704	hands together in the most genial fashion. He was such a
 11705	comfortable-looking man that it was quite a pleasure to look at
 11706	him.
 11707	
 11708	"'You are looking for a situation, miss?' he asked.
 11709	
 11710	"'Yes, sir.'
 11711	
 11712	"'As governess?'
 11713	
 11714	"'Yes, sir.'
 11715	
 11716	"'And what salary do you ask?'
 11717	
 11718	"'I had 4 pounds a month in my last place with Colonel Spence
 11719	Munro.'
 11720	
 11721	"'Oh, tut, tut! sweating--rank sweating!' he cried, throwing his
 11722	fat hands out into the air like a man who is in a boiling
 11723	passion. 'How could anyone offer so pitiful a sum to a lady with
 11724	such attractions and accomplishments?'
 11725	
 11726	"'My accomplishments, sir, may be less than you imagine,' said I.
 11727	'A little French, a little German, music, and drawing--'
 11728	
 11729	"'Tut, tut!' he cried. 'This is all quite beside the question.
 11730	The point is, have you or have you not the bearing and deportment
 11731	of a lady? There it is in a nutshell. If you have not, you are
 11732	not fitted for the rearing of a child who may some day play a
 11733	considerable part in the history of the country. But if you have
 11734	why, then, how could any gentleman ask you to condescend to
 11735	accept anything under the three figures? Your salary with me,
 11736	madam, would commence at 100 pounds a year.'
 11737	
 11738	"You may imagine, Mr. Holmes, that to me, destitute as I was,
 11739	such an offer seemed almost too good to be true. The gentleman,
 11740	however, seeing perhaps the look of incredulity upon my face,
 11741	opened a pocket-book and took out a note.
 11742	
 11743	"'It is also my custom,' said he, smiling in the most pleasant
 11744	fashion until his eyes were just two little shining slits amid
 11745	the white creases of his face, 'to advance to my young ladies
 11746	half their salary beforehand, so that they may meet any little
 11747	expenses of their journey and their wardrobe.'
 11748	
 11749	"It seemed to me that I had never met so fascinating and so
 11750	thoughtful a man. As I was already in debt to my tradesmen, the
 11751	advance was a great convenience, and yet there was something
 11752	unnatural about the whole transaction which made me wish to know
 11753	a little more before I quite committed myself.
 11754	
 11755	"'May I ask where you live, sir?' said I.
 11756	
 11757	"'Hampshire. Charming rural place. The Copper Beeches, five miles
 11758	on the far side of Winchester. It is the most lovely country, my
 11759	dear young lady, and the dearest old country-house.'
 11760	
 11761	"'And my duties, sir? I should be glad to know what they would
 11762	be.'
 11763	
 11764	"'One child--one dear little romper just six years old. Oh, if
 11765	you could see him killing cockroaches with a slipper! Smack!
 11766	smack! smack! Three gone before you could wink!' He leaned back
 11767	in his chair and laughed his eyes into his head again.
 11768	
 11769	"I was a little startled at the nature of the child's amusement,
 11770	but the father's laughter made me think that perhaps he was
 11771	joking.
 11772	
 11773	"'My sole duties, then,' I asked, 'are to take charge of a single
 11774	child?'
 11775	
 11776	"'No, no, not the sole, not the sole, my dear young lady,' he
 11777	cried. 'Your duty would be, as I am sure your good sense would
 11778	suggest, to obey any little commands my wife might give, provided
 11779	always that they were such commands as a lady might with
 11780	propriety obey. You see no difficulty, heh?'
 11781	
 11782	"'I should be happy to make myself useful.'
 11783	
 11784	"'Quite so. In dress now, for example. We are faddy people, you
 11785	know--faddy but kind-hearted. If you were asked to wear any dress
 11786	which we might give you, you would not object to our little whim.
 11787	Heh?'
 11788	
 11789	"'No,' said I, considerably astonished at his words.
 11790	
 11791	"'Or to sit here, or sit there, that would not be offensive to
 11792	you?'
 11793	
 11794	"'Oh, no.'
 11795	
 11796	"'Or to cut your hair quite short before you come to us?'
 11797	
 11798	"I could hardly believe my ears. As you may observe, Mr. Holmes,
 11799	my hair is somewhat luxuriant, and of a rather peculiar tint of
 11800	chestnut. It has been considered artistic. I could not dream of
 11801	sacrificing it in this offhand fashion.
 11802	
 11803	"'I am afraid that that is quite impossible,' said I. He had been
 11804	watching me eagerly out of his small eyes, and I could see a
 11805	shadow pass over his face as I spoke.
 11806	
 11807	"'I am afraid that it is quite essential,' said he. 'It is a
 11808	little fancy of my wife's, and ladies' fancies, you know, madam,
 11809	ladies' fancies must be consulted. And so you won't cut your
 11810	hair?'
 11811	
 11812	"'No, sir, I really could not,' I answered firmly.
 11813	
 11814	"'Ah, very well; then that quite settles the matter. It is a
 11815	pity, because in other respects you would really have done very
 11816	nicely. In that case, Miss Stoper, I had best inspect a few more
 11817	of your young ladies.'
 11818	
 11819	"The manageress had sat all this while busy with her papers
 11820	without a word to either of us, but she glanced at me now with so
 11821	much annoyance upon her face that I could not help suspecting
 11822	that she had lost a handsome commission through my refusal.
 11823	
 11824	"'Do you desire your name to be kept upon the books?' she asked.
 11825	
 11826	"'If you please, Miss Stoper.'
 11827	
 11828	"'Well, really, it seems rather useless, since you refuse the
 11829	most excellent offers in this fashion,' said she sharply. 'You
 11830	can hardly expect us to exert ourselves to find another such
 11831	opening for you. Good-day to you, Miss Hunter.' She struck a gong
 11832	upon the table, and I was shown out by the page.
 11833	
 11834	"Well, Mr. Holmes, when I got back to my lodgings and found
 11835	little enough in the cupboard, and two or three bills upon the
 11836	table. I began to ask myself whether I had not done a very
 11837	foolish thing. After all, if these people had strange fads and
 11838	expected obedience on the most extraordinary matters, they were
 11839	at least ready to pay for their eccentricity. Very few
 11840	governesses in England are getting 100 pounds a year. Besides,
 11841	what use was my hair to me? Many people are improved by wearing
 11842	it short and perhaps I should be among the number. Next day I was
 11843	inclined to think that I had made a mistake, and by the day after
 11844	I was sure of it. I had almost overcome my pride so far as to go
 11845	back to the agency and inquire whether the place was still open
 11846	when I received this letter from the gentleman himself. I have it
 11847	here and I will read it to you:
 11848	
 11849	                       "'The Copper Beeches, near Winchester.
 11850	"'DEAR MISS HUNTER:--Miss Stoper has very kindly given me your
 11851	address, and I write from here to ask you whether you have
 11852	reconsidered your decision. My wife is very anxious that you
 11853	should come, for she has been much attracted by my description of
 11854	you. We are willing to give 30 pounds a quarter, or 120 pounds a
 11855	year, so as to recompense you for any little inconvenience which
 11856	our fads may cause you. They are not very exacting, after all. My
 11857	wife is fond of a particular shade of electric blue and would
 11858	like you to wear such a dress indoors in the morning. You need
 11859	not, however, go to the expense of purchasing one, as we have one
 11860	belonging to my dear daughter Alice (now in Philadelphia), which
 11861	would, I should think, fit you very well. Then, as to sitting
 11862	here or there, or amusing yourself in any manner indicated, that
 11863	need cause you no inconvenience. As regards your hair, it is no
 11864	doubt a pity, especially as I could not help remarking its beauty
 11865	during our short interview, but I am afraid that I must remain
 11866	firm upon this point, and I only hope that the increased salary
 11867	may recompense you for the loss. Your duties, as far as the child
 11868	is concerned, are very light. Now do try to come, and I shall
 11869	meet you with the dog-cart at Winchester. Let me know your train.
 11870	Yours faithfully, JEPHRO RUCASTLE.'
 11871	
 11872	"That is the letter which I have just received, Mr. Holmes, and
 11873	my mind is made up that I will accept it. I thought, however,
 11874	that before taking the final step I should like to submit the
 11875	whole matter to your consideration."
 11876	
 11877	"Well, Miss Hunter, if your mind is made up, that settles the
 11878	question," said Holmes, smiling.
 11879	
 11880	"But you would not advise me to refuse?"
 11881	
 11882	"I confess that it is not the situation which I should like to
 11883	see a sister of mine apply for."
 11884	
 11885	"What is the meaning of it all, Mr. Holmes?"
 11886	
 11887	"Ah, I have no data. I cannot tell. Perhaps you have yourself
 11888	formed some opinion?"
 11889	
 11890	"Well, there seems to me to be only one possible solution. Mr.
 11891	Rucastle seemed to be a very kind, good-natured man. Is it not
 11892	possible that his wife is a lunatic, that he desires to keep the
 11893	matter quiet for fear she should be taken to an asylum, and that
 11894	he humours her fancies in every way in order to prevent an
 11895	outbreak?"
 11896	
 11897	"That is a possible solution--in fact, as matters stand, it is
 11898	the most probable one. But in any case it does not seem to be a
 11899	nice household for a young lady."
 11900	
 11901	"But the money, Mr. Holmes, the money!"
 11902	
 11903	"Well, yes, of course the pay is good--too good. That is what
 11904	makes me uneasy. Why should they give you 120 pounds a year, when
 11905	they could have their pick for 40 pounds? There must be some
 11906	strong reason behind."
 11907	
 11908	"I thought that if I told you the circumstances you would
 11909	understand afterwards if I wanted your help. I should feel so
 11910	much stronger if I felt that you were at the back of me."
 11911	
 11912	"Oh, you may carry that feeling away with you. I assure you that
 11913	your little problem promises to be the most interesting which has
 11914	come my way for some months. There is something distinctly novel
 11915	about some of the features. If you should find yourself in doubt
 11916	or in danger--"
 11917	
 11918	"Danger! What danger do you foresee?"
 11919	
 11920	Holmes shook his head gravely. "It would cease to be a danger if
 11921	we could define it," said he. "But at any time, day or night, a
 11922	telegram would bring me down to your help."
 11923	
 11924	"That is enough." She rose briskly from her chair with the
 11925	anxiety all swept from her face. "I shall go down to Hampshire
 11926	quite easy in my mind now. I shall write to Mr. Rucastle at once,
 11927	sacrifice my poor hair to-night, and start for Winchester
 11928	to-morrow." With a few grateful words to Holmes she bade us both
 11929	good-night and bustled off upon her way.
 11930	
 11931	"At least," said I as we heard her quick, firm steps descending
 11932	the stairs, "she seems to be a young lady who is very well able
 11933	to take care of herself."
 11934	
 11935	"And she would need to be," said Holmes gravely. "I am much
 11936	mistaken if we do not hear from her before many days are past."
 11937	
 11938	It was not very long before my friend's prediction was fulfilled.
 11939	A fortnight went by, during which I frequently found my thoughts
 11940	turning in her direction and wondering what strange side-alley of
 11941	human experience this lonely woman had strayed into. The unusual
 11942	salary, the curious conditions, the light duties, all pointed to
 11943	something abnormal, though whether a fad or a plot, or whether
 11944	the man were a philanthropist or a villain, it was quite beyond
 11945	my powers to determine. As to Holmes, I observed that he sat
 11946	frequently for half an hour on end, with knitted brows and an
 11947	abstracted air, but he swept the matter away with a wave of his
 11948	hand when I mentioned it. "Data! data! data!" he cried
 11949	impatiently. "I can't make bricks without clay." And yet he would
 11950	always wind up by muttering that no sister of his should ever
 11951	have accepted such a situation.
 11952	
 11953	The telegram which we eventually received came late one night
 11954	just as I was thinking of turning in and Holmes was settling down
 11955	to one of those all-night chemical researches which he frequently
 11956	indulged in, when I would leave him stooping over a retort and a
 11957	test-tube at night and find him in the same position when I came
 11958	down to breakfast in the morning. He opened the yellow envelope,
 11959	and then, glancing at the message, threw it across to me.
 11960	
 11961	"Just look up the trains in Bradshaw," said he, and turned back
 11962	to his chemical studies.
 11963	
 11964	The summons was a brief and urgent one.
 11965	
 11966	"Please be at the Black Swan Hotel at Winchester at midday
 11967	to-morrow," it said. "Do come! I am at my wit's end.  HUNTER."
 11968	
 11969	"Will you come with me?" asked Holmes, glancing up.
 11970	
 11971	"I should wish to."
 11972	
 11973	"Just look it up, then."
 11974	
 11975	"There is a train at half-past nine," said I, glancing over my
 11976	Bradshaw. "It is due at Winchester at 11:30."
 11977	
 11978	"That will do very nicely. Then perhaps I had better postpone my
 11979	analysis of the acetones, as we may need to be at our best in the
 11980	morning."
 11981	
 11982	By eleven o'clock the next day we were well upon our way to the
 11983	old English capital. Holmes had been buried in the morning papers
 11984	all the way down, but after we had passed the Hampshire border he
 11985	threw them down and began to admire the scenery. It was an ideal
 11986	spring day, a light blue sky, flecked with little fleecy white
 11987	clouds drifting across from west to east. The sun was shining
 11988	very brightly, and yet there was an exhilarating nip in the air,
 11989	which set an edge to a man's energy. All over the countryside,
 11990	away to the rolling hills around Aldershot, the little red and
 11991	grey roofs of the farm-steadings peeped out from amid the light
 11992	green of the new foliage.
 11993	
 11994	"Are they not fresh and beautiful?" I cried with all the
 11995	enthusiasm of a man fresh from the fogs of Baker Street.
 11996	
 11997	But Holmes shook his head gravely.
 11998	
 11999	"Do you know, Watson," said he, "that it is one of the curses of
 12000	a mind with a turn like mine that I must look at everything with
 12001	reference to my own special subject. You look at these scattered
 12002	houses, and you are impressed by their beauty. I look at them,
 12003	and the only thought which comes to me is a feeling of their
 12004	isolation and of the impunity with which crime may be committed
 12005	there."
 12006	
 12007	"Good heavens!" I cried. "Who would associate crime with these
 12008	dear old homesteads?"
 12009	
 12010	"They always fill me with a certain horror. It is my belief,
 12011	Watson, founded upon my experience, that the lowest and vilest
 12012	alleys in London do not present a more dreadful record of sin
 12013	than does the smiling and beautiful countryside."
 12014	
 12015	"You horrify me!"
 12016	
 12017	"But the reason is very obvious. The pressure of public opinion
 12018	can do in the town what the law cannot accomplish. There is no
 12019	lane so vile that the scream of a tortured child, or the thud of
 12020	a drunkard's blow, does not beget sympathy and indignation among
 12021	the neighbours, and then the whole machinery of justice is ever
 12022	so close that a word of complaint can set it going, and there is
 12023	but a step between the crime and the dock. But look at these
 12024	lonely houses, each in its own fields, filled for the most part
 12025	with poor ignorant folk who know little of the law. Think of the
 12026	deeds of hellish cruelty, the hidden wickedness which may go on,
 12027	year in, year out, in such places, and none the wiser. Had this
 12028	lady who appeals to us for help gone to live in Winchester, I
 12029	should never have had a fear for her. It is the five miles of
 12030	country which makes the danger. Still, it is clear that she is
 12031	not personally threatened."
 12032	
 12033	"No. If she can come to Winchester to meet us she can get away."
 12034	
 12035	"Quite so. She has her freedom."
 12036	
 12037	"What CAN be the matter, then? Can you suggest no explanation?"
 12038	
 12039	"I have devised seven separate explanations, each of which would
 12040	cover the facts as far as we know them. But which of these is
 12041	correct can only be determined by the fresh information which we
 12042	shall no doubt find waiting for us. Well, there is the tower of
 12043	the cathedral, and we shall soon learn all that Miss Hunter has
 12044	to tell."
 12045	
 12046	The Black Swan is an inn of repute in the High Street, at no
 12047	distance from the station, and there we found the young lady
 12048	waiting for us. She had engaged a sitting-room, and our lunch
 12049	awaited us upon the table.
 12050	
 12051	"I am so delighted that you have come," she said earnestly. "It
 12052	is so very kind of you both; but indeed I do not know what I
 12053	should do. Your advice will be altogether invaluable to me."
 12054	
 12055	"Pray tell us what has happened to you."
 12056	
 12057	"I will do so, and I must be quick, for I have promised Mr.
 12058	Rucastle to be back before three. I got his leave to come into
 12059	town this morning, though he little knew for what purpose."
 12060	
 12061	"Let us have everything in its due order." Holmes thrust his long
 12062	thin legs out towards the fire and composed himself to listen.
 12063	
 12064	"In the first place, I may say that I have met, on the whole,
 12065	with no actual ill-treatment from Mr. and Mrs. Rucastle. It is
 12066	only fair to them to say that. But I cannot understand them, and
 12067	I am not easy in my mind about them."
 12068	
 12069	"What can you not understand?"
 12070	
 12071	"Their reasons for their conduct. But you shall have it all just
 12072	as it occurred. When I came down, Mr. Rucastle met me here and
 12073	drove me in his dog-cart to the Copper Beeches. It is, as he
 12074	said, beautifully situated, but it is not beautiful in itself,
 12075	for it is a large square block of a house, whitewashed, but all
 12076	stained and streaked with damp and bad weather. There are grounds
 12077	round it, woods on three sides, and on the fourth a field which
 12078	slopes down to the Southampton highroad, which curves past about
 12079	a hundred yards from the front door. This ground in front belongs
 12080	to the house, but the woods all round are part of Lord
 12081	Southerton's preserves. A clump of copper beeches immediately in
 12082	front of the hall door has given its name to the place.
 12083	
 12084	"I was driven over by my employer, who was as amiable as ever,
 12085	and was introduced by him that evening to his wife and the child.
 12086	There was no truth, Mr. Holmes, in the conjecture which seemed to
 12087	us to be probable in your rooms at Baker Street. Mrs. Rucastle is
 12088	not mad. I found her to be a silent, pale-faced woman, much
 12089	younger than her husband, not more than thirty, I should think,
 12090	while he can hardly be less than forty-five. From their
 12091	conversation I have gathered that they have been married about
 12092	seven years, that he was a widower, and that his only child by
 12093	the first wife was the daughter who has gone to Philadelphia. Mr.
 12094	Rucastle told me in private that the reason why she had left them
 12095	was that she had an unreasoning aversion to her stepmother. As
 12096	the daughter could not have been less than twenty, I can quite
 12097	imagine that her position must have been uncomfortable with her
 12098	father's young wife.
 12099	
 12100	"Mrs. Rucastle seemed to me to be colourless in mind as well as
 12101	in feature. She impressed me neither favourably nor the reverse.
 12102	She was a nonentity. It was easy to see that she was passionately
 12103	devoted both to her husband and to her little son. Her light grey
 12104	eyes wandered continually from one to the other, noting every
 12105	little want and forestalling it if possible. He was kind to her
 12106	also in his bluff, boisterous fashion, and on the whole they
 12107	seemed to be a happy couple. And yet she had some secret sorrow,
 12108	this woman. She would often be lost in deep thought, with the
 12109	saddest look upon her face. More than once I have surprised her
 12110	in tears. I have thought sometimes that it was the disposition of
 12111	her child which weighed upon her mind, for I have never met so
 12112	utterly spoiled and so ill-natured a little creature. He is small
 12113	for his age, with a head which is quite disproportionately large.
 12114	His whole life appears to be spent in an alternation between
 12115	savage fits of passion and gloomy intervals of sulking. Giving
 12116	pain to any creature weaker than himself seems to be his one idea
 12117	of amusement, and he shows quite remarkable talent in planning
 12118	the capture of mice, little birds, and insects. But I would
 12119	rather not talk about the creature, Mr. Holmes, and, indeed, he
 12120	has little to do with my story."
 12121	
 12122	"I am glad of all details," remarked my friend, "whether they
 12123	seem to you to be relevant or not."
 12124	
 12125	"I shall try not to miss anything of importance. The one
 12126	unpleasant thing about the house, which struck me at once, was
 12127	the appearance and conduct of the servants. There are only two, a
 12128	man and his wife. Toller, for that is his name, is a rough,
 12129	uncouth man, with grizzled hair and whiskers, and a perpetual
 12130	smell of drink. Twice since I have been with them he has been
 12131	quite drunk, and yet Mr. Rucastle seemed to take no notice of it.
 12132	His wife is a very tall and strong woman with a sour face, as
 12133	silent as Mrs. Rucastle and much less amiable. They are a most
 12134	unpleasant couple, but fortunately I spend most of my time in the
 12135	nursery and my own room, which are next to each other in one
 12136	corner of the building.
 12137	
 12138	"For two days after my arrival at the Copper Beeches my life was
 12139	very quiet; on the third, Mrs. Rucastle came down just after
 12140	breakfast and whispered something to her husband.
 12141	
 12142	"'Oh, yes,' said he, turning to me, 'we are very much obliged to
 12143	you, Miss Hunter, for falling in with our whims so far as to cut
 12144	your hair. I assure you that it has not detracted in the tiniest
 12145	iota from your appearance. We shall now see how the electric-blue
 12146	dress will become you. You will find it laid out upon the bed in
 12147	your room, and if you would be so good as to put it on we should
 12148	both be extremely obliged.'
 12149	
 12150	"The dress which I found waiting for me was of a peculiar shade
 12151	of blue. It was of excellent material, a sort of beige, but it
 12152	bore unmistakable signs of having been worn before. It could not
 12153	have been a better fit if I had been measured for it. Both Mr.
 12154	and Mrs. Rucastle expressed a delight at the look of it, which
 12155	seemed quite exaggerated in its vehemence. They were waiting for
 12156	me in the drawing-room, which is a very large room, stretching
 12157	along the entire front of the house, with three long windows
 12158	reaching down to the floor. A chair had been placed close to the
 12159	central window, with its back turned towards it. In this I was
 12160	asked to sit, and then Mr. Rucastle, walking up and down on the
 12161	other side of the room, began to tell me a series of the funniest
 12162	stories that I have ever listened to. You cannot imagine how
 12163	comical he was, and I laughed until I was quite weary. Mrs.
 12164	Rucastle, however, who has evidently no sense of humour, never so
 12165	much as smiled, but sat with her hands in her lap, and a sad,
 12166	anxious look upon her face. After an hour or so, Mr. Rucastle
 12167	suddenly remarked that it was time to commence the duties of the
 12168	day, and that I might change my dress and go to little Edward in
 12169	the nursery.
 12170	
 12171	"Two days later this same performance was gone through under
 12172	exactly similar circumstances. Again I changed my dress, again I
 12173	sat in the window, and again I laughed very heartily at the funny
 12174	stories of which my employer had an immense r?pertoire, and which
 12175	he told inimitably. Then he handed me a yellow-backed novel, and
 12176	moving my chair a little sideways, that my own shadow might not
 12177	fall upon the page, he begged me to read aloud to him. I read for
 12178	about ten minutes, beginning in the heart of a chapter, and then
 12179	suddenly, in the middle of a sentence, he ordered me to cease and
 12180	to change my dress.
 12181	
 12182	"You can easily imagine, Mr. Holmes, how curious I became as to
 12183	what the meaning of this extraordinary performance could possibly
 12184	be. They were always very careful, I observed, to turn my face
 12185	away from the window, so that I became consumed with the desire
 12186	to see what was going on behind my back. At first it seemed to be
 12187	impossible, but I soon devised a means. My hand-mirror had been
 12188	broken, so a happy thought seized me, and I concealed a piece of
 12189	the glass in my handkerchief. On the next occasion, in the midst
 12190	of my laughter, I put my handkerchief up to my eyes, and was able
 12191	with a little management to see all that there was behind me. I
 12192	confess that I was disappointed. There was nothing. At least that
 12193	was my first impression. At the second glance, however, I
 12194	perceived that there was a man standing in the Southampton Road,
 12195	a small bearded man in a grey suit, who seemed to be looking in
 12196	my direction. The road is an important highway, and there are
 12197	usually people there. This man, however, was leaning against the
 12198	railings which bordered our field and was looking earnestly up. I
 12199	lowered my handkerchief and glanced at Mrs. Rucastle to find her
 12200	eyes fixed upon me with a most searching gaze. She said nothing,
 12201	but I am convinced that she had divined that I had a mirror in my
 12202	hand and had seen what was behind me. She rose at once.
 12203	
 12204	"'Jephro,' said she, 'there is an impertinent fellow upon the
 12205	road there who stares up at Miss Hunter.'
 12206	
 12207	"'No friend of yours, Miss Hunter?' he asked.
 12208	
 12209	"'No, I know no one in these parts.'
 12210	
 12211	"'Dear me! How very impertinent! Kindly turn round and motion to
 12212	him to go away.'
 12213	
 12214	"'Surely it would be better to take no notice.'
 12215	
 12216	"'No, no, we should have him loitering here always. Kindly turn
 12217	round and wave him away like that.'
 12218	
 12219	"I did as I was told, and at the same instant Mrs. Rucastle drew
 12220	down the blind. That was a week ago, and from that time I have
 12221	not sat again in the window, nor have I worn the blue dress, nor
 12222	seen the man in the road."
 12223	
 12224	"Pray continue," said Holmes. "Your narrative promises to be a
 12225	most interesting one."
 12226	
 12227	"You will find it rather disconnected, I fear, and there may
 12228	prove to be little relation between the different incidents of
 12229	which I speak. On the very first day that I was at the Copper
 12230	Beeches, Mr. Rucastle took me to a small outhouse which stands
 12231	near the kitchen door. As we approached it I heard the sharp
 12232	rattling of a chain, and the sound as of a large animal moving
 12233	about.
 12234	
 12235	"'Look in here!' said Mr. Rucastle, showing me a slit between two
 12236	planks. 'Is he not a beauty?'
 12237	
 12238	"I looked through and was conscious of two glowing eyes, and of a
 12239	vague figure huddled up in the darkness.
 12240	
 12241	"'Don't be frightened,' said my employer, laughing at the start
 12242	which I had given. 'It's only Carlo, my mastiff. I call him mine,
 12243	but really old Toller, my groom, is the only man who can do
 12244	anything with him. We feed him once a day, and not too much then,
 12245	so that he is always as keen as mustard. Toller lets him loose
 12246	every night, and God help the trespasser whom he lays his fangs
 12247	upon. For goodness' sake don't you ever on any pretext set your
 12248	foot over the threshold at night, for it's as much as your life
 12249	is worth.'
 12250	
 12251	"The warning was no idle one, for two nights later I happened to
 12252	look out of my bedroom window about two o'clock in the morning.
 12253	It was a beautiful moonlight night, and the lawn in front of the
 12254	house was silvered over and almost as bright as day. I was
 12255	standing, rapt in the peaceful beauty of the scene, when I was
 12256	aware that something was moving under the shadow of the copper
 12257	beeches. As it emerged into the moonshine I saw what it was. It
 12258	was a giant dog, as large as a calf, tawny tinted, with hanging
 12259	jowl, black muzzle, and huge projecting bones. It walked slowly
 12260	across the lawn and vanished into the shadow upon the other side.
 12261	That dreadful sentinel sent a chill to my heart which I do not
 12262	think that any burglar could have done.
 12263
