 12264	"And now I have a very strange experience to tell you. I had, as
 12265	you know, cut off my hair in London, and I had placed it in a
 12266	great coil at the bottom of my trunk. One evening, after the
 12267	child was in bed, I began to amuse myself by examining the
 12268	furniture of my room and by rearranging my own little things.
 12269	There was an old chest of drawers in the room, the two upper ones
 12270	empty and open, the lower one locked. I had filled the first two
 12271	with my linen, and as I had still much to pack away I was
 12272	naturally annoyed at not having the use of the third drawer. It
 12273	struck me that it might have been fastened by a mere oversight,
 12274	so I took out my bunch of keys and tried to open it. The very
 12275	first key fitted to perfection, and I drew the drawer open. There
 12276	was only one thing in it, but I am sure that you would never
 12277	guess what it was. It was my coil of hair.
 12278	
 12279	"I took it up and examined it. It was of the same peculiar tint,
 12280	and the same thickness. But then the impossibility of the thing
 12281	obtruded itself upon me. How could my hair have been locked in
 12282	the drawer? With trembling hands I undid my trunk, turned out the
 12283	contents, and drew from the bottom my own hair. I laid the two
 12284	tresses together, and I assure you that they were identical. Was
 12285	it not extraordinary? Puzzle as I would, I could make nothing at
 12286	all of what it meant. I returned the strange hair to the drawer,
 12287	and I said nothing of the matter to the Rucastles as I felt that
 12288	I had put myself in the wrong by opening a drawer which they had
 12289	locked.
 12290	
 12291	"I am naturally observant, as you may have remarked, Mr. Holmes,
 12292	and I soon had a pretty good plan of the whole house in my head.
 12293	There was one wing, however, which appeared not to be inhabited
 12294	at all. A door which faced that which led into the quarters of
 12295	the Tollers opened into this suite, but it was invariably locked.
 12296	One day, however, as I ascended the stair, I met Mr. Rucastle
 12297	coming out through this door, his keys in his hand, and a look on
 12298	his face which made him a very different person to the round,
 12299	jovial man to whom I was accustomed. His cheeks were red, his
 12300	brow was all crinkled with anger, and the veins stood out at his
 12301	temples with passion. He locked the door and hurried past me
 12302	without a word or a look.
 12303	
 12304	"This aroused my curiosity, so when I went out for a walk in the
 12305	grounds with my charge, I strolled round to the side from which I
 12306	could see the windows of this part of the house. There were four
 12307	of them in a row, three of which were simply dirty, while the
 12308	fourth was shuttered up. They were evidently all deserted. As I
 12309	strolled up and down, glancing at them occasionally, Mr. Rucastle
 12310	came out to me, looking as merry and jovial as ever.
 12311	
 12312	"'Ah!' said he, 'you must not think me rude if I passed you
 12313	without a word, my dear young lady. I was preoccupied with
 12314	business matters.'
 12315	
 12316	"I assured him that I was not offended. 'By the way,' said I,
 12317	'you seem to have quite a suite of spare rooms up there, and one
 12318	of them has the shutters up.'
 12319	
 12320	"He looked surprised and, as it seemed to me, a little startled
 12321	at my remark.
 12322	
 12323	"'Photography is one of my hobbies,' said he. 'I have made my
 12324	dark room up there. But, dear me! what an observant young lady we
 12325	have come upon. Who would have believed it? Who would have ever
 12326	believed it?' He spoke in a jesting tone, but there was no jest
 12327	in his eyes as he looked at me. I read suspicion there and
 12328	annoyance, but no jest.
 12329	
 12330	"Well, Mr. Holmes, from the moment that I understood that there
 12331	was something about that suite of rooms which I was not to know,
 12332	I was all on fire to go over them. It was not mere curiosity,
 12333	though I have my share of that. It was more a feeling of duty--a
 12334	feeling that some good might come from my penetrating to this
 12335	place. They talk of woman's instinct; perhaps it was woman's
 12336	instinct which gave me that feeling. At any rate, it was there,
 12337	and I was keenly on the lookout for any chance to pass the
 12338	forbidden door.
 12339	
 12340	"It was only yesterday that the chance came. I may tell you that,
 12341	besides Mr. Rucastle, both Toller and his wife find something to
 12342	do in these deserted rooms, and I once saw him carrying a large
 12343	black linen bag with him through the door. Recently he has been
 12344	drinking hard, and yesterday evening he was very drunk; and when
 12345	I came upstairs there was the key in the door. I have no doubt at
 12346	all that he had left it there. Mr. and Mrs. Rucastle were both
 12347	downstairs, and the child was with them, so that I had an
 12348	admirable opportunity. I turned the key gently in the lock,
 12349	opened the door, and slipped through.
 12350	
 12351	"There was a little passage in front of me, unpapered and
 12352	uncarpeted, which turned at a right angle at the farther end.
 12353	Round this corner were three doors in a line, the first and third
 12354	of which were open. They each led into an empty room, dusty and
 12355	cheerless, with two windows in the one and one in the other, so
 12356	thick with dirt that the evening light glimmered dimly through
 12357	them. The centre door was closed, and across the outside of it
 12358	had been fastened one of the broad bars of an iron bed, padlocked
 12359	at one end to a ring in the wall, and fastened at the other with
 12360	stout cord. The door itself was locked as well, and the key was
 12361	not there. This barricaded door corresponded clearly with the
 12362	shuttered window outside, and yet I could see by the glimmer from
 12363	beneath it that the room was not in darkness. Evidently there was
 12364	a skylight which let in light from above. As I stood in the
 12365	passage gazing at the sinister door and wondering what secret it
 12366	might veil, I suddenly heard the sound of steps within the room
 12367	and saw a shadow pass backward and forward against the little
 12368	slit of dim light which shone out from under the door. A mad,
 12369	unreasoning terror rose up in me at the sight, Mr. Holmes. My
 12370	overstrung nerves failed me suddenly, and I turned and ran--ran
 12371	as though some dreadful hand were behind me clutching at the
 12372	skirt of my dress. I rushed down the passage, through the door,
 12373	and straight into the arms of Mr. Rucastle, who was waiting
 12374	outside.
 12375	
 12376	"'So,' said he, smiling, 'it was you, then. I thought that it
 12377	must be when I saw the door open.'
 12378	
 12379	"'Oh, I am so frightened!' I panted.
 12380	
 12381	"'My dear young lady! my dear young lady!'--you cannot think how
 12382	caressing and soothing his manner was--'and what has frightened
 12383	you, my dear young lady?'
 12384	
 12385	"But his voice was just a little too coaxing. He overdid it. I
 12386	was keenly on my guard against him.
 12387	
 12388	"'I was foolish enough to go into the empty wing,' I answered.
 12389	'But it is so lonely and eerie in this dim light that I was
 12390	frightened and ran out again. Oh, it is so dreadfully still in
 12391	there!'
 12392	
 12393	"'Only that?' said he, looking at me keenly.
 12394	
 12395	"'Why, what did you think?' I asked.
 12396	
 12397	"'Why do you think that I lock this door?'
 12398	
 12399	"'I am sure that I do not know.'
 12400	
 12401	"'It is to keep people out who have no business there. Do you
 12402	see?' He was still smiling in the most amiable manner.
 12403	
 12404	"'I am sure if I had known--'
 12405	
 12406	"'Well, then, you know now. And if you ever put your foot over
 12407	that threshold again'--here in an instant the smile hardened into
 12408	a grin of rage, and he glared down at me with the face of a
 12409	demon--'I'll throw you to the mastiff.'
 12410	
 12411	"I was so terrified that I do not know what I did. I suppose that
 12412	I must have rushed past him into my room. I remember nothing
 12413	until I found myself lying on my bed trembling all over. Then I
 12414	thought of you, Mr. Holmes. I could not live there longer without
 12415	some advice. I was frightened of the house, of the man, of the
 12416	woman, of the servants, even of the child. They were all horrible
 12417	to me. If I could only bring you down all would be well. Of
 12418	course I might have fled from the house, but my curiosity was
 12419	almost as strong as my fears. My mind was soon made up. I would
 12420	send you a wire. I put on my hat and cloak, went down to the
 12421	office, which is about half a mile from the house, and then
 12422	returned, feeling very much easier. A horrible doubt came into my
 12423	mind as I approached the door lest the dog might be loose, but I
 12424	remembered that Toller had drunk himself into a state of
 12425	insensibility that evening, and I knew that he was the only one
 12426	in the household who had any influence with the savage creature,
 12427	or who would venture to set him free. I slipped in in safety and
 12428	lay awake half the night in my joy at the thought of seeing you.
 12429	I had no difficulty in getting leave to come into Winchester this
 12430	morning, but I must be back before three o'clock, for Mr. and
 12431	Mrs. Rucastle are going on a visit, and will be away all the
 12432	evening, so that I must look after the child. Now I have told you
 12433	all my adventures, Mr. Holmes, and I should be very glad if you
 12434	could tell me what it all means, and, above all, what I should
 12435	do."
 12436	
 12437	Holmes and I had listened spellbound to this extraordinary story.
 12438	My friend rose now and paced up and down the room, his hands in
 12439	his pockets, and an expression of the most profound gravity upon
 12440	his face.
 12441	
 12442	"Is Toller still drunk?" he asked.
 12443	
 12444	"Yes. I heard his wife tell Mrs. Rucastle that she could do
 12445	nothing with him."
 12446	
 12447	"That is well. And the Rucastles go out to-night?"
 12448	
 12449	"Yes."
 12450	
 12451	"Is there a cellar with a good strong lock?"
 12452	
 12453	"Yes, the wine-cellar."
 12454	
 12455	"You seem to me to have acted all through this matter like a very
 12456	brave and sensible girl, Miss Hunter. Do you think that you could
 12457	perform one more feat? I should not ask it of you if I did not
 12458	think you a quite exceptional woman."
 12459	
 12460	"I will try. What is it?"
 12461	
 12462	"We shall be at the Copper Beeches by seven o'clock, my friend
 12463	and I. The Rucastles will be gone by that time, and Toller will,
 12464	we hope, be incapable. There only remains Mrs. Toller, who might
 12465	give the alarm. If you could send her into the cellar on some
 12466	errand, and then turn the key upon her, you would facilitate
 12467	matters immensely."
 12468	
 12469	"I will do it."
 12470	
 12471	"Excellent! We shall then look thoroughly into the affair. Of
 12472	course there is only one feasible explanation. You have been
 12473	brought there to personate someone, and the real person is
 12474	imprisoned in this chamber. That is obvious. As to who this
 12475	prisoner is, I have no doubt that it is the daughter, Miss Alice
 12476	Rucastle, if I remember right, who was said to have gone to
 12477	America. You were chosen, doubtless, as resembling her in height,
 12478	figure, and the colour of your hair. Hers had been cut off, very
 12479	possibly in some illness through which she has passed, and so, of
 12480	course, yours had to be sacrificed also. By a curious chance you
 12481	came upon her tresses. The man in the road was undoubtedly some
 12482	friend of hers--possibly her fianc?--and no doubt, as you wore
 12483	the girl's dress and were so like her, he was convinced from your
 12484	laughter, whenever he saw you, and afterwards from your gesture,
 12485	that Miss Rucastle was perfectly happy, and that she no longer
 12486	desired his attentions. The dog is let loose at night to prevent
 12487	him from endeavouring to communicate with her. So much is fairly
 12488	clear. The most serious point in the case is the disposition of
 12489	the child."
 12490	
 12491	"What on earth has that to do with it?" I ejaculated.
 12492	
 12493	"My dear Watson, you as a medical man are continually gaining
 12494	light as to the tendencies of a child by the study of the
 12495	parents. Don't you see that the converse is equally valid. I have
 12496	frequently gained my first real insight into the character of
 12497	parents by studying their children. This child's disposition is
 12498	abnormally cruel, merely for cruelty's sake, and whether he
 12499	derives this from his smiling father, as I should suspect, or
 12500	from his mother, it bodes evil for the poor girl who is in their
 12501	power."
 12502	
 12503	"I am sure that you are right, Mr. Holmes," cried our client. "A
 12504	thousand things come back to me which make me certain that you
 12505	have hit it. Oh, let us lose not an instant in bringing help to
 12506	this poor creature."
 12507	
 12508	"We must be circumspect, for we are dealing with a very cunning
 12509	man. We can do nothing until seven o'clock. At that hour we shall
 12510	be with you, and it will not be long before we solve the
 12511	mystery."
 12512	
 12513	We were as good as our word, for it was just seven when we
 12514	reached the Copper Beeches, having put up our trap at a wayside
 12515	public-house. The group of trees, with their dark leaves shining
 12516	like burnished metal in the light of the setting sun, were
 12517	sufficient to mark the house even had Miss Hunter not been
 12518	standing smiling on the door-step.
 12519	
 12520	"Have you managed it?" asked Holmes.
 12521	
 12522	A loud thudding noise came from somewhere downstairs. "That is
 12523	Mrs. Toller in the cellar," said she. "Her husband lies snoring
 12524	on the kitchen rug. Here are his keys, which are the duplicates
 12525	of Mr. Rucastle's."
 12526	
 12527	"You have done well indeed!" cried Holmes with enthusiasm. "Now
 12528	lead the way, and we shall soon see the end of this black
 12529	business."
 12530	
 12531	We passed up the stair, unlocked the door, followed on down a
 12532	passage, and found ourselves in front of the barricade which Miss
 12533	Hunter had described. Holmes cut the cord and removed the
 12534	transverse bar. Then he tried the various keys in the lock, but
 12535	without success. No sound came from within, and at the silence
 12536	Holmes' face clouded over.
 12537	
 12538	"I trust that we are not too late," said he. "I think, Miss
 12539	Hunter, that we had better go in without you. Now, Watson, put
 12540	your shoulder to it, and we shall see whether we cannot make our
 12541	way in."
 12542	
 12543	It was an old rickety door and gave at once before our united
 12544	strength. Together we rushed into the room. It was empty. There
 12545	was no furniture save a little pallet bed, a small table, and a
 12546	basketful of linen. The skylight above was open, and the prisoner
 12547	gone.
 12548	
 12549	"There has been some villainy here," said Holmes; "this beauty
 12550	has guessed Miss Hunter's intentions and has carried his victim
 12551	off."
 12552	
 12553	"But how?"
 12554	
 12555	"Through the skylight. We shall soon see how he managed it." He
 12556	swung himself up onto the roof. "Ah, yes," he cried, "here's the
 12557	end of a long light ladder against the eaves. That is how he did
 12558	it."
 12559	
 12560	"But it is impossible," said Miss Hunter; "the ladder was not
 12561	there when the Rucastles went away."
 12562	
 12563	"He has come back and done it. I tell you that he is a clever and
 12564	dangerous man. I should not be very much surprised if this were
 12565	he whose step I hear now upon the stair. I think, Watson, that it
 12566	would be as well for you to have your pistol ready."
 12567	
 12568	The words were hardly out of his mouth before a man appeared at
 12569	the door of the room, a very fat and burly man, with a heavy
 12570	stick in his hand. Miss Hunter screamed and shrunk against the
 12571	wall at the sight of him, but Sherlock Holmes sprang forward and
 12572	confronted him.
 12573	
 12574	"You villain!" said he, "where's your daughter?"
 12575	
 12576	The fat man cast his eyes round, and then up at the open
 12577	skylight.
 12578	
 12579	"It is for me to ask you that," he shrieked, "you thieves! Spies
 12580	and thieves! I have caught you, have I? You are in my power. I'll
 12581	serve you!" He turned and clattered down the stairs as hard as he
 12582	could go.
 12583	
 12584	"He's gone for the dog!" cried Miss Hunter.
 12585	
 12586	"I have my revolver," said I.
 12587	
 12588	"Better close the front door," cried Holmes, and we all rushed
 12589	down the stairs together. We had hardly reached the hall when we
 12590	heard the baying of a hound, and then a scream of agony, with a
 12591	horrible worrying sound which it was dreadful to listen to. An
 12592	elderly man with a red face and shaking limbs came staggering out
 12593	at a side door.
 12594	
 12595	"My God!" he cried. "Someone has loosed the dog. It's not been
 12596	fed for two days. Quick, quick, or it'll be too late!"
 12597	
 12598	Holmes and I rushed out and round the angle of the house, with
 12599	Toller hurrying behind us. There was the huge famished brute, its
 12600	black muzzle buried in Rucastle's throat, while he writhed and
 12601	screamed upon the ground. Running up, I blew its brains out, and
 12602	it fell over with its keen white teeth still meeting in the great
 12603	creases of his neck. With much labour we separated them and
 12604	carried him, living but horribly mangled, into the house. We laid
 12605	him upon the drawing-room sofa, and having dispatched the sobered
 12606	Toller to bear the news to his wife, I did what I could to
 12607	relieve his pain. We were all assembled round him when the door
 12608	opened, and a tall, gaunt woman entered the room.
 12609	
 12610	"Mrs. Toller!" cried Miss Hunter.
 12611	
 12612	"Yes, miss. Mr. Rucastle let me out when he came back before he
 12613	went up to you. Ah, miss, it is a pity you didn't let me know
 12614	what you were planning, for I would have told you that your pains
 12615	were wasted."
 12616	
 12617	"Ha!" said Holmes, looking keenly at her. "It is clear that Mrs.
 12618	Toller knows more about this matter than anyone else."
 12619	
 12620	"Yes, sir, I do, and I am ready enough to tell what I know."
 12621	
 12622	"Then, pray, sit down, and let us hear it for there are several
 12623	points on which I must confess that I am still in the dark."
 12624	
 12625	"I will soon make it clear to you," said she; "and I'd have done
 12626	so before now if I could ha' got out from the cellar. If there's
 12627	police-court business over this, you'll remember that I was the
 12628	one that stood your friend, and that I was Miss Alice's friend
 12629	too.
 12630	
 12631	"She was never happy at home, Miss Alice wasn't, from the time
 12632	that her father married again. She was slighted like and had no
 12633	say in anything, but it never really became bad for her until
 12634	after she met Mr. Fowler at a friend's house. As well as I could
 12635	learn, Miss Alice had rights of her own by will, but she was so
 12636	quiet and patient, she was, that she never said a word about them
 12637	but just left everything in Mr. Rucastle's hands. He knew he was
 12638	safe with her; but when there was a chance of a husband coming
 12639	forward, who would ask for all that the law would give him, then
 12640	her father thought it time to put a stop on it. He wanted her to
 12641	sign a paper, so that whether she married or not, he could use
 12642	her money. When she wouldn't do it, he kept on worrying her until
 12643	she got brain-fever, and for six weeks was at death's door. Then
 12644	she got better at last, all worn to a shadow, and with her
 12645	beautiful hair cut off; but that didn't make no change in her
 12646	young man, and he stuck to her as true as man could be."
 12647	
 12648	"Ah," said Holmes, "I think that what you have been good enough
 12649	to tell us makes the matter fairly clear, and that I can deduce
 12650	all that remains. Mr. Rucastle then, I presume, took to this
 12651	system of imprisonment?"
 12652	
 12653	"Yes, sir."
 12654	
 12655	"And brought Miss Hunter down from London in order to get rid of
 12656	the disagreeable persistence of Mr. Fowler."
 12657	
 12658	"That was it, sir."
 12659	
 12660	"But Mr. Fowler being a persevering man, as a good seaman should
 12661	be, blockaded the house, and having met you succeeded by certain
 12662	arguments, metallic or otherwise, in convincing you that your
 12663	interests were the same as his."
 12664	
 12665	"Mr. Fowler was a very kind-spoken, free-handed gentleman," said
 12666	Mrs. Toller serenely.
 12667	
 12668	"And in this way he managed that your good man should have no
 12669	want of drink, and that a ladder should be ready at the moment
 12670	when your master had gone out."
 12671	
 12672	"You have it, sir, just as it happened."
 12673	
 12674	"I am sure we owe you an apology, Mrs. Toller," said Holmes, "for
 12675	you have certainly cleared up everything which puzzled us. And
 12676	here comes the country surgeon and Mrs. Rucastle, so I think,
 12677	Watson, that we had best escort Miss Hunter back to Winchester,
 12678	as it seems to me that our locus standi now is rather a
 12679	questionable one."
 12680	
 12681	And thus was solved the mystery of the sinister house with the
 12682	copper beeches in front of the door. Mr. Rucastle survived, but
 12683	was always a broken man, kept alive solely through the care of
 12684	his devoted wife. They still live with their old servants, who
 12685	probably know so much of Rucastle's past life that he finds it
 12686	difficult to part from them. Mr. Fowler and Miss Rucastle were
 12687	married, by special license, in Southampton the day after their
 12688	flight, and he is now the holder of a government appointment in
 12689	the island of Mauritius. As to Miss Violet Hunter, my friend
 12690	Holmes, rather to my disappointment, manifested no further
 12691	interest in her when once she had ceased to be the centre of one
 12692	of his problems, and she is now the head of a private school at
 12693	Walsall, where I believe that she has met with considerable success.
 12694	
 12695	
 12696	
 12697
